I watch her square the unused legal pad to the table edge and I think, with a flash of the old contempt that I am relieved to still own: *she came in here believing he'd be the weak link and she's leaving because he was the strongest witness she's faced all year, and she still thinks the interpreter is a wire.* That contempt is load-bearing. I lean on it. As long as I can still feel superior to Cantwell, I have not lost the thing that is me.

Pryor recesses us at 3:10. "We'll resume with the State's next witness Thursday," he says, in the baritone that reaches the back row without effort, and he nods at the gallery in that way that selects them, and as the jury files out he says, "Ms. Okafor-Vance, thank you — you make a hard thing look easy," and he gets the stress right, the silence in the middle of my name that my father spent forty years insisting on, and I incline my head a precise degree and say, "Your Honor," and I do not have an opinion about him. I empty the room out of myself. He is a man who is good to interpreters and there is a banner of his face by the cafeteria and Thursday I will stand in his courtroom again and that is the entire extent of what Gerald Pryor is to me.

Lamar finds my eyes across the well before he goes. He's tired — fourteen-hour-day tired, decade-tired, the specific exhaustion of a man who has spent five days being the most careful person in a room full of people who think care is his deficiency — and he signs to me, just to me, off the record: *good. you held it.* And then, because Lamar Stokes misses nothing, because he has been read wrong his entire life and has therefore become the finest reader I know, he adds, with a small tilt of the head, brows drawing in: *you went somewhere. middle of it. you came back.*

I sign back that it was nothing. A long day.

He looks at me for a moment longer than the lie deserves. Then he gives me the flat nod and goes to find his wife in the hall, and I am left holding the fact that the Deaf witness saw the gap in me that forty hearing people in a courtroom did not. I should not be surprised. It is the entire principle of the thing I am about to spend my nights learning. The Deaf always see the gap. It is the hearing who can't.

* * *

Desmond Lockwood is in the second-floor hallway by the vending machines, which is where he ambushes people, because the second floor is where the interpreter-scheduling office is and everyone in the building eventually has to go fight with it. He has a different folder under his arm today, a thin one, and he has the face of a man who has been rehearsing a sentence.

"How's Webb," he says, which is not what he came to say.

"The State's going to win it. Cantwell's good and she's losing anyway."

"And you?" He says it lightly, the way you'd hand someone a step they didn't ask for. "You good?"

And here is where I do the thing the bible of my own conscience will later make me watch myself do, frame by frame, the way I am learning to watch the tape. I build the case. Before Desmond has even told me what he wants, before he has laid one fact on me, I am already on my feet in the courtroom of myself, addressing a jury, and the defendant I am defending — the one client I cannot be neutral about, the one I will lie for, the one whose acquittal I need more than I need air — is me.

"I want to set your expectation," I say. My voice is level. My voice is always level; it is half of what I'm paid for. "I've been watching the early reel. The booking, the setup. The picture is — it's a digitized VHS from a county interview room in oh-nine, Desmond. The lighting in those rooms was a war crime. He's backlit half the time. The angle's wrong. And his signing —" I hear myself reach for it and I reach for it anyway, because it is the strongest brick in the wall and the wall is the only thing keeping the building up — "his signing is non-standard. Idiosyncratic. He fingerspells things that have perfectly good signs, he's got idioms I've never seen, he —"

"Ruth."

"— I'm telling you so you don't build a theory of the case on a tape that won't hold the weight you want to put on it. You're going to have an expert up there saying *the interpretation was flawed,* and the State's going to put the lighting and the angle and the non-standard source in front of the jury and your expert's going to have to concede every one of them, and *anyone* would have rendered that footage the way I —"

I stop. Because I have heard it. *Anyone would have rendered it the way I did.* I came down this stairwell two minutes ago not knowing what is on the tape at 14:22 — I have not watched 14:22, I have refused to watch 14:22, I have been *methodical,* I have told myself I am being methodical — and I have just, unprompted, with the whole force of my professional authority, delivered to a wrongful-conviction lawyer the closing argument for the defense of a render I have not yet seen.

Desmond has stopped too. He is looking at me the way you look at a witness who has just impeached herself and doesn't know it yet, and to his credit — to the frayed, tired, decent credit of a man who carries more cases than a person can carry — he does not say what he is thinking, which is: *I haven't told you anything is wrong with it. Why are you defending it?*

What he says instead is quiet. "I didn't ask you whether the render was sound." He shifts the thin folder. "I asked you whether you were good."

The hallway does the thing the courtroom floor did under me on day one, the half-second wrongness, the level going out of the world. I have been so busy lawyering for the render that I missed the question entirely. He didn't bring me the case to vindicate. He never did. I've known that since the hallway outside 4B, in some chamber of myself I keep sealed and stacked something heavy on — known it the way you know a diagnosis before the doctor finishes the runway. He didn't hire the original interpreter to prove the original interpretation was good.

You hire the original interpreter to break it.

"Watch the tape, Ruth," Desmond says. Not unkindly. He's already turning, already late for the scheduling office and the next case and the mortgage and the thing after that. "Watch fourteen twenty-two. Then tell me whether I'm wrong to file." He stops at the corner. "And if you can't tell me — if you watch it and you genuinely can't tell me what the man said — then you understand the problem better than my expert does, and I want you, not the expert, and you should be afraid, because I'm going to ask you to say that out loud in a room."

He's gone before I can build anything to put between us. I stand by the vending machines with my contempt for Cantwell already cooling in my hands, no longer enough to hold the building up, and I understand — too late, a half-second too late, three seconds too late, the lag of my whole life arriving at once — that the question that came off its hinge in the courtroom and the question Desmond just left in the hallway are the same question, and it has been loose in me since a man held a yellowed tape into the cold light eight days ago and I opened my mouth to defend myself and asked, instead, *faithful to what.*

* * *

I take the rapid home because I do not trust myself to drive, which is a sentence I have never had occasion to think before, and which I file, with a kind of clinical horror, as the first thing the tape has changed in my body that I cannot smooth off.

The lake is doing its gray thing past the train windows, the flat iron color it goes in the hour before an April storm that can't decide to be snow, and I hold my own hands in my lap and look at them. My father's hands, more or less — the long fingers, the squared-off nails he kept squared-off so that a fingertip placed on a drawing meant *exactly here* and not approximately here. *A tolerance of one millimeter,* he used to say, with the particular contempt of a man who had immigrated into a country he found sloppy, *is the difference between a bridge and a funeral.* He said it about welds and load paths and the men who signed off on them. He did not say it about hands that voice the dead and the accused. He did not know that his daughter would grow up to work to a tolerance finer than a millimeter — to the width of one finger, the direction of one movement — and would spend twenty-six years certain she was always, always, within tolerance.

I have a tape in my apartment that is going to tell me whether that was ever true.

I make tea I don't drink. I stand at the window a while. I am, I notice, doing all the things a person does to not press a button, and I despise the performance even as I cannot stop performing it, and at some point the despising becomes its own kind of permission, because I would rather press the button than keep being the kind of woman who circles it.

The television is still cued where I left it on day six, cued and waiting, the digitized file open, the timecode burned white into the lower corner of a frame from fifteen years ago: a county interview room the color of a bad tooth, a metal table, a young man I helped the State of Ohio put away for the rest of his life sitting on the far side of it with his hands in his lap exactly the way mine are in my lap now. The frame is paused at 14:09. Thirteen seconds short of the number Desmond said. I have, without deciding to, cued it to thirteen seconds before the worst thing I have ever done — close enough to make the not-watching a lie, far enough to keep the lie technically true. Even my cowardice is precise. Even now I am within tolerance of something.

I pick up the remote.

My thumb finds the button by the shape of it, the way my hands find everything, by shape and not by looking, because looking away from the source is the one sin I have never committed — and I do not press it. Not yet. I sit on the edge of the couch with the remote in my hand and the young dead-eyed shape of Tobias Rein thirteen seconds from moving, and I am three seconds behind a sentence that has not started, holding nothing in either hand, and for the first time in twenty-six years the irreversible thing is not a word I am about to say into a record.

It is a thing I am about to see, and not be able to un-see, and have to render — to the court, to Desmond, to a man dying in a hearing prison, to my father's squared-off nails, to the oath I raised my hand and swore this morning and meant — faithfully.

Faithful to what.

I press play.


# Chapter Six — Fourteen Twenty-Two

I have a protocol for tape, and I follow it that Saturday the way I'd follow it for anyone — which is the first lie I tell myself, because there is no anyone, there is only this, and I have cleared a whole day for it the way you clear a day for a surgery you are both the patient and the surgeon for.

The protocol is this. Big monitor, not the laptop — twenty-seven inches, because a hand is small and a finger is smaller and the whole case, if there is a case, lives in something the width of a finger. Curtains drawn, because the lake throws an afternoon glare across the glass and I will not lose a frame to my own east-facing windows. A legal pad I will not write on. The remote with the jog wheel, the one that lets me step frame by frame, the one I bought for exactly this kind of work and have used maybe nine times in twelve years. Water. The thermostat low, because I run hot when I concentrate and I want nothing in my body to distract from what's in my eyes. And the timecode visible in the corner, burned into the image by whatever county contractor digitized this in whatever basement, white numerals juddering against a 2009 interview room the color of old margarine.

I cue it to 14:00 and give myself twenty-two seconds of runway, because you cannot read the front of a sentence without the back of the one before it, and that is true on a tape exactly the way it is true in a courtroom, and I have spent twenty-six years being good at precisely this and I want that on the record before I describe what I did with it.

Here is the room. A table, bolted. A detective half in frame, a thick patient man named — the label doesn't say, the report would, I don't reach for the report. And Tobias. Thirty-one years old on this tape, which makes him a different man than the one Lockwood says is dying in Grafton now, and younger than I was the last time I let myself look at him, and his hands are already moving when I press play, mid-thought, because the camera caught him in the middle of a life the way cameras do.

I'd forgotten his hands. That's a strange thing to forget about a person whose hands were, for ninety hours across a trial, the entire content of my professional attention — but I had filed him under *closed*, and you don't keep the hands of the closed. They come back now and they are *wrong* in the specific way I'd told myself, in 2009, was simply non-standard. They are wrong the way a man builds a house with three of the tools and improvises the rest. He fingerspells where a fluent signer would have a sign — spells it fast and slurred and then, distrusting the spelling, makes a shape near it, an invented shape, his own, a sign that exists nowhere but in the private grammar of a man who was given his language late and incomplete and built the rest himself out of want. He topicalizes and then abandons the topic. He sets a referent in the space to his left and then, two sentences on, reaches for it on his right, because the discipline of holding a locus still was never drilled into him, was drilled *out* of him, by people who wanted his mouth instead of his hands.

I knew all this in 2009. I want to be honest. I knew his ASL was deprived. I rendered him anyway, cleanly, because that was the job, and I was good at the job, and a good interpreter does not leave a Deaf man stranded in front of a jury by reproducing his hesitations and his gaps and his invented signs as hesitations and gaps in English — you'd be handing the jury a reason to think him slow, evasive, less than. You give him fluency. You give him your fluency, on loan, because the alternative is cruelty.

That is what I told myself.

That is, I am beginning to understand, also a description of the crime.

I let the runway play. The detective's question — relayed, in 2009, by me; I can see the back of my own younger head at the edge of frame, a thing I am not ready to feel anything about — lands, and Tobias answers, and I voice him in my own head as I watch, the way you can't not, the way a musician can't watch a score without hearing it, and my younger render and my present render come out the same for twenty seconds and I feel, God help me, a flush of something that is almost pride. *Still right. See. Still right.* Twenty seconds of a man and his interpreter in agreement across fifteen years, and I am vain enough that it warms me, and I will pay for that warmth in about four seconds.

Because at 14:19 the detective leans in, and his shoulder comes across the frame, and he asks the thing the whole interview has been walking toward — *tell me what happened with the knife* — and Tobias goes still the way Lamar went still on Tuesday, except Lamar's stillness was contempt on a leash and this stillness is something I don't have a word for, a man at the edge of the only account he has of the worst night of his life, in a language that was never finished being given to him.

And then he shows them.

* * *

I am going to describe what is on the tape, and I am going to try to describe only what is on the tape, which turns out to be the hardest discipline I have ever attempted, because I keep wanting to describe what I *gave the court,* and they are not the same thing, and the whole horror of this Saturday is the size of the not-same.

He builds it in the air. He sets the dead man — he doesn't have the man's name-sign, or never had it, so he spells it once, badly, then plants a referent for him in the space at his own right side and leaves him there. He sets himself. He sets, between them, low, the table — the bolted table is in the room with us but he builds a different table, the table from that night, in the air. And then his right hand becomes a thing.

A flat hand, fingers together, edge down. A classifier. In his idiolect I can't be certain of the handshape the way I'd be certain of a fluent signer's — and I note that, I note it the way you note a measurement you'll have to take twice — but it is depicting a held object, something with an edge, moving through the space he's built, and the question that the entire rest of my life is going to be organized around is the question of *whose hand it is in* and *which way it travels,* and I step the jog wheel back and watch it again.

The hand moves. Toward the referent on his right — the dead man — or *from* it. The path of the movement encodes the subject and the object; this is not interpretation, this is the grammar, this is what the language *is;* a verb in his hands carries who-did-what-to-whom in the direction it travels, the same handshape going one way meaning *I drove this into him* and going the other way meaning *this came at me,* and the difference between those two sentences is the difference between a man who killed and a man who was nearly killed, and it is, on this tape, in this light, in this man's deprived and improvised and half-given grammar, the width of one finger.

I told the jury he drove it in.

I step back. I watch it again.

The hand comes up — and here is where the role-shift lives, here is the thing I have to read and cannot fully read, because in a fluent signer the body would *commit* to a character, would become Tobias-doing or become the-man-doing, the shoulders would turn and the gaze would set and you would know whose body you were inside — but Tobias's role-shift is partial, is a half-turn, is a man who was never taught the full grammar of becoming-another and so inhabits the moment ambiguously, occupying both bodies and neither, so that when the edge-hand moves I genuinely cannot tell, frame by frame, by-the-book, whether I am watching Tobias's account of his own arm or his account of the dead man's arm coming at him across a table in a kitchen fifteen years gone.

I could not tell in 2009 either.

I want to be very clear, because this is the part I will be cross-examined on for the rest of my professional life and possibly my actual one: I could not tell, and I told them anyway. I resolved it. I am a professional and the job is to resolve it, the job is to not stand in a courtroom and say *the witness's grammar is ambiguous as to agency,* because that sentence acquits no one and convicts no one and helps nobody and makes you look like you can't do the work — so you read the room and you read the man and you read the fifteen other facts the State has stacked up and you commit to the reading, you give them a clean declarative English sentence with a subject and a verb and an object all facing the same direction, and the court reporter's fingers don't even hesitate, and a flat hand with an edge becomes *he stabbed him,* and the jury writes it down, and it is the only record there will ever be of what those two seconds meant, because I am the record, because there was no one else who could read the hands and so my reading *became* the hands.

I step the wheel back. The white numerals stutter. 14:21. 14:22. The edge-hand rises and moves and I watch it move toward and away and toward, the same two frames, the same ambiguity, breeding.

And then I do the thing the reader should not let me do, the thing I am only able to confess because I am writing this down a long time afterward and can see my own hands in it the way I could not see Tobias's: I start to build the defense.

* * *

The lighting is bad. That's the first brick, and it is *true,* the lighting in this room is genuinely terrible, a single overhead throwing his hands into a soup of shadow, the kind of light no interpreter should ever have been asked to work under, and I feel the relief of it arrive in my chest like warmth — *of course, look at this light, who could read agency in this light* — and I let myself have the relief for almost a full second before I notice that the warmth is the exact same warmth I felt twenty minutes ago when my renders matched, and I distrust it now, because I am learning, this Saturday, to distrust the moments that comfort me.

The angle is bad. Second brick. The camera is over the detective's shoulder, three-quarter, not square to Tobias the way an interpreter needs to be square — I'd have been square to him in the room, I'd have had the full plane of his signing space, and this camera flattens it, and a flattened signing space loses depth, and depth is where the direction lives, so *of course* I can't read it now, I'm reading a photograph of a sculpture. True. Also true. Also a wall I am building between myself and a man in a cell.

And the signing is non-standard. Third brick, the load-bearing one, the one I lean the whole structure on. His ASL was deprived. His role-shift was partial. His classifiers were idiosyncratic. Any interpreter — *any* interpreter, I tell myself, and I can hear the cadence of it, it has the rhythm of a closing argument, it has the rhythm of a thing rehearsed — any interpreter working live, in that light, at that angle, with a source that broken, would have rendered it as I rendered it. Would have committed to the convictable reading. Would have given the court a clean sentence. I did nothing a competent professional wouldn't have done. The system asked me for fluency and I delivered fluency and *fluency is the job.*

I have, by 14:24, constructed an entire and frankly excellent defense, and the worst thing about it — the thing that will keep me up past one in the morning when I finally stop the tape — is that *it is good.* It would work. I have written closing arguments worse than the one I just gave myself, and watched them work, and watched men go home on them. Every brick is true. The light was bad. The angle was bad. The signing was deprived. A reasonable interpreter would have done as I did.

And underneath the excellent defense, the way water moves under ice, is the one fact the defense is built to keep me from touching, which is that a reasonable interpreter doing exactly what I did would have produced exactly this — a clean, fluent, sworn, irreversible English sentence about a man driving a knife into another man — over a source that does not, when you actually read it, when you stop your own mouth and just *watch the hands,* say that. Or say its opposite. Or say either. The defense is not wrong that anyone would have done it. The defense is just silent on the question of whether the thing everyone would have done was *true.*

I don't get there yet. I want to be honest about the order of operations. At 14:24 on that first Saturday I am still mostly in the defense, still mostly warm, still mostly telling myself *non-standard, bad light, anyone would have* — and I reach to step the wheel forward, to move past it, to get to the part of the tape where I am sure, where the renders match again and the warmth is clean —

and my hand stops on the wheel, and does not press it, and that is the first thing my body does that my mind has not authorized, and I sit with the white numerals frozen at 14:22 and an edge-hand caught mid-rise on a twenty-seven-inch screen, and somewhere underneath the ice the water keeps moving.

* * *

The room in 1991 was the color of old margarine too. I have only just noticed that, fifteen years into reading interview rooms for a living and thirty-three years out from that one — that the room where I learned what hands could do and the room where Tobias learned what mine had done are painted, in my memory, the same institutional non-color, the color rooms are when no one is meant to be happy in them for long.

Meg was propped at thirty degrees, which the nurse had explained to me was for her lungs, and which Meg had explained to me, with her good hand, was so she could see the door — because a woman who has lived her whole life watching the room, who navigates by eyes the way the hearing navigate by ears, does not lie flat in a strange place and let the world arrive at her back. The stroke had taken her right side and most of her left-side fine control, so her signing, that week, was a ruin of itself, was the great exacting signer I'd grown up under reduced to broad strokes and a face — but her face still worked, her face had always done half the grammar anyway, and her eyes worked, and her eyes that afternoon were on the door because the door was where the doctor would come from with the thing none of us had said yet.

I was nineteen. I had been interpreting for Meg, informally, since I was eleven and formally since the stroke, because the hospital's on-call interpreter was a man Meg had taken one look at and refused — *his hands are loud,* she'd told me, *he signs like he's selling me something* — and so it was me, her brother's girl, the one whose hands she had built sign by sign across a childhood, standing in the corner of an old-margarine room being the only road between my aunt and the men who would tell her how much of her life was left.

The doctor came through the door she was watching. He was not unkind. I want to be fair to him the way I am trying, too late, to be fair to the version of me at the edge of the 2009 frame: he was not unkind, he was just *blunt,* he was a man who had learned that softness wastes time and false hope is a cruelty, and he had a number, and he had a recommendation that followed from the number, and he sat on the rolling stool and brought himself to Meg's eye level, which I noticed and was grateful for, and he looked at her and not at me, which is correct, which is what you're supposed to do, you speak to the Deaf person and not to the interpreter, and he said the thing.

He said it plainly. He said it the way my father would have given a tolerance — a number, a consequence, the bridge or the funeral, no ornament. And he watched Meg's face for it to land, and Meg watched mine, because his words did not exist for her until my hands made them, because in that room, for that sentence, I was not a girl who loved her. I was the only thing the words could come through. I was the pane of glass.

My hands came up.

I have built my whole life on what I did next, and what I built was a vow, and the vow was a lie, and I have just spent a Saturday afternoon watching the same shape of moment play out in another margarine room with another non-standard signer and another life on the line and my own younger head at the edge of the frame — but I don't know any of that yet, not the connection, not on this first watch. On this first watch the memory is just there, uninvited, the way the dead come up the stairs in you when you've spent a day looking at the past. My hands came up in 1991 to render a doctor's blunt number to the woman who taught me what hands were for.

The fragment stops there. I make it stop there. I am not ready, this Saturday, to watch my own hands move in that room — I have just spent two hours watching Tobias's, and I have reached the edge of how much hand I can read in one day — so I let the door close on it, the doctor leaning in, Meg's eyes coming off the door and onto me, my hands lifted and not yet falling.

There will be time. That is what I tell myself, and it is the truest and the cruelest thing I tell myself all day, because there will be, there will be far more time with that room than I want, and it is going to cost me everything I think I learned in it.

* * *

I come back to the apartment and the lake and the low thermostat and the frozen frame the way you come back from a long underwater. It is past six. The light behind the curtains has gone from glare to nothing; I have lost the whole day and not noticed, which is the only mercy in it.

14:22 still sits there. The edge-hand still rises. The numerals still stutter when I touch the wheel, which I do, once, lightly, before I make myself stop.

I watch it a third time without the defense.

That's the only thing I do differently, and it is everything. I do not build the light or the angle or the deprivation; I sit with my mouth shut and I do not let myself voice him, do not let the English assemble, do not resolve a single thing — I just watch a flat hand with an edge rise in bad light over a table that isn't there, moving toward a man or away from him, in the grammar of someone who was never given enough grammar to make it certain, and I let it be exactly as uncertain as it is.

And it does not say what I said it said.

I notice that I have stopped breathing in the deliberate way and started holding it in the other way, the way you hold your breath at the top of a stair you've just understood isn't there.

I don't say the word. I want that on the record too — that even now, even at six in the evening with the day gone and the defense set aside and the hand rising in front of me for the fortieth time, I do not let myself form the word *wrong,* because the word *wrong* is a verdict and I am the one who renders verdicts, not the one who receives them, and to say *I was wrong* is to say *the record is wrong,* and the record is the only thing I have ever been, and a pane of glass cannot crack itself by looking.

So I don't say it.

I just reach over, in the dark, and turn the monitor off — not pause, off, the whole screen gone to black with my own face arriving faintly in the dead glass where Tobias's hand had been — and I sit in the unlit room with my legal pad on which I have written, across an entire day, in the careful unhurried hand my father gave me, exactly nothing.

Then I pick up my phone, and I find the thread with the CODA archivist who thinks I am a presumptuous hearing woman and is not entirely wrong, and I type the thing I have spent twenty-six years never needing to type, the sentence that ends the part of my life in which I was the best reader in any room, and I look at it for a long time before I send it.

*I need a second pair of eyes on something. A native pair. Can you read a tape with me.*


# Chapter Seven — The Width of One Finger

The archive is on the fourth floor of a building the university forgot it owned, above a shuttered dental clinic, and you reach Bee Bellamy by an elevator with a handwritten sign taped over the call button — OUT OF ORDER SINCE FEB, USE STAIRS, SORRY — so that by the time I get to her door I am winded, which I resent, and holding a thumb drive in a sandwich bag like a man delivering a kidney, which I also resent, and I have rehearsed in the stairwell exactly how I am going to frame this so that it does not sound like what it is.

What it is: I cannot read my own work anymore and I have come to a child to be told.

I'd put it differently to Lockwood. I'd put it differently to myself, all the way up four flights. *A second read. Standard practice. You confer; you check the source against the render; any competent shop teams it out.* That is true, and it is the kind of true I keep in the front pocket of my coat where I can reach it. The other true — that I have watched 14:22 sixteen times in three days and gone still at it and could not say the word *wrong* and could not say the word *right,* and that I am here because she is faster than me and I have known it for two days and hated knowing it — that one I leave in the stairwell.

She opens the door before I knock. She felt the floor move under my feet, probably; you learn the building's vibrations when sound isn't the thing you watch for. Her parents are both Deaf, she signs before she speaks, and she has the unbothered fluency of a person for whom this language was never a second language, never a credential, never a cathedral she built — just the air her house breathed. I have spent thirty-three years getting good at ASL. Bee Bellamy got it for free, in the crib, the way I got English, and some ungenerous part of me has never forgiven her the ease.

"You look like you climbed the stairs," she says. Out loud, for me, which is a courtesy and also a small needle — *I clocked your weakness on the way in.* "Welcome to the analog wing. Everything down here is dying. It's a hospice for media." She holds the door. "You brought the file?"

I hold up the sandwich bag.

She looks at the sandwich bag. She does not say anything about the sandwich bag. She lets it sit there in the air between us, the bag, my whole hearing carefulness rendered in Ziploc, and I feel my ears go hot, and I think: *she is twenty-seven and she has already won the first exchange and we have not sat down.*

* * *

The room is two long tables, a wall of labeled drives, a rack of decks for formats I haven't seen since I was the one in the booking room — Hi8, MiniDV, the institutional VHS with the typed labels gone the color of a smoker's ceiling. There is a monitor the size of a door. There is a window with lake light coming gray through it, the flat aluminum light of a Cleveland February that makes everything look like evidence.

She doesn't make small talk while she loads it. I'll give her that. She slots the drive, pulls the file, scrubs to the head of the reel without asking me where, and I realize she already knows the timecode, she has had this footage in her hands for weeks, she *digitized* it, this is her tape before it was ever mine again, and I am the visitor here. In my courtroom I am furniture with a face and the room arranges itself around my sightline. In Bee's room I am a woman who climbed the stairs.

"Before you say anything," she says — signing now, both of us in it, the way the room wants — *I read this already. When I scanned it. You don't sit with footage for a week, frame-stepping a man's hands, and not read it. So before you perform the neutral expert who's just here for a second opinion* — she lets the sign for *neutral* go flat and ironic, a little dead in the wrist — *tell me what you think you saw. Not what's defensible. What you saw.*

There it is. The thing I came up the stairs to avoid.

"Fourteen twenty-two," I say. Out loud. I don't know why I reach for English; the room is in ASL and she's faster in it and reaching for English is reaching for the front pocket of my coat. "Run it. I want you cold. I don't want to put it in your eyes."

She tips her head — *fine* — and rolls it, and the lake light and the dental clinic and my burning ears all go away, because the work starts, and the work is the one place I have never been a coward.

* * *

Here is the tape.

A small room the county painted the green of an old chalkboard. A table. Tobias Rein at thirty-one, before fifteen years took the weight off him, before the inside hollowed him out — broad then, dark-eyed, his hands moving in the wrong-footed, over-spelled, idiosyncratic way that I read in 2009 as *non-standard but renderable* and have started, in the last three days, to read as a man trying to play a violin with two strings.

He is telling them about the night. A detective off-frame is asking, through me — through the me of fifteen years ago, a voice I can hear under the picture, thirty-six and certain — *and then what happened.*

And Tobias builds it.

He sets the other man down to his right. Plants him there in the air, a locus, and his eyes go to that empty place to his right before his hands do, every time, so that the dead man — not dead yet, in the green room, just a man at the edge of the table that isn't there — lives in a specific square foot of space off Tobias's right shoulder for the rest of the account. He sets himself center. He builds the apartment around them the way Lamar built Lorain Avenue, the way every fluent signer builds a room before they tell you what broke in it.

Then the hand.

There is a knife in this. Everyone agrees there was a knife; the knife is the one fact the night left in the world that a hearing court could hold. Tobias's right hand takes the shape of it — index out, the depicting handshape for a thin blade, a CL:1 doing the work — and the hand moves, and *this is the place,* this is 14:22, this is the second and a half on which a man has spent fifteen years, and Bee freezes it before I can say so.

She doesn't freeze it where I freeze it.

I freeze it at the contact — at the end of the movement, where the blade-hand arrives at the locus off his right shoulder, because that is where my eye has lived for three days, the arrival, *did it land, who landed it.* Bee freezes it three frames earlier. At the origin. Before the movement. And she sits back, and she looks at it, the held frame, his hand in the shape of a knife at the start of a path it hasn't traveled yet, and she signs one word, slow, so I can't pretend to misread it.

*Whose.*

"What?"

*Whose hand.* She points at the screen, at the blade-shape, and her brows are down, the wh-question grammar sitting in her face — not a yes-or-no, a *which,* an interrogation. *You're watching where it goes. Watch where it starts.* She frame-steps it forward, one click, two — the hand rotating into the path — and she role-shifts in her chair, just her shoulders, becoming Tobias becoming the blade, and I see it, I see the thing I have been too far down the path to see.

He shifts.

A quarter-turn of the torso, so slight the bad light nearly eats it, so slight that fifteen years ago in a green room under a dying fluorescent I read straight through it — and a directional verb does not travel the way I rendered it. I rendered it *out.* Away from his body, toward the locus, toward the man off his right shoulder. *I-do-this-to-him.* The hand goes there, the man is there, the blade is there, subject-verb-object, the order you can convict in.

But the torque is wrong for that. The role-shift puts the blade in the *other* body for a frame — he is being the man, the man at the table-edge, and the hand with the knife in it comes *across,* toward the space where Tobias's own center was, and then there is the thing my eye has lived on for three days, the arrival, the contact, the end of the path — and read from where Bee froze it, from the origin and the turn, it is not a sentence I voiced in 2009.

In 2009 I voiced: *I cut him.*

From the origin, with the role-shift, with the quarter-turn that the light almost ate, it could as easily be: *he came at me with it.* The blade starting in the other hand. The path reversed. The same handshape. The same square foot of air. The width of one finger between a man who killed and a man who was being killed, and the finger is *his torso,* the few degrees of a shoulder turning, and I gave the court the version that travels left to right because left to right is how English reads and the body in the chair was speaking a grammar that does not read in any direction English has a word for.

I don't say any of this. I am aware that my hands have gone still in my lap, which on me reads, to her, the way Tobias's stillness read to me — not absence. The leash.

* * *

Bee is watching my face, not the screen. She has stopped frame-stepping.

*You see it,* she signs. Not a question. *I watched you see it just now. You went the color you went on the stairs.*

"I see an ambiguity," I say. English again, and I hear myself reach for the word the way you reach for a railing. "It's — the angle's bad, the light's bad, his idiolect is non-standard, there's no clean role-shift boundary, a reasonable interpreter could read it either —"

*Stop.* She doesn't sign it hard. She signs it almost gently, which is worse. *You're doing the thing.*

"What thing."

*The thing where you build the case.* She leans forward. Her signing tightens, gets economical, gets — I know this register, I've used this register on the stand, it's the register of a person who has stopped being polite and started being exact. *"The light was bad." "His signing was non-standard." You've said three sentences and all three of them are a lawyer's. You're not telling me what's on the tape. You're telling me why it isn't your fault that what's on the tape is what's on the tape.* She sits back. *My dad does this when he's caught. Everybody does it. I just didn't think you'd do it to me, because you're supposed to be the one who doesn't round.*

That lands somewhere I keep things. I let it. I'm proud, I'm vain about exactly the thing she just named, and the proof of the vanity is how badly I want to tell her she's wrong about the light — the light *was* bad — and how I know, even as I want to, that the light being bad and my having rounded are not the two ends of a contradiction. They are the same sentence. The light was bad and so I should have flagged it and instead I made it fluent. A bad source is exactly the source you do not smooth. I smoothed it. The smoothing is the thing the bad light should have stopped and instead the bad light was my permission.

"He was thirty-one," I say. It comes out smaller than I want. "I was thirty-six. I'd done a hundred of these. I read it the way the room needed it read, and I called that competence, and I have called it competence for fifteen years."

Bee doesn't soften. I'll write that down somewhere as the truest thing about her: she had every opening, right there, to be kind, and she did not take it, because she is not in this room to manage my feelings about my hands. She's in it for the man in the green room.

*So whose hand was it,* she signs.

"I don't know."

*No.* She points at the frozen frame, the blade-shape at the origin, the quarter-turn caught mid-torque. *That's not what I asked. That one — I don't know either. Maybe nobody knows. That's his to know and the tape's too sick to say.* Her hands come down flat. *I asked whose hand decided. The one in the green room you can't read, or the one in the booth that read it anyway and called it a sentence.*

* * *

I have built a life on a wall, and the wall has a name, and the name is *I only carry it across.* I am the pane of glass. I add nothing, I take nothing, a pane of glass cannot be guilty of the weather. I said almost exactly this to Lockwood eight days ago with a clean conscience. And here is a twenty-seven-year-old in a media hospice above a dead dentist, and she has not lectured me, she has not said one word about hearing privilege or the system or authorship, she has asked me a question I cannot answer, and the question has my whole cathedral in it.

*Why were you the one,* she's asking, under the asking. Not *did you make a mistake.* I could survive *did you make a mistake;* a mistake is a thing a faithful instrument has done *to* the truth, the truth still out there intact, me merely having failed to convey it. What she's asking is worse and I feel the floor of it: *why does a man's account of the worst night of his life become true at the moment your mouth closes around it and not before — why is the version that goes in the record the one that left your body and not the one that left his.*

The honest answer is that someone has to. The court can't read his hands. The stenographer can't take a torso turning four degrees. The record only keeps what can be typed, and I am the place where his hands become typeable, and the typing is the trial, and I knew all of this when I swore the oath and I called the knowing *neutrality.*

"Run it once more," I say. "From your mark. The origin."

She does. The green room. The man set down off the right shoulder. The blade-hand. The quarter-turn the light almost eats. The path that, watched from the start, comes *across.* And under it, faint, the ghost of my younger voice, thirty-six and to tolerance, laying down *I cut him* so clean the stenographer's fingers never hesitated.

I watch it the way you'd watch a thing you did, because I am watching a thing I did. Not the night. I wasn't at the night; the night is his, irreducibly, in a language nobody in that green room could read and maybe not even him. But the sentence — the sentence is mine. I authored the sentence. The jury convicted on the sentence. The sentence has a man in a cell who is dying in it, and I made the sentence out of a torso turning four degrees and a confidence I had organized my entire life around deserving.

*Faithful,* I'd said, this morning, four thousand mornings, my right hand up. *Faithful and accurate.* I'd meant it more than the lawyers meant theirs.

Faithful to what.

* * *

"There's something else," Bee says.

She's scrubbed back, all the way past 14:22, past the night, to the booking — the first minutes, the setup, Tobias being walked in, the off-frame voices arranging the room. She stops at 03:40 and slows it.

"Early," she says. "Before your voice really comes in, before the interview proper. There's a second person. Off-frame, camera-right. Somebody's signing to him — relaying — and then by six minutes in they're gone, and it's just you for the rest of it." She frame-steps. At the very edge of the picture, a hand. A wrist. A piece of a sleeve, signing inward, toward Tobias, and then a gap in the footage where the tape was stopped and restarted — the timecode jumps — and after the jump the edge of frame is empty and stays empty. "Scheduling thing, probably. They swap people. But it's weird they'd swap mid-setup." She shrugs. "Anyway. Not your fourteen twenty-two. Just — somebody was there at three-forty who isn't there by six."

I look at the hand at the edge of the frame. The wrist. The sleeve. It means nothing to me. It is a scheduling artifact, an interpreter-services swap, the kind of thing that happens forty times a week because the per-diem and the calendar and the budget do not care who is in the room as long as the line item closes. I file it where I file things that don't matter. *Somebody was there at the top who isn't there at the bottom.* I will not think about it again for a long time, and when I do, it will not be a scheduling artifact anymore, and I will be sick.

But that's weeks off. Right now there is only 14:22, and the blade at the origin, and the question I climbed the stairs to avoid and got anyway.

* * *

She walks me to the dead elevator out of some reflex of manners she'd deny having.

"I'll write it up for Lockwood," I say. "The fourteen twenty-two read. The role-shift, the directional ambiguity. That it's at minimum unresolvable and arguably exculpatory, and that the trial render foreclosed it." The professional voice. The front pocket of the coat. "He'll want it in declaration form."

Bee looks at me for a second. The lake light is behind her now, gray and flat, and she is twenty-seven and native and faster than me and she has just walked me through my own crime in my own language and she is not, I understand, going to let me leave with the declaration as a shield.

*Write it up,* she signs. *Sure.* Then: *But don't put my name on the part where I found it. Put yours.* Her face does something complicated — not unkind, just done pretending it's simple. *Because if a CODA archivist caught what the certified interpreter missed, that's a story about me. And it's not about me.* She nods at the stairwell, at the four floors down to the street, to the courthouse, to the man in the cell. *It's about who gets believed when two people read the same hands. You've spent your whole career being the one who gets believed. So you say it. With your name on it.* Beat. *Find out what that costs.*

She goes back inside before I can render an answer, which is the Deaf goodbye she's chosen for me — not the long one, the warm one, the one that takes ten minutes at a doorway. The short cold one. *We're done here.*

I take the stairs down. Four flights, my knees, the dental clinic, the street. And the whole way down I am holding the sentence I will write for Lockwood, clean and declarative and to tolerance, *the trial interpretation of the construction at timecode 14:22 foreclosed a grammatically available reading of the directional verb and accompanying role-shift,* and I understand, somewhere around the second-floor landing, that I am about to do it again — take a thing that happened in a body and make it fluent, make it typeable, make it mine — and that there is no version of this where I am the pane of glass, and that there may never have been, and that the man in the cell has known what I am only learning, the way Tobias goes still and the way the stillness is not absence but a leash, the way the Deaf in that green room saw the hand at 14:22 come *across* while the hearing room watched it go *out,* and got it right, and were not believed, because the one who gets believed is the one who can be typed.

The version the jury convicted on is not what the hands say.

The hands are on the tape. The hands are clear enough. And the only person in fifteen years who could read them wrong with that much fluency, that much speed, that much sworn and certain and requested-by-name authority —

— was me.

I sign nothing on the way out. There's no one to sign it to. But my hand comes up at the door, out of some habit older than the job, older than the oath, and I catch it halfway, and I put it back in my pocket, where the front-pocket truths live, and I find there is no longer anything in there I believe.


# Chapter Eight — The Pane of Glass

I drafted the declaration. The one Bee would not let me leave with as a shield — the clean, declarative, to-tolerance account for Lockwood, the gap reduced to *the degraded medium, the non-standard source, the reasonable interpretation of an ambiguous construction.* I sat at my kitchen table at eleven at night with the tape paused on Tobias's face and built it the way my father built a load path, clause by clause, every sentence true, every sentence a small clean weld. It would have held. Donovan could have stood on it.

Then I read it back, and I heard whose voice it was in.

It was in mine. The cool one. The one that empties the room out of itself and lets it refill with whoever's speaking — the one I have always told myself belongs to no one, because glass has no opinion and a faithful render adds nothing. And I sat at my own table and watched my own hands type a fluent, grammatical, *exculpatory* account of a thing that was not exculpatory, and I understood that I was doing the thing I do. I had taken an ugly, ambiguous, terrified truth and ironed it into a sentence a reader could believe, and the reader, this time, was me.

I deleted it. It took four keystrokes. I have thought, since, about how the whole of my professional life could be undone in four keystrokes, and how that is roughly how long it took me to undo a man's, fifteen years ago, in a room with no record of my hands.

* * *

I watched 14:22 one more time.

Not to argue with it. I had stopped arguing. I had been arguing for eight days — since the hallway, since the yellowed label, since *faithful to what* came out of my mouth before I could approve it — and I want to be precise about what arguing is, because precision is the only thing I have left to be: arguing is the noise a person makes while she decides which truth she is going to be able to live inside. I had been auditioning truths. *The lighting. The angle. The idiolect.* Trying them on, checking them in the mirror of my own competence, asking each one *can I wear you to the rest of my life.* And one by one they had failed the fitting, not because they were false — they were all partly true, that is the obscene part, they were all *partly true* — but because none of them could carry the weight of the thing I had finally let myself see, which was the direction of one movement.

I'm not going to walk you through the grammar again. Bee already did, in her workspace, faster and truer than I could, reading the role-shift off the screen while I was still setting up my objection — and watching a twenty-seven-year-old read my own life's craft more honestly than I could was its own small death, and I covered it, I made some dry credentialed remark about resolution and frame rate, and she let me, which was worse. You know what's at 14:22. The hand goes one way. I gave the court the other way. Read correctly it is, at the floor, ambiguous, and at the ceiling it is a man defending himself, and I stood in a courtroom in 2009 with my right hand raised and I resolved the ambiguity, in real time, three seconds behind, in the only direction a jury can convict on.

I watched it one more time. And the strange thing — the thing I did not expect, the thing I am ashamed of in a way I don't fully have language for yet — is that I felt the floor stop dropping.

Because here, finally, was a verdict I could render.

* * *

I have spent my whole life being unable to be guilty of anything.

That sounds like a complaint. It is a confession. A pane of glass cannot be guilty of the weather — I told you that on the first morning, I told it to you the way you tell a jury a thing you need them to stop examining, and I believed it the way I believe the oath, which is to say completely, which is to say it was the architecture I lived in. I am not in the sentence. I add nothing, I take nothing. Whatever the weather does to the man on the far side of me, the glass is innocent, the glass is *clean*, and I have kept my hands so clean for so long that I forgot hands were the kind of thing that could be clean or dirty at all. They were instruments. Instruments don't have a conscience. You don't apologize to a bridge for the funeral.

And now here was a thing I had done. A real one. With a direction and a date and a man at the end of it, in a cell, fifteen years deep, deteriorating in a language the prison cannot hear — and I want to tell you that what I felt, sitting there, was horror, and there was horror, but underneath the horror, holding it up like a load path, was something I have to name because not naming it would be the same render all over again:

It was relief.

I had finally done something. After fifty-one years of being the medium, the conduit, the faithful nothing through which other people's catastrophes politely passed — I had a catastrophe of my own. It was mine. I had authored it. My hand, my direction, my clean fluent inculpatory English laid down over a terrified man's broken account of the worst night of his life — *me*, finally, in the sentence. Guilty. The glass, it turns out, was never glass. The glass was a person, and the person did this, and I sat at my kitchen table and felt the specific, shameful, almost erotic relief of a woman who has spent her whole life refusing authorship and has finally, irreversibly, been caught holding the pen.

I want you to notice what I did there. I'll point it out, because no one pointed mine out to me in 2009, and that absence is the whole book.

I had just authored a render. A clean one. Fluent, grammatical, certain — *I did this, I alone, I broke him with my competence.* It went down smooth as anything I have ever voiced into a courtroom, smooth as *I was out front, locking the gate*, and like everything I render it foreclosed the alternatives, it resolved the ambiguity, it took an enormous ugly under-determined truth and ironed it into one sentence a reader could believe. And the reader believed it. You believed it. You're believing it right now, because I am the only voice you have, and I sound so sure.

I am the expert, and I am telling you the truth about my own guilt with the full forensic authority I have spent twenty-six years earning, and you have no way to check me, exactly the way the jury had no way to check me, and the thing I am most certain of in this entire chapter is going to turn out to be one more render that was cleaner than the truth.

But I didn't know that yet. At my kitchen table, at midnight, eight days in, I only knew the relief, and I mistook it for absolution, the way I have mistaken a great many costumes for the body underneath.

* * *

Then I made myself look at the gate.

I didn't plan to. It surfaced the way the dangerous things surface — sideways, off-frame, while I was looking at something else. I was thinking about Lamar Stokes, because I had Webb in the morning and some part of me was already in 4B, and I thought about the gate, the storefront on Lorain, *I was out front, locking the gate*, and I thought, idly, almost fondly, *that was a clean render*, and the fondness curdled in my mouth before I finished the thought.

Because he hadn't said *I was out front locking the gate.* You were there. You watched me do it. He topicalized it — *the gate, that's what I was doing* — brows up, the whole meaning hung on his face and fronted in the air, the grammar living where no court reporter can take it, and I gave the court subject-verb-object because subject-verb-object is the order they can convict in and the order they can acquit in and the order a stenographer's fingers don't stumble over. I smoothed it. I called it competence. I said it costs nothing, every interpreter does it forty times an hour, and I filed it and moved on.

And sitting at my table I felt the first edge of a thing I did not want, a thing far larger than 14:22, and I pushed it away with both hands.

Because if the gate was a smoothing — a small one, an innocent one, the most ordinary thing in the world — then it was the *same* smoothing. Same direction. Same hand making the same choice: ambiguity into fluency, topic-comment into the order a jury wants, the broken true thing into the clean believable thing. And if the gate was the same as 14:22, then 14:22 was not a catastrophe, it was a *Tuesday*. It was not the one time my hands failed. It was the thing my hands do. It was a *method*.

I did not look at that. I want to be honest that I did not look at it. I am telling you it was there, in the room, at the edge of the frame — the way the second figure was at the edge of the 2009 frame, the one I clocked and filed as a scheduling artifact and have not thought about since — and I turned away from it, deliberately, with the full strength of a woman who has just been handed a clean verdict on herself and would rather be the sole author of one terrible sentence than the habitual author of a thousand small ones. Because the first is a crime, and a crime can be confessed, and a confession can be paid for, and payment can buy you back your clean hands.

The second isn't a crime. The second is a *life*. And you can't atone for a life. You can only stop living it, and I was not ready, at midnight, to stop being the best in the building at the one thing I do.

So I looked back at 14:22, where the guilt was singular and mine and *manageable*, and I let the gate slide back off the edge of the frame, and I told myself I was being methodical.

I knew, the way you know the stair isn't there, that I was being something else.

* * *

I swore an oath that morning. I swear it most mornings; I have sworn it perhaps four thousand times. *I will faithfully and accurately interpret these proceedings to the best of your ability* — they put it as a question and you answer *I do*, like a wedding, and I have always meant it more than the lawyers mean theirs, more than the witnesses mean theirs, more than anyone in the room.

I sat at my table and I said the words to myself, in the dark, the way you'd press on a bruise to confirm it's yours.

*Faithfully.*

I had stood in a courtroom and raised my right hand and promised to faithfully interpret a man, and then I had interpreted him into a cell. Both of those things were true at the same time and had always been true at the same time, and the oath had not protected him, the oath had *convicted* him, because the oath does not ask *faithful to what.* It asks you to swear, and you swear, and the swearing is the costume, and underneath the costume your hand goes one way or the other and a stenographer takes down whichever way it went as if it were the weather and not a choice. I had made the choice. I had sworn I wouldn't and then I had, four thousand times, because there is no version of the job where you don't — and the oath, the sacred thing, the one sentence in the building I believed completely, turned out to be the instrument that laundered every choice I ever made into *transmission*.

I'd told you the oath was the only thing I believed. I want to amend the record. At midnight, eight days in, the oath stopped being a vow and became an accusation, and it has been one ever since, and it is reading this sentence over my shoulder right now.

* * *

Webb resumed at nine-oh-four.

Pryor took the bench, silver and baritone, said good morning to me by name with the stress in the right place, and I felt the old irritation that I notice it, and then I felt nothing, because nothing was what I had instead of sleep. Lamar was in the box in his good gray suit. He found my eyes, gave me the flat nod — *I see you, I know where you are, don't move* — and I set my feet and became furniture with a face, and the line ADA stood up to finish his redirect, and I raised my hand and swore the oath, and the word *faithfully* went through me like a wire through cheese.

And then Lamar started to sign, and I heard the gap.

Not a gap. There wasn't one — his signing was clean, native, unambiguous, a Deaf man giving sworn account in his own complete language, nothing deprived about it, nothing for me to break. But I heard it the way you hear your own pulse once you've been told to listen for it: the three-second lag opened up, the place I have ridden like a surfer for twenty-six years, the place where the sentence forms and I wait in the doorway for the floor plan — and for the first time in my life I stood in that doorway and did not trust the hand that was about to open my mouth. He topicalized something. A small thing. The same shape as the gate. And the lag stretched — three seconds, three and a half — while I stood there and watched myself decide which English to lay down, and *felt the deciding*, felt it as a choice and not a transmission, the load of it, the authorship of it, naked in my hands where I had spent my whole life pretending it wasn't.

I didn't freeze. I want that clear. I committed the sentence. I gave the court clean fluent English three seconds behind him and the stenographer's fingers didn't hesitate and not one person in that room saw anything but the best interpreter in the building doing the thing she is requested by name to do.

But I tasted it going out. Every render now tastes like 14:22. I cannot fix 2009 — 2009 is a tape, a transcript, a man already fifteen years gone into a cell — but 2009 was not behind me. 2009 was happening at nine-fifteen in the morning in Courtroom 4B in the present tense, in Pryor's room, with Lamar's clean true hands in front of me and my clean true English coming out three seconds late, and the only difference between this render and the one that buried Tobias Rein was that this time the source happened to be unbreakable, and I had stopped believing that difference was about *me*. I had started to suspect it was luck.

I finished the day. Lamar signed *clean, thank you, you're fast* across the empty well at recess, the highest thing one of us says to one of mine, and I signed back that he made it easy, and it was a courtesy and it was true and I wanted to be sick. I gathered my water and my legal pad on which I had written nothing, because I never write anything, because writing would mean I'd looked away — and I thought about all the things the record keeps, and all the things it can't, and how I had built a cathedral on the difference and called it a virtue.

* * *

So here is the verdict, rendered clean, because I am incapable of rendering it any other way and that incapacity is the whole of the case against me:

I did it.

There was a man, and he signed the worst night of his life into a camera in a language no one in that courtroom could fully read, and I was his only way across, and I took the broken under-determined terror of what he gave me and I made it grammatical. I made it fluent. I made it English a jury could believe, and they believed it, and they convicted him on a sentence that was mine — my direction, my hand, my fluency laid down over his deprivation like a clean sheet over something that needed to be looked at — and then I raised my right hand four thousand more times and swore I had never done such a thing, because I am the faithful one, I am the pane of glass, I add nothing, I take nothing, I am not in the sentence.

I am in the sentence.

I have always been in the sentence. A man is fifteen years into a cell because of where I put one finger, and I swore — *sworn, on a record, in my own voice, to be faithful* — and the record kept every word I said and not one of the words I authored, and that is not the worst of it, that is not even close to the worst of it, though I don't know that yet.

I only know I have a verdict on myself now, and I am holding it the way I held the tape in the hallway, out into the cold light, so someone can read the case number off the label.

It says my name. I got the stress right.



# Chapter Nine — Stillwater

Irene Stillwater would not take a phone call, would not answer a VRS call, would not answer the message Bee left through a mutual cousin, and the fourth time I asked Bee for a way in she put her phone down on the long archive table, screen up, and looked at me the way you look at someone who has asked you to do something slightly disgusting.

"She'll see you Thursday," Bee said. "Two o'clock. You go to her. Not a coffee shop, not the church, not somewhere with parking she has to think about. Her house. And Ruth—" Bee was already turning back to the wall of drives, done with me. "Don't bring the tape."

"It's the whole reason—"

"Don't bring the tape."

So I drove out past the airport on a Thursday in the last gray slick of May, the lake somewhere off to my right doing the flat colorless thing it does before summer remembers it, and I parked exactly where I was told not to think about parking, on a street of small post-war houses with aluminum awnings, and I sat in the car for ninety seconds being a professional. I am very good at being a professional. I have walked into rooms holding the worst day of a stranger's life in my hands and made my face a clean surface. I checked the surface in the visor mirror. I got the stress right on my own name. Then I went up the walk and stood on the porch and did not knock, because you do not knock on a Deaf woman's door, and I felt for the button.

There was no button. There was a square of tape where a button had been, and under it a bare wire, and I stood there for a second like a fool who has forgotten how doors work, and then the inner door opened anyway, because she'd been watching me from the window the whole time, watching me find the dead doorbell and have nothing to do with my hands, and I understood that the doorbell was not broken. The doorbell had been removed. The first thing Irene Stillwater ever said to me she said by making me stand on her porch with my hands empty and useless while she decided how long to let me.

She opened the storm door. She did not smile. She was sixty-three and built low and square and certain, gray hair cut blunt to the jaw, in a cardigan the color of a wet sidewalk, and she looked at me the way my father looked at a weld he already knew was bad.

*Come in. Take your shoes.* Flat. Not a welcome — an instruction, the brows neutral, no warmth riding under it, the way you'd tell a delivery man where to set the box.

*

I had rehearsed this. I want to be honest that I had rehearsed it, the way I rehearse nothing, because rehearsing means you don't trust yourself to find it live, and I trust myself to find anything live, that is the whole of what I am. But Irene I had rehearsed. In the car I had built it: I would establish that I knew what I'd done, I would not perform humility but I would *demonstrate* it, I would offer her the one thing a hearing professional can offer a Deaf elder she has wronged, which is to make myself useful to her purpose instead of mine. I had a sequence. I had, God help me, an opening line.

Her living room ate it in about four seconds.

Because the room was hers in a way I cannot fully render to you, and the failure to render it is the first thing that happened to me in that house. The furniture set in a clean horseshoe so every seat had sightline to every other seat — no one in an Irene Stillwater living room ever has their back to a conversation. The lamps low and warm and aimed at the hands, not the ceiling. A wide flat television on the wall, muted, captions off, a Deaf church service playing to no one, a man up front signing a sermon to a camera, and Irene's eyes flicking to it and away while she dealt with me, the way a hearing person leaves the radio on. A clear line of sight straight through to the kitchen window. And on every surface, photographs — Deaf banquets, Deaf weddings, a Deaf softball team in matching shirts, decades of a community I have served for thirty years and walked through like a contractor, billing by the hour, never once invited to the banquet.

She sat. She gestured me to the chair across, the one with the worst light, and I took it, and I put my useful, articulate, vain hands in my lap, and I began.

I did not get to my opening line.

*Stop,* she signed — one hand, flat, a small economical wall put up in the air between us, and her face did the thing her face does, which I will spend this whole chapter failing to describe, a stillness with weather behind it. *I know why you're here. Lockwood. The tape.* She spelled L-O-C-K-W-O-O-D fast and contemptuous, a hearing name handled with tongs. *You want to fix it.* Her hands stopped. She let that sit, and the brow lifted, barely, a question that was not a question. *Fifteen years and now you want to fix it.*

I started to answer and she was already moving, and here is where it began, the thing the chapter is actually about, the thing I did not see coming because I had spent my life being the fastest hands in any room: she was faster than me.

Not faster than me the way Bee is faster than me — Bee reads a frozen frame quicker, catches a classifier I have to rewind for. Irene was faster *live.* Her ASL came at the speed of a first language used among equals, regional, idiomatic, full of compressions I had to lunge after, signs run together and shaved down the way you shave down words you've said ten thousand times to people who don't need them spelled out — and I, who voice the most fluent Deaf witnesses in the county at full speed and never break stride, I was *behind.* I was three seconds behind a sixty-three-year-old woman in a wet-sidewalk cardigan and the three seconds did not feel like a surfer riding a wave. They felt like a man at the bottom of a pool watching the surface get further away.

She caught me lunging. Of course she caught me. She watched my eyes do the small backward flick that means I missed a sign and was reconstructing it from context, and she stopped, and she waited, and she let me catch up, and the letting was worse than anything she could have signed. In a courtroom I am the one who decides the pace. I slow the lawyers. I hold the room. Here a Deaf woman was *accommodating* me, the celebrated interpreter, slowing herself down so the hearing guest could follow, and we both knew exactly what that meant and exactly who usually does it for whom, and she did it without a flicker of mercy, as a demonstration, and I sat in the bad light and took it.

*You follow?* she signed, slow now, insultingly slow, each sign placed like a plate in front of a child. *Or do you need it again.*

"I follow," I said, and signed it, and heard my own voice come out of me into a silent room where it had no business being, and put it away.

*

She talked for a while and I will not pretend I got all of it. That is the first true thing I can give you about Irene Stillwater: I did not get all of it, and what I'm rendering to you now is already a render, already smoothed, already the cleaner thing, and you should hold that against me the way you should have held it against me in 2009.

What I got was this. She had known Tobias since he was a young man — *known* him, a sign she made by setting him in the space to her left and then touching her own chest, a closeness, not an acquaintance — through the church, through the long slow work the Deaf community does of finding the ones who got lost in the hearing world and bringing them in late, half-languaged, starving for a grammar nobody ever gave them. She did not say *deprived.* That is my word, the clinical one, the one I reach for because it lets me stand outside it. She set Tobias in the air and signed something tender and exact about his hands — that he signed *like a man translating himself,* she said, and I have kept that, I could not have made it cleaner, *like a man translating himself,* every sentence a small expedition into a country he'd been told as a child he wasn't allowed to enter.

And then she told me she had been in the courtroom in 2009. Every day. In the second row, where Tobias could see her, because she had figured out fast that no one else in that room was a face he could read, and a Deaf man on trial in a hearing court with no Deaf face to anchor to is a man drowning in a language with the lights off.

She set the courtroom in the air. She built it the way Lamar built the street, the way they all build, the real ones — the bench here, the jury box there, the witness stand, the well. And then she put *me* in it. She placed nineteen-years-younger me in the space where I had stood, off to the side, angled to the box, and she pointed at that locus, and her face changed, and I knew before her hands moved that whatever came next was the thing I had driven past the airport to receive.

*Your hands,* she signed, and held them up, my hands, a cruel little imitation of long articulate fingers, *so fast. So sure.* Her face went smooth and pleasant and competent, an imitation of a face, my face, the clean surface I check in visor mirrors. *Beautiful.* She made it ugly, the word, by making it admiring. *Everybody watched your hands. The lawyers. The judge. Twelve hearing people who never met a Deaf person in their lives — they watched your beautiful sure hands and they thought, that is what he is saying. That is him. The pretty hands are him.*

She dropped the imitation. Her own face came back, and it was not soft.

*They were watching you,* she signed. *Nobody in that room was watching him.*

*

I want to tell you what I did with that, because it is not flattering and the lack of flattering is the only honest instrument I have left.

I built a case.

It happened before I could stop it, the way it always happens, the way it happened at my kitchen table with the declaration — some bricklayer in me who never sleeps started laying the wall. *She is grieving,* the bricklayer said. *She loved him; she is sixty-three; she sat in that room helpless and she has carried it fifteen years and she needs it to be someone's fault and the someone is the only face she can still see, which is mine, and that is human and understandable and it is also* — and here the wall went up another course, clean and load-bearing — *it is also not evidence. She didn't read the tape. She isn't certified. She watched a trial through grief and she remembers it as a story about my hands because she couldn't read what was on them either, none of them could, that was the whole tragedy, and now she is doing to me exactly what she accuses the jury of doing to him: deciding the meaning of a thing she cannot fully read by watching the hands she's already decided about.*

I want you to see how good it was. It was a *good wall.* Every brick true. She was grieving. She wasn't certified. She had watched through love, which is the least reliable lens there is. I could have stood on that wall for the rest of my life.

And I almost said a piece of it out loud — the gentlest piece, the *I understand how it must have looked* piece, the piece that sounds like humility and is actually the first brick — and I got as far as lifting my hands, and Irene watched me lift them, watched me start to *be reasonable* at her, and she made a sound.

Deaf people make sounds. Not words. This was a short flat exhale through the nose, almost a laugh, the exact sound a person makes when they have predicted the precise stupid thing you were about to do and you have done it. And she signed, fast, not slow anymore, not accommodating anymore, done accommodating:

*There it is.* She pointed at my half-raised reasonable hands. *I watched you do it. Just now. The same thing. Something true comes at you and you—* she made a smoothing motion, both palms flattening the air, ironing it, *—you make it nice. You make it a sentence you can live with. You did it to him for the jury and now you want to do it to me in my own house.* The brows came down, wh-question, sharp. *Why did you come here, Ruth.*

And I gave her the rehearsed answer, because it was the only one I had loaded and there was no time to load another, three seconds behind, the held thing rotting in the air:

"To make it right."

*

She looked at me for a long moment. The church service flickered on the wall behind her, the silent man up front signing his silent sermon, and Irene's eyes went to it and came back, and something in her decided something, and what it decided was to stop being patient.

*Make it right.* She turned the phrase over, repeated it back at me in a register I can only call clinical, the way I repeat a witness's words to be sure of them before I commit. *For who.*

I started to answer.

*For Tobias?* she went on, not waiting, because she already knew. *Tobias is in Grafton dying in a language nobody there speaks. You can't make that right; that's already done; fifteen years is fifteen years and you don't get them back by feeling bad in my living room.* She let it land. *So not for him.* The brows lifted, false-innocent, devastating. *For who, Ruth.*

And I did not have a render.

I, who have never not had a render, who voice the unspeakable for a living, who can take a man's worst night and hand it to a jury in clean grammatical English three seconds flat — I sat in the bad chair and could not voice the simplest sentence in the room, because the simplest sentence in the room was *for me,* and she already had it in her hands, she'd had it before I came up the walk, she'd watched me find the dead doorbell and known the whole shape of me from how I stood there needing something to do with my fingers.

*You came to feel better,* she signed, and there was no cruelty in it now, which was worse, it was just true, the way a number is true, the bridge or the funeral. *You found out you broke something fifteen years ago and you cannot stand to be a person who broke something, because you have spent your whole life being the one who never breaks anything — the clean one, the faithful one, the glass. So you came to the Deaf woman to be told it's all right. To be useful. To be given a job so the breaking turns back into work, because work you know how to be good at.*

She set her hands in her lap, mirroring mine, a small exact mockery.

*I'm not going to give you the job.* Flat. Final. *I won't be the thing that makes your hands clean again. Find somebody else's grief to be good at.*

*

There is a kind of silence that is not the absence of sound. I have spent my life in it. It is the silence between a sign completing and the next one beginning, the place where I live, the three-second country, and in a hearing courtroom I own it. In Irene Stillwater's living room it owned me.
