I sat in it and had nothing, no brick, no wall, no clean surface, and for one of the few times in my adult life I understood from the inside what it is to be the person in the room whose meaning is being decided by someone else's faster hands — and to know they're not even wrong.

So I did the only thing left, which was the truth, and I did it badly, which is to say honestly, which is to say without a single clause to protect the clause behind it.

"I read his hands wrong," I signed. No voice this time. Just the hands, in her language, on her terms, badly. "At fourteen twenty-two. I made it cleaner than it was. I made it guilty. I think — I'm almost sure — he was telling them something the other way and I gave them the way they wanted." My hands were not fast now. They were not sure. They were a woman saying a true thing in a language she learned to be loved in and forgot she could be wrong in. "I can prove the gap. The tape shows it. But I can't make the proof matter without—" and here the held thing finally fell out of me, the real reason, the one under the rehearsed one — "without people who were there. Without you."

For the first time, something moved in her face that was not contempt and was not pity. It was attention. She had been watching me perform and now I had finally said a thing worth watching, and she leaned a centimeter into it, and I felt absurdly, shamefully, the old warm thing, the thing I feel when a render lands — *she's listening, I reached her, I'm good* — and I hated that I felt it even now, even here, vanity surviving in me like a weed through concrete.

She was quiet a moment. Then she signed something slow, and it was not for me, exactly. It was the thing she'd been deciding whether to give me since the porch, and her face when she gave it was the face of someone setting down a weight she'd carried so long it had changed the shape of her shoulders.

*You think you read him wrong.* She nodded, almost gentle. Then the gentleness went, and what came was the iron, the line that has not stopped ringing in me since, the line I am rendering to you now knowing I have already smoothed it, knowing there is no version of these hands I can give you that is faithful, which is the whole of the joke and none of it is funny:

*You read him wrong. Fine. But you sat in that room three seconds behind a man you couldn't read* — she held up three fingers, my three seconds, the country I live in — *and you never once turned around to see who else in that room could.*

She let the three fingers hang. Then she folded them away.

*You don't even know what was in the room with you, Ruth.* The brows neutral now, no question in them, just the flat dropped fact of it. *You never knew. You were too busy being the one who knew.*

*

I tried to ask her what she meant.

I tried the way I try everything, head-on, fast, *what was in the room, who else was there, Irene, what are you telling me* — and she watched my hands speed back up the instant I smelled information, watched the interpreter come back online and the woman go offline, and whatever had opened a centimeter in her face closed.

She stood.

In the Deaf world a goodbye is a long thing. I know this; I have stood in church basements and restaurant parking lots while goodbyes went on for forty minutes, the whole community refusing to release each other, story folding into story, *one more thing, one more thing,* the long sweet reluctance of people for whom presence is the only channel and absence is real in a way the hearing never have to feel. The long goodbye is one of the most beautiful things I know about the world Irene Stillwater belongs to and I do not.

She did not give me one.

She crossed to the door and opened the storm door and stood holding it, square and certain in the wet-sidewalk cardigan, and she looked at me until I stood, and when I had my shoes on and was on the threshold she signed exactly one thing, fast, final, the whole long warm grammar of Deaf farewell amputated down to a single closing sign aimed at me like a latch dropping:

*Go.*

And then, because she was not done, because she had decided I should carry one more thing down the walk to the car I'd parked where I was told not to think about it — she lifted her hand and gave me a name-sign.

I did not have one from her. The community gave me one years ago, half-fond, half-warning, and I will not tell you what it is yet because it is going to mean something later that it does not mean now. But Irene had never used it, and she used it now, on her porch, with the door open and her body already turning away — and the sign she made was not the community's. It was her own, made up on the spot, for me, the way you make a sign for a thing you have just understood the true shape of: she took the sign for *glass*, the flat clear pane, and she put it at her eyes like a mask, and then she let the hand fall, and the fall was the whole sentence.

A pane of glass held up to a face. A thing you look through. A thing that is between you and the world and tells itself it is not there.

She let the storm door swing shut, and the inner door behind it, and I stood on a porch in the last gray of May with a dead doorbell and no button to press, the most fluent hands in the county empty at my sides, named at last by the one person who had watched me up close and not believed a word my hands ever said — and somewhere behind me on a muted wall a man was still signing a sermon to no one, and I could not hear it, and for once that was not a thing that happened to other people.

I drove home not knowing what was in the room.

I have not stopped not knowing it since.


# Chapter Ten — The Visit

Grafton Correctional sits an hour southwest of the city in a county that grows soybeans and razor wire with equal commitment, and they make you wait in a room the color of a filing cabinet while a woman behind glass decides whether your paperwork is real. I had come with paperwork. I always come with paperwork. Lockwood had faxed the visit authorization and the interpreter designation and a letter on his frayed letterhead establishing that I was a member of the defense team, and I had brought my certification card, laminated, the photo nine years old, and I set all of it on the steel counter in a neat overlapped fan the way you'd lay out evidence, and the woman looked at the fan and then at me and said, "You here to sign for him or talk to him?"

It is a question I have answered a thousand times in courtrooms and never once been asked in a way I couldn't answer.

"Both," I said. "I'm certified. I'm on his legal team."

"Because if you're his interpreter we got a video thing." She said *video thing* the way you'd say a word for a relative you don't like. "VRS. Tablet on a stand. He can call out on it Tuesdays."

"He doesn't need a tablet today. I'm here in person."

She stamped something. I want to be honest about the size of what I felt, standing at that counter, because it was not noble. It was vanity, the small clean kind, the kind I run on. *He has been getting a tablet on a stand on Tuesdays.* Fifteen years. A man whose entire access to his own language, his own defense, his own self had been routed for fifteen years through a screen that froze when the prison wifi did, and I felt — under the horror, riding on top of it like oil on water — a flush of professional contempt for the *video thing,* and a flush of something worse than contempt, which was the certainty that I, in person, with my hands, would be *better.* That I was the cavalry. I had driven an hour to be the cavalry. I want that on the record before he gets to me, because he got to that first, faster than the woman behind the glass, faster than I did.

They put us in a contact room. Not the phones-and-plexiglass arrangement from the movies — a small block room with a bolted table and two bolted stools and a guard outside a window in the door, because a deaf man cannot use a telephone handset through glass, and somewhere in the architecture of this place that one fact had been grudgingly accommodated by giving him a table instead, which I'm sure they counted as a kindness and entered somewhere into a record as compliance.

Then they brought him in, and I revised everything I had driven down certain of.

I knew Tobias Rein was forty-six. I had done the arithmetic on the way down, 2009 plus the sentence, the way you'd reconcile an invoice. The man they walked in was older than forty-six the way a building is older than its years if no one fixes the roof. He was thinner than the tape. The tape was fifteen years ago and on the tape he was a big man going soft at thirty-one, and the man in the doorway had been planed down to the frame, the khaki uniform hanging off the shoulders, and there was a tremor in his left hand I clocked before I clocked his face — a fine continuous tremor, the hand never quite still, and I did the cold thing I do, the diagnostic thing, *that's neurological, that's not nerves, nobody's looked at that* — and I felt the medical clock start ticking in the room like a second guard.

He saw me see the hand. Of course he did. He had spent his life being seen wrong by people who thought they were seeing him; he had a sense for the angle of a hearing person's attention the way I have a sense for the end of a sentence. He didn't hide the hand. He did something worse. He held it up between us, the trembling hand, and turned it over slowly, front and back, *look, get a good look, you came to read hands didn't you,* and the contempt in it was so precise and so early that I hadn't sat down yet.

He sat. He looked at me for a while without signing. I have been looked at by judges and by the families of the convicted and by Lamar Stokes deciding whether I was competent, and I can hold a look; holding a look is half the job. I held it. The radiator-less room ticked. Then he raised his hands, and I'll tell you what he signed, and I'll tell you that his hands did not move like anyone I render in a courtroom, because the courtroom never sent me anyone like him, because the courtroom dropped the person who could have.

His ASL was — I reached, even then, even sitting there, for the professional word, because the professional word is my pocketknife and I take it everywhere — *idiosyncratic.* That is the word the field uses and it is a coward's word. What I mean is that his language had holes in it. He would build a clean phrase, fluent, fast, real ASL, the space organized, the verb agreeing, and then arrive at a place where the sign he needed did not exist in him, had never been put in him, and he would *route around the hole* — fingerspell, or improvise a sign on the spot and then look at me to see if I'd caught the improvisation, or substitute a near-thing and let the near-thing stand. A native signer doesn't do this. A native signer reaches and the word is there. Tobias reached and sometimes the word was there and sometimes he had built a footbridge over the place where the word should have been, years ago, alone, because no one had built him a road.

*You're the one,* he signed. He didn't fingerspell my name. He set me in the space in front of him — placed me, a locus, to his right — and then he pointed at that empty piece of air, not at me, at the *idea* of me he'd put there, *that one, you.* Then he made a sign I did watch his face for, because his face was doing the grammar: brows flat, mouth set. *The one whose mouth went on the record.*

I voiced none of this, of course. There was no court. There was only me, and him, and the thing I am — the thing that converts him into English even when no one has asked me to, even inside my own skull, a reflex I cannot switch off, I have tried — and so what reached me, three seconds behind his hands the way it is always three seconds behind, in my own interior voice that I cannot make stop sounding like a transcript, was: *You're the one. The one whose mouth went on the record.*

"Yes," I said. Aloud. Stupidly. He can't hear *yes.* I caught myself and signed it — *yes, that was me* — and I felt my own hands move and I had the disorienting half-second of being on the wrong side of myself, producing instead of receiving, and he watched my hands the way I had watched his, clinically, grading them, and I understood with a drop in my stomach that I was being interpreted. That for the first time in this whole business someone in the room was reading *me* for the gap between what I meant and what my hands said, and that he was better at it than I was going to be at hiding it.

* * *

Aunt Meg gave me my name-sign the summer I was twelve, in her kitchen, and she did not consult me, because that is not how it works, and she did not do it kindly, because that was not how she did things.

A name-sign is given, not taken. You do not get to choose your own; to choose your own is the mark of a hearing person who has read one book. The Deaf give it to you, and they give it for a reason, and the reason is usually a little bit at your expense, and you carry it for the rest of your life as a small honest verdict the community rendered on you before you were old enough to perform a self.

Meg had watched me for a year. I had been coming to her since I was small — my mother gone, my father a structural engineer who loved me in load paths and tolerances and could not, would not, build the kind of bridge a girl needs, so he had handed me to his Deaf older sister three afternoons a week and on the long summers, and Meg had taken me the way she took everything, with a sharp eye and no sentiment and the assumption that I would be made to be useful. I learned to sign in that kitchen the way you learn a thing you are drowning toward. By twelve I was fast. I was proud of being fast. I would catch her meaning before she finished and finish it for her, leap ahead, supply the end of her sentence to show her I'd understood — and one afternoon she stopped, mid-sign, and let her hands fall, and looked at me with the particular flat patience she kept for fools she had decided were worth the trouble.

Then she gave me my name-sign. The R-handshape — index and middle fingers crossed, the letter that starts my name — placed at the chin, the sign for *but no*, the spot where a near-truth becomes a correction, and drawn a small precise inch sideways. *R, but —.* The handshape of my name living at the place on the face where you say *that's almost right, that's not quite it, let me fix that.*

It meant, she let me know, that I was a girl who finished other people's sentences. She did not mean it as a compliment, exactly. She did not mean it as a cruelty either. She meant it as a *fact,* rendered onto my face for the community to read for the rest of my life — *here comes the one who'll tell you what you meant* — and she made the sign at me, once, slowly, so I would learn the shape of myself, and then she required me to make it back at her, to sign my own name correctly, the verdict on me produced by my own hand, and when I did she nodded, once, the way my father nodded at a weld that held, and went back to what she'd been saying.

I thought, for thirty-nine years, that it was a sign about competence.

* * *

*Why are you here.*

He didn't make it a question with his face. There is a way to ask in ASL — brows up, the body leaning the question across the table toward you — and he did not use it. He flattened it. He set it down between us like a thing he already knew the answer to and was making me say anyway, which is a technique, which is a *cross-examiner's* technique, and I have watched a hundred lawyers do it to witnesses and I knew exactly what was being done to me and it worked anyway.

*Lockwood sent me,* I signed. *To re-interpret the tape. The police interview. There's new —*

He cut me off. Not rudely; economically. His hand came up flat — *stop* — and he held it there, and then he disassembled my sentence the way I disassemble a witness's, picking the one piece that mattered.

*Re-interpret.* He repeated my sign back to me with a small alteration, a torque on it, so that the same sign came back meaning something a half-degree uglier. *You did it once. Now you want to do it again. Better.*

The word *better* he fingerspelled. B-E-T-T-E-R, slow, letting each letter sit, and then he looked at me over the spelled word with an expression I will not get out of my body for a long time, because it was not anger. I had braced for anger. Anger I could have rendered; anger has a clean English equivalent and I'd have known where to stand. This was *amusement.* The driest amusement I have ever had aimed at me. The amusement of a man watching the same machine that ran him over fifteen years ago shift into reverse and come back, beeping, careful now, to make it right.

I want to be clear that I tried to defend myself, because the reader who has come this far should know I am not brave. My hands started building it before I'd decided to — *the tape was degraded, the lighting in that room was —* and I got as far as the sign for *light* before I understood what I was doing, which was preparing the same case I'd been preparing in my own kitchen at midnight, the lighting, the angle, the degradation, *anyone would have,* and that I was about to make that case to the one person on the earth it had been made *against,* in his language, with the hands that had made it the first time.

I stopped. I let my hands fall. He watched me stop, and he watched me understand why I'd stopped, and something in his face shifted a quarter-inch — not warmth, I want to be exact, not forgiveness, nothing so cheap — but the recognition you give an opponent who has just seen the board the way you see it. *There,* his face said. *Now you're paying attention.*

*You came to feel better,* he signed. And here his language opened all the way up, the holes and all, the fluency and the footbridges both, and I am going to render it the way it reached me and I am going to fail, and the failing is the point, so I'll let you watch me fail. *You came—* and he built it spatially, set the whole architecture in the air between us: himself, low, placed near the table; me, placed high, standing over the version of him he'd set down. He made his hand into a person walking — the 1-handshape, a body, upright — and walked it across the table toward the low placed-thing that was him, and at the last second turned the walking hand *over,* palm up, *offering,* the gesture you make holding something out. *You walk in here carrying a thing,* the hand said, *carrying it like a gift,* and then he showed me the thing in the offered palm, mimed its small weight, and looked at it, and looked at me, and let his face fill with the dry amusement again, and the thing in the palm was nothing. He turned the palm and let the nothing fall.

*The thing you're carrying is for you,* he signed. *Not for me. You came to put it down. You picked me to put it down on because I'm—* and he hit the hole. I watched him hit it. The sign he wanted did not exist in him; some word for *available,* or *safe,* or *already broken so it won't show,* and he routed around it the way he routes around everything, improvised — pointed at himself and then made a flat horizontal sweep, *flat, finished, level,* a man who is already on the ground cannot be knocked down — and the improvisation was so much better than the missing word would have been that I had a flash of pure, awful professional joy, the joy of watching language do a thing English can't, and I hated myself for having it, in that room, with that man, while he told me I'd come to use him as a place to set my guilt.

I did not render it. There was no court. But I'll render it now, for the record I'm building, which is the only record that will ever hold any of this: *You came to put it down. You picked me because I'm already on the floor.*

And I had. God help me, I had.

* * *

The thing about a name-sign you receive at twelve is that you stop hearing it. It becomes the sound of you, except there is no sound, it becomes the *shape* of you, and people make it at you across rooms for forty years and you stop seeing the inch of sideways motion that means *correction,* you stop seeing the chin, the place on the face, you just feel yourself being summoned. *R-but.* Here she comes. The one who'll tell you what you meant.

Meg made it at me from a hospital bed in 1991 with the right side of her face not working and the left side working too hard, and I did not, then, think about what it meant. I thought about her hands. I thought about whether she could still — the stroke had taken the right side, the fine control, and her signing had gone from the most exact hands I ever knew to something effortful and asymmetric and I had to lean in to read her, I, who had spent a decade being faster than everyone, had to *slow down* and lean in and build her meaning out of damaged pieces, which is — I see it now, I did not see it then — exactly the thing I would be asked to do for Tobias Rein eighteen years later and would do badly and call faithful.

She made my name-sign at me from the bed. *R-but.* Come here. And under it, the second meaning, the one I was too young and too frightened to read: *you, the one who finishes my sentences. Be careful what you finish.*

I leaned in. I did not yet know what the doctor was going to make me say.

* * *

*Here's what you don't understand,* Tobias signed, and now he leaned in too, mirrored me without knowing he was mirroring a dead woman, brought his face close over the bolted table so the guard at the window straightened, and his ruined left hand trembled in the space between us while his right hand did the talking, clean and fast, no holes here, no footbridges, because this was the one sentence he had built completely and carried for fifteen years and knew the whole of.

*I have never once,* he signed, *in my whole life, said a thing and had it arrive.*

He let that sit. Then he built it for me, because he is a teacher the way the deprived are teachers, by showing.

*My parents,* he signed — and the sign for *parents* came out wrong, came out as a hole he'd footbridged decades ago, M-handshape touching the chin and the forehead but with a hesitation in it, a sign he'd reconstructed from somewhere because the natural one had been kept from him — *my parents heard me. They made me talk. They held—* and he took his own trembling left hand in his right, the way you'd take a child's hands, and pressed it flat to the table, held it down, *they held my hands down so I would use my mouth,* and the image was unbearable and he did not perform its unbearableness, he reported it, flat, a fact, *and when I talked, what came out of my mouth was not me. It was a thing they could love. And then I learned to sign and the signing was mine but it had holes in it where they'd held my hands, and a hearing person looks at the holes and reads—* hole, footbridge, improvisation, a sweep of the hand *—reads guilty.*

*And then I'm in a room,* he signed, *and a man is dead, and they bring a woman with fast hands—* he did not look at me when he signed *fast hands,* which was somehow worse than if he had *—and she takes what comes off my hands, holes and all, and she makes it* — and here he made the cruelest sign of the afternoon, made it slow so I would have to receive every frame of it: he made his hands into mine. He made the fast clean confident render-hands of a court interpreter, *fluent,* the very fluency I am paid for, and he ran a smooth unbroken sentence through the air, beautiful, gapless, the kind of English ASL-shaped sentence I have built ten thousand times and been proud of — and then he stopped, and pointed at the beautiful gapless thing he'd just made, and signed:

*That. The court believed that. Not me. That. The clean one. They convicted the clean one because the clean one made sense, and I never made sense in my life, because the people who loved me took the sense out of my hands before I could use it.*

There is no English keystroke for what his face did under that. There is no English at all. I am the best in my building and I am telling you the truth, which is the only currency I have left: I sat across a bolted table from Tobias Rein and he handed me the single most articulate account of his own destruction that I have ever received in either of my languages, and it lived in a body that the court had ruled could not be understood, rendered in an idiolect the court had economized into silence, and I, who carry meaning across for a living, who have built a cathedral on *I carry it across without dropping it* — I had no render for it. None. It does not go into English. It does not go onto a record. It went into me, and stayed, and is in me now, and the most faithful thing I can do is tell you that I could not be faithful to it, that I am setting down a fluent gapless version of a thing that had holes in it because holes in it were the truth, and that this — this exact failure, his hands and my poverty — is the whole of the case, and I did not understand that yet. I thought I was there to help him. I drove home in the dark thinking I had failed to help him.

* * *

He pulled back. The guard relaxed. Tobias looked at me for a long moment with his hands still, and I had the sense — I have it still — that he was deciding something, weighing me on a scale I couldn't see the markings of.

*Lockwood says I should let you do this,* he signed, finally. Flat again. The fluency banked. *He says you breaking your own work is—* fingerspelled, slow, a legal word someone had spelled to him through a tablet on a stand: D-E-V-A-S-T-A-T-I-N-G. He looked at the spelled word with that dry amusement. *Devastating. For the case.*

*It would be,* I signed. Carefully. *The interpreter testifying against her own —*

*I know what it would be.* The hand, flat, *stop.* *I've been a case my whole life. I know what's devastating for the case and what's devastating for the person. They're never the same thing.* He paused. *You should know that better than anyone. You're the one who decides which one goes on the record.*

And there it was — the séance whisper, except he didn't whisper it, he didn't make it a metaphor, he made it a fact about his Tuesdays and his life: that he had been summoned, again and again, into rooms full of hearing people who spoke for him and over him and arrived at a version of him that *made sense,* and that the making-sense was the killing, and that I was about to do it one more time, in reverse, for his benefit, and that he would let me, because being made sense of toward freedom was still being made sense of and at least this version got him out of Grafton, and that he despised needing me exactly as much as he was going to use me, and that both of those were true at full strength and neither one canceled the other.

He stood. The visit was not over but he stood, which was his to do, the only thing in fifteen years that had stayed his to do. The guard moved toward the door. And Tobias, standing, his ruined hand at his side, looked down at me — at me, *R-but,* the one who finishes the sentence, the one whose mouth went on the record — and he gave me the verdict, and he gave it in his own hands, and I am going to give it to you exactly, the footbridges and all, because it is his and not mine and the one thing I will not do is render it clean:

*You want to be the glass.* He made the sign for it — flat, vertical, a pane, his hand a window between us — and then he put his other hand behind the pane and pressed it forward until the flat hand was no longer a window but a thing pushing, a hand pretending to be a window, *a hand,* and he held it there, the costume and the truth in one shape, his trembling and his still hand both inside it. *There's no glass. There was never glass. Every time you think you're the window—* he tapped the pushing hand, twice, *—you're the hand. You just don't want to be the one who has to live in a world you touched.*

He didn't wait for me to render it. He knew I couldn't. He turned, and the guard opened the door, and the long ugly buzz of it took him back into a building that had no language for him, and left me alone at the bolted table with my fan of paperwork and my certification card with its nine-year-old photo and my fast clean useless hands, three seconds behind a man who had just told me, in the language I had built my life on, the one thing about myself I had organized that whole life never to hear.


# Chapter Eleven — The Freeze

I drove back into the city the morning after Grafton with both hands at ten and two and Tobias Rein's last sentence sitting in the passenger seat. *There's no glass. There was never glass.* I did not let myself look at it. You can drive seventy on I-71 with a thing in the car and refuse to look at it; I have done harder refusals. By the time I crossed into Cuyahoga County the lake had thrown up its usual June nothing, a flat pewter haze that is neither weather nor not-weather, and I had talked myself most of the way back to fine. I had a witness at nine. Webb was a clean case. Darnelle Okoro was a good signer and the State's afternoon, and I had voiced her once already, and all I had to do — all I have ever had to do — was carry it across without dropping it.

I want to set the room before I set what happened in it, because what happened in it happened in a room, and the room is part of the verdict.

Courtroom 4B has the worst light in the building. Everyone who works it knows this and no one fixes it, because the fixing is a work order and the work order is a budget line and the budget line dies the way they all die, two floors down, in the office where Pam loses her language fights. The overheads are the old recessed kind, half of them gone amber, and they throw a sourceless yellow that flattens everything and lies about depth, so that a hand a foot in front of a chest reads, in that light, as a hand *on* the chest. I have compensated for the light in 4B for years without naming it as compensation. That is the thing about competence. It eats its own evidence. You adjust for the bad light a thousand times and you never once write down that the light is bad, because writing it down would mean admitting the render was ever in question, and the render is never in question, the render is *me.*

Pryor took the bench at 9:02. He said good morning to me by name and got the stress right and I felt it land somewhere it hadn't landed before — not as courtesy now but as *attention,* a man noticing his instruments, and I told myself I was inventing that, because I had been to Grafton and Grafton makes you see hands behind everything. He had a good day's color on him. There was, I'd heard, a debate that week, the County Prosecutor thing, the thing with the banner, and he wore it well, the way some men wear a campaign like a tan. He did not know my name was on a tape in Desmond Lockwood's banker's box. To him I was what I had always been: the good one. The one you don't have to think about. The reason the Deaf witness doesn't become a problem.

Darnelle was already in the box.

She is twenty-six and she signs the way the young Deaf-of-Deaf sign now, fast and slangy and unbothered, with a whole register of face that the older generation keeps in reserve and she spends like she'll never run out. The first time I voiced her — no. I keep wanting to give this a number, a timecode, an exhibit tab, the way I'd index a transcript, because a thing with a number on it is a thing that holds still. The first time I voiced her, three weeks ago, she had testified from the bus shelter angle, and she was hard, harder than Lamar, because Lamar is a craftsman and builds you a clean room to stand in and Darnelle throws the whole street at you at once, overlapping, the way it actually arrived. I had ridden it. I had been three seconds back and I had ridden it and I had been, I'll say it, magnificent, because that is the truth and false modesty is just another kind of inaccuracy.

This morning Hurd put her back up for the redirect. Cantwell had spent the prior afternoon trying to muddy her sightline — *the shelter glass, the rain on it, the angle* — and Hurd wanted to clean it. Simple. Rehabilitative. The kind of redirect you could voice in your sleep.

Darnelle found my eyes. Gave me the young person's version of Lamar's flat nod, a quicker thing, *you again, good, go.* I set my feet. I became furniture with a face. I have a body that knows this part without me; it has done it four thousand times; it does not require my belief, only my presence.

"Ms. Okoro," Hurd said. "Yesterday defense counsel asked you about the rain on the bus shelter. I want to go back to that. When the two men came together — when you saw the contact — was anything blocking your view?"

And Darnelle showed me.

* * *

Here is what I need you to understand about the next ninety seconds, because everyone in that room experienced it as nothing and I experienced it as the floor opening, and only one of those is on the record.

She set the scene the way she'd set it three weeks before. The shelter to her left — she placed it, a locus, glanced to it. The two men across the way — she placed them, out to her right, in the air over the rail. Herself between. And then, to answer Hurd's question, to show that nothing blocked her, she ran the line: her eyes to the men, the unbroken track between, the rain a thing on the glass *behind* her and not between her and them. Spatial. Clean. The negation living in the geometry — *my view went there and stayed there* — the way it always does, the way I have voiced a hundred times.

I opened my mouth to give Hurd his clean answer. *Nothing blocked my view. I watched the two men the entire time.* I had the English already built. It was sitting in me, three seconds back, formed, ready, the way it is always ready.

And I heard the gap.

Not saw. *Heard.* Heard it the way you hear a wrong note in a room you thought was silent — the smoothing I was about to do, the SVO English coming up out of me to flatten her spatial grammar into a sentence a stenographer could take, *nothing blocked my view,* and underneath it, suddenly audible, the thing the English would throw away: that she had not signed an absence. She had signed a *presence.* She had built the rain *behind* her, placed it there, a real locus, deliberately — *the rain was there, behind me, not here* — and the placing of it, the fact that her hands had bothered to put the rain somewhere specific, was itself testimony. She was not telling Hurd nothing blocked her. She was telling him she had *checked.* That she had clocked the rain, located it, ruled it out — an act, a witness's discipline, the difference between *I happened to see* and *I made sure I could see* — and my clean English was about to convict the rain of irrelevance and leave her diligence on the floor of 4B where the record can't reach.

It cost nothing. It was the most ordinary smoothing in the world.

That is what undid me. Not that it was an error. That it was *not* one. That it was the gate. That it was 14:22. That my hands, left to themselves, were already reaching for the cleaner render the way they have reached every hour of my working life, *toward fluency, toward the convictable, toward the typeable,* and that I had spent a weekend learning to hear that reach and now I could not stop hearing it, and I stood in the worst light in the building with a formed English sentence in my mouth and I could not say it, because to say it was to do the thing, and I no longer knew — this is the part I cannot make survive in English, so I'll just set it down — I no longer knew if the gap I was hearing was *real* or if I had simply lost the nerve to commit a sentence, which is the one thing my whole profession is, the willingness to close my mouth around a meaning and make it the record.

The lag stretched.

Three seconds is the wave. Three seconds is where I live, where I have always lived, riding the back of the sentence. Four seconds is long. At five seconds a courtroom notices the way a room notices a held breath. I felt myself go past three and the English did not come out. I felt myself go past four and Darnelle's hands had already stopped, were waiting, her face holding the question she'd answered, *did you get it, why aren't you—,* and I felt myself go past four and into the place there is no name for because no one is supposed to go there, the place where the meaning is in your hands and your hands have a hand on the wheel and the wheel will not turn.

* * *

"Ms. Okafor-Vance?"

Pryor. The baritone, lowered, almost kind, the public version of a private alarm. I had a sentence to give him. I had *had* a sentence. I reached for it and it was contaminated now, every word of it, *nothing blocked my view* sitting there fluent and false and I could not certify it and I could not build the true one fast enough because the true one isn't English, the true one is a woman placing rain in the air behind her to show you she'd done her job, and there is no keystroke for placed rain, and the stenographer's fingers were resting and the jury had begun, one by one, to do the thing juries do, which is to find the Deaf witness's interpreter and look at her, because something had gone wrong with the channel and a channel is only visible when it fails.

I did the thing you are trained never to do and I did it because I had nothing else.

"Your Honor, the interpreter requests a repetition."

I said it in the third person, the way we are taught, the interpreter requests, the interpreter, not *I,* the grammar that keeps me a function and not a person — and I heard my own voice do it and I heard, for the first time in twenty-six years, how thin that grammar is, how it is exactly the costume Tobias had pulled off me across a bolted table eighteen hours before. *The interpreter requests.* As if there were an interpreter, separate from me, a clean instrument that had merely missed a word and needed it again. There wasn't. There was me, frozen, lying about which one of us was in trouble.

Pryor's brows did something small. "Of course." He turned to Darnelle, and then — because he is good, because he is genuinely good at this and that is the whole horror of him — he did not speak to her slow and loud the way Cantwell would have. He looked to me. "Please ask the witness to repeat her last answer."

So I had to sign to her. I had to turn my contaminated hands toward Darnelle Okoro and ask her, in the language I am the best in the building at, to do again the thing I had just failed to carry, and she read my hands, and her face changed — not confusion, she wasn't confused, she is twenty-six and quick and she understood instantly that the problem was not her, the problem was *me,* the famous one, the one who never needs a repeat — and something went across her young face that I will keep: a flicker of the exact distrust I had watched go across Irene Stillwater's face, the distrust the whole community keeps in reserve for hearing people who have decided they are the bridge. *Oh,* her face said. *You're one of them after all.*

She repeated it. Slower, now, the way you slow down for someone who has revealed they cannot keep up — which was its own small mercy and its own small humiliation, the witness accommodating the interpreter, the world turned a half-degree on its axis — and she placed the rain again, *there, behind me,* deliberate, and looked at me to make sure I had it this time.

And I made the choice. I want it on the record that I made it; I have spent my life insisting I make no choices, and here, cornered, in front of Gerald Pryor and twelve jurors and a girl who had just decided what I was, I made one, and I made it the way I have always made it, *toward the clean,* because the clean is fast and the room was waiting and a held courtroom is a kind of drowning.

"Nothing blocked my view," I said. "The rain was on the shelter glass, behind me. I was watching the two men the entire time."

I gave the rain four words and a preposition. *On the shelter glass, behind me.* I let her diligence survive as a location instead of a discipline. It is not nothing — a worse interpreter drops the rain entirely — and it is not what she said, because what she said was *I checked,* and I gave the court *it was behind me,* and those are not the same testimony, and I knew it as it left my mouth, and I said it anyway, because the alternative was to keep standing there not saying anything while a man's trial waited on whether the celebrated instrument could still close her hands around a meaning.

The stenographer's fingers moved. It went on the record. It is the record now. There is no version of Darnelle Okoro's redirect anywhere on this earth except the one I just told you and the one that went into the machine, and they do not match, and only I will ever know they don't, exactly the way it has always been, except that this time I knew it *while it happened,* and that is the new thing, that is the thing I brought back from Grafton, the knowing-while.

Hurd, satisfied, sat down. He thought it had gone well. He always thinks it has gone well.

* * *

Pryor called the recess himself, ten minutes early, which he did not have to do and which told me he'd seen more than he let the room see. The jury filed out. Darnelle came down from the box, and she did not give me the flat nod. She gathered her bag and found her mother in the second row and signed something to her, fast, low, angled away from me — *talked about,* I was being talked about, in a language I am supposed to own, six feet from my own body — and I caught a third of it the way you catch a third of a thing meant for you not to catch it, and the third I caught was the sign she made for me, which was not my name-sign, because she doesn't know my name-sign, but a descriptive thing, a small economical thing, her hand miming a mouth opening and producing — and then a flat little hitch, a stall, a stutter built into the hand. *The one whose mouth stopped.* She had named me in real time, the way the community always names you, a small honest verdict rendered onto the air before I was out of the room, and it was accurate, and that is the only kind that sticks.

I gathered my own things. My legal pad, on which I had written nothing, because I never write anything, because writing would mean I'd looked away — and I stood there in the emptying courtroom holding a blank legal pad in the worst light in the building, and I did the thing I do, the thing I had watched myself start to do at midnight in my own kitchen and had recognized, even then, as a costume.

I built the case.

It was the light. The light in 4B is genuinely bad, amber and flat and a documented hazard, and a placed locus a foot off the body reads false in it, and any interpreter alive would have hesitated on a spatial negation in that light. It was the speed. Darnelle is fast, slangy, young, a hard render, harder than Lamar, and I'd flagged her as hard three weeks ago, it's in my own assessment of her if I kept one, which I don't. It was Grafton. I had driven two hours and slept four and sat across a table from a dying man while he told me my whole life was a lie, and a tired interpreter is an entitled-to-one-repeat interpreter, the Code itself says correct your errors discreetly and expeditiously, a repeat is not a failure, a repeat is *professionalism,* it is the opposite of failure, it is the humility the field is always saying it wants —

I got that far. I got all the way to *humility,* standing there assembling my own defense out of true and exculpating facts, the lighting and the speed and the sleeplessness, every one of them real, every one of them admissible, and I heard it. The way I'd heard the gap. I heard myself doing for *Ruth* exactly what I had spent a weekend watching myself do for Ruth on a tape — building her a clean, fluent, exculpating render out of the ambiguous facts, smoothing the thing that happened into the thing that lets her keep her hands clean, *toward fluency, toward the survivable, toward the typeable* — and I understood that I am very, very good at this, that I am in fact the best in the building at it, that I have one client I cannot be neutral about and I have been representing her with total fluency for thirty years, and that the freeze in 4B and the render at 14:22 and the case I was building right now in the empty courtroom were not three things. They were one method. They were the same hand.

I did not finish the defense. For once I did not finish the sentence. I let it fall, the way I'd let my hands fall across a bolted table, and the not-finishing was the most honest thing I'd done in front of myself in fifteen years, and it terrified me more than the freeze had, because a person who cannot finish her own defense is a person who has begun, somewhere she can't see the bottom of, to plead.

* * *

Pam found me at the elevators. She had a folder against her chest and the harried, apologetic carriage of a woman who spends her days losing, and she said, low, "You okay? Pryor's clerk came down. Asking was there an issue with the interpreter in 4B." She said *the interpreter* gently, the way you'd hand someone back a word they'd dropped. "I said no. I said you're the best we've got." A beat. "You are."

"Thank you, Pam."

"Webb's got three more days minimum. Cantwell's still got her recross and then the doctor." She shifted the folder. "You're on all of them. You're the only one we've got cleared for Okoro's register and the budget won't —" She stopped herself, because she heard herself starting the other speech, the budget speech, the speech she gives me in hallways about the CDI she can't fund for Reyes in 7C, the Deaf father whose first language barely is one, *they keep kicking the second interpreter off to save the per-diem and I keep losing it,* and she did not give me that speech today because today I was the one who'd stalled in the box, and you do not, even Pam does not, tell the instrument that failed about the second instrument the county won't pay for, in the same breath, at the same elevators, while it is still failing.

But I heard it anyway. The speech she didn't give. *They keep kicking the second interpreter off.* I had spent a weekend looking at a tape with a Deaf woman on it at 03:40 who was gone by 06:00, and a line on an invoice I could not explain, and Pam had been standing two floors under me for years saying the same sentence about a different case, and I had filed it both times under *not my problem,* under *the system,* under the great exculpating elsewhere where I keep the things that aren't my hands. I looked at Pam holding her folder of lost fights and I felt the elsewhere get smaller. I did not say any of it. There was nothing to say that wasn't a render.

The elevator came. Pam got in. I told her I'd take the stairs, which was a lie, the stairs in that building are a half-mile of fire-code concrete, but I needed the door to close on something, and I watched it close on Pam's kind tired face and her folder of the county's unpaid debts to the unheard, and then I stood alone in the marble hall in the cold institutional light, fifty-one years old, the best in this building at the one thing I do, and for the first time in twenty-six years I was afraid to go back into a room and open my mouth.

Three more days minimum. Cantwell's recross, then the doctor, then whatever else. Darnelle Okoro on all of them, fast and slangy and watching me now, and a jury that had seen the channel flicker, and Pryor on the bench getting the stress on my name exactly right while he decided what he'd seen. I could not un-hear the gap. That was the thing I understood, standing in the hall, and it was worse than the freeze, because the freeze was a moment and this was a condition: I would walk back into 4B tomorrow and the day after and every render would arrive already split, the clean one and the true one, and I would have three seconds to choose, every time, in the worst light in the building, with a man's liberty on the wheel — and I no longer had the one thing the work requires, which is not skill. I have the skill. I have always had the skill.

What I had lost was the ability to believe I was the glass.

And the trial was not over. And the man in the box was not Tobias. And my hands were going to decide him anyway.



# Chapter Twelve — The Pattern

The storage unit in Garfield Heights costs me sixty-one dollars a month and has for nineteen years, and in nineteen years I have opened it perhaps a dozen times, which works out, if you want to do the arithmetic the way my father did all arithmetic, to about a hundred and sixteen dollars per visit to a corrugated metal box the size of a single-car garage in a facility off Granger Road where the man at the gate has never once looked up from his phone. I have always told myself I keep it for liability. An interpreter retains nothing — that is doctrine, that is the Code, you carry the message across and you do not keep a copy of the freight — but the early years, the nineties, before the field tightened its ethics, I had been handed working tapes, training tapes, the occasional defense copy a lawyer forgot to demand back, and I had boxed them and paid the sixty-one dollars and called it caution. It was not caution. I understand that now, standing in the cold roll-up doorway at nine on a Saturday morning with Bee behind me holding two gas-station coffees she has already decided are undrinkable. It was the same impulse that keeps a man's fingerprints in a system. You retain the thing you are not done being accountable for.

"This is going to take all day," Bee says. She is looking past me at the wall of banker's boxes, the dead formats stacked four high — Hi8, MiniDV, a brick of full-size VHS, two boxes of the little gray cassettes from a transcription rig I have not owned since the Clinton administration. "You know that. You know we're not finding the thing in the first box."

"We're not looking for a thing."

"You're looking for a thing." She hands me a coffee I don't want and steps in past me, into the cold, and sets her own coffee on a box and pulls the chain on the bare bulb, and the unit fills with that hard yellow shadowless light that makes everyone's hands look like evidence. "You want to find one tape where you got it wrong, so you can hold it up and go, *there, that's the disease, that's the tumor, cut it out, I'm clean.*" She is already reading a box label, her back to me, her ASL when she throws it over her shoulder gone sloppy-fast the way it goes when she is annoyed, the signs clipped, half of them aimed at the boxes and not at me. "That's not what a pattern is. A pattern isn't a tumor. A pattern is the shape of the hand that made it."

I do not answer that. I have learned, over nine chapters of being out-read by a twenty-seven-year-old, that the cost of answering Bee when she is right is that she gets to watch you concede, and I would rather concede in private. I take a box down. I find the deck — I had to buy the MiniDV deck off a man in Parma last week, forty dollars, he threw in a cable — and I run the first cable to the little field monitor, and the unit smells like dust and cold metal and the inside of old electronics, and we begin.

* * *

Here is what I expected, and I want it on the record so you can measure the distance: I expected nothing.

I expected to spend a Saturday confirming that I was good. That is the truth of why I came, under the reason I gave Bee and the reason I gave Lockwood, which were both about *thoroughness,* about *due diligence,* about building the strongest possible declaration by stress-testing my own archive against the standard I'd applied to 2009. The real reason was vainer and simpler. I had found one bad render. One. At 14:22, fifteen years ago, in a degraded medium, of a deprived signer, with a national reputation's worth of mitigating circumstance around it — and some part of me believed that if I watched twenty years of my own hands I would find them clean, render after render, witness after witness, and that the cleanliness would *contextualize* the one stain. One tumor in a healthy body is a tragedy. One tumor in a body riddled with them is a diagnosis. I came to be diagnosed healthy.

I want you to hold that, because the reader should know exactly how I walked in, the better to watch me be wrong.

The first tape is a 1997 aggravated robbery. A Deaf complainant, native signer, clean as a bell, and my render — thirty-one years old, hair I'd forgotten I had — is good. It is genuinely good. I feel the old warm proprietary thing, *look at her, look how fast,* and I let myself have it for about four minutes, and Bee, watching the same screen, says nothing, and I take that nothing as a verdict in my favor.

The second tape is where it starts.

* * *

1999. A deposition, civil, a workplace injury, a Deaf machinist named — the label says *Kowalczyk* — describing how the press came down on his hand. He is not a fluent signer. He is not deprived the way Tobias is deprived, but he is a late learner, mainstreamed, an adult who came to the language at thirty and signs it with an accent, a stiffness, English bleeding through the word order. And he is describing the machine.

He builds it in the air. Of course he does — that is what the language is *for,* it is built to show exactly this, the press, the descent, the hand. He sets the bed of the press in front of him and brings his other hand down toward it, flat, a classifier, the platen coming down, and the question on the table from the defense is whether his hand was *in* the press or *reaching into* it when it came down, because the whole case — sixty thousand dollars, a man's livelihood — lives in the difference between an accident and a violation of the lockout procedure. And he shows it. He shows it precisely. His hand goes toward the descending platen and stops at the edge — *at the edge,* the fingers crossing the plane of the bed and then the descent catching them, the body pulling back a fraction of a second too late, and the whole thing is *there,* the timing, the geometry, the half-second of human error that is also the machine's fault, it is all in the space between his two hands —

— and the version of me on the screen, thirty-three years old, says, "My hand was in the press when it came down."

I stop the tape.

"Rewind it," Bee says. She has come to stand beside me. She is not annoyed now. She has gone still the way she goes still when the screen is doing something, and I rewind it, and we watch it again, and the second time I see what she sees, which is what I did not let myself see the first time and did not see in 1999 at all.

He didn't sign *in.* He signed the reach. The reach and the descent and the catch, three things in a sequence, his hand *crossing into* the plane of the machine at the moment it closed — which is not the same as a hand that was lying *in* the machine when it closed, those are two different accidents, two different liabilities, two different sixty-thousand-dollar questions — and I had taken the sequence, the beautiful precise terrible sequence his body laid out in the air, and I had ironed it into a preposition. *In.* One word. A flat little English preposition with no time in it, no reach in it, no half-second of a man's hand crossing a line it shouldn't have crossed, and the deposition went into a record as *in,* and I do not know what it cost him because depositions don't come back to tell you, and I sit in the cold unit and feel the first thing that is not relief.

"That's a directional thing," Bee says. Gently, for her. "And a classifier. The reach is the verb. You flattened the verb into a — " she does the sign for *location,* a static point in space, and then she does *in,* and lets her hand sit there, dead, in the air. "You parked it. You took a moving thing and you parked it where the lawyers could find it."

"It's ambiguous," I say, and I hear it leave my mouth and I hear exactly what it is.

Bee hears it too. She looks at me. She does not sign anything. She just lets me listen to the word I just used — *ambiguous,* the word I used in Irene's living room about a tape I hadn't watched, the word that is the first brick — and she waits, the way she has learned to wait, and after a moment I say, "Forget I said that," and she says, out loud, in English, which she almost never does, "No," and rewinds the tape herself.

* * *

We find another one before lunch.

2003. A custody evaluation, a Deaf mother, the question of whether she had ever *threatened* the father or merely *described being afraid of* him — and the difference, the whole difference, the difference that determined where a child slept, lives in a non-manual marker, in whether the brows were up or down over a single sign, in whether the sentence was a report of her own fear or a conditional about what she might do, and the brows are *right there* on the tape, I can see them now, I could not afford to see them then because I was three seconds behind and the next sentence was already coming and you cannot voice an eyebrow, there is no English keystroke for an eyebrow, I have been telling you that since the first morning and I told it to you then like it was a charming limitation of my craft and not the place where I bury people.

And the render is clean. The render is grammatical. The render is a sentence a court can act on, which is exactly the problem, because the thing on her face was not a sentence a court can act on. It was a frightened woman marking the difference between what she felt and what she would do, and I gave the court the cleaner thing, the actionable thing, the *thing,* and her brows went into the air and out of the air and were never anywhere a record could hold them.

By the third one I have stopped saying *ambiguous.* By the third one I am not saying anything. I am just running the deck, and Bee is reading over my shoulder, and we are not finding the tumor. That is the thing I have to make you understand, because it is the whole turn of the day and it is worse than anything I came afraid of: we are not finding the tumor. There is no tumor. There is no single bad tape I can hold up and excise. There is a body of work, twenty years of it, and the gaps are *everywhere,* small, individually survivable, each one defensible on its own, each one a thing a board of my peers would call *within tolerance* —

— and they all lean the same way.

* * *

That is the sentence I do not want to write, so I am going to write it plainly and not dress it, because dressing it would be the disease performing the cure.

They all lean the same way.

Not randomly. That is what I came braced for — random error, the noise floor of a hard craft, the inevitable percentage you lose in any act of carrying meaning across a gap, the way a structural engineer accepts that no weld is perfect and builds the tolerance in. Random error I could have lived inside. Random error has no author; it is weather; a pane of glass cannot be guilty of the weather. But this is not weather. Weather scatters. This *sorts.* Every single time the source was ambiguous — every time a hand could have gone two ways, every time a brow lifted a sentence into a question or a conditional or a fear, every time a classifier showed a reach instead of a position, every time a Deaf person built something true and spatial and irreducible in the air in front of me — I resolved it. I am a resolver. That is what fast is. That is what *good* is, in the only definition I ever let matter: good is the interpreter who does not make the court wait, who does not say *I'm not sure,* who closes the gap and commits the sentence and lets the record move. And I closed every gap toward the same shore. Toward fluency. Toward grammar. Toward the clean actionable English a hearing court can convict on, act on, rule on, *use* — because the alternative, every time, in the half-second I had, was to hand the room an ambiguity, and I had built my entire life on never being the one who handed the room an ambiguity.

I did not lie. I want to be exact, because exactness is the last thing I own and I am not done owning it even now. I never once signed a thing I knew to be false. Every render was *defensible.* And that is the horror Bee was trying to walk me into all morning, the horror I kept trying to make smaller by looking for a tumor: it would be so much better if I had lied. A lie is an event. A lie has a date and a motive and a moment you could go back to and not do. This is not an event. This is a *method.* This is the shape of the hand that made it, exactly as she said, and the hand is mine, and it made the same shape ten thousand times, and every time it was doing its job perfectly.

"Stop," Bee says.

I have not been signing. I have been sitting in the yellow light with my hands open on my knees, and she is watching my face, and whatever is on it has made her put down the tape she was holding.

"You did the thing again," she says. "Just now. In your head. I can see it." Her hands are careful now, slow, the way Irene went slow, except Irene went slow to insult me and Bee is going slow because she is twenty-seven and she has just watched the most famous interpreter in the county figure out that she is the disease, and even Bee, who has no reason to spare me, is sparing me a little, and I hate that I can tell, and I hate that the part of me that notices she is sparing me is the same part that is, even now, *grateful,* because gratitude is warm and the rest of this is so cold. "You're already turning it into a confession. I can see you doing it. You're going, *it's all me, it was always me, I'm the rot* — and that's — Ruth, that's the cleanest render you've done all day."

* * *

I do not understand her at first. I want that on the record too, because it is the exact center of the day and I missed it, three seconds behind a twenty-seven-year-old the way I was three seconds behind a dying woman in 1991 and a deprived man in 2009, always three seconds behind the thing that matters, always arriving with my hands full of the wrong sentence.

"It *is* me," I say. "Look at it." I gesture at the deck, the boxes, twenty years of it. "You just watched it. The gaps are mine. The method is mine. I'm not arguing anymore, Bee, I'm agreeing with you — it's me, it's the way I work, I broke him the way I broke all of them, with my hands doing exactly what they were trained to do—"

"Yeah." She cuts me off, and there is something almost like anger in it now, the clean impatient anger of her generation at mine, the anger that has no patience left for a certain kind of beautiful self-laceration it has correctly identified as a luxury. "Yeah. It's you. It's also a court that built a machine that *needs* a person to do exactly what you do." She is fast now, done being careful, and I have to lunge to keep up, which is its own humiliation, to be lunging after the meaning of my own crime in the mouth of someone half my age. "You think you invented this? You think you're special? Every interpreter in that building does what you do. They have to. The whole system runs on somebody standing in the gap and *closing* it, fast, clean, no asterisks, because a court can't run on asterisks, a court can't convict on *maybe,* a court needs a sentence it can write down — so it hires a person to turn the maybe into a sentence and then it calls that person *neutral,* and when the sentence turns out to be wrong the person goes, *oh, it was me, I'm the rot,* and the machine just—" she snaps her fingers, an English gesture, sharp in the cold — "hires the next set of hands. You want to be the rot so bad. You'd rather be the whole disease than admit you're one—" she hits a hole, the way Tobias hits holes, except hers is rhetorical, she reaches for the word and finds it and it is worse than a hole — "*organ.* In a body that was built sick."

And I do the thing she is describing while she is describing it.

I feel myself do it. That is the new thing, the thing the last eleven chapters have done to me, the thing I would give a great deal to undo: I can feel the deciding now. I could not feel it for thirty years and now I can feel it every time, the small internal motion where a true thing comes at me and I reach for the shape I can live inside, and what she is offering me — *one organ in a body built sick* — is unbearable, and I reach past it, in real time, for the thing I came to the storage unit to find, which is *it's all me.* Because *it's all me* is a catastrophe I can hold. It has a perpetrator. It can be confessed. It can, in some warped accounting I have run my whole life on, be *paid.* And *one organ in a body built sick* — a system, a machine, a thing with no single defendant, a harm that every neutral hand produced and not one of them committed — that is not a catastrophe I can hold. There is no one to punish. There is no confession that closes it. There is just the grinding, and my hands inside it, doing their job, forever.

"I'd rather it be me," I say. Out loud. To a deaf woman, stupidly, in English, and then I sign it, badly, because my hands have gone slow and unsure, the way they go now. *I'd rather it be me.*

"I know," Bee says. Quiet. "That's the part that scares me about you."

* * *

We almost miss it.

It is past three. We have run eleven tapes and I have stopped keeping the warm thing and started keeping a different ledger, a cold running tally I will not show Lockwood and cannot stop counting, and Bee has gone to the front of the unit to stand in the daylight for a minute because the cold and the yellow light and the sheer accumulating weight of it have gotten to even her, and I am repacking the 2009 box — the working file, the duplicate documents Lockwood's office sent over, the paper that came with the tape — and a manila folder slides and fans, and I am crouched in the doorway gathering it, not looking, three of the pages already back in the folder before my eye catches on the one in my hand.

It is an invoice. Interpreter services. County purchase order, 2009, the trial dates, a vendor number, the rate sheet boilerplate I have read a thousand times because the rate sheet is the water I swim in. And it is itemized.

And there are two line items.

I read it the way you read a thing you do not yet understand is going to change everything — flatly, professionally, my eye doing the practiced scan it does over any county document, and it is only on the second pass that the shape of it arrives. Two line items. Two interpreters. Both dated to the trial. One of them is me — my old vendor number, the per-diem I billed in 2009, the figure that paid my rent that month. And the other one is not me. A second vendor number. A different rate code — I know the rate codes, I have lived inside the rate codes for twenty-six years, and this one is the code they use for — I sit down. I actually sit down, in the cold doorway, with the invoice in my hand, because the rate code is the one the county uses for a Certified Deaf Interpreter, and it is billed for two days, the first two days of a trial that ran nine, and then it stops.

"Bee."

She doesn't hear me. She is in the daylight, her back to me, on her phone.

"Bee." I am on my feet, I have crossed the unit, I have the invoice out in front of me like a thing that is hot, and she turns, and reads my face first the way she always reads my face first, and then takes the page, and goes very still, and her eyes move down it the way mine did, the scan, the second pass, the arrival.

She finds it faster than I did. Of course she does. She is reading the rate code before I can point to it, and her hand comes up to her mouth, an English gesture, the gesture of a person who has just understood something a beat ahead of the person who handed it to her, and over the top of the page her eyes come up to mine, and what is in them is not the gentleness from before and not the impatience from before.

It is the vanished figure on the tape.

I see her see it. I see the connection close in her, faster than it is closing in me, the thing she half-noticed at the booking in her own archive three weeks ago — *a hand, a wrist, a sleeve signing inward, and then the timecode jumps and after, empty* — the thing she flagged as *weird they'd swap mid-setup* and we both filed under artifact, both of us, the famous interpreter and the native reader, both of us looked directly at a Deaf woman signing to Tobias Rein and called her a scheduling error and moved on to the part of the tape that was about me. There was someone else in the room. The tape said so and we did not read it. And now the paper says so, in the one language the county cannot smooth or soften or round, the language of money, the rate code that does not lie because no one ever thought to make it lie: a second interpreter was billed, a *Deaf* interpreter, the kind they assign to a signer like Tobias precisely because a person like me is not enough — and she was there for two days, and then the county stopped paying for her, and I solo-rendered the rest.

"They sent her home," Bee signs. Slow. Not for my benefit this time — slow because she is working it out as her hands move, the way you slow down to read damaged signing, except the damage is in the record, the damage *is* the record. "Two days. Then—" she taps the line where the number stops, the white space under it where seven more days of a trial should have a second set of hands and do not — "nothing. They sent her home and they kept you."

"We don't know why," I say. The bricklayer is already awake in me, I can feel him, reaching for the trowel even now, even here, even with the floor gone out from under both of us — *we don't know it was the budget, we don't know it was a choice, a CDI can withdraw, can be unavailable, can have a conflict, there are a hundred reasons* — and Bee watches me start to lay it, watches the wall begin to go up, and does not stop me this time, because this time the wall does not matter. This time there is a number on a page with a date under it and a white space where a Deaf woman's hands used to be, and no wall I build is going to fill that white space, and we both know it, and she lets me see her know it.

"Ruth." She holds the invoice up between us, into the daylight at the mouth of the unit, the way Lockwood held the tape up in the hallway fifteen days ago so I could read my own case number off the label, the exact same gesture, a hand holding a piece of paper up to the light so the other person can read the thing that is about to take the floor out — and I think: *this is how it comes, then. Always a hand. Always a thing held up to the light. Always someone standing in a doorway making you read the part you skipped.*

She does not sign anything else. She just holds it there, two line items in the cold spring light, and lets me read the second one — the one that is not me, the one I have been looking at, on the tape and on the paper, for fifteen years, and have never once seen.

There was a second hand in that room.

And someone decided it should stop.


# Chapter Thirteen — Deaf Space

The Hollow runs its Deaf night on the second Friday, in the back room of a coffeehouse on Detroit Avenue that gave up on being a coffeehouse around nine and committed to being something better, and the way you know you've arrived at a Deaf event before you've opened the door is that the door has a sign taped to it — PULL HARD, STICKS — and the parking lot is full of people not leaving. Hearing people leave a building and the leaving takes a minute. Here there were four conversations going on the asphalt under the one working sodium light, hands moving in the orange dark, people half into their cars and not getting in, a goodbye I could have set my watch by if a goodbye out here ever ended.

I had not been to a Deaf event I wasn't being paid to attend in a very long time.

I want to be exact about that, because the inexactness of it is the thing. I have spent thirty years inside this community the way a surgeon spends thirty years inside bodies — intimately, expertly, professionally, and from the outside. I know the etiquette better than some of the people who own it by birth. I know not to touch a shoulder from behind, to wave low in the visual field, to flick the lights and not shout, to leave the path of a signed conversation the way you'd leave the path of two people carrying a couch. I know all of it. I have been *teaching* all of it, at the university, two evenings a fall, to rooms of hearing twenty-two-year-olds who think they're going to save somebody. And I walked up to the door of the Hollow on a second Friday in June and pulled hard because it sticks, and I felt, in the second before I was inside, the specific vertigo of a person about to be a guest in a house she has always told herself she lives in.

Bee was already there. Of course Bee was already there. Bee had grown up in rooms like this; rooms like this were the first rooms she was ever in, before language was a thing she had to acquire, when it was just the air her parents breathed. She saw me come in and did not come over. She lifted her chin a half inch — *you made it* — and went back to the three people she was standing with, two women and a man, all of them signing at the speed of people who do not need to be polite to each other, and one of the women glanced past Bee's shoulder at me and then back, and signed something fast and low, down by her hip, the way you say a thing you don't mind being seen but don't intend to be read, and the man laughed without sound, and I caught exactly one sign of it.

I caught my name.

Not Ruth. My name-sign. The R at the chin and the small sideways inch — *R-but,* the one Meg hung on me when I was twelve, the one the community has made at me across thirty years of banquets I billed by the hour and never sat down at — and the woman made it with a little torque on it, a flavor, the way you'd say a name you have an opinion about, and the man laughed, and I stood inside the door of the Hollow having traveled forty minutes to be in a room where the first thing that happened was that I was a punchline I couldn't quite hear.

I'd like to tell you I let it go.

* * *

The room itself was built right, which is to say built by Deaf people for Deaf eyes, and a hearing person notices it the way you notice good engineering, which is by not noticing it until you do. The light was everywhere and from nowhere — no lamp you could point to, no shadowed corner, the whole ceiling washed soft and even so that a face was readable from any angle in the room. The chairs didn't face a stage; they faced each other, clustered in loose open rings, every ring a sightline. The walls were a flat matte dark so the hands came forward off them. Somebody had killed the overhead music a venue like this would normally pump, and in its place the bass had been turned up past music into pure floor — you felt it in the soles of your feet, a pulse, the room keeping a time you didn't hear so much as stand inside. A wall-mounted strobe in the corner flashed amber every so often: the call-light, wired to the front door and the bar, so that *somebody wants you* arrived as a change in the light and not a sound. Three TVs, no captions, a Deaf creator's vlog running on one and a captcomedy clip on another and a basketball game on the third, all of it visual, all of it talking without talking.

It was the opposite of the room I make my living in. In 4B the light is bad on purpose — fluorescent, flattening, designed by no one for nothing — and the architecture puts the Deaf witness at the front, alone, facing twelve hearing strangers and a court reporter whose machine cannot take down a single thing his hands say, and the one readable face in the place is mine, and everybody watches *me.* Irene had said it on her porch and I had built a wall against it and the wall had held for about a day. *Nobody in that room was watching him.* Here, nobody would have watched me. Here I was the least readable person in the building, and I clocked the elders by the bar (a knot of them, three women and a thin old man in a Deaf-club windbreaker from some softball league that had folded before the millennium) and the kids by the basketball TV (fast, loud-handed, code-switching into signs I'd have had to ask about) and Bee everywhere, and started toward the one I'd come for.

I had come to find the back-channel to Irene, and to the thing Irene had thrown at my feet and refused to pick up — *you don't even know what was in the room with you* — and Bee, who would not explain to me why, had said the Hollow on the second Friday, and had said one name.

* * *

His name was Cy.

Bee had spelled it for me three times in the archive, slow, because I kept trying to make it into Cyrus or Silas and she kept stopping me — *just Cy, that's the whole thing, C-Y, don't fix it* — and she'd said he was old enough to have been around for everything, deep enough in the church side of the community to have known Tobias from before, and that he would talk to me or he wouldn't and there was no way to make him and I was not, under any circumstances, to lead with the tape.

I found him at the elders' end of the bar with a decaf going cold in front of him, a small dense man somewhere past seventy with hearing aids he plainly wore for the doctors and not for himself, a Deaf-school class ring gone thin on a thick finger, and the particular stillness of a man who has watched a great many hearing people walk toward him with a need on their face. He watched me do it now. I felt him grade the walk. By the time I reached him he had already decided several things about me and I had the disorienting sense — the one I'd had with Tobias, the one I keep having lately, the one I have spent my whole career on the other side of — of being interpreted before I'd said a word.

I had a name-sign and I used it, introduced myself the way you do, *R-but,* and watched it land on him and watched him not be surprised by it, which meant he'd known I was coming, which meant Bee had told the room more than she'd told me. He let me stand there. He did not offer me the stool. In a hearing bar that's rudeness; here it was just information, the same information the dead doorbell had been — *you may stand in my visual field and I will decide.*

*I know who you are,* he signed, finally. He didn't fingerspell *interpreter.* He made the sign for it — the two flat hands, the twist, the conduit, the thing that turns one language into another — and then he did a thing with it I have never seen anyone do, a thing I have been doing in my head ever since: he made the sign and then, on the back of it, without pausing, he tipped his head a few degrees and let his eyes go flat and bored, so that the same clean sign for *interpreter* arrived wearing an expression, and the expression said *the one who talks for us. The mouth.* He didn't sign a single insult. He let his face do it on top of a neutral noun, which is grammar, which is precisely the thing I am paid not to be able to render, and I stood there and received the most articulate contempt of my week delivered in a non-manual marker I could not have typed into any record on earth.

*You ran the courtrooms,* he went on. *Years. Everybody knows your hands.* The same flat eyes. *Beautiful hands.* Irene had said it too.
