They keep saying it like that, like a thing you'd say at a funeral, *didn't she look beautiful,* and I am beginning to understand that in this community my hands are spoken of the way you speak of a weapon that was used on someone you loved.

I did not lead with the tape.

I want credit for that, small as it is. Everything in me wanted to lay it out — *there was a Deaf interpreter, Cy, at the start of Tobias's trial, I have an invoice with two line-items, I have a figure on the tape who vanishes at six minutes, I need a name* — everything in me wanted to do what I do, which is take the fastest line to the information and trust my hands to carry the freight. And I held it. I asked him, instead, about Tobias. Before. The church. The long slow work Irene had described, of finding the ones the hearing world had lost and bringing them in late.

And something in his face changed, not toward me, but toward the subject, and he set his cold decaf to the side, and he began to tell me about a man I had convicted and never once met until a bolted table in Grafton three weeks ago.

* * *

He told it the way they tell things, which is to say he built it, set it in the air and walked me through it, and I am going to give you what I got and I am going to tell you, again, the way I keep having to tell you, that what I got is already less than what he gave.

He set Tobias young — placed a younger Tobias in the space to his left, low, and signed *small* over him, not body-small, *world-small,* a young man whose world had been kept small — and he showed me the church bringing him in. He showed me, with two hands becoming two hands, the way the Deaf elders had taught a grown man to sign, which is a thing I had never let myself picture: a man in his twenties sitting in a fellowship-hall folding chair being given, late, in pieces, by patient strangers, the language his parents had spent his childhood drilling out of his hands. *We gave him what we could,* Cy signed, and the *what we could* he made small and rueful, palms close together, *a little at a time, what was left of him to give it to.* And then he made a sign I watched land in my own chest like a dropped stone: he showed Tobias receiving the language and being grateful for it and *still* — Cy held up one hand, fingers spread, and slowly closed two of them down, leaving gaps in the spread, *holes,* the fingers that wouldn't come back up — *still full of holes. Because you can't give a man back the years they held his hands down. You can teach him to talk after. You can't put back the years he was supposed to be a child in a language.*

I knew this. I want to be honest: I knew this clinically. I have the word for it. I had used the word for it in my own kitchen, *deprivation,* held it up like the pocketknife it is and stood behind it. What Cy did was take the clinical word out of my hands and replace it with a man in a folding chair, grateful and gapped, and I felt the floor of my professional vocabulary tilt under me the way it has been tilting for three weeks, and I did the thing I have started to be able to do, barely, which is nothing. I did not reach for a better word. I let his stand.

*Then they put him in a court,* Cy signed.

And here he stopped building and just looked at me, the flat eyes, and I understood that we had arrived, by his road and not mine, at the thing I had come for, and that he had brought me here on purpose, the long way, so that when we got to it I would not be able to pretend it was about a procedural irregularity in an interpreter-services invoice.

* * *

*There was a woman,* he signed.

He placed her. To his right, opposite where he'd set young Tobias — a woman, and he made her with respect, his hands going careful and exact the way a person's hands go careful when they sign about somebody they admire and have lost touch with. *Deaf. Like us. A real one.* He fingerspelled it then, slow, the way you spell a title you want to land: C-D-I. *A Deaf interpreter. Certified. They had her at the start. The first days.* He set her in the space beside the young placed-Tobias, close, and ran a line between them — *she could reach him,* the line said, *she had the hands for his holes, she knew the country he came from because she'd had to build her own bridges over the same kind of water.*

I kept my face still. It is half of what I am paid for and I have never wanted it more than in that moment, because everything Lockwood and I and Bee had been digging out of fifteen-year-old paper — the half-line in the scheduling memo, the invoice with the two line-items I could not explain, the figure on the early reel who signs to Tobias close and low and is gone by six minutes — all of it was sitting in a man's hands at a bar on Detroit Avenue, alive, with a face on it and a respect on it, and if I let my own face do what it wanted to do, which was *light up with the joy of a hunch confirmed,* he would see it, and he would know I had come for a fact and not a man, and the hands would close the way Irene's had closed.

So I sat in it. I made the surface. And it cost me, because making the surface meant not letting myself feel the thing the surface was over, which was that there had been someone in that room who could read him, and I had stood three seconds in front of him for a whole trial and never once turned around to see her.

*What happened to her,* I signed. Carefully. Brows neutral. A question with no hunger in it that I could keep out.

Cy made the sign I have not been able to put down since.

He took the placed woman — set careful and close beside Tobias — and he removed her. Not dramatically. That is the thing. He did not slash her away or throw her off. He made a small flat economical gesture, a hand sweeping a thing gently off a table the way you'd clear a setting that wasn't needed, *sent home,* and his face while he did it was not angry. It was tired. It was the face of a man reporting a weather he has reported many times to people who came too late to do anything about it.

*Sent home,* he signed. *Partway through. They had the certified one already.* And he looked straight at me when he made *the certified one,* and pointed — not at me, at the idea of me, the locus where he'd set *interpreter* with its flat bored face ten minutes ago — *the fast one. The beautiful hands. Why pay for two.*

* * *

*Why pay for two.* I felt the sentence do its work in me before I could stop it — the small fast slide toward *that's a budget decision, that's a scheduling clerk and a per-diem and a calendar, that happens forty times a year two floors down from 4B, I watched it happen to Reyes last month, a CDI kicked off a custody case to save a line item, it isn't malice, it's a Tuesday* — and I must have let something of it onto my face, some loosening, some private relief at a thing turning out to be systemic and therefore not mine, because Cy's hands came up before I had moved mine.

*No,* he signed. Just that, flat, the way you stop a child reaching for a hot thing. Then he did with his face what he had done with the interpreter-sign: he gave me an expression and let it be the whole sentence. He let his eyes go knowing, almost gentle, and tipped his head a few degrees toward me, and I understood that he had watched the *budget* arrive in me and named it before I could — *you're doing it now. I can see you do it. You're making it a Tuesday so it stops being a man.* He held it there a beat. Then he signed, slow, *Either way you get to put it down,* and ran one hand off into the air, weight released. *All your fault — you put it down by paying. Nobody's fault, it's a calendar — you put it down by walking.* The flat eyes. *Same hand. Both times you set him down.*

I want it on the record that he caught me at the exact second I was doing it, in a language I am paid to read, and that the catching was cleaner than any wall I have ever built — because he was right, and I had not yet finished telling myself he was wrong. *It's all my fault* and *it's nobody's fault, it's a budget* are the same relief: one puts the whole weight on me where I can carry it, one lifts it off me where I don't have to, and either way the man in the room — whose only true reader was cleared off like a place setting, leaving him alone with my fast sure hands — gets smoothed into a sentence I can live inside. I did not argue it. That is the only progress I can report. I left his correction standing where he'd set it and asked him the next thing instead.

*Do you know her name.*

And Cy, who had brought me the whole long way to this on purpose, who had made me earn the woman by sitting through the man, looked at me for a while with the cold decaf going colder, and I felt him weigh it — weigh me, weigh whether the fast hands had come to do right or to do well, which Tobias had taught me three weeks ago are never the same thing — and whatever he saw, it was not enough yet, or it was too much, because he did the thing they keep doing to me, the thing I have done to a thousand witnesses and am only now learning the inside of: he gave me almost all of it and kept the last inch.

*She left after,* he signed. *Couldn't do it anymore. The courts.* He made the conduit-sign again, the interpreter-sign, and this time he broke it — made it and then let the two flat hands fall apart, the twist undone, a thing that stopped turning one language into another and just stopped. *She saw what they were doing to him and she couldn't stop it. You can't, you know. When you're the Deaf one in that chair. They don't have to listen to you. You're not the certified one.* The flat eyes again, but something under them now, something old and specific. *She told them. In the room. She told them the render was wrong.*

The room's amber call-light flashed in the corner — somebody at the door, somebody wanting in — and I barely registered it, because the floor I had been standing on, the floor I had nailed down at midnight three weeks ago with four keystrokes and a confession, *I rendered too cleanly, I alone broke him,* had just gone soft under my whole weight.

*She told them,* I signed back, and I could not keep the hunger out of it now, the surface cracked, *and what did they do.*

Cy lifted one shoulder. The smallest sign in his whole account and the heaviest.

*What do they ever do,* he signed. *She's Deaf. You're not.*

* * *

I have to tell you about Bee, because Bee is the reason I did not get the name that night, and the way Bee is the reason matters more than the name.

I had been so deep in Cy's hands that I had stopped tracking the room, which in this room is the cardinal sin, and when I finally surfaced — the call-light still pulsing, the elders' end of the bar shifting as a new arrival came through — I looked up and found that the social weather had changed and I had not read it changing. The woman who'd made my name-sign with a torque an hour ago was at Cy's other elbow now, and two more had drifted in, and the cluster had become a small court, and they were all signing at once, overlapping, the way they do, three threads braided and none of them waiting, and at least one of the threads was about me, and I was sitting inside it the way Tobias had sat inside his trial — present, central, discussed, and a beat behind every hand in the room.

And Bee was in it.

Bee had crossed the room without my clocking her and slid into the cluster and she was *handling* it — handling them, handling me, handling the temperature — and she did it the way you do a thing you were born inside, with no visible effort, her signing dropping into the regional, idiomatic, compressed register these elders used among themselves, the register I lunge after and reconstruct from context, and she was *funny* in it, she landed something that made the thin old softball man bark his soundless laugh, and she touched the torque-woman's arm and said something quick and low that took the edge off whatever the woman had been building, and she never once looked at me to check whether I needed rescuing, because it would not have occurred to her that I might. To Bee this was just a room. To Bee I was the person in the room who needed the most help and knew it the least.

I felt the old thing. I am going to be honest about the old thing because I have promised myself I will stop smoothing it. The old thing was not gratitude. A better woman would have felt gratitude. What I felt, watching a twenty-seven-year-old move through a room I have claimed for thirty years like she owned the deed and I'd been renting, was a small ugly flare of *I could do that,* immediately followed by the knowledge that I could not, have never been able to, will never be able to, because the thing Bee was doing is not a skill you acquire at the speed of mine — it is a thing you are *given,* in folding chairs, before you have a self, and no certification on earth confers it. Bee was doing in that room the thing I have spent my whole life doing in courtrooms, which is being the most fluent person present, and she was doing it in the one room where my fluency is a tourist's and hers is a birthright, and I sat at the bar next to a man who would not give me a name and watched my own protégée out-belong me, and I covered it. I made my face do *fond.* I am very good at making my face do things. I am not at all sure she didn't see straight through it, because that is the other thing about Bee, the thing that makes her my better and not my assistant: she reads the gap between what a face is doing and what's under it the way I read a directional verb, fast and without being told, and there is no keystroke for what she caught me at.

She peeled off from the cluster eventually and came and stood at my shoulder — in my visual field, low, the way you were taught, the way she didn't have to be taught — and she did not sit.

*You should go,* she signed. Not unkind. *You got what Cy was going to give you tonight. You push now, you lose him and you lose the next three people he tells about you.* She glanced back at the cluster, at the torque-woman. *They're deciding about you. Right now. Whether you're a person who broke something and came back, or a person who broke something and came back to look good doing it. Don't help them decide the wrong one by staying too long.*

*I need the name,* I signed.

*I know what you need.* She said it the way Irene had said it. They all say it that way, the people in this world, *I know what you need,* and they are always right, and the rightness is the worst part. *You'll get it. Not from him, not tonight. There's a woman who'll know it for certain and she's not here and she's not easy and you're not ready for her yet.* A beat. *And neither am I, so don't ask.*

* * *

I asked anyway — not about the woman, I had the sense to leave that — but about Bee, about the thing I had been not-asking for three weeks, because the room had cracked something open in me and I was tired of being the only one in it with a wall.

*Why are you in this,* I signed. *Really. You could have digitized the tape and handed it to Lockwood and been done. You keep showing up. You keep dragging me to porches and bars. Why.*

And Bee, who is twenty-seven, who is online and impatient and morally clearer than I will ever be, who has spent three weeks being right at me — Bee stopped being any of those things for about four seconds, and signed me a true thing, and I am going to give it to you the way she gave it, because it is hers, and because it is the one piece of this whole night I am sure I did not smooth.

*My dad,* she signed. She placed him — to her left, the way Cy had placed Tobias, the way they all place the people who matter, in the air where you can point back at them. *Deaf. DeafBlind now, at the end, but Deaf my whole childhood.* Her hands were careful the way Cy's had gone careful. *When I was eleven he had a hearing. Workman's comp thing, he got hurt on a job and the company fought it. And they brought an interpreter.* She made the conduit-sign, and then — she did the thing Cy had done, the thing this whole room apparently knows how to do and I am only now learning to see — she put a face on the neutral noun. But where Cy's face had been flat contempt, Bee's was something worse: a bright eager incompetent helpfulness, a face *trying,* a face that meant well. *Not a bad person. Just not good enough. In over her hands. And she smoothed him. I was eleven and I was sitting in the back and I could read both of them — I could read my own father and I could read what came out of her mouth, and they weren't the same man.* She let that sit. *She made him sound unsure when he was certain. She made his ASL into nervous English. And the certainty was the whole case — whether he'd known the platform was unsafe — and she rendered the certainty right out of him because she didn't have the hands to hold it, and he lost. He lost the case in the gap between what he said and what she said, and I watched it happen, at eleven, and there was nothing I could do because who listens to the deaf man's hearing kid in the back row.*

She looked at me. Not cruelly. That was the thing about it. There was no aim in it.

*So that's why,* she signed. *Not to catch you. People think that's why and it's not.* She glanced once at the cluster, at the elders, at the whole bright readable room. *It's because there's always a kid in the back row reading both of them and knowing the truth and having no standing to say it. I was that kid. Tobias never even had that — nobody in his family signs, there was no kid in his back row. I can't fix mine. My dad's hearing is gone, the case is gone, he's eighty next month and he can barely sign at all anymore.* And here the steadiness finally went, a hairline, the only one I saw in her all night. *But Tobias is alive. And the gap that convicted him is on a tape, for once, where somebody can actually point at it. How often does that happen. The gap is almost never on the tape.*

She straightened. The hairline closed.

*Go home, Ruth.*

* * *

I went home.

I drove east toward the lake with the windows down because June in Cleveland gives you maybe nine nights like that and you take them, and the whole way back the room stayed in my hands — the even light, the call-light pulsing amber for someone at a door, the elders deciding about me, Bee owning a deed I'd only ever rented, Cy clearing a Deaf woman off a table like an unneeded place setting and looking at me when he made *the certified one.*

For three weeks I had carried a sentence I built at my own kitchen table — *I alone broke him* — and felt the relief of it the way you feel relief signing a confession, because a thing that is all your fault is a thing you can pay for, and I would so much rather pay than be implicated, because paying has an end and implication doesn't. And the worst thing I learned at that bar was not that the sentence was too cruel to me. It was that it was too kind. There had been a woman in that room who saw the whole gap and *told them,* stood up as the one Deaf professional in it and said *that is not what he is saying,* and was not the certified one, and so was noise — and the room could not read her, because the room had me, three seconds behind a man I could not read, with my back to the only other reader in the place. *I alone broke him* let me keep being the one who knew. It even let me keep her invisible.

I do not have her name. I am going to get her name.

And I understood, somewhere around the Shoreway with the lake gone black and flat off my left shoulder, that getting it was going to be the easy part. The hard part — the part Bee said I wasn't ready for, the part with the woman who isn't easy — was that whoever she is, she is the one person alive who can tell me, with the hands to do it, exactly how much of the truth my fast sure render carried off a table fifteen years ago and threw away.


# Chapter Fourteen — What She Softened

Bee has a theory about how you find a person who has decided not to be found, and her theory is that you do not look for the person, you look for the paperwork the person could not take with them when they left. A certification does not belong to you. It belongs to the registry. You can quit court interpreting, quit the county, quit the whole hearing apparatus that ground you into quitting, but somewhere in a database your name is still attached to a credential number and a renewal cycle and a continuing-education ledger, because the system that discards you keeps better records of you than it ever kept of the people you were discarded for failing to protect.

"So she's in there," Bee says. We are at the long archive table, the dead-format decks humming, the lake doing its flat afternoon nothing through the high windows. Bee has three browser tabs open and a legal pad covered in the kind of notes I never take. "A CDI who worked Cuyahoga County in oh-nine. If she kept her cert current we can pull a directory listing. If she let it lapse—" Bee shrugs. "Then she really left. Then we're looking for a person, and people are harder than paperwork."

"How much harder."

"Depends how much she doesn't want to be found." Bee does not look up. "A Deaf interpreter who got dropped mid-trial and watched a man go down for it and walked away from the whole profession? That's not somebody who moved and forgot to forward her mail. That's somebody who *left.*"

I know the difference. I am, I am beginning to understand, somebody who never left — who stayed thirty years inside the exact machine that had once handed me a person I could not carry and let me drop him and called it faithful, and stayed not in spite of that but *because* of it, because staying let me keep being good at the one thing, and being good at the one thing was the only argument I had ever been able to make for myself.

But I do not say that. I watch Bee work the registry the way I'd watch a younger interpreter take a witness I could have taken better, and I keep my hands in my lap, and the name we are hunting stays a hole in the record where a person should be — a half-line in a fifteen-year-old scheduling memo, a second figure on a tape who steps back out of frame and does not come back. A woman the court spelled out of existence. I have spent two days now trying to put her back, and the work keeps stalling against the same wall, which is that I do not know what she is called, and you cannot pull a directory listing on a hole.

So I go home, and I do the thing I have been not-doing for two days, which is reach back past 2009 to the only other time I ever stood three seconds behind a person I loved and held a sentence in my hands that could decide them — to find out whether the hands that dropped Tobias Rein had ever, even once, been clean. I tell myself it is method. I tell myself I am looking for the pattern's origin, which is what a forensic person does. That is partly true. The other part is that I am nineteen again and I have been avoiding her for thirty years, and the avoiding has finally run out of room.

*

She was in the third bed by the window, on the fourth floor, the spring I was nineteen, and the stroke had been four days old and the doctors were no longer using the word *might.*

I have to be careful here, because I have told this to myself so many times that it has worn smooth, and a thing worn smooth is a thing you have stopped seeing, and I came back to it precisely to see it. So: facts. Margaret Okafor was sixty-one. The stroke had taken the right side of her body and most of the fine control on the left, which for a woman whose entire self lived in her hands was not a medical event, it was an amputation of the soul performed slowly and in public. She had been the most exact signer I ever knew. She signed the way my father welded, *exactly here, not approximately,* and four days after the stroke her left hand made shapes the way a person speaks through a mouth full of Novocaine — the meaning arrived, but bent, effortful, the consonants of it slurred, and I had to lean in over the rail and *build* her, lean in and slow down and assemble her sentences out of damaged pieces, I who had spent a decade being the fastest hands in any room.

They had offered her the on-call interpreter. The hospital kept one, a man, and she had refused him on the second day — *his hands are loud, he signs like he's selling me something,* she'd told me, the last fully clean sentence her left hand ever made, and it had been so like her, that contempt, that final flick of standard against a man who rounded, that I had laughed, and a nurse had looked at me laughing at the bedside of a stroke patient and decided something about me she was not wrong to decide.

So it was me. It was only ever going to be me. I had been interpreting for Meg informally since I was eleven and formally since the stroke took her hearing-English clean off her, the residual lipreading and the bit of voice she'd kept her whole life, gone in a night, so that the woman who had taught me that language is built and not transmitted was now entirely dependent on a nineteen-year-old's hands to build her a world. I was proud of it. I want that on the record, because it is the root of everything: I was *proud* to be needed like that. I had spent my childhood being handed off — a dead mother, a father who loved me in load paths — and here at last was a person who could not do without me, and I stood at that rail feeling the terrible warm thing I would chase for the rest of my life, the thing I felt in Grafton at a steel counter thinking I was the cavalry, the certainty that I, with my hands, would be *better.*

The doctor came on the fourth day to tell her where she stood.

*

He was not a cruel man. I have wanted him to be, across thirty years, because a cruel man would have made the rest of it simpler, and he refuses, even now, even in memory, to be simple. He was a tired man in his fifties with reading glasses pushed up into gray hair, and he came to the foot of the third bed by the window with a resident trailing him and a chart he barely looked at, and he was *blunt,* which is not the same as cruel, which is in fact the opposite of cruel if you believe, as my father believed, as this doctor plainly believed, that the kindest thing you can give a person is the true number.

He gave her a number. He gave her a consequence. He spoke to me — that was the arrangement, that was the violence built so deep into the architecture that no one in the room could see it was violence — he spoke *to me,* the hearing girl, about the Deaf woman in the bed, *tell her,* and I stood between them in the place I have stood my whole life since, the place that is nowhere the patient is and nowhere the doctor is, angled to the bed, and I received his English and turned to render it into her language with my hands.

And here is the sentence. Here is the thing I came back thirty years to look at without the smooth worn over it. He said — I have it exact, I have always had it exact, it is the most exact thing I own —

"The bleed did more damage than we hoped. There's a second event coming, and it will probably be the one that takes her. We're talking weeks. The intervention we discussed buys time but it's brutal and it won't change the ending. She needs to decide what she wants the weeks to be."

That was the source. A number — weeks. A consequence — *the one that takes her.* The bridge or the funeral, signed to me in spoken English by a man who believed he was being kind by being exact, and who was right to believe it, and whose exactness I then took into my hands to carry across, three seconds behind, into the language I had been loved and corrected in, to the woman who had built me.

I turned to the bed. Meg was propped at thirty degrees — for her lungs, and because she would not be a person who could not see her own door — and she was watching my hands already, the way she had watched my hands my whole life, waiting for me to tell her what the hearing man had said about her body. Her left hand lay on the blanket, ruined, ready to answer. Her eyes came up to mine.

And I want to show you what I did, in the grammar, because the grammar is the whole of it, because I have spent thirty years saying *I softened it* the way you say a thing to get past it, and the saying has let me not look at the actual hands, the actual choices, the specific places where a nineteen-year-old reached into a dying woman's sentence and changed the load it carried.

*

The doctor said *probably the one that takes her.* In ASL the certainty of that — *probably,* the near-sureness of it, the weight he had put on it with his tired flat voice — lives in the non-manuals, the brow, the slight forward press of the head that turns a possibility into an approaching fact. I had the sign for it. I had all the signs. I knew exactly how to set *a second event* into the space in front of me and let it come toward her with the brow-press that meant *this is coming, this is real, brace.*

I did not use the brow.

I signed *a second event is possible* — *possible,* brows neutral, the head still, a flat clean conditional, a thing that *might* arrive — when the doctor had not said possible, the doctor had said *probably the one that takes her,* and the width between those was not a word. There is no word that got changed. It was the press of my forehead I withheld. It was a degree of forward lean I did not give. The certainty lived on my face the way it had lived on his voice, and I declined to put it there, and a Deaf woman watching my face for the truth of her own death read *possible* off a daughter who had been given *probable* and could not bear to deliver it.

That is the gap. That is the first gap I ever made, eighteen years before Tobias Rein, and it lived in exactly the place his lived, in the part of the language the record cannot keep — not in a word a stenographer could have typed, but in a face. I omitted my own forehead. I have spent a career insisting the omission gap is the cruelest because it lives where nothing can catch it, and I learned that, I see now, at a rail on the fourth floor, by doing it.

*Weeks,* the doctor had said. I gave her *weeks* — I could not soften a number, a number is a number, even I could not reach into *weeks* and make it *months.* But he had said *the intervention buys time but it's brutal and it won't change the ending,* and what I signed was — I am going to give it to you straight, the way I would not give it straight to myself for thirty years — what I signed leaned. *There is a treatment. It is hard. It might give you more time.* I set *time* into the space as a thing you could still reach for. I let the *won't change the ending* go soft at the edges, fingerspelled fast and small and moved past, the way Tobias footbridges the holes in his own language, except I was not footbridging a hole, I was *making* one, I was building a gap in a true sentence and walking my aunt out over it.

I did not lie. I want to be exact, because exactness is the only confession I trust: there was no false word in it. Every sign I made was a sign the doctor's English could license, if you read his English generously, if you read it the way a frightened girl who loved the woman in the bed needed to read it. *Possible.* *Might.* *More time.* Each one a defensible render. Each one inside professional tolerance, if you squint, if you want it to be. I made his blunt true terrible number into a sentence with a door left open in it, and I did it with my face and my fingerspelling and the degrees of a lean, in the channels the record never keeps, and I told myself even then, even at nineteen, with my hands still in the air — *I only said what he said.*

It is the oldest sentence I own. It is older than the courtroom oath. I swore the courtroom oath for the first time at twenty-five and meant it, four thousand times since, *I will faithfully interpret* — but the real oath, the private one, the one underneath, I did not swear at nineteen at that rail. I broke it there, and then I built my whole life as the penance for the breaking, and got the penance exactly backward.

*

Meg read it.

Her eyes were on my face the entire time, because she had taught me herself that the face is where the grammar lives, taught me at her kitchen table at the place on my chin where my name-sign says *almost, not quite, let me fix that* — and she watched her daughter's forehead decline to deliver the press that meant *probable,* and she watched the *won't change the ending* go small and fast in my fingers, and her own ruined left hand lay still on the blanket and did not interrupt me, did not stop me, did not correct the render the way she had corrected ten thousand of my renders across nineteen years.

I thought — God help me, I thought it for thirty years — that she had not caught it. That the stroke had slowed her, that the morphine had blurred her, that for once in her exacting life Margaret Okafor had received a sentence from my hands and not seen the seam in it, and that I had successfully, lovingly, gotten away with it. I thought I had authored her a gentler world and she had simply lived in it.

I came back to this memory to find the pattern. I came back to confirm that the hand that dropped Tobias had a history, that the smoothing was a method and the method was old. And what I have instead, sitting in my apartment with the radiator knocking and the sleet starting at the glass, is the thing I always come away with, the wrong lesson worn so smooth I have mistaken it for the floor:

*I softened it, and softening kills. Never soften again.*

That is what I took from that rail. That is the whole of the vow. Whatever it cost — and it cost, it cost in a way I am not going to be able to keep from you much longer — it cost because I reached into a true sentence out of love and made it kinder than it was, and a person made a decision on the kindness, and the kindness was a lie even though every word in it was defensible, and I swore over the cost of it that I would never again let love put a degree of lean into a render. *Never soften. Never round. Never edit. Render exactly what the hands say and bear no authorship for what it does.* I built a thirty-year cathedral on that vow. I built it so high and so well that I have been, for three decades, the most faithful interpreter in Cuyahoga County, requested by name, sworn and certain, the pane of glass.

And the vow is wrong. I am beginning, this week, against everything in me, to be able to see that the vow is wrong — not because it is too strict but because it solves the wrong crime. I have spent thirty years guarding against *softening,* against the gap that bends a sentence kinder. I made that gap once, at nineteen, out of love. But the gap I made at 14:22 in 2009 was not a softening. It was a *smoothing.* It did not bend Tobias kinder. It made his broken, holed, terrified idiolect into clean grammatical English guilt, and clean grammatical English is not gentle, clean grammatical English is what you convict on. I built my whole defense against the wrong sin. I stood guard at the door love comes through and never noticed that competence was coming in the window the entire time, every hour, for thirty years, wearing a uniform that looked exactly like the thing I'd sworn to be.

*

"You went quiet on me," Bee texts, late, because Bee texts the way her parents sign, in the channel that does not require the other person to be available. *Found something. Call when you can.*

I do not call. It is past midnight and I am not fit to be read by anyone, and there is one more thing I have to do before I can let the present back in, which is the thing I came here for and have been circling, the thing the whole forensic exercise was a cover for.

I make myself line them up. The two gaps. 1991 and 2009. I have spent the day telling myself I came back to find a pattern, and now I make myself look at whether there is one, and what I find is worse than a pattern, because a pattern would mean the gaps are the same and I could draw one clean lesson across both of them and be done. They are not the same. One was a softening, made of love, in the channels of the face. One was a smoothing, made of competence, in the path of a verb. And the only thing they share — the only thread that runs from a rail on the fourth floor in 1991 to a green interview room in 2009 to a witness box I have not yet had to stand in — is the sentence I said to myself both times, the sentence I said tonight watching my own nineteen-year-old hands, the sentence I will say on the stand if I am not careful, the alibi I have worn so long it has grown into the skin:

*I only said what they said.*

Two true sentences, bent two different ways, by two different sins, thirty years apart — and one costume, draped over both, telling me I am not in either of them. I have been so certain, since the night I deleted that declaration, that I broke Tobias alone, that the gap at 14:22 was my method and my method only and the penance was mine to pay in full. I came back to 1991 to confirm it — to find the proof that my hands have always been the problem.

And what I have instead, at one in the morning, is a small cold question I cannot make go away, the kind of question that does not feel like a thought I am having so much as a thread someone has pulled in the back of a coat: *if I have been this wrong about the lesson of 1991 for thirty years — wrong about which sin it even was, wrong enough to build an entire false self on it — then what makes me so sure I have the lesson of 2009 right?*

What makes me so sure I broke him *alone?*

I turn the lamp off. I do not turn it off the way I turn off the monitor, decisively, a thing finished. I turn it off the way you set something down that you are not done with and cannot carry any further tonight. And in the dark, with the sleet finding the glass and Meg's ruined hand lying still on a blanket thirty years gone, the small cold thread does not let go, and for the first time since I deleted my own innocence in four keystrokes I am not certain — not certain of the one thing I came back here to make certain — and the not-certainty does not feel like absolution.

It feels like there is someone else in the room.

It always has. I just spent thirty years being too busy being the one who knew to turn around and see who.


# Chapter Fifteen — Two Line Items

The interpreter-scheduling office is two floors below the courtrooms and a different century of building. Upstairs is marble and brass and a baritone judge; down here is drop-ceiling, a carpet the color of a bad tooth, and Pam, who has worked the per-diems and the bookings for nineteen years and treats every certified-Deaf-interpreter request that crosses her desk as a small personal grief she is not allowed to act on.

She does not look glad to see me. People are usually glad to see me. I notice that she isn't, and I notice that I notice, which is the kind of thing I used to be too busy being requested-by-name to notice, and I am keeping a private tally now of all the doors that don't open the way doors open for the best interpreter in the building, and the tally is getting long.

"I need a file," I say.

"You need a court order," Pam says, not unkindly. "Or you need Lockwood to need it, and then I need a court order from him." She's eating a yogurt with a plastic spoon she's washed and reused; her keyboard has a layer of grime in the seams that says she's been at this desk longer than the carpet. "What year."

"Oh-nine."

She stops with the spoon halfway. Bee, behind me, signs nothing, which is its own kind of signing — she has put her hands still in her lap the way you go quiet in a room where someone is about to either help you or lie to you, and she wants to see which.

"Rein," Pam says. Not a question.

"You remember it."

"I remember the noise it made." She sets the yogurt down. "I don't have the file. The file's a court file, it's upstairs, it's sealed pending whatever you people are doing to it. What I have is the *scheduling.* Which nobody ever asks for, because nobody ever thinks the schedule is the thing." She is already turning to the second monitor, the one that runs the ancient county system, green text on black like something that should be extinct. "What do you want off it."

What I want is to be told I am wrong. I came down here, if I am honest — and I am trying, lately, to be honest, it turns out to be a muscle and mine has atrophied — I came down here certain. I have spent two weeks certain. The certainty has a clean shape and I have been living inside it like a man inside a verdict he wrote himself: *I rendered too cleanly; I made a deprived man's hands into grammatical guilt; I alone broke him.* It is a terrible thing to believe and it is, I had not let myself see until just now, watching Pam's grimed fingers find the year, a *comfortable* thing to believe, because a thing you did alone is a thing you can pay for alone.

"I want to know how many interpreters were on it," I say.

* * *

The thing about the record is that it is built to be read one way. *Let the record reflect.* The transcript reflects what I said into the room in 2009 — my English, his guilt, the verdict, fifteen years — and it reflects it perfectly, and it is a lie, and the perfection is how the lie holds. But the record has a back stairs. It always has a back stairs. The official version goes up the front in marble; the truth, if there is any, leaks out the back through the parts of the bureaucracy too dull to bother lying. The schedule. The invoice. The per-diem. The parenthetical a court reporter typed at 4:40 on a Friday and forgot.

"Two," Pam says.

I had the invoice already. Bee found it ten days ago in the digitized county batch — *Interpreter Services, State v. Rein, 11/2009,* two line-items, two per-diems, and I had stood in her dead-elevator archive and told her it was a clerical doubling, a re-bill, an error, the way I had stood in front of the tape and told her the vanished woman at 03:40 was a scheduling artifact. I am very good at telling people that the seam in the cloth is a flaw in the weave. I have a whole vocabulary for it. *Artifact. Doubling. Within tolerance.* My father had a tolerance of one millimeter and called the things outside it funerals; I have a tolerance the width of my own comfort and I call the things outside it clerical.

"Two interpreters," I say. "Both days?"

"That's the thing." Pam squints at the green text, scrolls, the cursor jumping line to line like something hunting. "No. Day one, two booked. Two confirmed. Two — here, two checked *in,* the desk stamped them both in at the Justice Center entrance, eight-fifteen, you and—" she stops. She does the thing people do when a name is about to come out of them and they decide, mid-breath, that it isn't theirs to spend. "And a second. A CDI. Day two, two booked. Day two—" the scroll slows. "Day two there's a confirm in the morning and a *cancel* in the afternoon, fourteen-ten, and it's not a witness cancel, it's not a no-show, it's a re-code. Somebody re-coded the second per-diem to a different matter. Same afternoon. Two-ten in the afternoon."

"Re-coded to what."

"*Reyes.*" She says it flat, and her mouth goes thin, and I understand that Reyes is not a fifteen-year-old name to Pam, Reyes is a *this-month* name, Reyes is the custody father in 7C whose first language barely is one and whose CDI she has watched get kicked off the calendar three times since April because a CDI is two line items and the county can only stomach one. The same hand that emptied that chair in 2009 is emptying it now, this week, two floors up, while I stand here. The machine did not break. The machine is *running.* It has never once stopped running. "They moved her to a cheaper fight," Pam says. "They always move her to a cheaper fight. There is always a cheaper fight."

Bee's hands come up out of her lap. *The afternoon she got moved* — she sets the trial in the air to her left, the place it happened, and the CDI in it, and then she takes the CDI *out,* a clean lift of the woman out of the courtroom and over to an empty space on the right, the cheaper fight, the re-code, and she leaves the courtroom standing there with the woman gone out of it — and then she points, hard, at the empty place where the woman had been, the chair nobody filled. She does not sign *and then he was alone with you.* She doesn't have to. The geometry is the sentence. The space says it. I read it the way the court reporter could never have read it, because it has no English keystroke, it is grammar that lives in the air to Bee's left, and what it says is: *they took the only person who could read him out of the room, and left you in it, and called what was left enough.*

"Who authorized the re-code," I say. My voice does something I don't like.

Pam looks at the green text for a long time.

"It's not on here," she says. "That's the part. You don't authorize a re-code in this system, you *request* it, and the request lives in a different module, and the different module got archived in the consolidation in—" she checks "—'19. So it's in the consolidation. Which is in cold storage. Which is being—" and here she stops eating her words and just looks at me, tired, a woman who has lost this exact fight a hundred times and is about to tell me I'm a hundred and one. "Ruth. They're purging the consolidation. The pre-2012 scheduling modules. There's a memo. Records retention, they're a decade past the hold, it's all going. Bonded shredder, the works." A small terrible shrug. "End of the quarter."

* * *

I want to put down, here, exactly the shape of what happened to my certainty in that drop-ceilinged room, because it is the closest I have come yet to understanding what I am and I do not entirely want to understand it.

For two weeks I have carried a clean sentence: *I alone broke him.* And in Pam's office the sentence did not get smaller. That is the part I expected and did not get. I expected relief — *it wasn't only me* — and there is some of that, low and shameful, the relief of a man told the cancer is in someone else's body too. But mostly the sentence didn't shrink. It changed *shape.* It stopped being a sentence I could stand at the front of holding the blame like a thing I'd carried in, and became a thing with no front, no single place to stand, no door — a *method,* a *machine,* a Tuesday-afternoon re-code at two-ten by a hand that isn't on the form — and I felt the vertigo of it, the specific nausea of a person who has organized her whole atonement around being the author of a harm and is now being told the harm has *no author,* that it was produced by a budget, executed flawlessly through her faithful hands, and that her faithfulness was not the alibi she thought and not the crime she'd decided either but the *instrument,* the clean tool the machine reached for because clean was cheaper than true.

I had wanted it to be my fault. I see that now and it is not flattering. I had *wanted* sole guilt, the way you want a diagnosis with a treatment, because a thing I did alone I can confess, and a thing I can confess I can pay for, and a thing I can pay for ends. A method does not end. A method is a way of seeing that I have been doing for twenty-six years and did not know I was doing, the way you don't know your own accent until you hear yourself on a tape, and there is no payment that closes it, there is only the rest of my life rendering, every render now a place where the same hand could make the same choice, and no verdict that lets me out.

I rendered exactly what I saw. I want that on the record, because it is true and because it is the worst thing I have to say. I rendered *exactly* what I saw — and what I saw was a deprived man's hands that *could not be faithfully rendered by a solo hearing interpreter,* because fluent ASL is not his ASL, his ASL is a language built in a house with the windows painted over, and the one person in the building who could have read those windows was re-coded to a cheaper fight at two-ten on a Tuesday, and I never even turned around to see the chair was empty. I was too busy being good. I gave them clean. Clean is what convicts.

"Faithful," I say, out loud, not meaning to, and Pam looks up because she thinks I'm asking her something.

I had sworn to be faithful. Four thousand times. I had meant it more than the lawyers meant their oaths. And the court — this court, this drop-ceiling, this green text, this woman washing her spoon — the court had made faithfulness *impossible* on the morning of day two, fourteen-ten, two-ten, take your pick, and then it had let me stand up and swear it anyway, and stand there being faithful to a thing that could not be faithfully done, and it had taken my faithful render and built fifteen years on it. The oath wasn't broken. That's the part that keeps moving in me. The oath was *kept.* I kept it. I was faithful to exactly what I could see, and being faithful to exactly what I could see is precisely how the man went away, because what I could see was already the wrong thing, was already a man without the interpreter he was legally owed, and faithfulness to a maimed source is just a cleaner way to lie.

* * *

My phone goes. It's the courthouse-app alert, the one that flashes — I keep it on flash now, an old habit borrowed from a Deaf world I serve and am not of, Bee set hers to flash because she lives where the doorbell is a light and I set mine to flash because it felt like the least I could do and even that is a vanity, even my humility is a costume — and the alert is Webb. Cantwell's filed something, and Pryor wants the interpreter in chambers at one-thirty on a scheduling matter, and I know what the scheduling matter is before I read it, because I have just spent forty minutes learning the grammar of how this building empties a chair.

They want to drop the second interpreter for the rest of Webb.

There are two Deaf witnesses left to recall — Lamar on a redirect Cantwell's trying to reopen, and Darnelle, whose mother sat in the gallery and watched her daughter's hands get called a *performance* by a defense attorney doing a little mocking flutter. Two Deaf witnesses, a long afternoon, the kind of afternoon where a single interpreter runs into the red by three o'clock and starts, without meaning to, without anyone in the room able to catch it, rounding. Conveying the gist. Smoothing. And there is a CDI booked for it — Pam shows me, scrolling forward fifteen years in the same green system to today, the same module, the same shape — a CDI booked for the Webb afternoons, because somebody upstairs once, correctly, decided two Deaf witnesses and a contested redirect was a two-interpreter day. And Pryor wants her dropped. *We have a certified interpreter already.* The exact words. I can hear them in his baritone, the one that carries to the back row, the one strangers feel selected by.

"He'll get it," Pam says, watching my face read the screen. "He always gets it. He's the judge. The judge says we're fine with one, we're fine with one." She washes her spoon in the cold coffee, dries it on a napkin, sets it back in the drawer for tomorrow. "I'll lose it by Thursday. I lose all of them by Thursday."

Bee is reading the same screen over my shoulder. She doesn't sign anything big. She makes one small sign, low, just for me, the way Lamar nodded at me across the well a lifetime ago — she signs *same,* a flat economical *same,* and then she tips her chin at the screen, at 2009 and today stacked in the same green font, *State v. Rein* and *State v. Webb,* two-ten on a Tuesday and one-thirty this afternoon, the same hand emptying the same chair fifteen years apart — and then she signs it again, *same,* and lets her face do the rest, and her face is not doing grief. Her face is doing the thing the Deaf do that the hearing read as cold and that is not cold, it is the look of a person who has been telling you a true thing for fifteen years and is watching you finally see it and is too tired to be glad. *Now you see it,* her face says. *Now, when there's a shredder scheduled. Welcome.*

* * *

So this is the state of it, and I will lay it out plain because plain is the only honest thing I have left and the elegies have started to taste like the costume too.

A Certified Deaf Interpreter was on the Tobias case. Day one she was in the room. Day two, at two-ten in the afternoon, a hand that is not on any form re-coded her to a cheaper fight, and I — faithful, certified, requested-by-name, the best in the building — finished the trial alone, rendering a man whose language I could voice fluently and read *wrongly,* and I did not turn around. That is no longer a thing I believe. As of this afternoon it is a thing the schedule says, a thing with a timecode, a thing as documented as the verdict and pointing the other way.

And the proof of it is in the pre-2012 consolidation, which is in cold storage, which is bonded for the shredder, which goes at the end of the quarter — and the hearing is the eighteenth of August, and the quarter ends before that, and I do not yet know the name of the woman they took out of the room, the only person alive who can stand up and say *I was there, and I was sent home, and I told them, and the room could not read me.*

There was a hand in that room that wasn't mine. The record kept every word I said and not one word of the hand that decided I would say them alone. That hand is being shredded at the end of the quarter.

"Pam." I am already moving, the one-thirty in chambers a clock now too, everything a clock. "The CDI. Day one. You started to say her name."

Pam looks at the green screen, and then at me, and for a second I think she's going to give it to me, the name, the thing I came down here without knowing I needed more than I needed to be told I was wrong.

Then her desk phone rings — it's the records-retention office, I can see it on the little display, COLD STG, the consolidation calling about the very consolidation, the machine phoning itself to confirm the burn — and she holds up one finger to me, *wait,* one finger, the width of it, and she picks up the receiver, and she says, "Scheduling," and she does not give me the name, and the quarter is ending, and somewhere in a cold room a woman's whole testimony is sitting in a box with a date on it, waiting for me to learn what she was called before the building forgets it ever called her anything at all.


# Chapter Sixteen — The Candidate

There is a campaign sign in the well of Courtroom 4B now, which is not legal, and everyone has agreed not to see it.

It is not a *sign* sign. It is a coffee cup. A reusable one, navy, the kind that costs more than coffee, set on the corner of the prosecution table where Hurd can reach it, and around its barrel runs a band of white type I read upside down on my way to the box at 9:01 — PRYOR, and under it the slogan, families, safety, the words worn smooth by my having walked past them by the cafeteria for forty mornings without consenting to read them. A campaign aide left it. Or Hurd carries it himself, a junior man advertising his loyalty in ceramic, which is the more likely and the smaller thing. Either way the candidate's name sits in the prosecution's corner of the room while the candidate sits on the bench above it in a robe, and the bailiff doesn't see it, and the clerk doesn't see it, and I see it the way I see everything in this room, which is to say I file it and I empty myself out and I raise my right hand.

*I will faithfully and accurately interpret these proceedings to the best of my ability.*

I mean it. I want that on the record, still, this far in — that on the morning the floor went out from under me I swore the oath and meant it, the way you mean a thing you have not yet learned to suspect. Pryor administers it himself today; the regular clerk is out and he likes the small ceremonies, likes the baritone of *do you solemnly swear,* and he gets the stress on my name right the way he always does, the silence my father insisted on kept intact, *Okafor-Vance,* and I give him the nod, and I do not look at the navy cup, and I take my position at the angle that keeps the witness's sightline to my hands unbroken, and the morning begins, and for eleven minutes I am still a person who does not know.

* * *

The witness is the medical examiner's investigator, hearing, a flat competent man named Polk who photographs the dead for the county and narrates his own photographs in the affectless cadence of someone who has done it four thousand times, and there is no signing in it, no Deaf party this hour, just English into English, the easiest work there is, and I should not even be up. But Lamar is back this afternoon for the State's rebuttal and Pryor's clerk, gone, left the schedule a mess, and so I'm parked in the box rendering nothing, sworn to faithfully interpret a proceeding that is happening entirely in the language the record is built to keep — which is its own small joke, the faithful render with nothing to render, the glass with no weather behind it — and I let my eyes do the slow professional drift they do when my hands are idle, the inventory drift, the reading of the room.

I read Pryor.

Not on purpose. I have been reading him for years the way you read weather, as a condition of the building, beloved, silver, the baritone, the inclusive glance. But today I am idle and the cup is in my eye-line and the cup makes him a *candidate* instead of a judge, makes the whole front of the room a thing with a future in it — *I put people away,* the pitch, the banners under the aide's arm three weeks ago, the office at the top of the ladder — and I do the thing I do with witnesses, the thing I cannot switch off, I start to build him. I set him in the space of my own attention and I look for the verb. Where have I seen these hands. Not signing hands; he doesn't sign; the hands that turn a page, that lift the navy cup, that once, the snag from the marble hallway, the snag I let go of in the shower and never got back — *I worked across a room from that man once. His table and mine. I can't place the case.*

And Polk says, into his microphone, in the flat investigator's drone, reading a date off an exhibit:

"—on or about the eleventh of March of two thousand nine—"

He means a different two thousand nine. He means nothing. It is a stray date in an unrelated foundation, a coincidence of the calendar, the kind of thing that means nothing forty times a day in a courthouse where every year is somebody's worst year. Polk says *two thousand nine* about a piece of paper that has nothing to do with me, and the number goes into the room and crosses the navy cup and lands on the silver man on the bench, and the snag I have not been able to place for three weeks places itself, all at once, the way a name arrives in the shower, except I am not in the shower, I am sworn, I am in the box, and there is nowhere to put my face.

It is him.

Not *a* prosecutor. *The* prosecutor. The table across the well in 2009 was his table. The voice that called Tobias Rein evasive to twelve hearing people who had never met a Deaf person was that voice, fifteen years younger and already that good, already the baritone, and I stood off to the side of that room at the interpreter's angle with my fast sure beautiful hands and I gave that voice the clean English it wanted and never once — Irene's three fingers, held up in her wet-sidewalk living room — never once turned around to see who else in the room could read what I was rounding, and the man I gave it to is sitting eight feet above me in a robe with his name on a coffee cup, running for the office that lets you do it to more of them, on the strength of having done it to Tobias.

I do not move. I am very good at not moving. It is half of what I am paid for. But something in me drops the way a load drops when a weld lets go — not a sound, my father always said, the bad ones don't make a sound, they make a *change in the geometry,* a span that was holding weight a second ago and now is not — and I sit in the box with my hands folded over a blank legal pad and feel the geometry of the whole building change while Polk drones about an exhibit and the navy cup catches the bad 4B light.

* * *

Here is what saves me, if *saves* is the word, and it isn't.

It is the work.

Because Pryor breaks for the morning recess at 10:40 and Lamar Stokes comes back at 1:15 for the State's rebuttal, and at 1:15 I have to stand up in that room, in front of that man, and render a Deaf witness *live,* three seconds behind, the cognitive emergency I have spent twenty-six years winning — and I have to do it with the floor gone, with the recognition sitting in me like a swallowed coal, and I cannot afford it, I cannot afford a single degree of it, because Damon Webb is thirty-three and going to prison or not going to prison through my hands this afternoon and Lamar Stokes is owed a faithful interpreter and not a woman having the worst recognition of her life in his sightline.

So I do the thing I know how to do. I empty myself out. I have emptied myself out over a dead mother and a dying aunt and a hundred strangers' worst days; I can empty myself out over this. I go down to the cafeteria and I do not look at the banner and I drink coffee that is not mine and I run the renders in my head the way I always do, and at 1:15 I stand up and raise my right hand and swear the day's second oath in front of Gerald Pryor and mean it, *faithfully and accurately,* and I set my feet, and Lamar finds my eyes and gives me the flat nod, *I see you, don't move,* and I become furniture with a face, and we go.

And it is the hardest live render of my career, and not for any reason the room can see.

Hurd takes Lamar back through the distance, the forty feet, the streetlight, rehabilitating him after Cantwell's two failed runs at the deaf man's eyes, and Lamar is a craftsman, Lamar cites the street clean, and I voice him, and the three seconds open up under me the way they always do — the surfer's gap, riding the back of the wave so I can see its whole shape before I speak it — except today the gap is not a country I own. Today the gap is the place where the coal sits. He sets the avenue in the air, the storefront, the lock, the gate dragged shut on its track, and I wait in the doorway of the sentence holding my breath for the floor plan to finish, and in the three seconds before I open my mouth I am not only assembling English, I am also, in the same three seconds, in the same working memory, holding the fact of the man on the bench, *he prosecuted Tobias, the cup says PRYOR, his career is the conviction the appeal will break, he is sitting in judgment of this room while a different room he poisoned waits to be reopened* — and the two things share the lag, the render and the recognition, stacked in the same three seconds, and the next sentence is already arriving.

I want to be exact about what this is, because the gallery thinks interpreting is a translation and it is not, it is a *load,* and the load has a limit, and I have spent my life living just under the limit and being proud of the margin, and this afternoon the margin is gone. Lamar lays a sentence on me. I hold it in the air. The recognition pushes up through the same channel — *eight feet above me, the navy cup, I gave him the clean one* — and for one half-second the held sentence and the swallowed fact occupy the same square inch of me, the way two referents cannot occupy the same locus in the signing space without one of them collapsing, and I feel the thing I felt in Irene's living room, the man at the bottom of the pool watching the surface get further away, except now I am the surface, I am the one everyone is depending on to be the surface, and the next sentence is here.

"—I was under the streetlight," I render, three seconds back, level, mine, "nothing between us. I saw it the way you'd see it across this courtroom."

It is right. I check it the way you check a weld you can't see the back of, by the feel of the load coming through it, and it holds, and I do not let my eyes go to the bench, and Lamar does not see the coal because there is nothing in my hands to show it, and Hurd is pleased and doesn't know why, and Pryor on the bench nods his beloved nod at the testimony, and I render the most important afternoon of Damon Webb's life across the well of the courtroom belonging to the man who built his campaign on the afternoon he took from Tobias Rein, and no one in the room knows a fight occurred, and the fight is not with Cantwell this time, the fight is the simple animal labor of keeping a sentence in the air while the floor is gone, and I win it, and winning it costs me something I will not get back, because I learn this afternoon that I *can* render through it, that the surface holds even when the swimmer underneath it is drowning, which is a thing I would have been happier never to know about myself.

* * *

Pryor breaks at 4:05 — *we'll resume with rebuttal in the morning, the jury is excused, counsel remain* — and there is a small housekeeping thing, an exhibit dispute, ten minutes of lawyers, and I am released from the box and I gather my water and my blank pad and I do not run. I want it on the record that I did not run. I walk out into the marble at the interpreter's unhurried pace, the pace that says nothing is happening, and the second the heavy door closes behind me the thing I held in the air all afternoon comes down, and I have to stop with one hand flat on the cold institutional wall the way Irene made me stand on her porch with my hands empty, and breathe, and be a person for a minute in the hallway before I can be a professional again.

Lockwood is there.

Of course Lockwood is there. He has a way of materializing in the marble outside 4B the way he materialized the first morning with the banker's box, a tired sharp man in a coat he hasn't replaced since the recession, and he reads my face — he is good at faces, it is half of *his* job too — and whatever he reads makes him stop the thing he came to say and ask a different one.

"You know," he says. Not a question.

"Know what." I am still building the wall. Some bricklayer in me started laying it the second I saw him, the reflex, the case for why none of this is what it is, and I hear myself reach for the flat professional nothing. "Desmond. I'm coming out of a witness. Give me a—"

"You placed him." He says it gently, which is worse. "Pryor. You finally placed him."

And I look at Desmond Lockwood in the marble hall with the lake going gray somewhere out past the parking structures, and the wall I was laying does not get its next course, because he is not surprised. He is not telling me a thing. He is confirming that I have caught up to a thing *he already knew,* and the speed at which I understand that — that Lockwood has known since before he ever stood outside this courtroom with a yellowed tape — is the speed at which the afternoon gets worse.

"You knew," I say.

"Ruth—"

"When you handed me the tape." My voice is not level now and I do not have the margin to make it level. "In the hall. Three weeks ago. You knew the prosecutor on Rein was the sitting judge in the courtroom you found me *in.* You knew I was working his room when you walked up to me holding his case in a box."

"Yes."

The honesty of it stops me, which I think is what it's for. Lockwood does not lie to me; he is too tired and too good for that; he just declines to say the thing until the thing is useful, which is its own kind of authorship, and I am, even now, even here, the woman who notices that, the woman who catalogs everyone's small withholdings while she drowns over her own.

"You should have told me."

"Would you have looked at the tape," he says, "if I'd told you the first thing you'd find at the bottom of it was a man with a campaign?"

I don't answer, because the answer is no, and we both know it's no, and he knows it the way he knows everything about how to move me, which is the same way I know how to move a jury, which is to say we are two instruments in a hallway each handling the other and each calling it candor.

* * *

He walks me to the window, the one I always find, the cold one over the parking structures, and he tells me the rest of it standing shoulder to shoulder so neither of us has to do it to the other's face, the way you tell a diagnosis.

"Here's where it actually is," Lockwood says. "Forget the moral version for a second. The legal version." He has a way of laying it out flat, the load paths, my father would have liked him. "I didn't hire the best interpreter in the county to confirm a render. I told you that's why, in my office, and I let you believe it, because you needed to believe it to come look. But that was never the play." He pauses. "The play is *you.* The original interpreter, the interpreter of record, the one the State will say was certified and faithful and unimpeachable — getting up under oath and saying the render the conviction rests on was authored, not transmitted. There is no expert in the country who can break that interpretation the way the woman who made it can. You're not on the team, Ruth. You're the exhibit. You always were."

I knew this. I have known it since the elevator in his building, since Irene said *make it right, for who.* But hearing him say it flat, in the marble, with the coal still hot in me from the box — *you're the exhibit* — does a thing the knowing didn't, which is that it locates me exactly: in the courtroom of the man whose conviction I am the exhibit against, rendering his rebuttal, sworn to him this morning, the weapon Lockwood loaded fifteen years late.

"And the obstruction," I say, because I am an investigator now whether I want to be or not, because the work is the only floor I have left and I go stand on it. "You said *forget the moral version.* That means there's a procedural one and it's gone bad."

He almost smiles. "There she is."

"What's he doing."

"He's not doing anything." Lockwood says it carefully, because the careful version is the true one and the true one is worse than a cartoon. "That's the whole — there's no smoking gun, Ruth, there's no order with his fingerprints on it. What there is, is that the interpreter-services records from 2009 — the scheduling memos, the invoices, the thing you and Bee have been digging for, the proof that a CDI was on that trial and got pulled — those live in a county records office that is, right now, *consolidating.* Purging. Forty-year-old files going to a contractor to be scanned-and-shredded or just shredded, on a schedule, for space. Routine. Nobody's hand on it." He lets that sit. "And the motion to unseal the 2009 interpreter-services file — the one I filed to get those records into the appeal before they're gone — is being opposed. Not by Pryor. Pryor can't touch the Rein appeal, he's recused, he's the old prosecutor, he'd never be that stupid. It's being opposed by the County, by Donovan's office, on grounds that are *completely reasonable* — finality, the age of the records, the burden, the fishing-expedition argument, *the defense wants to reopen a fifteen-year-old murder on a paperwork theory.*" He turns from the window finally, and looks at me, and his tired face has the thing in it I have been refusing all afternoon. "And the County Prosecutor's office is the office Pryor is running to *lead.* The lawyers fighting my unsealing motion are the people who will work for him in November if he wins. He doesn't have to make a single call. He doesn't have to do one improper thing. The system that economized on Tobias's language fifteen years ago is the same system that's about to shred the proof of it, on a routine schedule, defended by reasonable lawyers, in service of a conviction that is a *campaign asset* — and not one person in that chain is doing anything you could put in front of a bar committee."

There is a silence then, the kind that is not the absence of sound. I have spent my life in the three-second country, the gap between a sign completing and the next one beginning, and I stand in a longer one now, at a cold window, while the thing Lockwood has laid out arranges itself into its true and unbearable shape: that the worst thing in this story has no author. That I drove three weeks toward a villain and found a *records-retention schedule.* That Pryor will keep the conviction not by burying it but by *being beloved while it buries itself,* his clean baritone hands nowhere near the shredder, *I put people away,* the record a thing he can stand on precisely because the record can no longer be checked.

The record. It comes to me with the cold of the window in it. For fifteen years the 2009 transcript has been the truth in this building — the voiced render, my voice, the clean English the jury convicted on, *let the record reflect* — and it was the truth because there was no way to check it against the hands. The tape gave us a way. And now the *other* record, the bureaucratic one, the seam, the scheduling memo and the two-line-item invoice that prove the court pulled the one person who could have read those hands — that record is being fed to a contractor in a van, on a Tuesday, for *space,* while the man it would convict runs for the office that decides which of us gets sent into a room to be made sense of toward a cage. The record was authority once. It became concealment. And now, on a schedule nobody chose, it is becoming nothing at all, which is the safest thing a record that hides a crime can become.

* * *

"How long," I say. The investigator. The floor.

"The unsealing motion's heard the same week as your hearing. Adeyemi can order the file held — *if* it still exists when he orders it. The records contractor's first pickup is—" Lockwood checks his phone, and the smallness of the gesture is the whole horror of it, a man checking a delivery date for the proof of a man's destruction — "they couldn't tell me. 'Rolling.' Could be three weeks. Could be Monday. They scan by box and the boxes go in order and nobody at the county can tell me which box 2009-interpreter-services is in or when it ships, because to them it is a box, Ruth, it is a cubic foot of paper in a queue."

A cubic foot of paper. The width of one finger, I think, and then I make myself not think it, because it is exactly the kind of thing I would say to sound wise, the two-clause devastation, the move I have made my whole life to put a clean rim on a thing so I don't have to keep holding it — and it is not mine to say. The width of one finger was Tobias's torso, a few degrees of shoulder, a real thing on a real tape. This is a different measurement and I do not get to make it pretty. This is a cubic foot of paper in a van, and a man dying in Grafton in a language no one there speaks, and a navy coffee cup eight feet above the witness box, and a woman who can render through anything standing at a cold window having just discovered there is no bottom to the thing she agreed to do because she thought it would prove her hands were clean.

"Then I have to find Delia before the box ships," I say. "Whatever she has — her own copy, her log, her memory — if the county's file goes to the contractor, she's the record. She's the only record that can't be put in a van."

"Yes."

"And I have to do it while rendering his rebuttal every morning." I hear the thing in my own voice and I do not bother to hide it from Lockwood, who has already seen the floor go out of me once today. "I have to stand up in that man's courtroom and be faithful to his trial while I race his system to save the proof that he broke a different one."

Lockwood looks at me for a moment. Then he says the thing I will carry down the marble and out into the gray, the thing that is not comfort, because Lockwood does not deal in comfort, he is too tired and the cases are too many.

"You wanted to be the glass," he says — and he does not know he is repeating Tobias, does not know a dying man made the same shape at me across a bolted table a week ago, the pane and the hand behind it, and that is the worst of it, that two people who have never met have both looked at me and reached for the same word. "You wanted to be the thing the truth passes through. Well." He shifts the strap of his bag, tired, already turning toward the elevators and the next impossible case. "Now you're the thing it's racing through. And it's losing."

* * *

I do not take the rapid home tonight. I sit in the car in the Justice Center structure for a long time with the engine off, in the gray concrete light, not being a professional, and I let myself do the arithmetic I have been refusing since 10:40 this morning.

Fifteen years ago I stood at the interpreter's angle in a courtroom and gave a young prosecutor named Gerald Pryor a clean, fluent, inculpatory English sentence built out of a deprived man's broken hands, and the prosecutor called those hands evasive, and the jury convicted the clean sentence, and the prosecutor went on the bench on the strength of it, and is now running for the office at the top of the ladder on the strength of it, *I put people away,* and the proof that the court took away the one Deaf interpreter who could have read what I rounded is in a cubic foot of paper in a queue for a shredder, defended by reasonable lawyers who will work for him in November, and I am the exhibit, the weapon, the original faithful hands, sworn to him again this morning, and the only thing in this whole machine that did anything *improper,* the only single hand you could name and point at and bring before a committee —

is mine.

I sit with that. I want to be honest that I sit with it the way you sit with a verdict you have wanted, because there is a relief in it that I recognize from my kitchen table at midnight, the relief of *me, finally, in the sentence* — that if it is all mine then it is *findable,* it has an author, it can be confessed and paid for and resolved, and a thing that can be resolved is a thing I know how to be good at. The bricklayer in me wants the whole load to come down on my one hand because one hand is a guilt and a guilt is *clean* and a system is a *life,* unending, unatonable, with no rim you can put on it to make it a sentence you can live with.

And then I make myself look at the thing I keep turning away from, the thing Irene put in the air with three fingers and Lockwood put in the air at the window — *you never knew what was in the room.* Because if the worst hand is mine, then Pryor's clean baritone is innocent, and the dropped CDI is an accident, and the shredder is just space, and I get to be the sinner and everyone else gets to be the weather. And I have built my whole life on being the one who reads the gap, and I am sitting in a parking structure trying to round the largest gap I have ever seen into a sentence small enough to be only my fault, because my fault is a thing I can hold and the truth is not.

I do not finish the arithmetic. I am not ready to finish it. I start the car.
