But I know one thing now that I did not know this morning, and it is not a comfort and it is not clean and it does not fit on the chin of my face where the correction lives: the conviction I am trying to break is the floor the man on the bench is standing on to climb, and to take it out from under him I will have to get up in his courtroom and his system and say, under the same oath I have sworn four thousand times and meant, that the most faithful render of my life convicted a man the court could not hear — and that the proof of it is in a van, and the clock is *rolling,* and somewhere southwest of the city in a county that grows soybeans and razor wire a man's left hand will not stop shaking, and nobody has looked at it, and the box ships when it ships.

I pull out of the structure into the gray. The lake is somewhere off to my left, doing the flat colorless thing it does before summer remembers it. I drive toward a woman I have not yet found, with a clock I cannot see the face of, through the city of the man whose name is on a coffee cup, and I do not turn the radio on, because for once I am not pretending the silence is a thing that happens to other people.


# Chapter Seventeen — Delia Hart

There is no roster of the people a courthouse used and discarded. There is a roster of the people it certified, which is a different thing, and that is where I start, because it is the only door I have a key to.

I find her the way you find anyone in a small world that does not want to be found: by spending the credit you have left. The state interpreter registry lists active credentials only — Delia Hart is not in it, which tells me she let hers lapse, which tells me something already, before I have her phone number — so I call the certifying body and trade on twenty-six years of being a name they recognize, and a woman in Columbus who remembers me from a workshop I gave in 2014 confirms, off the record, that a Delia Hart held a CDI credential out of the Cleveland area and let it expire in 2010, the year after, and that the last good address they have is a P.O. box, and I think, *of course it's a box, everything in this case is in a box,* and I do not say it out loud because there is no one in the car to say it to.

Bee gets me the rest in an afternoon, because Bee is twenty-seven and the Deaf world runs on a network I am not on. She does not give it to me as a favor. She gives it to me with a condition, signed flat across the dead archive's video link, the lake going white behind her.

*I'll find her. But I'm coming with you, and I'm going in first, and if she says no, it's no — not "let me explain," not "five minutes," no. You don't get to do to her what the court did. You don't get to keep her in a room until she gives you the thing you need.*

I start to say I would never, and stop, because the bricklayer in me — and I am tired of him, I have been tired of him for three weeks and twenty-six years — had already begun, somewhere below the sentence, drafting the version where I am gentle and reasonable and Delia, moved, agrees. I had built the scene before I'd found the woman. I let Bee watch me not finish it.

*Okay,* I sign. *You first.*

*And Ruth.* She holds the next thing a beat, the way she does when she is about to be kind in a way that costs me. *She left because of this. Whatever you think you're walking into — you're walking into the worst day of somebody's professional life, fifteen years late, to ask her to do it again. Bring less of yourself than you think you need.*

* * *

Delia Hart lives in Lakewood, in the upstairs half of a double on a street of doubles, over a landlady who keeps the porch light on a timer and the hostas cut back hard. There is a real doorbell, which I note the way I note everything now — it works, it is lit, it is wired to a transmitter inside that throws the lamps, because Delia, unlike Irene, did not weaponize her threshold; she just made it livable. Bee presses it and we watch the front-room curtain do the small thing curtains do, and then the inner door opens before the storm door does, and a woman stands behind the glass and looks at the two of us and does not open the second door yet.

She is fifty-eight and built like someone who used to carry things. Reading glasses pushed up into iron-gray hair. A cardigan the color of wet slate, sleeves shoved to the elbow, forearms a signer's forearms, and a face that reads us off the porch in under a second — reads Bee, the easy native legibility of her, *one of ours,* and then reads me, and I watch the read land, because Delia Hart does not bother to hide it the way the courthouse trains you to hide it. Her eyes go to my hands. Everyone's eyes go to my hands; it is the most-looked-at thing about me. But Delia looks at them the way I look at a tape.

She opens the storm door. She does not invite us in. She stands in the gap of it, holding it on its pneumatic arm, and she signs — to Bee first, deliberately, the address of it angled so that I am, for the opening sentence of this encounter, a person being talked near.

*She's the one.* Not a question. A placement. Delia sets me in the air to her left, fixes a locus there — *that one, the interpreter* — and leaves me in it.

Bee, to her credit, does not soften it. *She's the one.*

And Delia looks at me then, full on, and what is in her face is not the thing I drove to Lakewood braced for. I had braced for Irene's flint, for the door closing. This is worse, because it is recognition. She looks at me the way one tradesman looks at another across a job that went wrong — not hatred, not even blame, exactly, but the flat terrible collegiality of *I know precisely what you did, because I would have been the only other person in that building who could have done it, and I am the one who watched you do it alone.*

*Fifteen years,* she signs, and the sign for the span of years runs back over her shoulder into the past where ASL keeps the past, *and they send you.*

*Nobody sent me.* I sign it before I can manage the register, and it comes out wrong, comes out defending, and I see her clock the defense the way Irene clocked it, the way they all clock it, because the Deaf in this book read the gap in me the way I once believed only I could read it in them.

*Somebody always sends you,* Delia signs. *That's what you are. The one they send.* And she steps back from the door — not a welcome, a permission, a ruling — and lets us into a front room with two lamps already on against the gray and a card table by the window with a jigsaw half-built on it, edges done, the middle still a thousand loose pieces, and she does not offer us coffee, and she sits, and she makes us stand a moment in her space before she lets us sit, the small sovereignty of a person deciding when.

* * *

I am going to give you what she gave me, and I am going to try not to put a clean rim on it, because the whole sin of my life is the rim.

She does not start with 2009. She starts with the kitchen, which is to say she gets up, mid-silence, and goes to the kitchen and comes back with a single glass of water for herself and none for us, and that is a sentence too, and then she sits and tells me what she does now, because she has decided I will hear the cost before I get the testimony. She fits captions, freelance — quality-checks the auto-captions on training videos and depositions for a vendor, sitting in this room flagging every place the machine turned a Deaf person's deposition into a sentence they did not say. She fixes the gap for nine dollars a video, alone, in a double in Lakewood, for the rest of her life. She tells me this without self-pity and without mercy, watching my face, because she wants me to do the arithmetic she has done: that she was the most qualified person in a felony courtroom and now she is a quality-control technician for a robot's mistakes, and the distance between those two things is the exact distance the system moved her.

*You know what a CDI is,* she signs. *Say it. Out loud, the way you'd say it to a jury. I want to see your mouth make it.*

And I do, because she is the one setting terms now and I have spent my life respecting the person who holds the floor. "A Certified Deaf Interpreter," I say, to the room, to the jigsaw, to the lamps. "A Deaf interpreter who works in tandem with a hearing interpreter when the Deaf person's language is — " and I stop, because the next word is *non-standard,* is *deprived,* is one of the clean flattening words, and she is watching me reach for it, and I reach instead for the true one and it is worse. "When the man's language has holes in it that a hearing stranger's hands can't bridge. When his ASL was built without full access and only another Deaf person, only someone who came up the same broken way, has the hands to find what he means in the places where the language was never put in him."

Delia watches me say it. Something in her jaw moves.

*Good,* she signs. *Now you know what you took the place of.*

* * *

This is the part the record does not have, and I am the record now, so here it is.

She was there from the first morning. She signs it plainly — not a performance, a deposition she has given herself ten thousand times in this room — both of them checked in at the Justice Center entrance the same quarter hour, day one, she and the celebrated young hearing interpreter with the fast sure hands, and they did the thing the tandem does, the thing that is the whole quiet art of it: she sat where Tobias could see her, and I sat where I could see her, and Tobias signed into his holes and his footbridges and his improvised private grammar, and she — Deaf, raised the long way, fluent in the dialect of damage — caught what he meant and built it into ASL my hands could carry, and I carried it into English. Two relays. Two interpreters. A Deaf bridge and a hearing one, because the county had been told, correctly, by someone who knew the law, that one bridge would not reach.

*And then,* Delia signs, *day two, after lunch.*

She does not dramatize it. That is the thing that undoes me. She gives it to me at the speed of fact. Day two, after lunch, somebody — she never saw the order, there was never an order to see — and she role-shifts, just barely, becomes for half a second a tired clerk leaning into a courtroom doorway, the *we've-got-this-handled* tilt of a head, and then she is herself again — somebody decided the county had a certified interpreter already, and what was the second one for, and the per-diem was bleeding, and there was a custody matter down the hall that needed a body. And she was re-coded. Not fired. Not ruled on. *Re-coded.* Moved on a screen from one chair to another by a person solving a budget, two floors below the floor where Tobias was on trial for his life.

*I told them,* she signs.

And here her hands change. They have been level, controlled, a professional giving an account. Now they tighten, and the signing space gets small and hot and close to her body, and I am watching a woman go back into the worst room of her life and stand in it.

*Before they walked me out. I told the lawyer — yours, the young one, the prosecutor — I told the bench. I said: the render is wrong. I said it in the hallway and I said it through the only person who could voice me, which was you, Ruth, which means I had to ask the woman whose render I was calling wrong to tell the court I was calling it wrong.* Her face does something I will not be able to put down faithfully no matter how many times I try, and I will try. *And do you know what happens when the Deaf interpreter says the hearing interpreter got it wrong?*

I do not answer. There is no answer that is not a confession.

*Nothing happens,* Delia signs. *Because who am I. I'm the one they already sent home. I'm the one whose credential the room can't see because the room only credentials the mouth. You correct errors discreetly, that's in the code, we both swore it — you correct them up the chain. But there is no chain above the certified interpreter. She is the top of the language. She is the court's whole ear.* She lets her hands fall, briefly, into her lap, the Deaf equivalent of a long breath, and then lifts them. *So I told the room the truth, and the room heard a Deaf woman being difficult about a hearing professional, and the hearing professional voiced my objection in her own clean steady voice because she is faithful, she renders everything, even the thing accusing her — and then I was gone, and you stayed, and the trial went nine more days through your hands, and a jury convicted the cleanest sentence in the building.*

I sit in Delia Hart's front room and I understand that in 2009 my own faithful mouth carried the words of the one person who could have saved him, and carried them accurately, and that my accuracy is what made the objection sound like noise. I rendered her perfectly. That is how she disappeared.

* * *

I want to tell you I sat with that cleanly. I did not.

The bricklayer — no. Not the bricklayer. I am done lending him my best metaphor; my father gave me load paths and I have used them to wall myself in for thirty years, and Delia Hart is sitting across a jigsaw watching me do it, and I would rather she didn't. So let me just say it the plain way, the way she'd respect: the reflex came. The reflex that the lighting was bad and the order was the county's and *I only voiced what she signed,* the same alibi one rung sideways — *I rendered her objection faithfully, I added nothing, I am not the one who didn't believe her* — and it got about four words into building itself before I caught it, in front of her, the way I am learning to catch it, too slow to be proud of and just fast enough to be ashamed.

Because *I only voiced what she signed* is the thesis with my name on it. It is *I only said what they said* aimed at the one person who tried to stop me. I have spent this whole case discovering that neutrality launders guilt, and here is the cleanest proof I will ever find, and it is that I faithfully transmitted the woman who was trying to tell the court my faithfulness was a lie, and my transmission is what buried her.

She reads all of this off my face. She is, after all, the best reader of faces I have ever sat across from, better than me, better than Bee, a woman who spent her life finding meaning in the places language failed. She watches me not finish the wall and something in her — not forgiveness, I want to be exact, it is not forgiveness — something in her settles a half-degree, the way a witness settles when she's decided you're at least not going to lie to her.

*You didn't know I told them,* she signs.

"No."

*You thought it was just you.*

"I — " and the honest answer is the one that indicts me worst. "I wanted it to be just me."

And Delia Hart, who has every reason in the world to leave me in that, surprises me. She does not soften it. But she gives me the next true thing instead, which is harder.

*I wanted it to be just them,* she signs. *The whole fifteen years. I told myself I objected, I did my part, I'm clean, they're the machine. I sat in this room fixing a robot's captions telling myself I was the conscience they wouldn't hear.* Her hands slow. *But I knew his holes, Ruth. I had his hands. I was the one person in that building who could have stood up and not been moved — could have refused the re-code, sat down in the well, made them carry me out on camera. And I packed my bag because the per-diem was real and I had a mortgage and the law moves you and you go. I had an alibi too. Mine just had better grounds than yours.*

We sit with that, the two of us, two instruments who each spent fifteen years deciding the harm belonged to someone else, looking at each other across a half-built puzzle, and I understand that I did not come to Lakewood to find a witness. I came to find a mirror, and she is one, and she knew it before I knocked.

* * *

Bee, who has been letting this happen, who has the grace to know when she is not the center of a room, finally signs the question I cannot, because it is the lawyer's question and someone has to be the lawyer.

*Do you have anything? On paper.*

Delia looks at her a long moment. Then she gets up, again at her own speed, and goes — but not yet to the table. She goes first to the kitchen, the way she went for the water, and through the doorway I see the thing I will file the way I file everything: against the kitchen wall, a chest freezer, a dead one, the compressor gutted, the lid up for a moment while she reaches past it, and inside, where there should be meat, there are videotapes standing on end in the dry cold — Hi8 and MiniDV and a few of the gray transcription cassettes, the formats that rot in a Cleveland basement, kept here in the silica-dry where the damp can't reach them. A woman who turned the carcass of an appliance into a vault because she would not trust one institution in this county to keep what she knows. She does not show it to me on purpose. She lets me see, through a doorway, that she has needed no archive but her own, and lets the lid down, and comes back to the front room and to the card table, and lifts the edge of the jigsaw box, and underneath it, flat, is a manila folder gone soft at the corners the way paper goes when it has been moved between hiding places for fifteen years and opened on bad nights — the one record she keeps closer than the freezer, closer than the bed — and she does not hand it to us. She holds it.

*My day-book,* she signs. *Two thousand nine. The carbon of my own invoice — my copy, not the county's, the one they can't shred because it isn't theirs. My note from day two: re-coded 2:10, told Pryor and the bench the render at the table was unreliable, no action taken, walked out 2:40. Dated. In my hand. The day I said it and the time I said it.* She sets the folder back down on the box, on top, in the open, a thing now visible in the room, and rests two fingers on it. *This is the record that didn't go in a van. You found that out from somebody. The lawyer.*

"Lockwood. Yes."

*He's been calling the box.* The P.O. box. *Six weeks. I didn't answer because I knew what it was. A lawyer only finds me when the machine wants something off the shelf again.* Her hands go flat, final, a ruling delivered. *I'll tell you what I told the box, except now I'll say it to your faces so there's no rendering it. I have the proof. It dies with me or it goes in the record, and I haven't decided which, and you do not get to decide it for me by being sad in my front room. I sat in that courtroom fifteen years ago and told the truth and watched it become nothing because a hearing person voiced it. If I do this, I am not handing it to you, Ruth, to fix your hands with. I am handing it to him. To Tobias. And if it goes in, I go in with it — I sit in that box and I sign and somebody, not you, voices me, because I will never again let my truth go to a jury through the mouth that buried it the first time.*

It lands exactly where she aims it, which is the center of the one thing I came for. I came to Lakewood needing Delia Hart to be my proof. And she has just told me that if she becomes the proof, I will not be allowed to be the instrument — that the price of finally getting the truth into the record is that my hands, the famous faithful hands, will be barred from it, by the one person who has the standing to bar them.

It is the most just sentence anyone has handed me in this whole case, and it cuts the floor out from under the version of redemption I did not know I was still carrying.

* * *

I think we are done. I am gathering the shape of myself to stand. And then Delia signs the thing that I do not hear, that I will not hear for weeks, that the reader is going to hear right now and carry around like a stone while I walk out of this house lighter than I have any right to be.

She is looking at the folder when she does it, not at me, almost to herself, the way you say the thing you've never told anyone because the room has finally gone honest enough to hold it.

*You want to know what I'd have rendered,* she signs. *That's why you came, under all the rest of it. You want me to tell you the true sentence so you can know how far off yours was.* She lifts her eyes. *I'll tell you the truth no lawyer wants. I had his hands. I knew his holes better than anyone alive who wasn't his blood. And I watched 14:22 — they let me see the tape once, before — and I could not tell you, then or now, what he signed.*

I take it as comfort. God help me, I take it as comfort — I hear *even Delia couldn't be sure, so the gap is real, so I am not uniquely a butcher* — and I let it be a kindness, and I file it under L2, under *the system, the dropped CDI, the not-only-me,* and I miss entirely the floor she has just opened under the whole case, because I am still standing on the question *how wrong was my render,* and she has just told me, in the plainest hands in Lakewood, that the question has no bottom. That the truest reader the man ever had looked at 14:22 and found *no render at all.* That what he signed could not be caught, by her, by me, by anyone, because the language to catch it was taken out of his hands by people who loved him before the night ever came.

I do not understand that yet. I am going to understand it in a room full of frozen frames with three Deaf people I have wronged, and it is going to be the worst thing I learn.

For now I just sign, *thank you,* and Delia does not sign *you're welcome,* because I am not, and that is honest too.

* * *

Bee and I stand on the porch under the working light. The hostas are cut back to nothing for the winter that is months off; Delia is the kind of person who does things early. Behind us the inner door has already closed; the storm door took longer, because she stood in the gap of it a moment and watched us go, the way you watch to be sure a thing leaves.

Bee signs, low, just to me, *That went better than you deserved.*

*I know.*

*She's going to do it.* Bee is certain in the way Bee is certain about the Deaf world, which is the way I am certain about nothing anymore. *Not for you. She decided in there. The folder was already on top of the box before we knocked — she had it out. She'd already decided to be talked into it and she just needed to find out whether you'd lie to her, and you didn't, mostly, so she will. But she meant the other thing.*

*What other thing.*

Bee looks at me, and there is something careful in her face, something that knows more than she's going to push, because Bee has learned that I have to walk into the worst rooms on my own feet or I don't believe I'm in them. *That somebody else voices her. On the stand.* A beat. *You know what that means for Lockwood's whole play.*

I do. It comes up through me cold, in the working porch light, with the street of doubles going on into the gray. Lockwood's entire strategy is *me* — the faithful hands, on the stand, breaking my own render. And Delia has just made the proof conditional on my agreeing to be, at the end of all this, exactly the thing I have always refused to be.

Silent. Off to the side. Not the voice in the room.


# Chapter Eighteen — The Good Parents

Delia gave me one thing before she set her terms, and the thing was a name, and the name was not Tobias's.

We were still in her kitchen — Delia keeps a kitchen the way Irene keeps a living room, the table angled so the light falls on the hands and not in the eyes, a Deaf-built room — and she had spent an hour confirming, on her own terms, in her own time, the thing I had driven across the county hoping she would confirm: that she had been there in 2009, that she had been the second figure on the tape, that the court had taken her off the trial the way you take a chair out of a room you have decided is too crowded. She told it without giving me the comfort of her telling it *to* me. She told it to the air over my left shoulder, the way you address a thing you are not sure deserves your face yet.

And then, near the end, when I asked her — carefully, because by then I had learned in that kitchen that I did not get to ask Delia Hart anything quickly — when I asked her what she had seen at 14:22 that I had missed, she did not answer the question I asked. She went still in the particular way I was learning meant she was deciding how much of a true thing a hearing woman could be trusted with, and then she signed something that was not about the tape at all.

*You want to know why his hands have holes in them,* she signed. *Go ask the people who made the holes. They're not hard to find. They thought they were saving him.* A pause, the brow doing the work the hands had stopped doing. *They still do.*

She would not say more than that. She set her terms — she would not hand me her retained copy of the scheduling document until she had decided I would not use it the way the last court used her, which is to say used it once and discarded it; she would think about whether she would testify at all; she would call Bee, not me, because Bee was Deaf-raised and Bee's word she would take and mine she would not yet — and she stood, which in Delia's kitchen meant the conversation was finished, and I gathered my coat and my unwritten pad and drove home with one more name lodged in me, except it was not a name, it was a fact: that somewhere in Cuyahoga County there were two people who had loved Tobias Rein into the shape that put him in a cage, and that they did not know they had done it, and that I was now going to have to go and look at them.

* * *

His mother is alive. His father is not.

It took Bee two days and a single phone call to a deacon at the Deaf Lutheran church to find her, because the church remembers everyone, and because a woman named Eleanor Rein had been, for forty years, the kind of hearing mother the church both pitied and resented in exactly the proportion they had once pitied and resented Ruth Okafor-Vance — the hearing one who came to the edge of the Deaf world and stood at the edge of it her whole life believing she was doing the loving thing.

She is eighty-one and she is in a memory-care wing in Parma, off Ridge Road, in a building that smells the way all of them smell, of warmed food and cleaning solution and the particular flat air of a place where the windows are sealed so no one can wander out. The good ones smell like this too. This is one of the good ones. I want to be fair to it; I have spent my life being unfair to rooms designed to manage the people I render, and I am trying, this month, to stop.

Bee comes with me, and I am not too proud anymore to be glad of it, which is its own small admission. Bee comes because Eleanor Rein will trust a young woman with a soft face over a fifty-one-year-old in a charcoal blazer who looks, Bee says, *like a deposition,* and because — though neither of us says this — I am not sure I can sit across from this woman alone and stay the thing I am supposed to be, which is an investigator, and not become the thing I keep wanting to be, which is a judge.

The aide walks us down. Eleanor is in a chair by the sealed window in a wing of sealed windows, small, very upright, her white hair set the way women her age set it on a Sunday whether or not it is Sunday, and her hands are folded in her lap, and her hands are the first thing I read, because her hands are the only language I fully trust and I cannot help it. They are hearing hands. They are still. They do not know they are language. They have spent eighty-one years not knowing they could have been the whole of how she reached her son, and they are folded now in a lap, at peace, in a wing where her son cannot reach her and she does not fully remember that he is in a place from which no one can.

"Eleanor," the aide says, loud, the loudness people use on the old and the Deaf, the loudness that is its own small audism, "you have visitors."

Eleanor Rein looks up, and her face does a thing I have seen a thousand times on the faces of the hearing parents of Deaf children, a thing I had not, until this month, understood I was implicated in — she looks at me, and she does not look at my hands. She looks at my mouth. She has spent her whole life looking at mouths, because mouths were where she decided the real talking happened, and she taught her son to look at mouths too, taught it to him with a love so total it took his hands away, and here she is at eighty-one still doing it, reading my mouth for the meaning, in a wing where no one will ever sign to her again.

* * *

I am going to tell you what she told us, and I am going to try to tell it the way she told it, which is the only honest way, because the horror of it — and there is a horror in it, a quiet one, the worst kind — is not in any cruelty. There is no cruelty in it anywhere. I went to Parma braced for cruelty the way I went to that fourth-floor rail thirty years ago braced for a cruel doctor, and Eleanor Rein, like the doctor, refuses to be cruel, refuses it so completely that the refusal is the thing that takes the floor out from under you.

She is having a good day. Bee asks, and the aide confirms it in the hall — *she's clear today, she comes and goes, today she's mostly here* — and so for an hour Eleanor Rein is *mostly here,* and she talks to us about her son, whom she believes, I think, is still a boy, or believes it in the parts of her that come and go, and she talks about him with a pride so unguarded it is like watching someone undress without knowing the curtain is open.

"He was so bright," she says. Her voice is thin and certain. "That was the thing nobody understood. The doctors acted like — like it was a tragedy, like we should grieve. And I told Walter, I said, our boy is not a tragedy, our boy is *bright,* and I will not have him raised like a tragedy." Her folded hands tighten, a small old fierceness. "We were going to give him the world. Not a — not a corner of it. The whole world. The hearing world. *His* world, because it was going to be his, I was not going to let anybody put him in a little room and lock the door and call it kindness."

Bee is very still beside me. Bee, whose parents are Deaf, whose whole self is built in the language Eleanor Rein decided was a little room. I do not look at Bee. I keep my eyes on Eleanor's mouth, the way Eleanor keeps her eyes on mine, two hearing women reading each other's mouths in a sealed wing, and I let her tell it.

"They wanted us to do the signing." She says *the signing* the way you say the name of a road you decided not to take, with a small distaste that is really fear worn smooth. "The school for the deaf, they wanted him to go and live there, *live* there, away from his mother, and learn — the gestures." She makes a small dismissive motion with one folded-loose hand, and it is, I realize with a cold drop, the closest thing to a sign she has made in the whole hour, a hearing woman's contemptuous little flutter, the exact gesture Cantwell made at Lamar Stokes in Courtroom 4B to tell a jury that a language was a mime. The same flutter. The defense attorney and the loving mother, the same hand. "And I read everything. I want you to know I *read* everything, I wasn't ignorant, I read the books, and the books — the good doctors, the modern ones — they said if he learned the gestures he'd never learn to *talk,* he'd be cut off, he'd only ever have the deaf, and I thought, no. No. My son is going to be able to talk to anyone. My son is going to walk into any room in the world and *belong.*"

She tells us about the speech therapy. Three afternoons a week, then more. She tells us about the long evenings at the kitchen table — I do not flinch at the kitchen table, I make myself not flinch — the long evenings teaching him to feel her throat with his small hand while she made the sounds, *feel it here, Toby, feel the buzz,* teaching him to read her lips in the mirror, the two of them side by side in the mirror making faces at themselves for hours so that he could learn to make the faces that hearing people make when they talk, so that no one would ever know, so that he could *pass.* She tells it as the central labor of her life, which it was. She tells it as love, which it was.

"And his hands," she says, and here her voice does something, here for one moment the coming-and-going clears entirely and she is all the way present and eighty-one and certain, "his hands wanted to — children's hands want to *talk,* did you know that, they'll just start, the deaf ones, if you let them they'll just start making the shapes — and I would take his hands." She unfolds her own, finally, and holds them up in front of her, the first time she has raised them, two small old hearing hands in the flat light of the sealed window. "I would take his little hands and I would hold them, gently, I was never rough with him, never, and I would put them back down in his lap, and I would say, *use your words, Toby. Use your words. Show Mama your words.*"

She lowers her hands back into her lap. She folds them. She is so proud. She is telling us the thing she is proudest of in her whole life.

"And he did," she says. "He learned to talk. He could talk to anyone." And then the good day flickers, the way the aide said it would, and something passes behind her eyes, some draft from the parts of her that come and go, and her certainty thins for just a moment into a thing underneath it that I think she has not let herself look at directly in forty years. "He stopped, though," she says, quieter, to the window now, not to my mouth. "When he got older. He didn't want to talk to me anymore. He'd go off with — with those people, the deaf ones, from the church, and he'd come back and he wouldn't — he had a way of looking at me." She trails. "Walter said it was just — boys. Just a boy growing up. But he had a way of looking at me, like I was the one who couldn't — " and the draft takes her the rest of the way, the present-day fog comes up over the good day like the lake coming up over the shore, and she stops, and her hands are folded, and she says, brightly, to no one, "He's so bright. Everybody always said. Our boy was never a tragedy."

* * *

I want to be precise about the gap in that room, because precision is the only thing I have ever owned and I am not going to abandon it now just because it has stopped consoling me.

The gap in Eleanor Rein's son is not a gap between a true sentence and a false one. That is my gap, the gap on the tape, the gap at the rail — a thing that was said one way and rendered another. Hers is worse, and it is worse in a way I did not have a name for until I sat in a sealed wing in Parma and watched a woman hold up two hands she had used, with total tenderness, to take a language out of a child. The gap in Tobias Rein is between *what she meant* and *what she made,* and those two things have no proportion to each other, no exchange rate, no width-of-one-finger you could point at and say, *there, that's where it went wrong.* She meant the whole world. She made a man who, thirty years later, sat across a bolted table from me in Grafton and signed, in his beautiful broken holed idiolect, *I have never once in my whole life said a thing and had it arrive,* and footbridged the holes where the signs should have been because his mother, who loved him without limit, had held his hands down at a kitchen table and told him to use his words.

He has no first language. That is the fact under all the other facts, and I have known it intellectually since Bee first told me what a language-deprived signer is, and I did not *know* it, in the body, until Parma. There is no language that is *his,* that he was given whole and early and without somebody's hand pressing his hands back into his lap. The hearing world's language he was drilled in but cannot fully hold, because you cannot fully hold a language you can only see a third of on a moving mouth. And the Deaf world's language he came to late, in his teens, from the church, hungry, in pieces, building it himself out of whatever the older Deaf would give him after his mother had spent his whole childhood teaching him it was a little room — and a language built late and in pieces and against your mother's love has holes in it, permanent ones, the holes Delia named, the holes the certified hearing interpreter — me — smoothed over in 2009 into clean grammatical English a jury could convict on.

A love that built a man with no native tongue. I sat in that wing and I felt the pull of it, the terrible clean pull, the same pull I felt in my own kitchen at midnight reaching for the sentence that made it all my fault — because here, at last, was an *answer.* Here was a thing you could stand up in a courtroom and say. *He could not produce the exonerating account because the account requires a language he was never given. His parents, out of love, took the tool he needed to defend his own life. The love convicted him.* It is true. Every word of it is true. And it has a shape I recognize, and the shape is the shape of a clean render — a fluent, fundamentally moving English sentence built over a broken source, the kind of sentence that wins a case and is, in the precise way the whole of my life has taught me to mistrust, *too smooth.*

* * *

She was in the third bed by the window, and on the fifth day she decided.

I have to tell you what she decided, even though I cannot yet tell you what it cost, because the cost is a thing I am still not able to carry all the way to you, and because the deciding and the cost are not the same event — the deciding I have looked at; the cost I have spent thirty years arranging the furniture of my mind so as not to have to.

The doctor had given her the number on the fourth day — *weeks, the intervention buys time but it's brutal and won't change the ending* — and I had given her the number softened, the brow-press withheld, *possible* where he said *probable,* the *won't change the ending* let go small in my fingers. And then there was a day. There is always a day, between the prognosis and the decision, the day the family sits with the number, except Meg had no family but me, no hands but mine, and so the day she sat with the number she sat with the number I had given her, which was not the number she had been given.

On the fifth day a different doctor came, younger, with the forms — because that is what comes after the deciding-day, the forms, the consent or the refusal, the brutal intervention to be initialed or declined — and Meg, propped at her thirty degrees so she could see her own door, watched my hands lay out the choice. *They can do the procedure,* I signed. *It is hard on the body. It might give you more time.* I built *time* into the space again as a thing you could reach for, the way I had built it the day before, because I had told myself one soft render and now I was inside it and had to keep building the world I had started, the way you cannot half-soften a thing, the way the second lie is only the maintenance of the first.

And Meg looked at the choice I had built her, the choice with the door left open in it, *might, more time, hard but possible* — and she lifted her ruined left hand off the blanket, the hand that four days before had made the cleanest contempt I ever saw and now made shapes like a mouth full of Novocaine, and she declined it.

She declined the intervention. She chose the weeks as they were. *No,* she signed — *no* is one of the signs the stroke could not take from her, *no* lives deep, *no* is the last sign to go — *no, I want to go home. Take me home. I don't want the thing in the hospital. I want my own bed and my own window and you.* And the younger doctor, watching the Deaf woman's ruined hand, turned to me — *what did she say* — and I rendered it, faithfully, the only faithful render in the whole episode, the only sentence I carried across without bending: *She declines the procedure. She wants to be discharged. She wants to go home.*

I gave them that one clean. I have thought about it since: that the one sentence I rendered with perfect fidelity, in the whole of 1991, was the sentence in which my aunt chose her death on the strength of a prognosis I had authored gentle. I was so careful with *that* render. I made sure they understood her exactly. I made sure her *no* arrived whole and undiminished and her own — a decision made freely, a competent adult declining treatment, her wishes honored to the letter — and I have spent thirty years telling that part as the part where I did right by her, where my faithful hands carried her true wish across, and never once let myself finish the sentence the other way: *I rendered her decision faithfully, and she made it on a world I had rendered to her false.*

The forms were signed. She was going home. And on the fifth day, watching her ruined hand make *no,* I felt the warm thing again — relief, that she would not have to suffer the brutal intervention, that I had spared her, that the gentle world I had built her had let her choose gently — and the relief was so close in temperature to the relief I would feel thirty years later deleting a declaration at a kitchen table that I cannot now, having felt them side by side, pretend they were ever different feelings.

I am not going to tell you what going home cost. Not yet. I do not have it in my hands yet. But I am going to tell you the shape of it, because the shape is the rhyme, and the rhyme is the reason I am sitting in a memory-care wing in Parma watching another woman who authored a person's reality out of love and called it saving them: a consequential decision, made by someone I cannot fully reach, on the strength of a softened render, by a hand that thought it was being kind.

* * *

We are in the car in the lot in Parma, Bee and I, and I am not driving yet, and I make the mistake — and I know, even as I make it, that it is a mistake, that it is exactly the move, that I am reaching for the clean sentence — I make the mistake of saying it out loud.

"That's it," I say. "That's the chapter that frees him. The parents. Lockwood can — a man whose own mother took his language. The deprivation has a *cause,* and the cause is — it's terrible, it's true, it's a jury's heart in a glass. The love convicted him."

And Bee, who has been silent since the wing, who has been carrying her own thing out of that room — her Deaf parents, her whole built self, the flutter Eleanor Rein made at *the signing* — Bee turns in the passenger seat and looks at me, and she is not gentle about it, and I am glad, later, that she is not gentle about it.

"No," Bee says. She signs it and says it both, *no,* the deep sign, the last one to go. "That's the version that makes you feel something in your chest, Ruth. That's the version that goes in a movie. Don't you dare hand it to a jury as the answer."

"It's *true.*"

"It's *true* and it's not the *crime.*" Her hands are fast now, native-fast, the speed Irene used on me, the speed that leaves me a half-beat behind in my own car. "His mother is a cause. His mother loved him stupid, into a little half-language, fine, that's *why* his hands have holes. But you know who took the one Deaf interpreter who could read the holes out of that courtroom?" She does not wait. "Not Eleanor Rein. Eleanor Rein wasn't in that courtroom. A scheduler in a basement re-coded Delia onto a cheaper fight at two-ten in the afternoon to save a per-diem. And you know who looked at a man whose mother had taken his language and told twelve hearing people he was being *evasive?* Not his mother. A prosecutor with a future. And you know who took his holed, deprived, footbridged hands and made them into a clean English murder a jury could sign their name under?" She stops. She makes herself say it level, which is worse than if she'd thrown it. "That wasn't his mother either."

I do not answer. The lake is not visible from the lot in Parma; there is only the sealed building and the ridge of the road and the flat early-summer sky, and I sit in it.

"His parents are the saddest part," Bee says, quieter now, and the quiet is the thing that lands it. "I'm not saying it isn't sad. I sat in there too. My parents could've been her — they almost were, my grandmother begged them to do the oral thing, they almost — " she stops, and I understand for the first time that Bee did not come to Parma only for Tobias, that some of what is on her face is the version of her own life where her grandmother won. "It's the saddest part. But the sad part isn't where the crime is. The sad part is just where the *crime had something to work with.* You want it to be the parents because the parents are over. The parents are eighty-one and dying in a chair and they can't hurt anybody anymore and you can put it all on them and feel terrible and feel *finished.*" She looks at me, hard. "The thing that broke him is still running. It re-coded a chair this week, in Webb. It's defending an unsealing motion right now. It's got its name on a coffee cup. You don't get to bury this in a memory-care wing in Parma because the wing is the part you can cry about."

* * *

She is right. I know she is right the way I knew Tobias was right when he said *you're the hand,* the way I knew Irene was right when she said I never turned around to see who else was in the room — which is to say, completely, and at a cost, and against the entire grain of a self I built precisely so I would never have to be this kind of right.

Because the clean version, the parents-did-it version, is *mine.* It is a render in exactly my own register: fluent, moving, true at the level of every word, and false at the level of the whole — a sentence with a door left open in it, a sentence you could make a jury weep into, a sentence that lets a softened, smoothed, fundamentally *kinder* account stand in for a truth too broken to be carried whole. I have spent this month learning that my whole life is that move. And here it is, offered to me one more time, dressed as compassion, dressed as the win, dressed as the thing that would free Tobias and let me feel something hot and clean in my chest at the rail of his cell — *it was love, it was always love, the love is what convicted him* — and it is, I understand now, sitting in a lot in Parma with a young Deaf-raised woman who refused to let me have it, the most seductive render I have ever been tempted to make, because the source it smooths over is not a deprived man's hands. The source it smooths over is a system. And a system is harder to render than a knife.

So I set it down. I want it on the record that I set it down — not nobly, not easily, not the first time it was offered, but the second time, after Bee made me, after I had already reached for it once in the dark of the front seat and started to lift it to my mouth. I set down the love. Not because it isn't true. Because it is the wrong size. Because the love is a tributary, and I have been standing at the mouth of it mistaking it for the river, the way I have mistaken so many small clean things for the whole.

The river is still running. Bee is right about that too. While I sat in Parma being moved, a clock I cannot see the face of was rolling toward a shredder; an unsealing motion was being opposed by reasonable lawyers in service of a navy coffee cup; Delia Hart was in her Deaf-built kitchen deciding whether a hearing woman could be trusted with the one document the court did not destroy; a man's left hand was shaking in Grafton in a language no one there speaks; and a courtroom two floors above the basement where they re-code the chairs was, that very week, in *Webb,* emptying another chair on the same logic — *we have a certified interpreter already, do we really need two* — live, present-tense, with my own faithful hands inside it every morning.

I start the car. I do not say anything else about the parents, then or — I think — ever, except this, here, set down once where I can see it and then carried no further: that Eleanor Rein, eighty-one, folding her hands in a sealed wing in Parma, took her son's hands out of the air with so much love it disabled him for the world that would one day try him, and that she is the saddest part of this and not the worst part of it, and that the difference between the saddest and the worst is the whole of what I came back from Parma understanding — and that I almost, in a parking lot, rendered the saddest as the worst, because the saddest had a face I could put on a witness stand, and the worst has no face at all, only a schedule, and a cup, and a basement, and my hands.

I pull out onto Ridge Road. Bee texts before we reach the highway — she does not say it out loud, she puts it in the channel that does not require me to be available, the way her parents reach her, the way Delia will only reach her — and I read it at the light.

*Delia called me. Not you. Me.* A pause-length, a second bubble. *She'll give us the document. One condition. She wants to hear what you're going to say on the stand BEFORE you say it. She wants to know if you'll tell the truth or if you'll tell the version that's easy on you.* A third bubble, and I sit at the green light a half-second too long reading it, long enough that the car behind me leans on the horn, the sound arriving in the channel I have, the channel Tobias does not, the channel his mother spent his whole childhood trying to give him by taking his hands away.

*She says she's heard a hearing person promise to tell the truth in a courtroom before.*


# Chapter Nineteen — I Only Said What They Said

Delia Hart keeps the tapes in a chest freezer.

I saw it the first time, in Lakewood — caught it through the kitchen doorway while she went for the paper, a dead appliance against the wall with tapes standing on end inside it where there should have been meat — but she did not open it to me then. The first time she let me look at the paper; that was the part she'd decided I could be trusted to see. This is the second time, and she has decided to let me look at the rest, which is its own kind of verdict, and the rest is the freezer: the compressor pulled out of it fifteen years ago, lined with the silica packets you find in shoeboxes, the carcass of an appliance turned into a dry vault for Hi8 and MiniDV and a few of the gray transcription cassettes — the formats that rot in a Cleveland basement the way everything rots here — because the videotapes are the only record she made with her own hands and kept with her own hands and never once let the county touch.

I notice this the way I notice everything, which is to say I file it and I do not say it, because the thing it tells me about Delia Hart — that she has spent fifteen years not trusting a single institution to keep what she knows — is a thing she did not invite me back into her kitchen to have observed by the woman who is, in the long arithmetic of why she stopped doing this work, partly the reason.

I want it on the record, since I'm trying to keep an honest one now, that I did not earn the second visit. Bee found her the first time — I spent two days working a certification roster and an expired VRS number and a community that closed around the name like water, and got nothing, and Bee made one call to a woman who knew a woman, in the language I am fluent in and not *of,* and we were in Lakewood by Thursday. And it was Bee Delia called afterward, not me, to set the one condition that bought this morning: that before she put anything of hers into a courtroom through anyone's hands, she would hear, from my own mouth, what I intended to say on the stand. So that is why I am here a second time. Not as the investigator come back for more. As a person summoned to audition the truth for the one witness who will not be lied to.

She is fifty-eight and she signs the way Meg signed — *exactly here, not approximately* — which is a thing I have only ever seen in Deaf signers of a certain age and discipline, the ones for whom a sloppy sign is a kind of lie. Her kitchen is the same Deaf room it was the first time: the table pushed to the wall so the sightlines open, a lamp on a swing arm aimed where hands go, a baby monitor on the counter rigged to a light instead of a speaker, for a grandchild somewhere down the hall. She has made coffee she is not going to drink and set out three cups, which is hospitality and also a way of giving her hands somewhere to rest between sentences so I can't read her when she doesn't want to be read.

*Say it,* she signs. Not a greeting. We are past greetings; she gave me the porch and the placement-in-the-air the first time, and there is no second performance of it. She sets the cup down and her hands come up, brows level, the floor handed to me with no runway. *You drove back here to tell me what you'll say in his courtroom. So. Tell me. I'll know if it's the true version or the one that's soft on you. I've watched a hearing person say both before, and I'll tell you a thing about them — the soft one is always cleaner.*

* * *

Here is where I have to be careful, and where I am going to fail at it, because I am the narrator and I am also the suspect, and the thing I had to do in Delia Hart's kitchen was rehearse, out loud, in a language she could read better than I will ever sign it, the confession that frees a man — and I could not get three sentences into it before it stopped being a confession and started being a defense, and she let me get all the way to the bottom of the difference before she said a word.

I began where I thought the truth began. With L2 — with her. I told her what I knew now and had not known then: that she was the CDI on the Tobias trial, scheduled because a language-deprived defendant is legally owed one, present day one, working in tandem with me, relaying between Tobias's idiolect and my fluent foreign hands the way the law requires, two interpreters in a chain because one is not enough for a man whose language was built in a house with the windows painted over. That on day two, at the time the schedule says, she was re-coded off and sent to a cheaper fight, and did not come back, and I finished the trial alone. I placed it in the air the way I have learned to place things — the courtroom to my left, herself in it, then herself *lifted out* of it, the gesture Bee made in Pam's office, because it is the true shape of the event and any honest hand makes the same shape.

She watched me build her own removal in my hands and she let me. And when I'd set the last of it down — when I had laid out, accurately, the proof that it had not been only me — I heard my own voice keep going, into the part I had not meant to say out loud, the part that always comes next.

*It wasn't my decision to drop you,* I signed. *I didn't authorize the re-code. Interpreters don't get told — the re-code lives two floors down in a module headed for a shredder. I was a per-diem the same as you. The tape was degraded. The lighting in that interview room was the worst in the building. His signing was non-standard, idiosyncratic, gap-ridden — anyone certified would have rendered it the way I —*

I stopped my own hands. Not because Bee said anything; Bee was in the doorway with her coffee, letting Delia have the room, the point not being to spare me. I stopped because I caught the shape of the wall under the words — *I only,* one more time, the same animal wearing a sworn statement now — and I caught it three signs in, too slow to be proud of and just fast enough to be ashamed, which is the only speed I have learned to catch it at this whole spring.

But three signs is three signs, and Delia Hart had already read every one of them.

*There,* she signs. Not loud. Almost tired. *Three sentences. I told you. You came here to tell me what you'll say under oath, and you're already telling me why none of it lands on you. That's not the testimony. That's the next thing you build instead of it.*

* * *

She does not look angry. That is the part I was not ready for. I came braced for an angry Deaf woman — I had a posture ready for it, a contrition I could perform — and Delia Hart is not angry, she is *tired* in a way I recognize because it is the exact tiredness Lockwood has, the exact tiredness Pam washes her spoon through, the tiredness of a person who has watched a true thing fail to land so many times that the landing of it stopped being the thing they hoped for.

And then she signs the thing that takes the floor out, and she signs it to me directly, slowly, the way Meg made my name-sign once so I would learn the shape of myself.

*You think this is about whether you're good,* Delia signs. The sign for *good* sits a beat too long in the air, ironized, the way only a fluent face can ironize a clean sign. *You drove an hour to a stranger's kitchen, twice now, to find out if you're a good person who made a mistake or a bad person who made a mistake. That's the whole question you keep bringing into my house.* She lets her hands fall, briefly, the punctuation of contempt, then lifts them again. *I'm not going to answer it. Nobody alive can answer it, and it isn't the question. The question is who gets to decide what a man said. And the answer in that room, for nine days, was you. Not because you were bad. Because they sent me home and you were what was left, and you were good enough that nobody could tell the difference between a faithful render and a sentence you could live with.*

* * *

I want to tell you that I received that with grace.

I did not receive it with grace. I received it the way the alibi receives the evidence that kills it, which is to say I tried, one more time, to make it a smaller thing than it was, and Delia let me, the way you let a child finish a wall they've already been told is going to be knocked down.

"I rendered what I saw," I said. Out loud, this time, because some part of me had decided that saying it in English, into the room, would give it a foundation it didn't have in my own head. "That's the whole of it. I didn't soften him — I have never softened anyone, not since I was nineteen, I built my entire life on never softening — I rendered *exactly* what I saw. Whatever I did wrong, it wasn't that I changed his words. I gave the court precisely what was in front of me."

Bee opened her mouth. Delia stopped her with two fingers, *let her,* and watched me say it, and when I was done she did a thing I will carry the rest of my life, which is that she signed my own sentence back to me.

*I only said what they said,* Delia signs — and she does it in *my* register, she does my hands, she does the clean fluent confident interpreter's English-on-the-hands that I produce, she role-shifts into *me,* the celebrated faithful instrument, and she makes the shape I have made four thousand times when a lawyer asks if I'm certain, the shape that means *I am a conduit, I add nothing, hold me harmless* — and then she lets the role-shift drop, becomes herself again, a Deaf woman in her own kitchen, and signs, in her own register now, plain: *That sentence. That exact sentence. Do you know who else says it?*

I don't answer. The kitchen has gone very quiet, the baby-monitor light blinking on the counter, a grandchild breathing somewhere down a hall in a house where the alarms are made of light because the people in it cannot be woken by sound.

*Everybody,* Delia signs. *The cop who only ran the camera. The clerk who only filed it. The juror who only voted with the room. The prosecutor who only tried the case the system handed him.* Her hands are very economical now, very flat, very cold, the way Lamar's went cold on the stand, the way the most devastating signing always goes — not bigger, smaller, the violence concentrated. *I only did what the court let me do. That's mine. That's the one I say at three in the morning. I objected and they overruled me and I packed my bag and went to the cheaper fight because a CDI who refuses a re-code is a CDI who doesn't get the next call, and I had a mortgage too, and I told myself for fifteen years that I only did what the court let me do.* She holds my eyes. *It's the same sentence, Ruth. It's the only sentence the whole building knows. Everybody in that room only said what they said. That's not the defense. That's the crime.*

* * *

I have spent this entire account trying to give you the gap — the millimeter between the said and the rendered, the direction of one movement, the width of one finger — because the gap is the thing I know how to show you and the thing I have organized my life around. And I have to tell you now that there is a gap in this chapter too, and it is not on a tape and it is not in anyone's hands. It is the gap between the sentence I had been living inside for thirty years — *I only transmit; I am the pane of glass; a pane of glass cannot be guilty of the weather* — and the thing Delia Hart just put on the table, which is that the sentence was never a description of what I do. It was a *liturgy.* A thing the whole building chants, each instrument in its turn, the cop and the clerk and the juror and the prosecutor and the interpreter, each one of us holding up our two clean hands and saying *I only,* until the sentence has passed all the way down the line and a man is in Grafton with a tremor nobody will look at and not one of the hands that put him there is dirty, because each hand only did its part, and a harm that every hand made is a harm that no hand made, and *that* — I am sitting in a kitchen understanding this with my coffee gone cold — *that* is the machine. Not a villain. A liturgy. The building is a machine for saying *I only* until the guilt has nowhere to land.

And I was not outside it being the glass. I was a verse in it. The cleanest verse. The one they could trust to say *I only said what they said* and mean it, and be believed, and be requested by name, because I never editorialized, never summarized, never softened — I just took whatever was in front of me, however maimed, however unrenderable, and gave it back to the room as a sentence a jury could convict on, faithfully, accurately, to the best of my ability, so help me, and the better I was at it the more perfectly the machine worked, because the machine does not need a liar. A liar is an event. A liar can be caught. The machine needs a *faithful instrument* — it needs me — because only a faithful render launders cleanly, only a faithful render can sit in the record for fifteen years and never once flag itself as the place where a man was authored into guilt.

I had it backwards my whole life. I thought my faithfulness was my innocence. My faithfulness was my *usefulness.* It is the precise quality that made me the most effective component in a machine for producing harm without authors, and I have a national certification in it, and people request it by name.

* * *

"So I'll testify," I said.

I said it too fast, and I heard, the instant it left me, what it was. It was the wall again, dressed in better clothes. It had stopped being *it wasn't my fault* and become something that wore the costume of taking responsibility, and was not — was, in fact, the same animal: a way out. *Let me fix it. Let me be the one who breaks the render. Let me get up under oath and author the truth out loud and immolate my career and be, at the end of it, clean again — a different clean, a sacrificial clean, but clean, the kind of clean you earn by paying enough for it.* The bricklayer had not stopped laying brick. He had just changed materials. He was building a cathedral of atonement now instead of a wall of defense, and it was the same hand, and it wanted the same thing, which was a verdict on me I could survive.

And Delia Hart — who is a mirror of me one rung over, a Deaf instrument silenced by the same machine, who has had fifteen more years than I have to learn what I learned this afternoon — saw the new costume the way she saw the old one.

*There it is,* she signs, almost gently. *The next one. You can't help it. You're going to walk into that courtroom and break your own render and ruin yourself, and somewhere under it you'll be hoping it makes you the glass again — the noble glass, the glass that broke itself on purpose.* She shakes her head, the small Deaf headshake that negates a whole clause being signed under it. *It won't. That's the thing nobody tells you about putting your hands down. It doesn't make you clean. It just makes you a person who touched things, who has to live in a world she touched, with no glass to stand behind.* And then, because Delia Hart is not in the business of comforting me and never agreed to be: *Tobias already told you this. He told me you'd come down here looking for the same thing, and that I should let you find out you can't have it from me either.*

That stops me — that Tobias and Delia have talked, that the dying man in Grafton has been, in the language no one there will speak with him, somehow ahead of me the whole way, narrating my approach to the people I think I am arriving at as a rescuer. *You're the hand,* he'd signed across the bolted table. *You just don't want to be the one who has to live in a world you touched.* I had filed it as his judgment landing. It was a prophecy, and it has been coming true in two kitchens and a living room while I drove from one to the next believing I was gathering witnesses.

* * *

So this is where the alibi died, and I want to mark the exact spot, because a thing as large as the death of how a person has understood herself for thirty years should have a location, and this one does: it died at a Deaf woman's kitchen table, in the afternoon, over coffee nobody drank, when Delia Hart signed my own sentence back to me in my own register and then named everyone else in the building who chants it.

*I only said what they said.* It is gone now. It cannot be repaired, and I am not going to pretend the loss of it is a kind of freedom, because it is not, it is a kind of falling. For thirty years that sentence was the floor. I stood on it over Meg, I stood on it over Tobias, I stood on it over a hundred strangers' worst days — *I only transmit, hold me harmless, I am not in the sentence* — and the floor is gone, and I am the thing that is left when you take the floor out from under a person whose whole architecture was the floor: a woman who has to author, every time, with no costume, knowing it, and answer for it, and has no idea how to live that way and is fifty-one years old and is going to have to learn.

And I understand, with the cold of Delia's kitchen in me, what the climax is going to require, and it is not what I told myself in the car. It is not a sacrifice that buys me back. It is worse than that and smaller than that and it is the only door left. To free Tobias, the conviction has to fall, and the only thing that can take it down is the one instrument the State will swear was faithful and unimpeachable getting up under oath and saying — not *I made a mistake,* which is a sentence the system can absorb, a single dirty brick replaced and the wall stands — but the thing the system cannot survive: *there is no faithful render. The record you convicted on was authored. I authored it. So did the court that took away the one person who could have read him. The man's own language could not carry his defense, and we performed fidelity over the absence, and I am the proof, because I am the most faithful render in the building and I am telling you under oath that the most faithful render in the building cannot be trusted to know what a man said.*

It will not make me clean. Delia has made sure I know that now. It will end my career, and it will not buy the career back as a soul, and Irene will not forgive me, and the community owes me nothing, and Tobias is going to use me and despise needing me and be right to. I am going to do the one thing my entire life was built to refuse — stand up in a court and *author* — and there is no version of it where I walk out the glass again. That door is bricked. Delia bricked it with two fingers while a grandchild breathed down a hall in a house full of lights.

* * *

Bee drove us back, because I was not fit to. We were almost to the highway when her phone went off on the dash mount, the flash going, and she pulled over to read it because she does not text and drive, she is twenty-seven and immortal in every way except that one, and she handed me the phone without a word and let me read it myself, which was a mercy and not a mercy, the way everything is in this book now.

It was Lockwood. Two lines.

*Records contractor moved up the pre-2012 pickup. Cold storage ships Monday a.m. — not "rolling," Monday. County's still opposing the unseal; Adeyemi won't rule before the hearing.*

And under it, the second line, which is the one that put the cold of Delia's kitchen all the way into my chest:

*If Delia's copy is partial, the only proof the CDI was ever in that room ships Monday. Get whatever she has by Sunday or it's her word against a record that no longer exists.*

I sat in Bee's idling car on the shoulder of the highway with the lake somewhere off behind us doing its flat gray nothing, holding a phone with a delivery date on it for the proof of a man's destruction, and I understood that the floor I had spent the whole afternoon losing had a clock running under it now too. The alibi was dead. The climax's terms were set — author the truth out loud or Tobias stays in Grafton until the tremor finishes what the court started. And the seam, the one record that proved I had not been alone in that room, the only thing standing between *I only said what they said* and the truth that no single hand had to be the author — that record was a cubic foot of paper in a queue, and the queue had a date now, and the date was Monday, and it was already Thursday night, and the folder Delia showed me the first time was not the whole of it. The whole of it was frozen in a dead appliance in her kitchen, behind a door she had just decided, this afternoon, watching me fail to lie to her, that she might be willing to open — for Tobias, never for me — and I had until Sunday to be the kind of asker she would open it for, which is the one kind of asker I have spent my whole life not knowing how to be.

Bee pulled back onto the highway. I did not turn the radio on. I have stopped pretending the silence is a thing that happens to other people, and I am not yet sure what is supposed to happen to a person who has run out of floor and is still, somehow, expected to drive.


# Chapter Twenty — The Room Could Not Read It

Irene called me back herself.

Not Bee, not the cousin, not a relay. A text — she texts the way she signs, shaved down, every wasted word planed off: *Thursday. 2. Come.* No greeting, no question mark, no name, because she knew I'd know the number and she knew I'd come, and the knowing was its own small dominance, the way the dead doorbell had been: she set the terms and let me feel her setting them. I read it four times standing in the analog wing with Bee watching me read it.

"She called you back," Bee said, not looking up from the drive she was labeling. There was something careful in it. "Huh."

"Is that strange."

"She closed the door on you pretty hard." Bee capped the marker. "Irene doesn't reopen doors because she feels bad. She reopens them because she's decided something." She finally looked at me, and what was in her face was not reassurance. "Whatever she's decided, Ruth, it's not for you. Don't walk in there thinking it's for you."

I drove out past the airport again, the same street of post-war houses, the same place I was told not to think about parking, except now it was June and the awnings threw hard little shadows and the lake off to the right had gone the flat hot silver it goes in summer, and I did not rehearse anything. I want that noted, because in Chapter Nine I rehearsed, and the rehearsing was the first thing she ate. This time I went up the walk with nothing loaded. I had learned that much. I stood on the porch in front of the square of tape and the bare wire and I waited with my hands empty, and I had stopped experiencing the empty hands as a humiliation and started experiencing them as the correct posture, which I think was the only progress I had made as a human being in fifteen years, and it had cost me everything I used to be.

She let me stand there a while. Then the inner door opened.

*

The room was the same horseshoe, the same lamps low and aimed at the hands, the same wall of photographs from banquets I was never at. The television was on, captions off, the muted Deaf church service running to no one — except this time it was not a sermon. It was an old recording, grainy, a Deaf choir signing a hymn in unison, twenty pairs of hands rising and falling together in a silence the camera couldn't fill, and Irene left it playing the whole time, and I came to understand by the end that it was not background. She had put it on for the same reason a hearing person plays a record at a wake. She was sitting with something.

She did not make me take the bad chair this time. That registered. She let me sit where I could see her clean, full light on her hands, and that small reallocation of the room told me Bee was right: a decision had been made, and the decision had rearranged the furniture of how Irene was going to treat me, and I did not yet know toward what.

She let me settle. Then she signed, and her signing was not the fast contemptuous compression of the first visit, the regional shorthand she'd used to drown me. It was slow. Deliberately, terribly slow — not the insulting slow of *do you need it again,* but the slow of a person choosing each sign because each sign was load-bearing and she would not have me mishear one of them and turn it into something I could live with.

*You did the work.* A statement. Her eyes went to the choir on the wall, came back. *Bee told me. The storage unit. The papers. The woman who got sent home.* She spelled none of it contemptuously now. *You found the shape of it.* The brows lifted, not a question — an assessment, the way my father would look at a structure and grant, grudging, that the load was finding ground. *Most of it.*

"Most of it," I signed back. No voice. I'd learned that too.

*The part you found.* She set it in the air between us, a thing on a table. *A Deaf interpreter. Hired for him. Sent away to save money. You solo after that.* Each clause placed and left to stand. *You think that's the part you didn't know.*

"Isn't it."

And Irene Stillwater looked at me with an expression I had never once seen on her face, in this room or in my reconstructions of the 2009 courtroom, and it took me a moment to identify it because I had spent four months braced for her contempt and her grief and her withholding and had not prepared for this, which was *pity,* clean and cold and total, the pity you feel for someone about to learn the thing that makes the rest of their suffering look like a warm-up.

*No,* she signed. *That's the part you can put in a file.*

She turned off the television. The choir vanished mid-hymn, twenty hands frozen and then gone, and the room went into the particular silence of her house, the silence that was not absence but attention, and she turned back to me and began to tell me what was in the room.

*

I am going to give it to you the way she gave it to me, and I am going to fail, and I want the failure on the record before I start, because the failure is the chapter. Irene built the 2009 courtroom in the air the way she built it in Chapter Nine — the bench, the box, the well, the second row where she sat every day so Tobias had one face he could read — but this time she did not put my hands at the center of it. This time she put a different person at the center, off to my left, in the place where the second interpreter stands when there are two, and she signed the woman's name.

She fingerspelled it slowly. D-E-L-I-A. And then she gave her a sign — a name-sign, the one the community had given Delia Hart years ago, and I will not gloss its mechanics here because to gloss it would be to do to it the thing the whole chapter is about; it was a sign that meant *the one whose hands find the holes,* placed at the temple, a sign about a particular kind of seeing — and the moment Irene made it, made it *fond,* made it with the small softening a name-sign carries when you have loved the person it belongs to, I understood that I had spent four months treating Delia Hart as a line item. A vendor number. A rate code on an invoice in a storage unit off Granger Road. A *seam.* And to Irene she was a person with a name-sign about her hands, and the distance between those two facts — the line item and the woman — was the exact distance the court had traveled in 2009, and I had traveled it too, in my own investigation, four months ago, last week, building my case for who authored the harm out of paperwork because paperwork was a thing I could hold without it holding me back.

*Delia was there the first day,* Irene signed. *Beside you. Where she belonged.* She placed Delia in the air, then placed nineteen-years-younger me beside her, and for one moment the two of us stood together in the space the way we were supposed to have stood — the hearing interpreter and the Deaf one, the tandem, the thing the law requires for a man like Tobias precisely because a man like Tobias cannot be reached by one set of hands alone. *She watched him sign. Then she watched you voice it. That's her job — she reads him, she reads you, she tells the room when the bridge between you has a gap in it.* The brows came down. *That's the whole reason she's in the chair. To catch the thing you can't catch about your own hands.*

She let that sit. I felt it go in.

*The first day,* Irene signed, *she caught things.*

*

She showed me one.

I had not asked her to. I want that clear — I did not ask Irene to reconstruct the testimony for me, did not deploy a single one of the techniques I use to extract a clean account from a witness, because somewhere in the first four signs I had understood that I was not interviewing her. She was doing this *to* me, on her schedule, and my job was to receive it and not smooth it, which is the hardest job there is for hands like mine.

She built a small moment from the first day of the trial. Tobias on the stand — and she rendered his signing the way it actually was, the deprived idiolect I had finally learned to see on the tape, the holes where a native signer would have a sign and he had a fingerspelled approximation, the reaching, the footbridges. He was trying to say something about the dead man. Something about the dead man's hands, Irene signed — and she placed the dead man in the air off to the right, the same locus he occupies at 14:22, and she showed me Tobias trying to describe what the dead man's hands *did,* and stalling, and reaching for it twice, and landing on a construction that was — she made it precisely, made the ambiguity itself, the half-turn of the torso, the classifier that could be a grab or could be a strike depending on a direction the deprived grammar didn't fully commit to —

— and she stopped, and she became, for one second, *me.*

She role-shifted into nineteen-years-younger me. She put on my face, the clean competent surface, and she lifted my fast sure hands, and she voiced — silently, her mouth shaping the English while her hands went still the way mine go still when I'm voicing and not signing — she voiced the clean version.
