The committed version. The version that picked one of the two readings and made it grammar a jury could hold. I could not hear the English she was mouthing and I did not need to. I had said it. I knew exactly which way I had resolved it, because resolving it is the job, because a jury cannot convict on *it could be a grab or it could be a strike,* and I had given them the strike, because the strike was a sentence and the ambiguity was not, and in 2009 I had filed that under *competence* and moved on, the way I filed the gate, the way I have filed ten thousand small resolutions that all, every one, leaned the same way.

Then Irene dropped my face. And she became Delia.

She shifted her body left, into the place where the second interpreter sits, and she put on a different posture entirely — alert, leaning, a professional catching something mid-flight — and Delia's hands came up, and Delia did the thing a CDI is in the chair to do, the thing the whole tandem exists for: she signed a correction. Small. Discreet. The way the code requires, *correcting errors discreetly and expeditiously,* a flag raised between colleagues — *wait, that's not what he — the direction, you've reversed the —* Irene built Delia building it, the quarter-turn restored, the path put back the other way, the reading I had foreclosed restored to the air by the one person in the room qualified to restore it.

And then Irene became the room.

*

This is the part I will fail at, so I will be plain.

She showed me what happened to Delia's hands. She showed me the prosecutor — she did not name him, she built him as a shape, tall, sure, a hearing man in a good suit who I now know was Gerald Pryor and who I will get to in its own time because his hand is its own chapter — she showed him turning toward the disturbance, toward the Deaf woman in the interpreter's chair whose hands had come up out of turn, and she showed his face do a thing I have seen a thousand times and never once been on the receiving end of: the polite, total, institutional incomprehension of a man watching a language he does not know and has decided he does not need to know, deciding that what he is seeing is not *correction* but *interruption.* Not signal. Noise.

She showed the judge's gavel — she made the gavel with one fist, brought it down, and the bringing-down was not loud in the silent room and that was somehow worse, a soundless gavel falling on a soundless objection. She showed someone leaning toward Delia. She showed the words that get said in a courtroom when a thing the room cannot read is happening in the corner of its eye — *let's keep it moving, the interpreter will please confine herself, is there an issue with the interpretation* — and the terrible joke folded inside that last question, *is there an issue with the interpretation,* asked of the room, asked of *me,* the certified one, the one whose interpretation it was, because in that courtroom there was exactly one person whose answer to *is there an issue with the interpretation* counted, and it was the person who had made the interpretation, and she had not flagged an issue, because she did not know there was one, because the only person who knew there was one was sitting in the second chair signing it into a silence the room had already decided was a malfunction.

Irene became Delia one more time. She put Delia's hands up — and then she showed them stop. She showed the moment a professional understands that the channel is closed. That there is no procedure by which a Deaf interpreter, in tandem, corrects the record over the head of the certified hearing interpreter whose render is the record, because the entire architecture of the room runs in one direction: the hands go to the certified interpreter, the certified interpreter goes to the room, and there is no return path, no upstream, no way for the truth to travel back against the current of who-the-court-has-decided-to-believe. Delia could read Tobias. Delia could read me. Delia could see the gap with the clarity of the one bilingual person in the building, the way I see gaps now, the way I saw the gap on the tape four months ago and went still. And it did not matter, because the room had no organ that could receive what she saw. She was the smoke detector going off in a house where no one had ever learned what the sound meant, and so they took the battery out to make it stop.

*She put her hands down,* Irene signed. Slow. *And she never put them up again. Not that day. Not the next.* The brows neutral, the fact dropped flat, the way she drops the worst things. *Day two they sent her home. You think that's the wound — that they sent her home.* She shook her head, one economical turn. *They sent her home because she was already gone. She went home in her chair on the first day, the minute she understood the room. The paper just caught up.*

*

I had built a case, in Chapter Nine, against Irene. I had laid it brick by brick while she watched, and she had caught me at it, and I had not done it again with her since. I want to tell you I did not do it now. I want to tell you that.

But I felt it start. I felt the old machinery turn over, the part of me that survives by finding the load-bearing flaw in any account that indicts me — *she didn't see all of it, she's reconstructing, fifteen years is fifteen years, memory under grief is the least reliable instrument there is, Delia raised her hands once and Irene has turned one discreet flag into a martyrdom, and even if every word of it is true it is still true that no one could have known, that the room's deafness is not a thing a single hand commits, that I was an instrument of it and an instrument is not an author* — and I felt the whole structure start to assemble itself with the speed and the false integrity it always has, every joint true, and I understood with a kind of nausea that I was, right now, in Irene Stillwater's living room, doing the exact thing the chapter was describing. Watching a Deaf woman tell me the truth and deciding, in the corner of my eye, that what she was showing me was not signal. That there was an issue with the interpretation.

I did not finish it. Not because I am good. Because she watched me start.

Of course she watched me start. She had watched me start in Chapter Nine and she watched me start now, the small backward flick of the eyes, the held breath of a person assembling a rebuttal, and she let her hands rest in her lap and she waited, exactly the way she'd waited before, and the waiting was the whole correction, because there is no rebuttal you can build with someone watching you build it who already knows the shape of the wall. The structure fell apart in my hands before I'd laid the third course. Not because she argued. Because she *saw,* and being seen building it is the one thing the building cannot survive.

*Go ahead,* she signed, almost gentle. *Say the part you're holding.* The brows up, false-innocent, the most dangerous face she has. *"No one could have known."* She made it in clean English-order, mocking the very shape of the sentence, holding it up so I could see what it was. *That's the one, isn't it. That's the brick.*

I put my hands down.

"Delia knew," I signed.

*Delia knew.* Irene nodded, slow, and there was no triumph in it, which was worse, it was just the next true thing being set on the table. *Delia knew. I knew — I was in the second row and I am not certified and I am not an expert and I read him cleaner than you ever will because I have known him thirty years and I watched you give the room the wrong man and I knew.* She let it land. *Half that gallery knew. The Deaf half. We sat there and we watched the room convict a version of him that we could all see was wrong, and we could not get one sign of it across the well, because the well only runs one way.* Her hands slowed to almost nothing. *The room was full of people who could read him. The room was just built so that none of them counted.*

*

I want to be precise about what she did not say, because the precision is the only respect I have left to offer her and it is the whole of what the book is for.

She did not say *neutrality is a costume.* She did not say *the court convicts its own deafness.* She did not deliver a thesis, and if I have made any of this sound like a thesis it is my failure, my rendering, my old reflex to take a thing that lived in a body and iron it flat into a sentence a reader can hold — the precise crime, performed on the page, in the act of describing the crime. She did not explain anything. She *showed* me a room, and let the room be the argument, and trusted me to assemble what it meant, which is the opposite of what I do for a living. I assemble it for the jury so they don't have to. She made me do my own assembling, in her language, on her floor, and the thing I assembled put my own hands at the center of it after all, not as the author of the gap — she never said I was the sole author, she is far too exact for that, she knows there were many hands and a procedure and a budget and a man with a campaign she was building toward in the air the way you build toward a thing you're not ready to name — but as the *organ.* The one the room used to do its not-hearing. The certified, sworn, beautiful, sure-handed channel through which the room's deafness got laundered into a verdict, so that no single deaf ear in the building had to be blamed for not hearing, because the hearing had been *outsourced,* to me, and I had performed it flawlessly.

The room was not deaf because no one in it could read him. The room was full of people who could read him.

The room was deaf because it had appointed one person to hear for it, and built no way to tell her she was wrong.

That is the séance. I had been circling the word for months without letting myself land on it, the way you circle a thing in the dark you can feel the shape of and don't want to touch. A séance is a room that gathers to receive the words of someone who cannot speak for himself, through a single chosen medium, and then believes the medium, because believing the medium is the entire point of convening — the medium's authority is not a bug in the séance, it is the *function,* you do not hold a séance and then cross-examine the spirit. The court had convened to receive the words of a man it could not hear, through me, and it had believed me, and when the Deaf woman in the second chair tried to say *the medium has it wrong,* the room did the only thing a séance can do with a heckler: it removed her, so the spirit could keep speaking in the voice the room had already decided to trust.

I had spoken for the dead the way a medium speaks for the dead. And Tobias was not even dead. He was sitting right there, alive, full of language, holding his own account in hands that could have given it if the room had been built to take it — and the room had reached past the living man to the medium, and asked the medium what he meant, and written down the answer.

*

Irene was tired. I could see it now that the worst of it was out — the particular exhaustion of a person who has set down a weight, which is not relief, which is the ache where the weight was. She had carried this for fifteen years. She had carried the knowledge that she had been in a room where the truth was signed and the room could not read it, and that she had watched it happen, and that there was nothing in the architecture of justice that would let her testimony count for more than a disturbance, because the moment a Deaf person's account of a courtroom contradicts the certified record, the system has a ready answer, and the answer is *she is not qualified to assess the interpretation,* and the answer is correct, by the system's own rules, which is what makes it unanswerable, which is what made her carry it for fifteen years instead of being believed in fifteen minutes.

*Why are you telling me,* I signed. *You wouldn't give me the job. You said find someone else's grief to be good at.* I was not being defensive now. I genuinely did not understand. *What changed.*

She looked at me for a long time. The choir was off; the room was just the room.

*Nothing changed,* she signed. *I still won't be the thing that makes your hands clean. That's not what this is.* She leaned forward, into the good light she'd given me, the light I could read her by. *I'm telling you because you finally did a thing nobody in that courtroom did in 2009, and it took you fifteen years and a tape and that girl and a storage unit to do it, and it's the smallest thing a person can do and it's the only thing that was ever missing.* Her hands stopped. She made me wait for it.

*You turned around.* She placed it exactly. *You sat three seconds behind a man for two weeks in 2009 and you never once turned your head to see who else in that room could read him. And four months ago you finally turned around.* The brows came down, and what was in her face was not forgiveness, I want that absolutely clear, it was not forgiveness — it was something harder and more useful, it was *recognition,* the thing she had withheld from me at the door in May because I had not earned it and gave me now because I had. *I'm not telling you so you'll feel better. You're not going to feel better. I'm telling you because there's going to be a room again — his room, the new one, the hearing — and this time there's going to be one person in it who knows to turn around. And I want to know what you do.*

She sat back.

*That's the whole reason,* she signed. *I want to see what you do in the room when you already know the room is deaf. Whether you talk to it anyway. Whether you make it listen, or whether you just become the channel again because the channel is the thing you know how to be good at.* The flat dropped fact of it. *I'm not helping you, Ruth. I'm watching you. There's a difference and you of all people should know it.*

*

I drove home not building anything.

That is the truest sentence I can give you about the difference between the woman who left Irene Stillwater's house in May and the woman who left it in June, and it is not a flattering difference, it is barely a difference at all, it is one inch — the inch between a hand that smooths and a hand that doesn't — and one inch is the whole subject of my life, and I have spent fifty-one years insisting it doesn't exist in me and four months learning it is the only thing in me. I drove past the airport with the lake gone silver and I did not assemble a single brick of defense, did not turn Delia into a reconstruction or Irene into grief or the whole account into a thing I could file, and the not-building was so unfamiliar that I had to keep checking for it, the way you keep touching a tooth that's been pulled.

Here is what I had, instead of a wall.

I had a room that was not deaf. That was the thing that would not leave me, the thing that had moved the floor — I had spent four months believing the tragedy of 2009 was a tragedy of *deafness,* a room full of hearing people who could not read a Deaf man and so failed him by accident, an absence, a vacancy, no one's fault because no one could have known. And the room had not been deaf. The room had been *full of people who could read him.* Irene. Delia. Half the gallery. The truth had been signed in that room, by the woman in the second chair and the man on the stand and the woman in the second row, signed in the only language that could carry it, signed *correctly,* in real time, while it was still time enough to matter — and the room had been built, deliberately and beautifully and with the full sincerity of its oaths, so that none of the people who could read it counted, and so that the one person it had appointed to hear could not be told she was wrong.

You cannot fix a room that couldn't hear. That is no one's crime; that is weather.

But the room could hear. It just decided what the hearing would be, and appointed me to be it, and then refused every correction, and that is not weather, that is a *choice,* enacted by everyone and authored by no one, which is the only kind of crime the building knows how to commit, and the only kind it cannot punish, because there is no defendant — only a procedure, and a budget, and a sworn instrument with beautiful hands who did exactly, exactly what she was asked.

And now there was going to be another room. His room. August, Judge Adeyemi, the hearing I had been counting down to for eleven weeks like a clock I thought I understood. And I had thought, until this afternoon, that my job in that room was to bring the proof — the tape, the invoice, the re-code at 2:10, the gap at 14:22 — and lay it out clean so the room could finally receive what it had missed. To be the channel one more time, the good channel, the channel that carries the truth across instead of dropping it.

Irene had just told me the truth doesn't get dropped. It gets *signed,* perfectly, into a room with no way to take it.

So the proof was not the problem. The proof had been in the room in 2009, in Delia's raised hands, and the room had not had a problem reading it. The room had had a problem *counting* it. And no tape, no invoice, no clean grammatical render of the gap, would fix a room that could read the truth and was built not to count it — unless someone stood up inside the architecture and broke the one rule the whole machine depended on, the rule that the certified instrument cannot be contradicted, by being the certified instrument who stands up and contradicts herself.

There was exactly one person in the world who could do to the new room what no one had been able to do to the old one. One person whose answer to *is there an issue with the interpretation* the court was structurally obligated to hear, because she was the interpreter, and she was the record, and a record cannot be ruled out of order for disagreeing with itself.

I parked where I always park. I sat in the car. I did not check my face in the visor.

Somewhere out past the airport a sixty-three-year-old woman was sitting in a room with the television off, having set down fifteen years of a thing no one would receive, and she was not waiting to see whether I would win.

She was waiting to see whether I would turn around in the room while it was still time enough to matter — or whether, when the room reached past the living and asked me what he meant, I would do the thing I have always known how to be good at, and answer.


# Chapter Twenty-One — Discovery

The county evidence locker for the old years is not downtown. It is a warehouse off a service road in Garfield Heights, three doors down from my own storage unit, which I notice and do not say to Bee, because it is exactly the kind of thing the universe does to a person who has spent her life believing in tolerances — files her crime and her atonement in the same row of corrugated bays, $61 a month, climate uncontrolled, and lets her drive past one to reach the other for nineteen years without once making the connection.

A man named Trell runs the intake window, behind glass, the way everything in this story is behind glass — the prison, the scheduling office, the bench. He has Lockwood's order on a clipboard and he reads it twice, and the second time he reads it slower, and I have learned to hate the second reading.

"This is a court file," Trell says. "This is evidence. You can look. You can't take."

"We don't need to take it." Lockwood is not here; Lockwood is in chambers across town being told no in a more expensive room. It is Bee and me, and Bee has the field equipment from the archive in a hard case — a deck, a converter, a drive — because we are not here for the transcript. The transcript I have. The transcript is the lie that is perfect. We are here for the thing under it.

"We need to copy a tape," I say. "Your 2009 master. The police interview, Rein. We have an order for supervised duplication."

Trell looks at the order a third time. Then he says the sentence I have been bracing for since Pam washed her spoon in cold coffee.

"That one's flagged for retention review."

* * *

I want to be precise about what a *retention review* is, because the word sounds like care and it is the opposite of care. It is the bureaucracy deciding a thing has aged past the point where anyone is required to keep it. It is a box getting a date stamped on its side. It does not mean *destroy.* It means *eligible to destroy,* which in a building that runs out of space the way this county runs out of money is a distinction the width of one shredder cycle, and I am standing at a window in Garfield Heights learning that the only physical object in the world that preserves what Tobias Rein's hands actually did — the only record that is not my voice — has a date on its side, and the date is soon, and Trell can't tell me how soon, because to Trell it is a box.

"It hasn't gone yet," Bee says. She has put her professional face on, the archivist's face, the one that knows the inside of every records officer's job better than they do. "Retention review means it's pending. Pending means it's still here. We have a court order for supervised duplication that predates the review flag. You let us copy it today, you're not destroying anything and you're not releasing anything — there's a chain of custody, I'll sign it, you'll sign it, the master never leaves your sight. If it ships before we copy it, and it turns out a post-conviction court wanted it, that's a different kind of Tuesday for you."

Trell is quiet. Bee has done the thing I cannot do, which is speak to a man behind glass in the dialect of his own liability, and I stand there and let her, and I notice — pettily, I will own it, it is the kind of small ugly accounting I do — that she is better at this than I am too, that the list of things the CODA archivist is better at than the celebrated interpreter has gotten long, and I file the resentment where I file everything, behind the glass of my own face, and I am ashamed of it and I keep it.

"Supervised," Trell says finally. "You break it, I report it. It stays on the cart. You touch the cart, not the box."

* * *

The master is a VHS. Of course it is a VHS. Everything that matters in this case is dying of the format it was born in.

Trell wheels it out on a cart and it sits there in its evidence sleeve with a case number and a date and a chain-of-custody log going back to 2009, a column of initials and times, the last entry from years ago, and I look at that column and I understand that I am about to add my initials to the bottom of it — *the original interpreter, re-handling the original evidence, to break the original conviction* — and there is no box on the form for what that is, so it will just look like a duplication, a Tuesday, the same way the re-code that emptied Delia out of the room looked like a Tuesday on Pam's green screen. The worst things in this case all look like Tuesdays.

Bee threads the deck. The tape is bad. We knew it would be bad — I have watched the digitized master a dozen times, the blooming whites, the smeared lows, *you cannot add light to 2009* — but the digital file Bee made years ago was made from a first-generation dub, and this, the cart, the sleeve, the column of initials, is the original tape, the actual oxide, the thing the camera wrote that night, and it is worse. It has a tracking flutter at the head that her deck fights and half wins. It has a dropout at 06:11 that eats a second of the booking and gives it back. And when the timecode crawls toward the middle of the reel, toward the number I cannot stop seeing on the inside of my own eyelids, the picture does a thing it does not do on the dub.

"Stop," I say.

Bee stops it. 14:08. Fourteen seconds short.

There is a seam in the tape. Not in the picture — in the tape itself, the physical strip, a place where the oxide has been stressed, a horizontal band of noise that rolls up through the frame like a wave passing under a boat, and it sits across the picture from about 14:04 to about 14:19, which is to say it sits across 14:22, which is to say it sits across the only fifteen seconds of this man's life that the court should have looked at and could not read.

"That's not on the dub," Bee says.

"No."

"That's degradation since the dub was made." She is leaning in, her archivist's face gone very still. "That's the master continuing to die after we already copied it once. Ruth. This band is going to get worse every time the tape is played. Every pass takes oxide off it. The thing we need to read frame by frame is the exact thing the tape is losing first."

I look at the rolling band of noise sitting across 14:22 and I do the arithmetic I have been doing all week, the arithmetic of objects against clocks. The digital dub is clean enough that Bee read the quarter-turn off it, that Delia read his idiolect off it as close as anyone living can — but the dub is a copy, and Donovan will say so, will stand up at the hearing and say *this is a degraded copy of a degraded copy, the master itself is the evidence, and the master shows—* and point at exactly this, at the band of noise the court's own neglect put across the frame, and argue that the only thing the original master proves at 14:22 is that nothing can be proved at 14:22, which is true, which is the most dangerous kind of true, because it is true for the wrong reason. The master is unreadable not because the truth isn't there but because the court let the truth rot in a sleeve for fifteen years.

"Copy it now," I say. "All of it. Best pass we can get. And document the band — log the timecode of the degradation, photograph the frame, so when they say *the master's no good* we can say *yes, and here is exactly when it became no good, and it was in your custody the whole time.*"

Bee is already rolling tape. "You're learning," she says, not looking at me, and I cannot tell if it is a compliment, and I decide, because I am trying lately to be honest and honesty turns out to be a muscle, that it does not matter whether it is a compliment, because she is right.

* * *

Delia's copy is the thing that can't be put in a van, and Delia does not trust me, and both of those are correct.

I have been to her kitchen twice now and she has not let me forget what it cost the community to be found by me at all. She is fifty-eight and she left court interpreting fifteen years ago and she did not leave it gently — she left it the way you leave a church that let something happen at its altar — and she has spent those fifteen years being the conscience nobody subpoenaed, and now the building wants its records and I am the building's hands, and she knows it. Thursday, at the kitchen table, she told me the alibi I had been living inside for thirty years was the whole building's liturgy, and watched it die on her floor over coffee neither of us drank. Thursday night Lockwood's text moved the consolidation's pickup off "rolling" and onto a Monday, and the carbon she has kept fifteen years under a jigsaw box acquired the same clock as the master downtown. So this is the weekend, and she has agreed to bring the folder out one last time before the building she has never trusted forgets it ever called her anything at all.

She would not do it at the kitchen again. She said the kitchen was for the conversations and the Hollow was for the hand-off — neutral ground, Deaf ground, a place that was hers and not mine the way the well in 2009 was theirs and not hers — and the Hollow on an off-night afternoon is empty and lightless and the chairs are still set facing each other for the Friday that hasn't happened yet, and Delia sits in one of them and makes me come to her, the way Irene made me, the way they all make me, because the one consistent dignity the Deaf in this story have reserved for themselves is deciding the distance I have to cross.

She has a folder. She does not hand it to me.

*I kept everything,* she signs, and her hands are unlike Ruth's and unlike Bee's and unlike Irene's — they are a CDI's hands, a professional's, but a Deaf professional's, which is a thing I am only now, this late, learning to read as its own register: she signs *to* me but she signs the way you sign to a hearing person you have decided not to perform fluency for, plainly, withholding the music, making me work. *Fifteen years. The scheduling slip with my name on it. The day-two re-code, the carbon. My certification log for that week. The note I wrote to the file the night they sent me home.* A pause, her eyes flat on mine. *I wrote a note to the file. Nobody read it. I kept the carbon of the note nobody read.*

I ask if I can see it and she does not move the folder.

*Here is what I want to understand first,* Delia signs, and now she sets something in the air between us, places it the way Bee placed the empty chair, the way the Deaf in this book keep building the sentence in the space so I cannot mishear it: she sets the courtroom on her left, fifteen years deep, and she sets a figure in it — herself — and then she sets a second figure beside the first, at the interpreter's angle, and the second figure is me, and she leaves us both standing there in the air, the Deaf interpreter and the hearing one, side by side in a room that only kept one of us, and she points at the hearing one, at the air-me, and her face does the question before her hands do.

*When they took me out,* she signs, *you stayed. You did not say a word.* The hands are very economical now. *Fifteen years I have asked the air why the famous one let them. And now the famous one drove out here to ask me for my carbon so she can be the hero who breaks the case.* She lets that sit in the lightless room. *So. Before I give you the folder. Tell me I am wrong about why you want it.*

And I cannot.

That is the whole of it and I will put it on the record plainly because the elegant version is exactly the costume Delia would see through in a second: I want the folder because it frees Tobias and I want the folder because freeing Tobias makes me the woman who broke the case she helped build, and those two wants live in the same hand, and there is no millimeter between them I can stand in and call myself clean. I came out here with a sentence ready, a good one, *this isn't about me,* and Delia has set the room in the air and put me in it at the angle where I stayed silent while they carried her off, and the sentence dies in my chest, because she is the one person who watched me do the thing I am now pretending I would never do.

*I want it because it frees him,* I sign. My signing is good. Delia watches it be good with no expression at all. *And I want it because I would rather be the one who breaks this than the one who has to live having built it. Both. At the same strength. I'm not going to pretend one of them isn't there, because you'd see it, and you've already spent fifteen years watching hearing people pretend.*

Something moves in her face. Not forgiveness. I have stopped expecting forgiveness; nothing in this case bends that way and neither does she. What moves is something colder and more useful — recognition, maybe, the recognition of an instrument by another instrument, one rung apart.

*You want the truth on the record,* Delia signs, slowly, *the way I wanted it on the record. I wrote a note. You'll write a— what. A declaration. An affidavit.* She almost laughs and there is nothing warm in it. *We are the same, you and I. We both think if you get the words exactly right and put them in the file, the room will finally hear. I have a carbon of a note that proves the room does not hear. I kept it fifteen years so I could give it to somebody who hadn't learned that yet.*

She slides the folder across the empty chair between us. She does not let go of it for a second after my hand is on it, so that for one beat we are both holding it, the Deaf interpreter and the hearing one, the carbon of the unheard note suspended between our four hands in a lightless room — and then she lets go, and it is mine, and she signs the thing I will carry up the service road and into the rest of this:

*A record is just a thing you keep so you can prove, later, that nobody listened.*

* * *

The motion goes to Adeyemi on a Thursday and I am not in the room for it, but Lockwood tells me afterward, at the cold window I always find, the one over the parking structures, because it is the only place in this building where two people can stand shoulder to shoulder and say true things sideways.

"He'll hear it," Lockwood says. "Adeyemi. He'll hear the language-access argument, he's a serious judge, he's not going to wave us off. But." He has the tired flatness he uses for load paths. "Donovan's going to make it about finality and she's going to be *right* about enough of it that he has to take her seriously. And the unsealing fight—" he turns his phone in his hand, the small horrible gesture, a man checking the ship date on a man's destruction— "the County's opposition came in. Reasonable. Beautifully reasonable. The records are past their hold; the burden's on us; the proof we want is in a consolidation that's lawfully scheduled for destruction and they are under no obligation to interrupt a retention schedule for a fishing expedition." He looks at me. "Nobody's hand on it. I told you. The worst thing in this case still has no fingerprints, and now it has a calendar."

"How long," I say. The investigator. The floor.

"Monday came and went — the contractor's truck didn't." He says it without relief, because it is not relief. "Slipped back to 'rolling.'" He says the word the way you'd say a tumor's grade. "Could be three weeks. Could be next Tuesday. The opposition freezes the date into a maybe, which sounds like air and isn't — it just means the worst thing in this case keeps its no-fingerprints a little longer. We have Delia's carbon now, which is huge, which is the thing they can't shred — but Donovan will say a carbon's not the original, the original's in the consolidation, the consolidation's going, and a Deaf interpreter's fifteen-year-old recollection of a note nobody filed is exactly the kind of unverifiable—" He stops. He doesn't finish it because we both know how it finishes.

And then he tells me the rest of it, the thing he has driven here to say, and he says it to the glass and not to me, because it is the kind of thing you do not do to a person's face.

"You know what it's going to take, at the hearing. To make any of this matter." A pause. "It's not Delia's carbon and it's not the band on the tape. Those are foundation. What it takes is *you,* Ruth. On the stand. Sworn. Saying that the render the conviction rests on — your render, the certified, faithful, requested-by-name render — was authored, not transmitted. That there was no faithful version. That the most accurate hands in the county made a man into a sentence the court could convict, and you were those hands, and you are telling the court, under oath, that the oath you swore in 2009 produced a lie." He turns from the window. "You understand what that does to you. Not him. You. You say that on the record, in this county, and you don't get to be an interpreter anymore. The board will have to act on it. The defense bar that requests you by name, the DA's office that requests you by name — they're requesting a woman who got up under oath and testified that her own faithful render is unreliable. You will have, with your own mouth, decertified yourself. You'll be unhireable by lunch."

I look at the lake doing its flat colorless thing out past the structures, and I feel some defended part of me begin, all on its own, to assemble the case for why this is fine — why a woman who sacrifices her whole profession to free an innocent man is a woman who comes out *clean* at last, redeemed, her hands washed in the grand gesture of their own ruin. The part of me reaching, even now, even in the immolation, for a version of the immolation that makes me good. *Even my martyrdom is a costume.* I do not say it out loud. Delia gave me a better sentence at the Hollow, the rimless one, and I am going to try to hold it instead: that walking into that courtroom to end myself is not absolution. It is just the only render left.

"Yes," I say. "I understand what it does to me."

"And you'll do it."

"Yes."

Lockwood nods, once, and does not say thank you, because he is too tired and because we both know he is asking me to do a thing that serves his case more than it serves me, and we both know I would do it anyway, and the reader of this — whoever finally reads the record I am keeping, the carbon of my own unheard note — should not be sure he is wrong to ask.

* * *

Webb resumes in the morning and I have to go back into Pryor's courtroom and be faithful.

That is the thing nobody warns you about a clock: you cannot stand and watch it. It runs while you do other things. The tape is dying in its sleeve in Garfield Heights and the consolidation is queued for a van and Delia's carbon is in a folder in my bag and the hearing is the eighteenth and the unsealing fight is live and the quarter is ending — and tomorrow at nine I have to stand in the well of 4B and raise my right hand to the man whose conviction all of this is racing to break, and swear to faithfully interpret, and then render a Deaf witness *live,* three seconds behind, with my faith in my own hands now a documented ruin.

Because there are two Deaf witnesses left in Webb. Pam told me. Lamar's redirect that Cantwell reopened, and Darnelle Okoro recalled — Darnelle, whose hands a defense attorney called a *performance*, Darnelle whose mother sits in the gallery — and the CDI who was booked for those afternoons has been dropped, *we have a certified interpreter already*, the exact 2009 words in the exact 2009 baritone, and so it will be me alone, in the red by three o'clock, on the construction the court would never know I was rounding, and I have spent this whole investigation learning, in slow forensic horror, that *me alone* is the precise shape of how a man goes away.

I know the gap is coming. That is the difference now, that is the new and unbearable thing: in 2009 I rounded a deprived man into guilt and did not feel the rounding, rode the three-second lag like a surfer and was proud of the margin. Tomorrow I will stand in the lag and I will *hear* the gap arrive, the way I have heard it arrive on every render since the night I deleted my own clean declaration in four keystrokes — the clean version and the true version both surfacing in the same three seconds, and three seconds to choose, every time, in front of the man on the bench, on the record, with a Deaf witness's liberty riding on whether the most faithful hands in the county can still bring themselves to commit a sentence they no longer believe is faithful.

I put Delia's folder on my kitchen table next to the cued tape and the hearing notice, and I sit with the three of them in the bad wiring and the radiator knock, and I do not open the folder, because I already know what is in it and because the thing in it is not the thing I am afraid of tonight. The thing I am afraid of is nine in the morning. The thing I am afraid of is my own raised hand.

Because every clock in this is counting now, all at once, braided — the hearing, the shredder, the dying tape, the boy in Grafton whose left hand will not stop, Webb's verdict coming, Delia's carbon that proves the room does not hear, the oath I will swear at the hearing to faithfully interpret my own unfaithfulness — and the nearest face on any of those clocks, the one whose hour-hand I can actually see, is the one in Courtroom 4B at nine a.m., where Darnelle Okoro is going to lift her hands and build a true thing in the air in front of Gerald Pryor, and I am going to stand three seconds behind it with the whole weight of what I have learned this month in my chest, and a man's freedom is going to depend on whether I round her clean the way I rounded Tobias, or whether — for the first time in twenty-six years, in the worst possible room, on the record — I finally refuse to.

I do not know which I will do. That is the worst sentence I have written yet, and it is the only honest one: I am the most faithful render in the county, and I do not know, when the gap arrives in the morning, whether I will be able to keep being faithful, or whether the only faithful thing left will be to stop.

The tape sits cued. The folder sits closed. My hand sits flat on the table where, in eleven hours, I will raise it.


# Chapter Twenty-Two — The Crisis

There is a thing that happens to a swimmer who has decided, against all training, to look down. The water does not change. The light at the surface does not change. What changes is that she can no longer pretend the dark under her is a floor.

I take my oath at 9:02 on a Wednesday in the worst light in the building, and I mean it, and the meaning has a hole in it now that the meaning did not used to have. *I will faithfully and accurately interpret.* I do. The clerk lands my name wrong, *Okafor-Vance,* stress on the wrong syllable. Eight days ago I would have catalogued it and let it go. This morning it sits in my chest like the first of a long column of things I am not going to fix.

Pryor takes the bench. His cup is already there, navy, banded white, *PRYOR — families, safety,* an illegal object that twelve jurors and one bailiff and one stenographer and I have all agreed not to see. He says good morning to me by name and gets the stress right, and I am no longer able to receive that as courtesy. I receive it as a man who is very good at the surface of things being kind to another person who is very good at the surface of things. We are, he and I, the two best surfaces in the room.

Darnelle Okoro is in the box.

* * *

She is twenty-six and she does not dress for the room; she dresses for herself, which the room reads as defiance and which is only a young woman declining to apologize for her own face. Her mother is in the gallery, third row, where Darnelle can find her between questions. I take my position — angled to the box, the bench a degree off my right, Darnelle's sightline mine to hold — and she clocks me, and there is a thing in how she clocks me now that was not there in March, on the first day, before the redirect where I froze. She watched my mouth stop once. She files me, now, the way you file a tool that has slipped: usable, watched.

She is the State's whole morning. Webb put a man through a storefront on Lorain; three hearing witnesses are consistent and dull; the case actually turns, the way these things do, on the two Deaf witnesses the jury keeps wanting to disbelieve because disbelief is easier than attention. Lamar is the craftsman. Darnelle is the one who saw it from the other angle, from the bus shelter, and who signs the way weather happens — fast, total, the grammar coming all at once and in the wrong order for my mouth. Hurd needs her placed. He needs the jury to know exactly where she stood and exactly what stood between her and the men, because Cantwell is going to spend the afternoon trying to put the rain between them.

"Ms. Okoro," Hurd says. "Where were you standing when you first saw the two men?"

And Darnelle builds it.

This is the part the gallery cannot see and I have spent a life being paid to make them not need to. She does not say *I stood at the bus shelter.* She takes the air in front of her and makes a city out of it. The shelter goes here, to her left, low and near; she sets it with the flat of her hand and her eyes go to it and leave it there, a real place now, a locus she can point back to for the rest of the hour. The storefront goes across, to her right and far. The two men go into the space between, and she does not name them yet, she *places* them, two upright fingers leaned a few degrees toward each other, the lean itself already telling me something Hurd has not asked.

I wait. I am three seconds behind her and I ride it the way I have ridden it for twenty-six years, letting the sentence finish forming in her hands before I open my mouth, because you cannot voice the front of an ASL sentence in English until you have seen the back of it. The lag is the work. I have lived in the lag my whole professional life and called it home.

"I was at the bus shelter on the south side of Lorain," I say, when the shape is whole. "The two men were across the street and to my right, in front of the storefront."

Clean. True enough. Hurd nods. He thinks the morning is going to be easy.

* * *

It is not the morning that breaks me. It is one question in the afternoon, and I can feel it coming the way my father said you could feel a span coming under load — not a sound, a *change in the quality of the silence,* the structure telling you, before anything visible happens, that the next thing is going to ask more of it than it has.

Cantwell stands at two-fifteen and does the slow-and-loud thing she does, faces Darnelle, lets the jury watch her *strain* across the unbridgeable channel to the deaf girl. I render her at speed, no slowing, no shout, because Darnelle is not slow, the room is, and I will not lend my hands to that lie. Cantwell walks her back to the shelter, to the night, to the rain that was on the glass. She is building toward the only thing she has: that Darnelle could not really have seen, that the rain and the dark and the distance and the *deafness* — she will not say deafness, she will gesture at it, a little flutter of her own hands, the cartoon of signing she performs for the jury whenever she wants them to feel how unreliable the channel is — that all of it together adds up to a young woman who is guessing.

"And at the moment the larger man moved toward the other man," Cantwell says, slow, loud, "you would agree — you can't be certain — which of them moved first."

Darnelle's mother shifts in the third row. Darnelle does not look at her. She looks at Cantwell, and then she answers, and what she does with her hands is the cleanest piece of testimony I have seen in this trial and I cannot say it.

She role-shifts.

She turns her shoulders and becomes the larger man for half a second — the lean, the weight coming forward off the back foot — and then she snaps back and becomes herself, watching, and then she does a thing I have to slow my own breathing to keep up with: she runs the directional verb between the two loci she set this morning, between the place the larger man is and the place the other man is, and the verb *moves,* and the direction it moves is who-went-toward-whom, and it is the whole afternoon, it is the whole case, it is the difference between an assault and a thing that happened between two men, and —

It is ambiguous.

Not to her. To her it is precise. She knows exactly what she saw and she is showing it to me with total command. But the verb's path crosses through a partial role-shift she does not fully commit, the way fast native signers don't fully commit, because they don't need to, because the face is carrying the rest — and on Darnelle's face, riding under the hands, is a non-manual marker I have ten years of certainty about and zero English keystrokes for: the brows, a particular set of the brows, that I read instantly as *this is what it looked like from where I stood, and where I stood is the truth, and also I am telling you it happened fast.* The hedge and the certainty in the same face. The *from where I stood* and the *it happened fast* braided into one expression that the stenographer cannot type and the jury cannot read and that lives, entire, in the width of a few muscles between Darnelle Okoro's eyebrows.

And I have to render it. Now. The next sentence is already arriving in her hands.

* * *

Here is what twenty-six years built in me, and here is what it built it *for:* to take that — the unresolved verb, the half-committed role-shift, the brow that means two things — and to give the court one clean English sentence it can write down.

I know the sentence. It arrives in my mouth before I authorize it, fully formed, the way they always arrive, the way the gate arrived, the way *he stabbed him* arrived in a different room fifteen years ago in this same building under a different bad light. *The larger man moved toward him first. I saw it clearly.* That is the render. It is fluent. It is grammatical. It is the order a jury can convict in. It resolves the verb's ambiguous path into a committed direction, it irons the half-role-shift into a subject and an object, it takes the brow's two meanings and keeps the one the State needs — *I saw it clearly* — and throws away the other one, the *from where I stood,* the small grammatical honesty that this is testimony from a body in a place and not the voice of God.

And it is not what she said.

It is *near* what she said. It is the cleaner thing, the survivable thing, the thing that costs nothing and that every interpreter alive renders forty times a day. It is the thing I rendered in March, the first time she sat in this box, and the thing I rendered in 1999 about a man's hand in a press, and the thing I rendered in 2003 about whether a mother was afraid or dangerous, where a child slept, and the thing I rendered at fourteen twenty-two —

The lag stretches.

Three seconds. Three and a half. I am holding her sentence in the air and the next one is forming under it and the courtroom does the thing a courtroom does when the interpreter's mouth does not open on schedule, which is that the silence acquires a texture, and heads begin, very slightly, to turn. Hurd's pen lifts. Cantwell, who has been waiting for exactly this since the day my mouth stopped in March, goes still in the predatory way of a lawyer who smells the witness's channel failing. The stenographer's hands hover. Pryor's eyes come off his notes and find me.

Four seconds.

I have the clean sentence in my mouth. All I have to do is what I have always done. All I have to do is be the pane of glass. All I have to do is let the weather pass through me and call it not my weather. The render is *right there,* and it would hold the case, and it would hold my career, and it would hold the last thing I have left that I built, which is the word that follows me down the hallways of this building, the word I swore again this morning at 9:02 with a hole in it.

I look down.

And the dark under me is not a floor. It is a man in a different chair fifteen years ago whose deprived, terrified, half-built signing I took and made *articulate,* made *fluent,* made into a sentence twelve people could put a man away on — not because I lied, but because I was *good,* because clean English came faster than the truth and I had sworn never to soften and never noticed I had never stopped *smoothing,* and the smoothing is the one that built the cell. It is Darnelle in March. It is the verb I committed when the verb did not commit itself. It is forty in a row, all leaning one way, all leaning toward the sentence the room wants, and I am the hand they lean from.

I have one second left before the silence becomes damage.

* * *

I do the thing no interpreter is supposed to do.

I drop my hands — Darnelle's sightline first, I will not blind her, I angle so she keeps my face — and I do not voice the render. I turn a half-degree to the bench, and I speak, on the record, in the first person, which an interpreter is never the first person, an interpreter is a wire, an interpreter is a costume worn over an absence, and I say:

"Your Honor, the interpreter needs to address the court."

It comes out level. I have spent a lifetime keeping my voice level; it is half of what I am paid for; it does not fail me even now, even doing this. The levelness is the last competent thing I will do in this building and I spend it on my own destruction.

Pryor's face does not move much. He is too good for that. But something happens behind it, a recalculation, a man who has sat on this bench and across this well for thirty years and knows, instantly, that this is not a request for a water break.

"The interpreter will state her need," he says. The baritone, deployed. The room leans in.

"The witness's last answer," I say, "contains a construction I cannot faithfully render into a single English sentence." I hear the word *faithfully* leave my mouth and I feel it turn over in the air, the oath inside out, the vow declaring its own impossibility. "The verb she used encodes direction — which person moved toward which — and the direction is, in the source, not fully resolved. There is also a grammatical marker on her face that qualifies the certainty of the statement, and that marker has no equivalent in spoken English. Any single sentence I give the court will resolve an ambiguity that the witness did not resolve. It will be *cleaner* than what she said. I am telling the court that the clean version would be mine, not hers."

The silence after that is a different silence than the one before.

"I would ask," I say, into it, "that the court permit a repetition, and that a Certified Deaf Interpreter be brought in to work in tandem, so that the witness's actual construction — and not my rendering of it — reaches the record. I cannot, by myself, give you what she said. I can only give you what I would make of it."

* * *

I have, in the saying, done several things at once, and I feel each of them land like separate stones.

I have told twelve jurors that the woman they have been instructed to receive the deaf girl *through* is unreliable. I have told them the channel has a hand on it. I have, in front of Damon Webb's lawyer, handed her the gift of a State witness whose testimony was, by the State's own interpreter's sworn admission, *authored* — and I watch Cantwell's face do the math, watch her decide whether to move for a mistrial now or save it, watch the whole afternoon become, for her, a door she did not expect to find open.

I have told Hurd, who is decent and out of his depth and who needed this morning to be easy, that his case just acquired a wound he cannot suture, because the wound is the witness's voice itself.

And I have told Gerald Pryor — the judge, the candidate, the man with the cup, the man who fifteen years ago stood where Hurd is standing and called a different deaf defendant *evasive* to twelve people and won, the man whose career is a conviction built on a render exactly like the one I just refused to make — that an instrument can refuse to launder. On the record. In his courtroom. While, two weeks out, a motion sits in this building arguing that the conviction his name is built on rests on a render that was never faithful, and the woman making that argument's case is *me,* and I have just stood up in his own room and demonstrated, live, in front of a jury and a court reporter and the press of his own future, the precise thing his career cannot survive being true.

He knows. I watch him know it. I watch him understand that I am not having a crisis of nerve. I watch him understand that this is worse — that it is a crisis of *belief,* and that I am going to be useful to Lockwood in exactly the measure that I am dangerous to him.

"Approach," Pryor says. Quiet. The baritone gone conversational, which is the most dangerous register he has.

* * *

What happens at sidebar is on the record and off the jury's hearing, and I am not part of it and I am the entire subject of it, which is a thing I have done to other people my whole life and have never once had done to me. Hurd and Cantwell go up. I stand at my position, holding nothing, three seconds behind a conversation I am not in, and for the first time in twenty-six years I am the thing in the room being talked *about* instead of the thing the room is being heard *through.*

I catch pieces. Cantwell's word *mistrial,* offered and not yet pressed, a card she is showing the bench to watch its face. Hurd's word *certified* — *she is a certified interpreter, Your Honor, requested by name, there has never been* — and the sentence dies there because there is no end to it that helps him. Pryor's voice, low, the words *the integrity of these proceedings,* and *the court cannot have its interpreter,* and a thing I do not fully catch but that has the shape of *if every render can be challenged, then no deaf witness can ever* — and there it is, the knot, the whole knot, surfacing at a sidebar in *Webb* two weeks before it will surface at a hearing in *Rein,* the question that has no floor under it: if I am unreliable, then so is the instrument, and if the instrument is unreliable, then the Deaf cannot be tried *or* believed, and I have not freed anyone, I have unmade the room.

Darnelle is watching me from the box. Not Cantwell, not the bench. Me. Her hands are still in her lap. And she does a small thing, a private thing, not for the record, the way Lamar did across the empty well in March when he told me I was fast — she lifts one hand a few inches and makes, slowly, deliberately, so I cannot mistake it, a sign I know, the sign her people gave me without my knowing, the GLASS held up flat at her own eyes like a mask, the pane you look through, the thing that tells itself it isn't there. Irene's name-sign for me. Somehow Darnelle has it. Of course she has it; it is a small world, two handshapes wide; of course they passed it to her.

And then she lets the glass fall, the hand dropping open, and makes a different shape, her own, unmistakable: a flat hand, fingers together — a hand, just a hand — and she touches her own chest with it once.

*You're not glass,* she is telling me, the way her whole community has been telling me, the way Tobias told me through bolted steel and Irene told me across her airport living room. *You never were. There's the hand. There you are.*

I have spent thirty years building a self out of the conviction that I was the one thing in the room with no fingerprints. And a twenty-six-year-old witness whose certainty I almost authored away is sitting in the box correcting my grammar.

I am not glass. I am a hand. I have always been a hand. I say it to myself in the present tense for the first time, and the present tense is the worst part, because it means it was never past, it means every render I ever made was a hand and not a window, it means the cell I helped build fifteen years ago was built by a hand that has my knuckles.

* * *

Pryor recesses at two-forty.

He does it cleanly — *we'll take fifteen minutes,* the jury filed out, no expression he doesn't choose — but I have read faces under bad light for a quarter century and I read his as the door swings, and what is in it is not anger. Anger I could survive. What is in it is the cold, total attention of a man recalculating a campaign, a record, a conviction, a future, all at once, against an instrument that just announced, in open court, that it cannot be trusted to do the one thing the entire machine requires it to do.

The interpreter-scheduling office will get a call before I reach the elevator. I know how this building works; I helped it work this way. By tonight there will be a sentence somewhere — an email, a note in a file, a word said at a desk two floors down where Pam loses every budget fight she fights — and the sentence will not say *Ruth Okafor-Vance refused to launder a render.* It will say something procedural and deniable and clean. It will say *concerns regarding reliability.* It will say *pending review.* It will be a faithful render of my ruin that keeps every word that can be typed and not one of the words that are true.

I gather my water and my legal pad on which I have written nothing, because I never write anything, because writing would mean I'd looked away, and I have not looked away, I have looked *down,* and there is a difference, and the difference is the rest of my life.

In the marble hall, in the cold institutional light and the floor-wax and the holding-floor smell that lives under everything in this building, my phone is already lit. Lockwood. Three words, and I read them standing where, fifteen years and twenty-six years and one Wednesday afternoon ago, I was the best in this building at the one thing I do.

*What did you do.*

I do not know yet if it is a question or a verdict. I do not know yet whether I have just become his exhibit or destroyed his case before it is filed. I only know that two weeks from now there is a courtroom that will ask me, under oath, to faithfully interpret — and that I have just proven, in public, in the courtroom of the man who most needs it to be a lie, that I cannot. That no one can.

I raise my hand to type back, and for a moment — one moment, three seconds, the lag I have lived in my whole life — I hold it there above the glass and do not move it, because I no longer know what a true answer looks like, and I have only ever known how to give the clean one.


# Chapter Twenty-Three — Unhireable

The word arrives by email at 6:41 the next morning, and it is not even addressed to me.

I am cc'd. That is the part I keep going back to, later, when I am trying to be precise about the exact shape of the thing, the way I have always been precise about exact shapes — I am a *carbon copy.* The Office of Court Interpreter Services, Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas, writes to a distribution list I have been on for twenty-two years, the per-diem roster, every certified hand the building keeps in its phone, and somewhere in the body of it, in the flat gray font of a thing nobody reads, is a sentence about a "scheduling adjustment" to the assignments calendar, "effective immediately, pending review," and my name is in the column under *reassigned,* and the man taking *State v. Webb* off my hands going forward is named in the column under *to,* and I read the *to* before I read anything else because that is the kind of woman I am, even now, even at 6:41 with the radiator knocking and the lake doing its flat colorless nothing at the glass.

The *to* is Gareth Pell.

I have to sit with that. I make myself a coffee I do not drink and I sit with Gareth Pell.

Pell is fine. I want to be fair, and I find that I cannot quite be fair, and I want that on the record too, because a woman who is only ruined and noble is a woman lying to you. Pell is *fine.* He is competent, he is fifty-eight, he came up two years behind me and has spent the intervening quarter-century being good enough — good enough is a real thing, it keeps a courtroom moving, it does not get a witness killed on an ordinary Tuesday — and he rounds. He summarizes. He gives a court *the gist.* I have watched Gareth Pell take a witness's whole spatial account of a parking lot, the cars set in the air, the swerve, the angle of the impact built clean in the space in front of the man's chest, and render it *the vehicles collided,* and sit down, and feel he had done his job, because in the room the system requires he had. For twenty-six years I have kept a private contempt for Gareth Pell folded in my chest like a clean handkerchief, the contempt of the surgeon for the man who calls himself a surgeon and means *I cut things,* and I have taken a small ugly pleasure in being the one the lawyers asked for by name while Pell got the arraignments and the bond hearings and the assignments nobody fought over.

And now Pell is hireable, and I am not, and the difference between us — the only difference that has ever mattered to me, the difference my whole life is built on — is that Pell would have rendered the ambiguous construction clean and sat down, and I stood up in the middle of it and told the court I could not.

I keep score. I have always kept score. I keep score at 6:41 in the morning over a coffee I will pour out, and the score reads: *the man who rounds is in my chair, and I am a carbon copy.*

* * *

Lockwood calls at nine. He does not say *how are you holding up,* which is one of the reasons I can stand him.

"They've moved you off Webb," he says.

"I'm aware. I'm cc'd on my own removal. It's very modern."

"Continuance," he says, ignoring the joke the way you ignore a tic in someone you've decided to keep. "Cantwell filed last night, the second you sat down. Manifest necessity, the integrity of the interpreted record, the whole thing. Pryor took it under advisement. He could declare a mistrial. He could continue and re-empanel with a new interpreter and let the testimony stand. He could do a lot of things." A pause, the careful one, the one he uses when the careful version is the true one. "What he's actually going to do is whatever keeps the conviction he's running on the furthest from the word *interpreter.* So. Probably a continuance, a new interpreter, the record cleaned up, the jury reminded that the matter is technical and that they should disregard the interpreter's, ah — episode."

*Episode.* I let the word sit in the cold of my kitchen. Yesterday afternoon I stood in Courtroom 4B and broke open the fourth wall of my own neutrality — held the sentence in the air past the three seconds, past four, past the place where a held render becomes a kind of drowning, and then I did the thing no interpreter is supposed to do, I did not commit and I did not round, I turned to the bench and I said *the interpreter cannot faithfully render this construction; it is ambiguous in the source; the court needs the witness to repeat, or it needs a Deaf interpreter, or it needs to be told it cannot be certain* — and the room had looked at me the way a room looks at a piano that has begun to speak. And now it is an *episode.* The system has a word for the instrument that refuses to launder, and the word is the word you use for a fainting spell, a small medical event, a thing that passes.

"Ruth." Lockwood, on the line, reading my silence the way he reads faces. "It was the right call. For Webb."

"I know it was the right call for Webb." I do know. That is not the part that is hard. Damon Webb is thirty-three and a new interpreter will come in clean and the construction will get repeated and read properly because I made the room stop, and that is a man's liberty I did not let my hands quietly decide on an ordinary afternoon, and I would do it again. "I'm not asking whether it was right for Webb."

"What are you asking whether it was right for."

I don't answer, because the honest answer is *me,* and I am not ready to look at the honest answer, and Desmond Lockwood, who is too tired to lie and too good to let me, lets the silence run a beat longer than is comfortable, and then he says the thing he called to say.

"The board's going to convene on it."

The board. The state certification board. The body that gave me, twenty-six years ago, the credential that is the whole architecture of my name. I feel the floor of the morning do the small wrong thing the floor does.

"Somebody complained," I say.

"Somebody always complains. Pell, maybe. The ADA. Doesn't matter who." His voice is even. "An interpreter who stands up in open court and announces she can't render — that's a competence question, technically. That's exactly the box the form has. *Did the certified interpreter fail to render the proceedings?* And the certified interpreter is going to be asked to answer that under oath, in a room, in front of people who certify hands for a living, and the true answer — the answer you'd actually give, the answer you've been building toward since you watched 14:22 — is *yes.* Yes, I failed to render. Because there was no render. Because there's no such thing." He lets it land. "You understand what that means. You go in front of that board and tell the truth, and they don't hear an interpreter who got brave. They hear an interpreter who decertified herself out loud. You'll never be sworn in this county again. Probably not in the state. The thing your whole name is — *requested by name* — it ends in that room, and it ends in your own voice."

I stand in my kitchen with the lake going gray and I find that I am not surprised, and I find that the not-being-surprised is its own kind of vertigo, because I have spent thirty years organizing my entire self around being the one trustworthy thing in a room of liars, and the trustworthy thing is about to testify, under oath, that it cannot be trusted — and the worst part, the part of me that reaches to wall it off the second I feel it, is the small clean voice that says *good. Now you'll be clean. You'll have paid.*

"Ruth?"

"I heard you," I say. "Unhireable. You can say the word, Desmond. I've been cc'd on worse this morning."

"Unhireable," he says, because he is not a man who softens a thing to spare you, and because — I think, later — he knows that *I* am, that softening is my original sin, and that he will not commit my sin at me even as a kindness. "That's the word. After thirty years. That's where this goes."

* * *

Here is the thing I do then, and I am not proud of it, and I am putting it on the record anyway, because the whole book is a record and I have learned this month what it costs to keep the ugly thing off one.

I go to see Tobias to be told that I am good now.

I do not phrase it that way to myself. I phrase it as *he should hear it from me, that I broke a render in his prosecutor's courtroom, that I am about to lose the credential, that I am paying.* I phrase it as *due diligence.* I drive the hour southwest into the county that grows soybeans and razor wire with the radio off, and I tell myself the whole way down that I am going to Grafton to inform a client of a development in his case, and somewhere under that, in the place where the true reasons live, where my hands learned to listen before I could reliably spell, I am driving down to a bolted table to lay my ruin in front of a dying man and have him look up from it and tell me it counts.

The contact room is the color of a filing cabinet. Tobias comes in thinner than a week ago — I did not think he could get thinner; he is older than forty-six the way a building is older if no one fixes the roof, and the roof is not being fixed — and the left hand has the tremor in it still, the fine continuous one that nobody at Grafton has looked at, that ticks while he signs like a second guard standing in the corner of the room keeping its own time. He sits on the bolted stool across the bolted table and he reads my face before I have gotten one sign up, because he has been read his whole life and learned to read back, learned it as a survival, and what he reads makes the corner of his mouth do something I have learned to be afraid of.

*You came down here to show me something,* he signs.

His ASL is the thing it has always been, the thing that is the whole injury of his life — fluent-seeming and then a hole, a place where a sign was never put in him, footbridged across with fingerspelling or an improvised shape or a borrowing, the language of a man whose hands were held down so he would use his mouth. I read him the way I have learned to read him, which is to say worse than Delia reads him, worse than the people who loved him should have let anyone have to read him, and I render him in my own head clean, the way I render everything clean, and I catch myself doing it — *catch myself,* the verb has teeth now — and I make myself sit in the gaps instead of bridging them.

I tell him. I sign it plainly, no rim on it. *I broke a render in court. On the record. In front of Pryor.* I sign Pryor's name with the name-sign the courthouse Deaf gave him years ago, the one that is not kind. *I told the court I could not faithfully interpret. The board is going to review me. I will probably lose my certification.* And then I stop, and I do not say the next thing, the *and I did it because of you, because of 14:22, because I will not be that woman again,* I do not say it, but it is in my hands anyway, in the way a thing you do not sign is still in the air if your face is on, and Meg taught me the face is where the grammar lives, and Tobias reads my face.

He looks at me for a long moment. The tremor ticks. And then he laughs — not out loud, the body-laugh, the shoulders, the air pushed out the nose, the laugh of a man who has just watched someone walk into a wall he could see from across the room — and he signs, and his hands are suddenly very economical, very flat, the way Lamar's go flat when he is about to put a knife in cleanly, except this is not Lamar's craftsman's flatness, this is the flatness of a man who has been at the bottom of this exact pool for fifteen years watching hearing people splash around at the top and call it drowning.

*You drove an hour,* he signs, *to tell me you fell on a sword.*

I start to answer. He does not let me have it; he keeps going, and in Deaf space, on Deaf terms, I am the one who is a beat behind, the one who does not own the frame, and he owns it completely.

*Let me tell you what you actually came for.* He sets it up in the air between us — sets *me* in the space to his right, a locus, Ruth, the woman across the table, and he points at the locus, and then he builds the thing I came for and hangs it off the point of his finger so I cannot look away from it. *You came so I would do the thing.* His face does the thing his face does, the savage amusement, the contempt held on a leash and then, for one sign, let off it. *Look at her. She broke herself for me. Now she's clean.* He role-shifts — turns his shoulders a few degrees, becomes *me,* becomes a version of me, hands folded, face arranged into something I recognize with a lurch as my own courtroom stillness, the stillness I am paid for — and the cruelty of seeing myself rendered by him, accurately, the way I have rendered a hundred Deaf people accurately and sent them to be convicted by my accuracy, is a thing I do not have a word for in either language. He snaps back into himself. *That's what you came for. Not to tell me a fact about my case. To stand where I could see you bleed.*

*That's not—*

*It is.* Flat. Final. The tremor ticking. *And here is the part you don't want.* He leans in over the bolted table, and the fluorescent light is in the gray of his face, and he signs the thing slowly, the way Meg made me make the R-but slowly when I was twelve so I would learn the shape of myself, so the shape would go in and stay in:

*You think falling on the sword is the end of the debt. It's not the end. It's the first day.* He holds the sign for *first* — the thumb, the one finger, the small specific gesture — and he holds it a beat too long, the way you hold a thing you need to land. *You broke a render. Good. Welcome. Now you have to live every day for the rest of your life knowing your hands decide, and deciding anyway, out loud, where everyone can see, and never once getting to be the glass again. That's not a punishment that ends. That's a person.* His hands open, palms up, the gesture that is not a sign, the human one, the one that means *this is what I have, this is all of it.* *I've been that person since before you put me in a cage with your clean English. You don't get to do it for a week and call it square.*

And then he sits back, and the tremor ticks, and he signs the last of it almost gently, which is the worst, because Tobias Rein is not a gentle man and the gentleness costs him something and he spends it on me anyway, the way Lockwood spent his honesty, the way the people who can least afford to be kind to me keep being the ones who are:

*You wanted me to be the priest.* A small flat movement. *I'm not the priest, Ruth. I'm the guy you ran over. I can tell you it hurts. I can't tell you you're forgiven for it. That's not mine to give and you knew that when you got in the car.*

I do not have a render for it. I am the best court interpreter in Cuyahoga County and I sit at a bolted table in Grafton with my hands empty and I do not have a render for a dying man telling me, accurately, that I came to him for absolution and that absolution is not a thing he has, was ever given, will ever have to give. The thing I have spent my whole life doing — carrying a meaning across without dropping it — fails me completely, because there is nowhere to carry this to. It is already here. It is already mine.

* * *

I sit in the Grafton parking lot for a long time with the engine off, in the flat county light, soybeans on three sides and the wire on the fourth, and I do the arithmetic I drove an hour to avoid doing.

I came for the clean-hands story. I have been telling myself a story since the parking structure two nights ago, since the kitchen table, since Irene's porch — the story where if I bleed enough, author the gap loudly enough, immolate the credential publicly enough, I get to the other side of it and the other side is *clean.* Paid. Square. A guilt confessed and a guilt confessed is a guilt *closed,* and I am very good at closing things, I have made a thirty-year cathedral out of being good at closing things, *the render is faithful, the case is over, stack something heavy on the box.*

And Tobias, who has spent fifteen years being authored by other people's hands, who knows the difference between what is devastating for the case and what is devastating for the person and has never once gotten to confuse them, looked at me across a bolted table and told me there is no other side. There is no square. *It's the first day.* The thing I keep reaching for — the rim I can put on it, the sentence small enough to live with, the *I broke a render, therefore I am good now* — is the same move I have been making my whole life, the same move I made at nineteen in a hospital room, the same move I made at 14:22, the smoothing, the rounding, the clean fluent English laid over a thing that does not have a clean shape. I am trying to render my own ruin faithfully and there is no faithful render. There is just the gap, and me in it, and the rest of a life of standing in it with no costume on.

I sit with that. And then I notice the shape my clean-hands story has actually been: a confession sized to fit one hand. If the whole load comes down on mine — if I authored him alone, decertify myself alone, pay alone — then Pryor's baritone is innocent and the dropped CDI was an accident and the shredder is just space. Then I get to be the sinner so that everyone else gets to be the weather. I hear Lockwood at the cold window, *you never knew what was in the room,* and I see Irene's three fingers in the air in her wet-sidewalk living room, and I understand that the sole-author story and the clean-hands story are the same story, the same smoothing, run in opposite directions — both of them a render of the truth cleaner than the truth, because mine is a thing I can hold and the rest of it is a thing I cannot.

I take out my phone, in the Grafton lot, with the wire on one side and the soybeans on the other, and I call Lockwood, and when he picks up I do not say hello.

"I'll do it," I say. "The stand. The hearing. Put me up."

A silence. He is careful, even now, even getting what he has wanted since he stood outside 4B with a yellowed tape. "You understand what it costs. The board, the certification, the —"

"I'm not doing it to be clean." The words come out of me in the flat county light and I hear them arrive in the air the way a render arrives, irreversible, a thing said that cannot be unsaid, and for once in my life I am not afraid of the irreversible, I am choosing it. "That's the part I had wrong. I thought I'd get up there and confess and that would close it, that I'd author the gap loud enough to buy my way to clean hands." I watch a guard cross the far lot, small, ordinary, a man going to his car. "There are no clean hands. There was never glass. I'm a hand, Desmond.
