I've been a hand the whole time, and I authored a man into a cage with the cleanest English of my life, and I don't get forgiven for it, and that's not the reason to get up there."

"What's the reason."

"The reason is the record." And I feel the word turn over in my mouth, the word I have worshipped for twenty-six years, *let the record reflect,* the transcript that became the truth because there was no way to check it against the hands. "Delia objected in that room in 2009 and the room couldn't read her. Irene watched it and the court believed me over the Deaf people in front of it. The CDI got carried off to save a per-diem and the proof of it is in a cubic foot of paper in a queue for a shredder. None of that is *in* anything, Desmond. It's in a seam. It's in an invoice and a re-code and an old woman's memory and a tape nobody can read but me — and the only way any of it gets into the record, the actual record, the one that becomes the truth, is if somebody who was *in the room,* somebody the State will swear was certified and faithful and unimpeachable, gets up under oath and puts it there." I close my eyes in the gray light. "I'm the only one who can enter it. Not because I'm good. Because I'm the one who was holding the pen. The exhibit you've been telling me I am — fine. I'll be the exhibit. But I'll be an exhibit that talks."

There is a long pause, and then Desmond Lockwood, who deals in cases and not in comfort, who has carried more wrongful convictions than a person can carry and bought no new coat since the recession, says a thing that is not comfort and is the closest he comes:

"That's the first thing you've said about this case that wasn't about you."

"I know," I say. "It took me a while."

* * *

I drive back toward the city in the going-gray afternoon, the lake somewhere off to my right doing the flat thing it does before summer remembers it, and I let the size of what I have just agreed to arrange itself in the three-second country where I live, the gap between a thing completing and the next thing beginning.

The hearing is eleven days out. Adeyemi, Monday, nine a.m. Eleven days, and the proof is barely secured — Delia found, Delia's own retained copy in a folder in a house I have now sat in, but the county's file, the official seam, still in a box in a queue, the contractor "rolling," *could be three weeks, could be Monday,* and the unsealing motion heard the same week as the hearing and opposed by reasonable lawyers who will work for Pryor in November. Eleven days, and a man dying in Grafton with a tremor nobody has looked at, the medical clock and the moral one running together, *will he be free in time to be free at all.*

And somewhere in the city ahead of me, in a county machine I helped build and can no longer be sworn into, the tape is waiting on a server with my hands on it, cued to 14:19, the place I have promised myself for a month holds the answer — the clean wrong thing I rendered as guilt, the verb I can finally make face the other way, the coin I have been saving to mint into a man's freedom. Once the hearing is underway, in the days between its days, we will sit down with it, the four of us, the best readers of him alive, and run it frame by frame.

I am the best in the building. I have read that tape a hundred times. I drive north toward it with no costume on, the credential dissolving behind me and the clean hands I built a life on already in pieces on the floor of Courtroom 4B, certain I am driving toward the answer.

I do not let myself wonder what I will do if the frames do not have one.


# Chapter Twenty-Four — The Hearing

The post-conviction hearing convenes at nine on a Monday in a courtroom on the seventh floor that I have worked in maybe forty times, and the first thing I do, the thing I cannot stop myself doing, is read it.

I read rooms the way other people read faces. Courtroom 7C is not 4B; the light is better, the windows give onto the lake instead of the parking deck, and somebody this century repainted the gallery rail. But it is the same machine in a cleaner housing, and I stand in the well at eight-fifty with my hands empty — I am not the interpreter today, there is a per-diem named Okafor on the schedule who is not me, a courtesy interpreter Lockwood arranged for Tobias because the county would have sent whoever was free and I will not let whoever-is-free near him again — and I take the inventory I have taken four thousand times. Where the bench sits. Where the witness will sit. The arc of sightline a Deaf man in that chair will have, and the two seats in the second row that ruin it, and the bailiff who is going to stand exactly where Tobias cannot see around him unless someone tells the bailiff to move, and nobody will tell the bailiff to move, because the room is built for people who hear, and the room does not know it is built for anything at all.

Judge Adeyemi takes the bench at 9:02. She is not Pryor. I want that on the record of my own head, because for fifteen years the only judge in this story was the one who is not in this room — recused, elevated, two floors and one election cycle away, his name on a cup and a banner and a conviction he is running on — and the absence of him is its own kind of presence, a chair pulled back from a table where no one sits. Adeyemi is sixty, unhurried, has a reputation for reading the briefs and a reputation for not suffering theater, and she looks out at a gallery thinner than a trial gallery and fuller than a motion docket usually draws, and she lets the silence sit one beat longer than the clerk expects, which tells me she has read the briefs.

Tobias is brought in at 9:06.

I have been bracing for it since the parking lot and the bracing does nothing. He is in the county's tan, which hangs off him the way the uniform hung off him in Grafton, except now I am seeing it in the better light and the better light is a cruelty — it shows me the gray under the brown of him, the way the skin has gone slack at the wrists, the left hand riding the air with its fine continuous tremor, the second guard keeping its own time. He is forty-six and the room reads him as old and the room is not wrong, he is old, he is a building no one fixed. They seat him at the defense table next to Lockwood and he does not look at Lockwood. He finds the sightlines first — he reads the room before he reads any person in it, the way I do, the way the unheard learn to — and then he finds the gallery, and his eyes go to the second row, and Irene is there.

She came. I did not know she would come. *I'm watching you, not helping you,* she signed at me in her living room with a hymn going soundless on the wall, and here she is in the second row exactly where she sat for nine days in 2009 so that one man in a hostile room would have one face he could read, and she is doing it again, and the doing of it is not for me. Delia is three seats down from her, in a slate cardigan, her reading glasses pushed up into the iron-gray hair, her hands folded in her lap the way you fold your hands when you have decided not to perform anything for anyone. Bee is at the rail with a laptop she is not allowed to open yet. Four of the best readers of Tobias Rein in the world are in this room, and not one of them is going to be permitted to read him aloud, because the room has one channel and the channel is sworn, and the channel today is a stranger named Okafor.

Tobias's eyes finish their circuit and land on me.

I have rehearsed nothing for this and there is nothing to rehearse. He does not sign. His hands stay on the table, the left one ticking. He just looks at me across the well with the savage tired intelligence that has been reading me since the first jail visit, reading me better than I read him, and I make myself not look away, because looking away is the thing I have always been allowed to do and I am done being allowed it. Whatever passes between us is not forgiveness and is not absolution and is not even, quite, recognition. It is closer to a man checking the gauge on a machine he is about to depend on and has every reason to believe will fail him. *Welcome,* the look says, the way he said it at the bolted table. *Now you have to live in it.*

* * *

Donovan opens for the State, and she is good, and I want to be exact about how good, because the easiest lie I could tell you is that the woman defending this conviction is a villain.

Kate Donovan is thirty-eight and she stands without notes and she does not raise her voice, and she gives Adeyemi a frame so clean and so reasonable that I feel the gallery — the hearing half of it — settle into it like a coat that fits.

"Your Honor, this is a motion to vacate a fifteen-year-old murder conviction," she says. "The petitioner was tried. He was represented. He was provided a certified sign-language interpreter — a nationally certified interpreter, in fact, the same interpreter, I'll note, that both the State and the defense in this county have requested by name for a quarter of a century." She does not look at me. She does not have to; the room does the looking. "A jury heard the evidence and convicted. The conviction was affirmed on direct appeal. And now, fifteen years later, the petitioner asks this Court to set it all aside — not on new evidence of innocence, Your Honor. There is no such evidence. He asks the Court to set it aside on the theory that the *interpretation* was imperfect."

She lets *imperfect* sit there, a small word, a reasonable word.

"Every interpretation is imperfect. Every translation between any two languages is imperfect. That is not a constitutional defect; it is the nature of language. If this Court vacates a conviction because a render between ASL and English was not a mathematical identity — if *that* is the standard — then I would respectfully suggest to the Court that no Deaf defendant has ever received a fair trial in the history of this state, and none ever can, and every conviction obtained through an interpreter, in every courtroom, is void." A beat. "The petitioner is not asking you to free one man. He is asking you to declare that the courthouse cannot hear. The State asks the Court to decline that invitation, and to honor the finality that fifteen years and a jury's verdict have earned."

She sits.

It is the knot. It is Donovan's whole case in ninety seconds, and the worst thing about it, the thing that puts a cold hand flat against my sternum, is that the first half of it is true. Every render *is* imperfect. I have spent a month proving exactly that to myself with a jog-wheel and a storage unit and a dying tape, and here is the State picking up my own discovery and turning the blade around: *fine, the render was authored — they all are — and if authorship is your standard then you have not exonerated a man, you have unmade the institution, and a court will not unmake itself to free one defendant it has every procedural reason to keep convicted.* I sat in 4B two weeks ago and I said a version of this knot out loud to a bench, in the first person, and I felt the room look at me like a piano that had begun to speak, and now Kate Donovan has said it better than I did and she has said it on the side of keeping a dying man in a cage, and she is not wrong about the logic, and the logic is the trap, and the trap is the thesis, and I cannot answer it. Lockwood cannot answer it. No one in this room can answer it, because the book the law is written in does not contain the page.

* * *

Lockwood opens, and he does the thing he told me he would do, which is not to answer the knot — you cannot answer it, not yet — but to make the hearing about a smaller, harder, more concrete thing the State would much rather keep abstract.

"The State says interpretation is imperfect," Lockwood says. He is rumpled, he is tired, he has not bought a new coat since the recession and he is wearing the recession coat to the most important hearing of his year. "Agreed. The State wants the Court to believe this case is about an *imperfect render* — a normal, unavoidable, blameless imprecision, the kind that happens in every trial. It is not. This case is about a render that the State *knew* was inadequate, and economized away the safeguard against, and then characterized to a jury as the truth."

He walks to the easel. He does not have showmanship in him; he has facts and a tired man's stubbornness, which in this room reads as more honest than showmanship, which is its own kind of technique and I notice him use it.

"The petitioner is a language-deprived, late-identified Deaf adult. That is not an insult; it is a clinical and linguistic fact, and the State does not dispute it. His native language was taken from him as a child. His ASL is real but non-standard — built, the experts will testify, without full access. For exactly this kind of defendant, the law and the standards of the profession require a Certified Deaf Interpreter — a Deaf professional who relays between a deprived signer and a hearing interpreter, in tandem, because a hearing interpreter alone, however gifted, will render him *fluently* and therefore *wrongly.*" He turns. "In 2009 this Court provided that CDI. She was present day one. And on the afternoon of day two, to save a per-diem, the county *re-coded* her off this man's murder trial and onto a cheaper matter two floors down — and let a single hearing interpreter carry the rest of a capital case alone. We will prove that with the county's own records. We will prove it with the interpreter-services invoice. We will prove it with the testimony of the CDI herself, who is in this courtroom."

Donovan is up before he finishes. "Objection. Foundation, and frankly relevance. Counsel is testifying. Whether a second interpreter was scheduled and rescheduled is a budgetary detail; it is not a structural defect, and a fifteen-year-old scheduling record—"

"The scheduling record," Lockwood says, "which the county is in the process of feeding into a shredder as we speak, Your Honor, under a routine consolidation that happens to be finishing the same week as this hearing—"

"Counsel."

"—and which we have obtained anyway."

"*Counsel.*" Adeyemi does not raise her voice either; this is a quiet room full of people fighting for their lives without raising their voices. "Mr. Lockwood, you'll have your witnesses and your exhibits, and Ms. Donovan will have her objections to each of them, and I will rule on each of them, in order. This is an opening, not an offer of proof. Sit down. Both of you, lower the temperature. We have all week."

We have all week. I write it on the inside of my skull where I write the things that matter. The hearing is not a moment; it is a siege. And every document Lockwood just promised — the invoice, the re-code, Delia's carbon — is going to have to come in over a Donovan objection that is, every time, *reasonable*: a carbon is not an original; a fifteen-year-old recollection is not reliable; a budget code is not a constitutional violation. She is going to make the room fight for every inch of the seam, and some of the inches she is going to win, and the truth is going to have to be assembled in front of a judge out of fragments that a careful, intelligent, decent lawyer is contesting one at a time, because that is what the truth always was in this story — a thing in a seam, surviving in the margins of a record built to lose it.

And underneath all of it, unspoken, in nobody's opening, is the largest fact in the room: that to actually break the conviction, Lockwood is going to have to put me in that witness chair. Delia's carbon is foundation. The dying tape is foundation. The re-code is foundation. But foundation is not the building. The only person who can stand up under oath and say *the render the jury convicted on was not what the hands said, and I know because they are my hands* — is me. The State requested me by name for twenty-six years. The defense is about to call me to testify that the woman they requested by name cannot be trusted. I am not a witness to this hearing. I am the exhibit, and the exhibit is going to have to talk, and Donovan is sharpening the cross that turns my own competence into the reason no one should believe a word of it.

* * *

The day grinds. Lockwood puts on a linguistics expert who explains topicalization and directional verbs and classifiers to a court reporter who cannot type a single one of them, and Donovan cross-examines him into conceding, accurately, that ambiguity is not the same as error and that experts disagree, and I sit in the gallery behind the defense table and watch the séance assemble itself in plain daylight.

That is the word that has been arriving in me for weeks and I have kept it at arm's length because it can go purple and because Irene gave it to me as a lived thing and not a thesis, and I will not turn it into a thesis now. But I cannot not see it. Here is a room. It has convened, with great seriousness, with sworn officers and a stenographer and the full weight of the state, to determine what a man said. And the man is sitting twelve feet from me, alive, with hands, and the room will not receive what he says through those hands; the room will only receive it through a sworn medium, and the medium in 2009 was me, and the medium today is a stranger named Okafor, and the four people in this room who can read him truest are seated in a gallery behind a rail with their hands in their laps because the well only runs one way. We have gathered to hear a man we have arranged not to hear. We have built a beautiful machine for asking a question and a second machine, bolted to the first, for making sure the answer can only come back in a language that cannot carry it. I watch Tobias watch the expert's hands and I watch his face do the thing his face does, the contempt held on its leash, and I think: he has sat through this before. He sat through nine days of this. He has spent his whole life in rooms convened to hear him through someone else's mouth, and he has never once heard his own account arrive.

He catches me watching. He signs, low, fast, just for me, his hands barely leaving the table so the bailiff won't startle — *they're doing it again* — and there is no bitterness in it, which is the worst part, there is only the flat recognition of a man identifying a process he knows better than anyone in the room. I want to sign back *I know,* and I cannot, because I am furniture in this room too now, a witness who has not been called, and the well runs one way and today it does not run through me. So I do the only thing the rules of the room leave me: I keep my eyes on his hands a beat longer than I keep them on anyone's, the way you hold a door you are not allowed to open, and I let him see that the holding is deliberate.

* * *

The cost, when it comes for me, does not come in the courtroom.

It comes at the recess, in the marble corridor, when I step out for water I will not drink and the lake light is coming flat through the tall windows and a woman my age goes by pushing a man in a wheelchair, an old Black man with a blanket over his knees, and she is signing down to him as she walks, one-handed, easy, *almost there, almost there, I've got you,* and it opens in me without permission, the way it has been threatening to all month, and I am nineteen.

I was nineteen and the room was on the fourth floor and Meg was propped at thirty degrees so her ruined lungs could work and so she could see the door, and the second event the doctor had warned me about came in the night between the fifth day and the sixth, the way he had said it might — *probably,* he had signed, through me, and I had given her *possible* — and it took her the way he had said it would, fast, while a treatment she had declined sat unused in a chart because I had built her a world in which declining it was the reasonable thing to do.

That is the cost. I have carried it for thirty years and I have never once said it plainly, even to myself, because saying it plainly is a thing I do not do, because plainness is the one fidelity I could not bear from my own hands: *Meg died because I softened it.* The doctor told the truth and I rounded it and on the rounded truth she made a choice, the one choice the stroke had left her able to make cleanly, and the choice was no, take me home, I want my own bed and my own window and you — and I rendered that *no* with a perfect, ringing fidelity, the one perfectly faithful render of my entire nineteenth year, because by then there was nothing left to soften, the softening had already done its work upstream, and all that was left was to carry her death-sentence to a doctor without dropping a single word of it. She chose to go home. She went home. She died there, in her own bed, three weeks before the violence would have, on the strength of a prognosis I had authored gentle, and I have spent every day since at the same altar building the same cathedral out of the same brick: *never soften again. Render exactly what the hands say. Bear no authorship for what it does.*

I stand in the corridor with the empty water cup and I do not cry, because crying is a thing that happens to the surface and this is happening underneath, where the surface holds while the swimmer drowns. The woman with the wheelchair is gone around the corner. *Almost there.* I never got to say almost there. I said *possible* when the word was *probable,* and a woman who taught me that language is built and that you are responsible for what you build went home to die on a sentence I made smaller so it would hurt her less, and it did hurt her less, for three weeks, and then it hurt her exactly as much as it was always going to, and I was the one who decided, at nineteen, in a hospital room, that I had the right to choose how much of the truth a Deaf woman could stand. I have been deciding it ever since. I decided it at 14:22 with a different woman's life in a different chair. The hand is the same hand. The mercy is the same mercy. I have just never let myself stand in the same hallway with it before.

* * *

When the recess ends I go back in, and I take my seat in the gallery, and Adeyemi gavels the room — a real gavel, a sound, not the soundless one Irene built in the air to show me how an objection dies — and Lockwood rises and says, "Your Honor, the defense calls its next witness," and for one cold second the floor tilts and I think he means me, *now,* and my hands go ice at the ends, and then he names the linguistics expert again for redirect and the floor levels and I breathe, and I understand that this is going to be the shape of the rest of the week: the call I am dreading is coming, and it is not coming yet, and every time Lockwood stands I am going to die a little waiting to hear my own name.

Because that is what we have come to do. Strip away the experts and the invoices and the dying tape, and the whole architecture of this hearing narrows to a single chair and a single woman who will sit in it and swear, under the oath she has sworn four thousand times and meant, to faithfully and accurately tell this court that the most faithful render of her life is the reason a man is in a cage — and then sit there while Kate Donovan, who is not stupid and not cruel and entirely right about the logic, asks her the question that has no answer: *if you cannot be trusted, then who can; and if no render is faithful, then you have not freed this man, you have unmade every room like this one, and a court does not unmake itself.*

I look at Tobias. He is watching Lockwood, and his left hand is ticking on the table, keeping its own time, the medical clock and the legal clock and the thirty-year clock all running together now toward the same Monday, and I think I finally understand the whole shape of what I am carrying up to that chair — the dropped CDI, the silenced Deaf room, the prosecutor with the cup, the seam in the queue, and my own hands at the bottom of all of it, the cleanest English in the building — and I think I am walking toward the answer.

I am the best in this building, and I am about to be wrong about the one thing I am certain of, and I do not know it, because that is the only way you are ever wrong about the thing that matters. I think I am carrying the truth up to that stand to enter it on the record at last. I think the truth is *I rendered him wrong.* I have read that tape a hundred times and the truth is waiting for me at 14:22 like a held sentence, and in four days four of us are going to sit down in Bee's dead-elevator archive and run it frame by frame, the best readers of him alive, and we are going to find out together that the held sentence was never there to be voiced — that his hands, the night a man died, do not say a clean any thing, because the language to say it was taken out of them before the night ever happened — and that the answer is not the one I am rehearsing.

Adeyemi gavels the day closed. The deputy comes for Tobias, and the wheelchair turns, and his left hand keeps ticking against the armrest as they roll him out, the one part of him still telling the truth on its own clock. The gallery empties around me. And when the room is down to the bailiff stacking chairs, I cross the well — not the gallery side, where I have sat for two days, but the other side, the witness side — and I stand at the chair I am going to have to fill on Thursday, and I put one hand flat on its cold wooden back, and I find my sightline from it: the bench, the bailiff who will block it, the second row where Irene will sit reading Tobias and not me. I have set up four thousand witnesses in this chair. I have never once stood where they stand. I make myself stand there until the bailiff asks if I need the room locked, and then I take my hand off the chair and I go.


# Chapter Twenty-Five — The Prosecutor's Choice

The second day of the hearing is the day Lockwood goes looking for the word.

I do not understand, at first, that this is what he is doing. He spends the morning on foundation — the dull, load-bearing labor of getting a fifteen-year-old county invoice admitted over a Donovan objection that contests the chain of every hand the paper ever passed through, and he wins it by inches, and the inches are exhausting, and Adeyemi keeps the temperature low and rules and rules and rules. By the lunch recess we have established, on the record, in a courtroom, that a second interpreter was billed for two days of Tobias Rein's nine-day trial and then was not. That is all. Two line items, certified as business records, sitting in evidence like a stone someone finally lifted out of a wall, and the wall still standing.

I sit in the gallery behind the defense table where I have sat for two days, not the interpreter, not anymore, a per-diem named Okafor signing carefully to a man no one will let speak — and I watch Lockwood, and I think he is building toward Delia. That is the obvious next move. You prove the CDI existed; you put the CDI in the chair. I am wrong about what he is doing, the way I am wrong about most things in this room that are not made of hands, and I will not understand my wrongness until after the lunch he does not eat.

He eats a granola bar at the counsel table while Donovan goes to make calls, and he reads, and what he reads is not the invoice. It is the 2009 trial transcript. The whole brick of it. He has read it before — he has read it a hundred times, it is the thing the appeal is built against — but he is reading it now the way I read a tape, slow, with a pencil, looking for a place where the clean thing on the page is concealing the broken thing that produced it. And at quarter to one he stops, and puts the pencil flat across the page like a man marking a depth, and looks up, and finds me in the gallery, and does the small thing with his chin that means *come here.*

"Read this," he says, and turns the transcript around so the page faces me, and puts his finger under a line.

It is the prosecutor's closing. Pryor's closing. I have never read it. In fifteen years I have never once read the closing argument of the trial I interpreted, because I told myself the closing was not mine, the closing was lawyers talking to a jury in a language the court reporter could keep without help from me, and I had no part in it, and a pane of glass does not stay after hours to read what the lawyers said about the light it let through.

The line under his finger says: *Watch his hands when he tells you what happened. Watch how they move. This is a man who has spent his life being hard to understand, and he has learned to use it. He is evasive. He is — and I'll use the word, because you've all seen it now — he is deceptive. The defense wants you to believe his confusion. Ladies and gentlemen, his confusion is a strategy.*

I read it twice. The first time I read it as English, the way the jury heard it, the way it is meant to be read, a closed and persuasive sentence built by a competent lawyer to do a thing to twelve people, and it does the thing, you can feel it do the thing even now, even cold, even fifteen years dead on a photocopied page. The second time I read it the way I read 14:22, which is to say I read it for what it is concealing, and the floor does the half-second thing it has been doing to me since a man held a yellowed tape into the cold light of a marble hall, and I have to put my hand flat on the table.

Because I was there. I was three seconds behind those hands. And those hands were not evasive.

* * *

Here is what I knew, standing in that 2009 courtroom, that I let myself forget I knew, the way you forget a number you do not want to add to a sum.

Tobias's signing was not deception. It was *deprivation.* It was a man reaching for a word that had been taken out of him before he was old enough to keep it, and finding a hole, and footbridging the hole the only way he had — fingerspelling at half-speed, building a shape with his shoulders that meant *roughly this,* substituting a sign that was near the thing but not the thing, the way you point at a color you cannot name. I rendered it fluent. That was my crime and I have confessed it to myself in the dark until the confession went smooth as a worry-stone. I made his holes into grammatical English because grammatical English was easier than the truth and because the room could only keep what could be typed. I have owned that. I will own it on a stand in front of a court if Lockwood makes me, which he will.

But what I did with my hands, Pryor did with a word, and his was worse, and I never once let myself see it, because seeing it would have meant the gap was not only mine, and I needed the gap to be only mine, because a thing that is only mine is a thing I can carry, and a thing that is shared is a machine, and you cannot carry a machine, you can only stand inside it and be one of its hands.

He took the deprivation — the thing I had smoothed into fluency, the broken reaching I had paved into sentences — and he renamed it. He stood in front of twelve hearing people and he looked at a man who could not make his own language arrive and he called the failure-to-arrive a *tactic.* He called the hole a *hiding place.* He took the single most defenseless fact about Tobias Rein — that no account he gave of any night of his life had ever once landed clean in another person — and he authored it into the thing that convicted him: *his confusion is a strategy.* He rendered a deprived man as a clever one. And a clever man who is hard to understand is a man with something to hide, and a man with something to hide pushed a knife into someone, and the jury wrote it down, and it became the truth, because the record only keeps what can be typed and what got typed was *deceptive.*

"I voiced that trial," I say to Lockwood, and my own voice sounds wrong to me, flat, the voice I use for a render I cannot vouch for. "I never read his closing."

"I know," Lockwood says. He is watching me the way you watch a structure take a load you are not sure it was rated for. "You weren't supposed to. You're the conduit. The conduit goes home."

* * *

We do not put it in that afternoon. You cannot put a thing like that in the way you slide an invoice across a clerk's desk. Lockwood spends the rest of the day on smaller stones, and after the gavel he takes me and Bee to the cafeteria on the holding floor, the one under the banner I have walked past forty times without reading, the one with Pryor's face on it and a slogan about families and safety, and we sit under the man's photograph and try to figure out how to put his choice into a record, and the irony of where we are sitting is not lost on any of us and none of us says it, because saying it would make it small.

"The closing isn't enough," Bee says. She has her laptop open; she has pulled the trial docket; she reads it faster than either of us, the way she reads everything faster than either of us. "A closing is argument. Donovan'll say *that's advocacy, that's what a closing is, you're allowed to call a defendant deceptive, the jury's instructed it's not evidence.* And she's not wrong. People say worse than that in closings every day and nobody vacates."

"It's not the closing alone," Lockwood says. "It's the closing *plus* what he knew. A prosecutor's allowed to call a man deceptive. A prosecutor is not allowed to call a man deceptive when he has been told, on the record or off it, that the man is not deceptive, he is *unrenderable* — and then proceed as if he'd never heard it, and let the thing that would've told the jury the truth get walked out of the building at two-ten on a Tuesday and never make the docket."

He says *unrenderable* and I flinch, and neither of them sees it, and I am glad neither of them sees it, because I do not have a name yet for why the word puts a cold finger on the back of my neck. I think it is just the size of it. I think Lockwood reached for a big word and it landed big. I do not know — I am sitting under a campaign banner with two people who are better at this than I am, and I do not know — that the word he just reached for as advocacy is the exact word that is waiting for me four days out in a dead-elevator archive, that it is not a metaphor, that it is going to turn out to be a *finding.* I file it the way I filed Delia's *I couldn't tell you what he signed.* I file it as comfort. I file it as *the case is bigger than my hands.* I am the best in the building at hearing what a sentence is concealing and I cannot hear the thing this one is concealing because it is concealing it from *me,* on purpose, the way I have spent thirty years concealing things from juries, and the instrument is always the last to read its own render.

"Delia told him," I say. "She told Pryor to his face. *The render is wrong.* Two-ten, before they walked her out."

"Delia told him," Lockwood agrees. "And Irene knew, and half that gallery knew, and not one of them is in the transcript, because a Deaf person correcting a certified interpreter is, procedurally" — he turns his hand over, an empty hand — "noise. But here's the problem, Ruth, and it's the only problem. Proving he was *told* is not the same as proving he *chose.* The instant I say *he knew and proceeded anyway,* Donovan stands up and points at the one fact that buries it." He nods at me, once, almost an apology. "The certified interpreter of record signed off on every render and is only now, fifteen years later, deciding she got it wrong. The State was entitled to rely on its certified interpreter. Pryor was entitled to rely on his. And I'm asking a court to find a sitting judge committed misconduct because he believed the credentialed professional in the room instead of the uncredentialed one." He lets that land. "The credentialed professional is sitting in my gallery, Ruth. Her name is on every page. His deniability has a face and it's yours."

I look at him. Bee has gone still over her laptop.

"My credential is his alibi," I say.

"Your credential is his alibi." He doesn't say it unkindly; he says it flat, the way he reads a docket number, a man stating a fact he has checked twice and does not enjoy. "I'm not going to walk you through the rest of what that costs us in here, under his banner, while his people watch you not eat. I'll let him do that himself, on a transcript, where it counts." He glances up at the photograph over his own shoulder — GERALD PRYOR, the slogan about families — and back down at the closing on the table. "Right now I just need you to know the floor we're standing on."

I read the banner, finally, all the way, fifteen years too late, the way I read the closing too late, the way I read everything in this building too late except the hands, and even the hands I read too late by three seconds, which is the only speed I have ever been able to read anything.

* * *

The unsealing is the lever, and the unsealing is the thing that is buried, and the two facts are the same fact.

Lockwood explains it to me at the table while Bee starts making the calls only Bee can make, in the dialect of liability and favor and *I'm Deaf-Services, I just need to confirm a retention date,* and it is the cleanest demonstration of the thesis I have ever been handed, cleaner than any tape, because there is no villain in it at all and a man's freedom is dying inside it anyway.

The 2009 interpreter-services authorization — the document that would say, in a clerk's hand, *who* re-coded Delia off Tobias's murder trial and onto Reyes, and at whose direction, and with what notation — is not in the live system. It is in a module that got folded into a 2019 consolidation, and the 2019 consolidation is in a records-retention purge, and the purge is being executed by a bonded shredding contractor on a rolling schedule, and the rolling schedule is, depending on which day you ask the office that phones itself, either three weeks out or next Tuesday. No one is shredding it. That is the part I cannot make my hands accept. No one is standing over the machine. There is no order from Pryor, no call, no fingerprint — there could not be, he is far too careful, he has spent his whole life being careful — there is only a retention schedule that predates the appeal and a contractor doing exactly his job and a county that throws away its own past for space the way it always has, and at the bottom of the schedule, by the most ordinary administrative gravity, is the single sheet of paper that would put a hand on a choice. The proof of the willed choice is going to be destroyed by no one's choice at all. The machine erases the evidence of the machine, on time, on budget, under a banner, and the worst hand in the story does not have to lift a finger, because the system was already built to lift it for him.

"He doesn't have to do anything," I say.

"He doesn't have to do anything," Lockwood says. "That's the genius of it, and he didn't even design it. He just *inherited* a machine that protects him, and all he has to do is" — the empty hand again — "not interfere with the routine. Let finality work. Let retention work. Let the County oppose the unsealing on perfectly reasonable grounds — finality, age, burden, fishing expedition — argued by lawyers who'll be working for him in November and who genuinely believe they're just defending an old conviction against a desperate appeal. He doesn't make a call. He doesn't have to. The call was made in 2009 and the building has been finishing it ever since."

"Adeyemi can order the unsealing," I say. "Today. She can sign it."

"She can. And Donovan'll argue she shouldn't — relevance, the danger of putting a sitting judge's discretion on trial by proxy in a post-conviction hearing he isn't even a party to — and some of that's right, and Adeyemi reads the briefs, and she's not going to torch a fifteen-year conviction *and* implicate a colleague on the bench on a Tuesday afternoon unless she's sure." He looks at me. "She's not sure. She won't be sure off an invoice and a carbon. The thing that makes her sure" — and here it is, the thing he has been walking me toward since quarter to one, the thing the whole afternoon was foundation for — "is you. On the stand. Saying you were there, and you heard the hands, and the hands were not what he called them, and you know it because you are the one who made the record he called them *out of.* You're the only person alive who can stand between his characterization and the truth, because you're the one whose render he characterized."

* * *

He files the motion to unseal before the gavel the next morning, and he files a motion to expand the record to include the 2009 closing, and he gives Adeyemi a sentence I will carry: *Your Honor, the State will say a closing argument is not evidence. We agree. We do not offer it for its truth. We offer it as the act it was — a characterization of a Deaf man's language, made by the prosecutor, to a hearing jury, that the prosecutor had specific reason to know was false; and we say that act is the authorship at the heart of this conviction, performed one rung above the interpreter and entered into the record as argument because it could not have survived entry as fact.*

And Donovan stands up, and she is good, and she does exactly what Lockwood said she would do, and the worst thing about it is that she is not wrong.

"Your Honor, the petitioner is now asking this Court to try a sitting judge of this very court — in absentia, by inference, in a proceeding to which he is not a party and at which he cannot defend himself — on the theory that fifteen years ago he made an argument to a jury that the jury was instructed was not evidence. And the *only* support for the claim that the argument was made in bad faith is the testimony, not yet given, of the certified interpreter of record" — and now, for the first time in two days, Kate Donovan turns and looks at me, level, not unkind, doing her job — "who certified every word at the time, who is the petitioner's own retained expert, and who has a documented, recent, and frankly profound interest in the proposition that her own work cannot be trusted. The render the State convicted on and the render the defense now disowns are the same render, by the same hands, sworn the same way. The defense wants this Court to credit those hands when they implicate the prosecutor and discredit them when they convicted the defendant. It cannot be both. Foundation, Your Honor. The closing comes in over my objection, if at all, for what it was — argument — and not for the bad faith counsel cannot prove without a witness who unmakes herself to give it."

She sits. And I sit in the gallery and I feel the trap settle into a shape I did not expect, because she has just told the room, accurately, that the instrument is the obstacle — that the same hands cannot be the State's reliable conduit in 2009 and the defense's reliable accuser now. He hid his choice behind my credential then. The closer Lockwood comes to him, the more the whole case narrows back onto me, the hands, the one load-bearing thing in the room, exactly as it narrowed in 2009. I keep waiting for the case to get bigger than my hands. It keeps getting smaller, down to them, down to the width of one finger, down to a chair in the well I have not climbed into yet.

Adeyemi does not rule. She takes the unsealing motion under advisement and tells Lockwood she will hear his offer of proof on the closing when the defense puts on its case, which is a way of saying *put your interpreter in the chair and we'll see,* which is a way of saying the whole thing now rests where it has rested since a man held a tape into the light: on me, on the stand, on a sentence I have to commit to and cannot take back.

Tobias is watching me when I look up. His left hand is ticking on the table, the slow continuous tremor, the second guard keeping its own time, the medical clock and the legal clock and the thing that runs underneath both. He has read the whole exchange off the lawyers' bodies and Bee's small relayed signs at the rail, and he knows what just happened, he always knows what just happened, he has spent his life in rooms where the thing happening to him is conducted in a language he has to reconstruct from faces. He signs, low, just for me, his hands barely leaving the wood so the bailiff won't startle — *the man with the banner.* A flat statement. Not a question. Then he tips his chin a half-inch toward the gallery, toward the door, toward the building beyond it, toward the cafeteria two floors down where the man's face hangs over the trays, and he signs one more thing, slow, his face doing nothing at all, which on him is its own kind of verdict: *he gets to be the glass now too.*

I do not sign back. The bailiff is three feet away and the room is wrong for it and there is nothing I could put in the air that would be true. So I just hold the look until the deputy's hand comes down on the back of his chair, and then I let it go.

Bee finds me in the corridor after the gavel, before I have my coat on, her phone in her hand and two spots of color high on her cheeks that I have learned mean she has been on the phone being someone she is not. She has spent the recess in the dialect of liability and favor — *I'm Deaf-Services, I just need to confirm a retention date* — and she has gotten an answer out of the office that phones itself, which is more than Lockwood's three subpoenas have gotten out of it in a week, because a subpoena is a fight and a friendly voice asking an ordinary question is a Tuesday.

"The authorization's in the 2019 box," she says. "Module forty-one. I got the retention clerk to read me the line off her screen." She does not look triumphant. She looks like someone who has run to the edge of a thing and seen how short the edge is. "It's flagged for the contractor. Not three weeks. The box went out on the truck this morning, Ruth. It's at the vendor's intake. Bonded shredding, rolling schedule, first-in-first-out — and module forty-one is near the front of the queue because the County loads them by date and that box is old."

"How near the front."

"She wouldn't say. Couldn't say. It's the vendor's queue now, not the County's; once it leaves the building nobody here can tell you where in the line it sits, only that it's in it." She turns the phone over in her hand, the way Lockwood turns over his empty palm, the way Delia turned hers, the gesture of this whole machine — everyone holding up a hand with nothing in it. "Lockwood's filing for an emergency hold first thing. The vendor runs on a schedule and the schedule doesn't know our hearing exists. So it's the hold against the queue. Paper against a truck."

The sheet of paper that would put a hand on a choice is somewhere in a line of boxes at an intake dock in an industrial park nobody in this story has ever driven to, riding the most ordinary administrative gravity there is, and no one is standing over the machine, and no one will have to, and the only thing between it and the blade is a motion Lockwood has not filed yet against a contractor who is doing exactly his job — and the contractor will win, on time, on budget, the way the building always wins, unless a judge who has not ruled on anything yet rules on this one thing before the morning run.

I stand in the marble corridor under the cold institutional light, and two floors below me a man's face hangs over a tray of food and a slogan about families, and somewhere in a queue I cannot see a box is moving toward the front of a line by no one's choice at all, and I do the only thing left that is mine to do, which is the thing I am worst at: I wait, with my hands empty, for a building to decide whether to keep the one piece of paper that proves it knew.


# Chapter Twenty-Six — A Reasonable Defense

You do not get to put a sitting judge on the stand in a hearing he is recused from. Lockwood knew that the morning he filed the motion to unseal, and he filed the motion anyway, because the motion was never going to win the document — the document is on a truck, or it is in a queue behind a truck, or it is, by now, behind whatever the truck became — and a motion you cannot win is still a lever you can pull, and what it pulled was Gerald Pryor into a conference room on the nineteenth floor of a building two blocks from the Justice Center, on a Thursday, under the most reasonable circumstances anyone could have designed.

Here is how reasonable. Lockwood noticed his deposition — narrow, surgical, *limited to the 2009 interpreter-services arrangements in State v. Rein,* a scope Donovan negotiated down comma by comma until it fit on a single page — and the State moved to quash, and the State's grounds were good: a judicial candidate cannot be dragged into every post-conviction fishing trip a defense lawyer floats, finality matters, the deponent is not a party, the burden is harassing, the prejudice obvious. Adeyemi could have granted it. She did not rule. She let it sit, the way she lets everything sit, the way the whole machine lets everything sit until the sitting becomes the answer. And before she could be made to choose, Pryor's own people withdrew the motion to quash.

I want you to sit in that the way Lockwood made me sit in it, at the counsel table, while Bee read me the email off her phone. *He's sitting for it. Voluntarily. Limited scope, two hours, a court reporter, no video.* The State fought to keep him out of the chair and then, before a judge could order him into it, his own campaign walked him to it on its own feet — because a candidate who *refuses* to answer questions about the killer he put away is a candidate with something to hide, and a candidate who answers them, calmly, fully, on the record, with nothing to fear, is a candidate who *cleared it up.* He is not avoiding the séance. He is volunteering to officiate it again, in daylight, for the cameras that aren't allowed in the room but will be allowed the press release.

"He's not afraid of us," I said.

"He's not afraid of us," Lockwood said. "He's *using* us. We're a venue." He looked tired in the way he gets when the law is about to be correct. "Be in the room, Ruth. Sit behind me. Don't sign anything to Tobias's people in there, don't react, don't be anything but my retained expert taking notes. I need you to hear him say it."

"Hear him say what."

"I don't know yet," Lockwood said. "Whatever it is, you'll be the only one in the room who hears what it's covering."

* * *

The conference room has a long table and a view of the lake, gray and flat and going nowhere, and a court reporter named Inez whom I have worked beside a hundred times and who nods at me like we are at a funeral, which we are, we are always at a funeral, this whole profession is a wake for sentences that have already died. Donovan is there with a second-chair I don't know. Lockwood is there with a legal pad and Bee and me. And Pryor comes in at ten exactly, alone, no entourage, a good navy suit with no flag pin on it because the flag pin would read as campaign and this is a *legal proceeding, ladies and gentlemen,* and he shakes Lockwood's hand and he shakes Donovan's hand and then he turns to me and puts out his hand and says, "Ruth. Good to see you. You did beautiful work for me once. I never forgot it."

And I shake it. I shake the hand. My own hand goes out and takes his and I feel the dry warm grip of the man who stood across a well from me fifteen years ago and called a deprived man clever, and I shake it, because the alternative is a *scene,* and I do not make scenes, I am the one person in every room who does not make scenes, and the discipline that makes me good is the discipline that makes me complicit and I have known that for nineteen chapters of my own life and I still shook his hand, and I want that on the record, mine, the one only I keep: *I shook it. Nobody made me. The body that will not make a scene is the body that built the record he is about to defend.*

He is sworn. Inez swears him — *do you solemnly swear that the testimony you are about to give will be the truth* — and he says "I do" in the baritone, and I feel the word land in my sternum like a struck thing, because it is the same family of word, it is the cousin of the word I have said four thousand times, *faithfully and accurately,* and he means it. That is the part I was not ready for. He means it the way I mean mine. He raises his hand and he swears to tell the truth and there is not a flicker in him, not a tell, not a bead of sweat at the temple, because as far as Gerald Pryor knows he is about to do exactly that.

* * *

Lockwood is good. He does not open with the closing. He does not open with *evasive.* He opens with the dullest possible thing, the way you walk a witness onto thin ice by talking about the weather, and he asks Pryor to describe, in his own words, his ordinary practice in 2009 with respect to interpreter scheduling in cases involving a Deaf defendant.

And Pryor answers like a man who has nothing in the world to hide, which is the worst thing he could possibly be, and I take it down in my head the way I take down a witness I am about to voice, three seconds behind, listening for the topic under the comment, for the brow-raise on the face of the sentence, for the thing he is fronting and the thing he is letting go small — and what I hear, God help me, is a competent public servant giving an accurate account of a broken system he did not build.

"In 2009," Pryor says, "the County had a list. Court interpreter services maintained the roster, scheduled the per-diems, and the prosecutor's office relied on it the way we relied on the lab and the medical examiner and the clerk. I didn't book interpreters, Mr. Lockwood. I'm not sure I knew how. I'd have called services if I'd thought there was a problem, and I'd have trusted them to fix it, because that's what they were for. They sent a certified interpreter." A small open gesture, both hands, a man showing they're empty. "I want to be precise, because I know that's the currency in this room. I relied on the credential. The State of Ohio said Ms. Okafor-Vance was qualified to interpret a criminal proceeding. She was. She is. I had no reason on earth to second-guess the certification of the most requested interpreter in the building, and frankly it would have been improper for me to try. I'm a lawyer. I'm not a linguist."

There it is. He says it cleanly and he says it early, before Lockwood can spring it, because his people prepped him and they prepped him *well,* they found the line in the bible too — *I'm a lawyer, I'm not a linguist* — and he lays it on the table face-up, defusing it, owning it, making it sound not like an alibi but like *modesty.* And I sit behind Lockwood and I feel the thing I was warned about and did not believe I could feel, which is the pull of it. The undertow. Because he is *right.* He is a lawyer. He is not a linguist. He could not have read the tape. He could not have read the hands. He could no more have caught the quarter-turn at fourteen twenty-two than the jury could, than the bailiff could, than anyone in that room could except the two Deaf women in the gallery and the one hearing woman with her hands in the air, and the two Deaf women were noise and the hearing woman was me, and I rendered it clean, and he relied on me, *exactly as the system told him to,* and where, in that, is the crime, and whose hand was it, and I have been asking that question since a man held a tape into the cold light of a marble hall and I am no closer to an answer and the man who is the answer is sitting eight feet away telling me, with total sincerity, that he is glass.

That is the word he uses. Not in those words. But that is the word.

* * *

Lockwood moves him forward an inch at a time. He establishes that Pryor knew Tobias's signing was non-standard — Pryor concedes it readily, *of course it was unusual, I remember thinking he was hard to follow, that's not a secret, that's why we had an interpreter at all* — and I watch Lockwood set the trap one board at a time, and I watch Pryor walk onto every board like a man crossing his own living room.

"You'd agree," Lockwood says, "that a defendant who is hard to follow, whose language is unusual, is a defendant for whom the quality of interpretation matters more, not less."

"I'd agree with that as a general proposition," Pryor says. "Sure."

"And you'd agree that if the interpretation in such a case were inadequate, the consequences would fall on the defendant. Not on the State. Not on you."

"Mr. Lockwood," Pryor says, and here he leans forward, and here is the first place I see the lawyer in him wake all the way up, the old trial muscle, "I'd agree that's a serious risk in any case with any language barrier, and I'd agree the system should guard against it, and I'd agree, if you're asking me, that the system in this county in 2009 was underfunded and overbooked and held together with tape, and that good people did the best they could inside it, and that it was not good enough, and that men have surely been hurt by that and I lose sleep over the ones I can name. I'd agree with all of it." He sits back. "What I won't agree to is the next sentence, the one you haven't asked yet, because I can see it coming. You want me to agree that *I knew,* in that case, in that chair, that the interpretation was inadequate, and proceeded anyway. And I didn't. I will say that under oath as many times as you'd like to hear it. I did not know."

And Lockwood, who is good, who is so good, lays down the carbon.

He does it gently. He has Inez mark it. He slides a photocopy across the table — Delia's day-book carbon, the one we drove to a freezer vault to get, *re-coded 2:10, told Pryor and the bench the render at the table was unreliable, no action taken, walked out 2:40, dated, in my hand* — and he reads it into the record, the whole line, in his flat tired voice, and then he asks the question the entire afternoon was foundation for.

"Judge Pryor. A Certified Deaf Interpreter, a Deaf professional whose job was precisely to read the language you found hard to follow, told you to your face, on the second day of that trial, that the render at the table was unreliable. And you proceeded. How do you answer that?"

The room is very quiet. The lake does nothing. Bee has gone still beside me, and Inez's hands are resting, waiting, and Donovan does not object because Donovan wants to hear the answer as much as we do, and Pryor looks at the carbon for a long moment, and I watch him, I watch the millimeter, because the millimeter is the only thing I have ever known how to read, and what I see is a man being genuinely, carefully honest, which is so much worse than a man lying that I have to put my pen down.

"I don't remember her," Pryor says.

* * *

He lets that sit, and then he does the thing that I will carry out of this room, the thing that is worse than denial, the thing that is the whole book in one breath, and he does it *kindly,* which is the unbearable part.

"I want to be careful here, because I can see what this carbon means to your client and I'm not going to insult it." He folds his hands. "I'm telling you the truth, which is that I do not have a memory of a deaf interpreter telling me anything in that trial. That doesn't mean it didn't happen. Ms. Hart kept a contemporaneous note; I have no reason to doubt her note; if she wrote that she told me, I'll accept that she believes she told me, and I'll accept she may well have. But Mr. Lockwood — *told me* how?" He turns one hand over, the empty hand, the same empty hand Lockwood turns over, the same empty hand Delia turned over in her front room, the gesture is *contagious,* it is the gesture of this entire machine, everyone in the chain showing each other a hand with nothing in it. "She's Deaf. She couldn't tell me anything except through an interpreter. And the interpreter in that room" — he does not look at me; he is too disciplined to look at me; the not-looking is its own indictment — "was the certified one. So if Ms. Hart objected to the certified interpreter's rendering, that objection reached me *through* the certified interpreter's rendering. Do you understand the position I'm describing? The only channel I had to be told the channel was broken was the channel. I had one set of hands in that room I was permitted to rely on, and the law told me to rely on them, and everything anyone said to me, including, apparently, that I shouldn't, came to me *in that voice.*"

He says *in that voice* and he still does not look at me, and the whole room hears the not-looking, and I sit in the chair behind Lockwood and I understand, finally, completely, the architecture I have been standing inside my entire adult life. He is not lying. I voiced Delia's objection in 2009. I have known that since Lakewood — *my accuracy is what made the objection sound like noise; I rendered her perfectly; that is how she disappeared.* I knew it as my crime. And here is Pryor, fifteen years later, taking the exact same fact and wearing it as his exoneration: *the warning came through the warned-against instrument, so how was I to weight it.* The thing I hold as my guilt, he holds as his innocence, and they are the same fact, the same square foot of air, the same hands, and the difference between confession and acquittal is nothing but which way the sentence is facing, which is the only thing I have ever truly known about my own language and I learned it again, in English, from a man defending himself, and it is *true.*

"Let me put it plainly," Pryor says, "because I respect this process and I respect you, and I respect — " a small nod, not at me, past me, at the idea of me — "the work that was done in that case. I tried the case the system handed me. The system handed me a certified interpreter, a complete interview, a confession on tape, and a jury of twelve. I argued the evidence as I found it. The jury convicted; I didn't. If the system that handed me those things was broken — and you may be right that it was — then the place to fix it is the system, the funding, the rules about deaf interpreters, the things I have, for the record, *advocated for* from the bench. It is not to come back fifteen years later and tell a man who relied in good faith on the credentials his state issued that he is the author of a harm he had no way to see." He spreads the empty hands one last time. "I was an instrument too, Mr. Lockwood. We all were. That's not a defense I'm proud of. It's just the one that's true."

* * *

Lockwood does not flinch and I love him for it. He bores in for another forty minutes, and he is relentless, and he is precise, and he gets Pryor to concede the underfunding and the overbooking and the general inadequacy and the foreseeable risk, and he gets exactly nothing that a court could hang a finding of misconduct on, because there is nothing, because the genius of it — Lockwood said it under the banner and I did not fully believe him — is that there is no fingerprint, there was never going to be a fingerprint, the man did not commit the crime, the *building* committed the crime and the man stood in it and did his job and the job was the crime and *that is not actionable,* you cannot vacate a conviction because a prosecutor was a competent functionary of a deaf machine, the law has no cause of action for *complicity-shaped-like-diligence,* it never has, it is not built to, it is the machine, you cannot indict a machine using the machine's own forms.

And the closing. Lockwood saves the closing for the end, the *his confusion is a strategy,* and he reads it into the record and asks Pryor whether, knowing what he now knows about Tobias's language, he stands by having called a deprived man deceptive.

And this is the only place all afternoon where I see Gerald Pryor's face do a true thing.

It is small. If you were not me you would miss it. A tightening at the jaw, a half-second where the baritone is not loaded yet, where the prepared man is for one beat just a man who is sixty and has put a lot of people away and is being asked to look at the seam of one of them. And then it closes, the way my face closes, the way I have spent a career teaching my face to close, and he says: "I stand by the argument I made on the evidence I had. I'd have made it about any defendant whose account didn't hang together, and his didn't, and a prosecutor is allowed to say so, and the jury was instructed it wasn't evidence." A beat. "Do I wish I'd known then what you're telling me now? Of course I do. I'm a human being. But you're asking me to feel guilty for not knowing a thing that, by your own theory, only one person in that room could have told me — and she told me in the voice of the only person who could have told me she was wrong." The empty hands. "I don't know how to be guilty of that. I've tried. It won't hold."

I believe him. That is the thing I have to write down even though it costs me, because the whole book has been me learning to stop laundering and I will not launder this: *I believe him.* He is not a villain. He is me, fifteen years on and one rung up the ladder and a hundred IQ points of self-regard heavier, wearing a navy suit with no flag pin, and he said *I only argued the evidence as I found it* in the exact key in which I said *I only said what they said,* and his is the same costume as mine, the identical costume, and he wears it better because he had it tailored, and there is no court in this country that can take it off him, because the costume is not a lie. That is what nobody told me when I was nineteen and learning to be a pane of glass. The costume is not a lie. It is *true,* and that is precisely why it is the most dangerous garment a person can put on, because a lie can be impeached and the truth cannot, and *I only did my part* is true for every single hand in the chain, all the way up, all the way down, and a thing that is true for every hand is a thing no hand has to answer for, and a man broke at the bottom of it, and the hands are all clean, and they are clean because they are *telling the truth.*

* * *

We do not get him. I want to be clear, because I spent fifteen years wanting somebody to be guilty of Tobias and now I have sat in a room with the man closest to it and watched the law slide off him like water off a coat, and there is no scene where he breaks, there is no scene where he confesses, there is no scene where the gallery gasps, because this is not that kind of room and that is not how the harm was made. The deposition ends at 11:54. Pryor stands, buttons his coat, thanks Inez by name, tells Donovan he'll see her Thursday, and on his way out he stops at my chair — I have not said one word in two hours, I have been furniture with a face, the thing I am best at — and he leans down a little, courteous, and he says, just to me, in the warm dry voice:

"For what it's worth, Ruth. I never doubted you for a second back then." A small smile, nothing in it but kindness, which is the worst thing that has ever been aimed at me. "Don't let them make you doubt yourself now. You were the best in that building. You still are."

And he goes. He gives me the thing I organized my whole life to deserve — *you were faithful, you were accurate, you were the one true instrument in the room* — he hands it to me as a gift on his way out of a deposition about a man his fidelity and my fidelity buried together, and he means it as kindness, and it is the most precise act of violence anyone has committed against me in this entire ruin, because he is *right,* I was the best in the building, and the best in the building is exactly the instrument the building needed, and my excellence is not separable from the harm, it was the *delivery mechanism* for the harm, and he just told me my excellence is intact, and he is correct, and I want to be sick.

Lockwood watches the door close behind him. Bee has not moved. Inez packs her steno machine with the small precise motions of a woman who has typed worse.

"Well," Lockwood says, finally, to the room, to nobody, to the lake. "There's no hand to hold there. He's right that there's no hand." He rubs his eyes. "I knew there wouldn't be. I needed it on a transcript anyway."

"Needed what on a transcript?" Bee says. "He gave us nothing. He gave us *I relied on the certified interpreter.* That's worse than nothing, Des, that's — " she stops, and I watch her get to it, watch the twenty-seven-year-old who reads everything faster than the rest of us get there a full beat before I do, the way she always does, and the sting of it is so old now it is almost a comfort. "Oh. *Oh.* He just put it under oath."

"He just put it under oath," Lockwood says.

* * *

It takes me the elevator ride down to understand what they understood in the chair.

Pryor gave us nothing a court can use against *him.* That door is shut; it was always shut; Lockwood knew it walking in. But in shutting it — in being so reasonable, so deniable, so *true* — Gerald Pryor stood under oath and laid the entire weight of the 2009 conviction onto a single load-bearing column, and named the column, and the column is me. *I relied on the credential. I relied on the certified interpreter. The only channel I had was the channel, and the channel was the State's qualified instrument, and her name is on every page.* He spent two hours, voluntarily, building the most airtight foundation imaginable for a single proposition: *that conviction stands or falls on whether Ruth Okafor-Vance's render of Tobias Rein can be trusted.* He did it to save himself. He thought he was pouring concrete around his own feet. And he was — but the same pour, the identical pour, encased the one fact Lockwood has been trying to get a court to feel since the day he handed me a yellowed tape: that there is exactly one person alive who stands between the truth of what Tobias signed and the record the State convicted on, and that the State's own prosecutor has now sworn, on a transcript, that he relied on her completely.

"He made you the case," Lockwood says, in the lobby, quiet, not happy, because none of this is happy. "He didn't mean to. He meant to make you his shield. But a shield and a witness are the same object, Ruth, they're the same fifteen-square-inches of metal, the only difference is which way you're facing when you hold it up." He looks at me, and there is something in his face I have not seen before, something that is not strategy. "He just spent two hours swearing under oath that everything in that room came down to your hands. Now you get to climb into the chair and tell the court what your hands actually said."

And there it is — the elevator opening, the cold institutional lobby, the lake through the glass doing its nothing — there is the place the whole machine has been leaning me toward since Chapter One, the chair in the well, mine to climb into now not as the faithful instrument but as the broken one, with the State's own theory of the case sworn into a transcript that makes me the hinge of it. Pryor cannot be convicted. The system cannot be convicted. The only thing left that can free Tobias Rein is the one thing I have not yet truly seen — what is actually *there,* at fourteen twenty-two, in a deprived man's hands, on a dying tape, in a band of degradation eating the exact frames where his life is — and I am going to sit down with Irene and Delia and Bee in the days before the hearing and I am going to find it.

I think I know what I am going to find. I think I am going to find the exculpatory sentence I rendered as guilt — the verb facing the other way, the blade in the other body, the truth that was always there if anyone in 2009 had had the hands to read it. I think the decode is going to hand me, at last, the thing I can carry into that chair and say out loud: *he did not say what I said he said; I can prove it; here is the width of one finger that is the rest of his life.*

I am wrong about that, and I do not know I am wrong, and I have four days left to be wrong in. But that is four days off, and a frame-by-frame off, and right now there is only a closing door and a man who shook my hand and told me I was faithful and meant it, and a transcript on the nineteenth floor that has, without a single fingerprint, made my unfaithfulness the only door left in the building — the door I broke, the door I am the key to, the door I now have to walk through and stand in and be looked at, with no costume on, in front of a court that is going to ask me to prove that the most faithful hands in Cuyahoga County were the thing that convicted a man, and to swear to it, faithfully and accurately, to the best of my ability.

Faithful to what.

I still don't have an answer. But for the first time I understand that the question is not rhetorical and never was, that somebody is going to make me say the answer into a record, under oath, with Inez or someone like her taking down every word that can be typed and losing every truth that can't — and that the answer, whatever it turns out to be, is going to be the last render of my life.


# Chapter Twenty-Seven — The Unrenderable Account

Delia will not do it in her own house. I understand why without being told: a person who has kept fifteen years of proof in a dead chest freezer does not invite the thing she's been freezing into her kitchen. So we do it at the archive, at Bee's table, on Bee's monitors, in the one room in Cuyahoga County built to hold old footage and treat it like a body — climate-controlled, the light killed down, a wall of black equipment that hums the way a hospital hums, the sound of a machine keeping something alive that should have died on its own.

Bee has pulled the cleanest file the county will give her. It is not clean. The 2009 master is dying in the evidence locker in Garfield Heights, and what we have is the digitization Bee made before the rot got bad, and even that carries the band — the gray weather rolling across the lower third of the frame, worst from about 14:04 to 14:19, a curtain of degradation drawing closed over the exact place where a man's life is, the way Delia warned me it would, the way the master is doing to itself a little more every time someone slides it onto a head. *The crux frames are the first thing the neglect destroys.* I read that as a problem to be solved. I came here believing the band was an obstacle in front of the answer. I did not yet understand it was a mercy — that the tape rotting over fourteen twenty-two is the tape refusing to make us look at the thing it has already decided we cannot have.

Four chairs. Bee at the keyboard, because the machine is hers and the hands on the jog wheel should be the youngest. Delia to her right, reading glasses down, the slate cardigan, forearms on the table like a woman about to do close work with a scalpel. Irene at the end, in her own coat still, because Irene does not take her coat off in rooms she does not own. And me, where I have put myself my whole life, angled so I can see everyone's hands at once and be seen by all of them, the interpreter's position, the furniture-with-a-face position, except there is no one here to interpret for. Every person at this table reads the source. For the first time in twenty-six years I am in a room about ASL where I am the *least* qualified reader present, and I came in thinking that was humility, and it was vanity wearing humility's coat, because underneath it I still believed that when the four of us put our eyes on fourteen twenty-two I would be the one who finally said the true sentence out loud.

*Run it,* Irene signs. Not to Bee. To the room. *Begin.*

* * *

Bee takes us in slow from 14:00, real-time first, so we can see the shape of him before we cut him into frames. And there is Tobias at thirty-one, in the booking room, the bad ceiling light, the detective's shoulder in the corner of the frame — *thick, patient,* I wrote, the man unnamed on the tape — and Tobias's hands come up into the deprived idiolect I have been carrying in my body for fifteen years, the fluent run and then the hole, the footbridge, the fingerspelled patch where a sign was never put in him.

I watch the four of us watch him. Delia leans in at 14:06 and her face does something — not a flinch, a *recognition,* the way you'd recognize a handwriting — and she signs, low, just to the table, *there, that one — that's his, he uses it for "happened," he uses it for "they say," he uses it when he doesn't have the word,* and Bee nods like she's heard it before from Delia and I have never heard it in my life. I rendered this man for nine days and I did not know he had a sign that meant *I don't have the word,* and Delia knew it in six frames because Delia had his hands and I only ever had his mouth's worth of them.

*Stop,* Delia signs. *Fourteen nineteen. Go in.*

And we go in.

* * *

This is the part the room was built for. Bee takes us to single frames and we live there. The band is below the worst of it now, thank God, riding the bottom edge, so his hands are mostly clear — his hands, which is all the tape ever kept of him, his hands and not his face, and the face is where half his grammar lived and the face is gone soft in the resolution, a smear where the brows were, and I feel the first cold thing move through me, because I have spent four days promising myself the answer was *on the tape,* and the tape does not have his face.

Bee advances. Frame. Frame. Frame.

He sets the dead man down. Off his right shoulder, a locus, the way I saw it in October, the way Bee sharpened it in October — the dead man placed in the air to his right. He builds the table between them, a flat surface, both hands. And then the right hand goes to the edge-classifier, the flat hand fingers-together, the thing I voiced fifteen years ago as a knife, the thing Bee restored in October to a blade that *could* have come the other way, *he came at me with it,* the quarter-turn the light nearly ate putting the edge in the other body for a single frame —

and Delia signs, *No.*

Not no like *you're wrong.* No like *wait.* No like a surgeon's hand going still over the open place.

*Again,* she signs. *The hand. Frame back. Twice.*

Bee rolls it back. The right hand crosses the built table. Forward. Back. Forward.

And Delia looks at it for a long time, longer than I have ever watched anyone look at four frames, and what she signs next she signs slowly, in the register she uses with me, the one that withholds the music, except there is no music left to withhold, there is just a Deaf woman who had this man's hands telling the truth into a room that does not want it:

*I don't know what that is.*

* * *

I think I laugh. Some small wrong sound comes out of me, because for one second I take it as modesty — Delia being careful, Delia being exact, Delia refusing to overstate the way the good ones refuse to overstate — and I start to hand her October's answer, the way Bee read it, the exculpatory torque, *read from the origin,* and Delia turns her whole body toward me, the role-shift of a person becoming nothing but herself, and stops me with a flat hand, palm out.

*You think I'm being humble.* A beat. The reading glasses come off. *I'm not being humble. I'm being the only person at this table qualified to tell you it can't be read, and I'm telling you. It can't be read. I had his hands.
