I knew his holes better than the man who held them, better than his mother, better than God, and I am looking at fourteen twenty-two with no clock on me and no judge above me and no budget under me, fifteen years to get ready for this exact four frames — and I cannot tell you whether that hand is going out or coming in. Not "I can't tell the court." I can't tell* you. *Here. Now. With everything.*

The machine hums. The band rolls along the bottom of the frame like water under a door.

*Read it from the origin,* I sign, and I hear my own hands begging. *Bee found it. The torque. He's inside the other body, the blade comes across —*

*I see the torque,* Delia signs. *I saw it before your friend was born.* She is not unkind. She is worse than unkind; she is precise. *A half-turn that doesn't commit. You read it as a knife in 2009 because a knife was the sentence the room could use. Your friend read it as "he came at me" in October because by October you all needed it exculpatory. And both of you are reading the same thing I'm reading, which is a man who never had a clean way to say toward or away in the first place, because nobody ever gave him the hands to build the difference. The turn isn't ambiguous because the tape is bad. The turn is ambiguous because* he *is. Because they made him that way. He doesn't have an away. He doesn't have a toward. He has* this —

and she does it, she does the half-turn herself, her own trained Deaf body taking on his deprived one, the educated hands deliberately breaking themselves down into his — the run, the hole, the footbridge — and she runs the edge-classifier across the built table and stops it at the exact place his stops, neither in nor out, suspended, a blade that is not going anywhere because the language it lives in was never finished,

*— and that's not a sentence, Ruth. That's a wound with a knife-shape in it. There's no faithful render of a wound.*

* * *

I do not break yet. I want that on the record, the one I keep, the only one. I sit at Bee's table with the band rolling and I do the last thing the old me knows how to do — I reach, not for the bricklayer, he is dead, I will not call him up, but for the wish under him, the wish that outlived him, the wish that there is an answer in the world and that I am the one with the hands to reach it. I turn to Irene.

Because Irene reconstructed the room. Irene put Delia at the center and the séance in the air and made me see that the room was full of people who could read him. *Full of people who could read him.* So if the room was full of readers, then the reading exists, then somewhere in these four frames is the thing the readers read, and I have three of the best in the world at this table, and I sign to Irene, *You said the room could read him. You said it. Read him.*

And Irene Stillwater, sixty-three, in her coat, who has distrusted me for fifteen years and never once softened it, looks at me with something I have not earned and will not be given again, which is pity.

*I said the room could read the gap.* The hands are slow now, load-bearing, the way she signs when she has decided you are finally able to hold it. *I never said the room could read* him. *Those are not the same thing. We all knew the render was wrong — Delia knew, I knew, half that gallery knew. We knew you'd taken something ambiguous and made it certain. That's a thing you can know without knowing the truth. You can know a coin landed crooked without knowing which face it would have shown. Fifteen years I have hated that you made him certain.* Her hands stop. Start again, lower. *And I sat in that second row every day so he'd have one face he could read, and Ruth — I could not read* him *either. He'd look at me to be understood and I'd give him back the face that says "I'm with you," and inside I had nothing, because there was nothing to catch. His own people. We loved him in a language his parents took out of his hands before he could hold it, and we could not read him, and we let you and the court believe somebody could, because the alternative —*

She stops. For the first time since I have known her, Irene Stillwater looks for a word and does not find it cleanly, and the not-finding is the most honest thing anyone has done in front of me in fifteen years.

*— the alternative is that there was a man in that chair and nobody on earth could say what he meant, and you cannot put that in front of a jury. A jury needs a sentence. So everybody gave it one. You gave it the worst one. But there was never a true one to give.*

* * *

Here is where the floor goes.

Not loud. I keep expecting these to be loud and they never are; the worst things in this whole ruin have all arrived in the warm dry registers of reasonable people — Pryor's kindness, Delia's care, now Irene's pity — and the floor does not crack, it just stops being there, the way the band stops being a problem to solve the second you understand it is not hiding the answer; it is rolling over the place where everyone agreed to pretend there was one.

I have spent four days — I have spent fifteen years — walking toward a sentence. *He did not say what I said he said.* I rehearsed it. I was going to climb into the chair and lay the width of one finger on the rail and say *here, this verb, it faces the other way, here is the rest of his life, I can prove it.* I had the exculpatory render in my mouth like a coin I'd been saving. I came into this room to have it minted.

And the four best readers alive to this case have put their eyes on fourteen twenty-two with everything — no clock, no budget, no judge, fifteen years of getting ready — and the answer is that there is no coin. The verb does not face the other way. The verb does not face *a* way. I did not render it wrong. I rendered *the unrenderable.* I took a thing that had no inside and gave it one, gave it a clean grammatical English inside, *he stabbed him,* because clean was the only thing the room could swallow — and the sickest joke of the whole machine, the thing I have to write down even though it ends me, is that my *guilt* was the same render. Chapter eight, midnight, the remote in my hand: *I rendered too cleanly, I authored guilt, I alone broke him.* I made my own crime fluent. I gave my own sin a clean inside, a knowable shape, a verb that faced one way — *I did this* — because a guilt you can name is a guilt you can pay, and I have spent fifteen years rendering *myself* the way I rendered him, smoothing an unbearable ambiguity into a sentence I could survive.

There is no faithful render. I have said those words to myself for nine chapters of my own life as a thing I *did* to a man. They are not a thing I did to a man. They are the *weather.* They were always the weather. Tobias Rein was a man whose account of the night that ended a life existed in a language that could not carry it, in hands that had been emptied of *toward* and *away* before he was old enough to need them — and there is no version of the room, not the 2009 room, not this room, not a room with Delia in the well and Irene in the box and God on the bench, where that account becomes a sentence, because the account is not a sentence. It is a wound with a knife-shape in it. And the court did not convict the wrong reading of it.

The court convicted the *fact that it could not be read.* It looked at a man it could not hear, and rather than say *we cannot hear this man* — the one thing the law is built never to say — it reached for the only instrument that would turn his silence into language a jury could vote on. Me. And I gave it language, faithful and accurate, to the best of my ability, and the language was a sentence, and the sentence put him away, and the sentence was *true the way a transcript is true:* internally perfect, bearing no relation to a thing that happened in the world.

*Run it once more,* I sign. My hands barely work. *From fourteen nineteen. All of it.*

And we watch it again, the four of us — the run and the hole and the footbridge and the half-turn and the blade that goes nowhere, the band rolling under it, the smear where his face was — and nobody at the table moves to read it this time, because there is nothing to read, there was never anything to read, and the only sound is the machine humming to keep alive a thing that died the moment a hearing world decided a Deaf child should *use his words, show Mama his words,* and folded his hands back into his lap.

* * *

Bee is the one who says it, finally, because Bee is twenty-seven and has not yet learned that some things you only sign small and never lift loud enough to be true.

*So we've got nothing.* Her hands are angry. Bee is always a little angry and she has earned it tonight. *Four days out and we've got — what. We can't say he's innocent. We can't even say the render was* wrong, *not really, because Donovan asks Delia "what did he actually sign" and Delia has to say "I don't know," under oath, the best reader in the room, "I don't know" — and that's the ballgame, that hands Donovan the whole thing, "if your own expert can't tell us what he said, how can you ask us to vacate a verdict" —*

*Bee,* Delia signs.

*— we walked into this to find the truth and the truth is there isn't one, and you can't put "there isn't one" in front of a judge, a judge needs —*

*A sentence,* Irene signs. *I know. I just said that.*

*— so what was it* for, Bee signs, and her hands crack on it, the CODA who has spent her life being the kid in the back row reading both of them, *all of it, the freezer and the band and Pryor's deposition and Ruth burning her whole life down — what was it* for *if the answer is that there's no answer —*

And Delia reaches across the table and stops Bee's hands. Just covers them. Holds them still the way you'd hold a bird. And signs, with her free hand, the thing I will carry out of this room for the rest of whatever my life turns out to be, the line that belongs to her because she earned it across fifteen years of fixing a robot's mistakes for nine dollars a video:

*It was for this. This is the truth.* She lets go of Bee's hands. *Not what he signed. That he could not be signed. That a man sat in a chair and the room could not hear him and convicted him for the silence. That's not nothing, child. That's the worst thing I know, and it's true, and it happened, and nobody has ever once made a court hold still long enough to look at it.* She turns. She looks at me. *There's one way to put "there is no faithful render" in front of a judge. You put up the most faithful render in the building, and you let her say it about her own hands, under oath, until the room can't pretend it didn't hear.*

* * *

So that is the answer. That is the thing I drove four days toward and it is not the thing I thought I'd find; it is the opposite of the thing, it is the absence where the thing was supposed to be — and it is worse, and it is truer, and it is the only door left.

I am not going to climb into that chair Monday and prove what Tobias said. There is no *what.* I am going to climb into that chair and prove that *there is no what* — that the conviction the State is defending, the conviction Pryor swore on his own innocence rested entirely on my hands, the conviction that is the campaign and the career and the fifteen years of a Deaf man dying in a hearing cage — rests on a sentence I authored over an absence, a fidelity performed over a silence, a séance the room held to convict its own deafness and called justice because the alternative was admitting it had never heard a word.

And I understand, finally, sitting at Bee's table with the band rolling, what Lockwood meant and what Tobias meant and what Irene has been watching for: that this is unanswerable. That the second I say it — *there is no faithful render* — Donovan rises, and Donovan is not stupid and not cruel and some of what she says is true, and she asks the only question there is, the question that has been waiting for me since a man held a yellowed tape into the cold light of a marble hall: *Ms. Okafor-Vance, if no render is faithful, then no Deaf person can ever be tried, or believed, or heard, by any court, ever — you have not come to free this man, you have come to tell us the courthouse cannot exist — and an instrument that has just sworn it cannot be trusted is asking us to trust it now. Which is it.*

I do not have an answer. The book has no answer. I am going to walk into that room in four days and let the truest thing I have ever learned be turned, by a competent woman doing her job, into the thing that unmakes every Deaf trial in the country — and there is no render, faithful or otherwise, that gets me across that.

Bee kills the monitor. The frame goes black — his hands gone, the band gone, the smear where his face was gone — all of it back into the humming dark where the county keeps the things it broke. Irene stands and puts on the coat she never took off, and on her way out she stops at my chair the way Pryor stopped at my chair. Except Irene does not tell me I was faithful. Irene looks down at me and signs, slow, one last load-bearing thing:

*Monday I'll be in the second row. Same as before.* A beat. The hands again. *Not for you. For him. So he has one face he can read while you tell them what nobody could.*

And she goes. And Delia goes, into the Lakewood dark, to her freezer and her fireproof box and her nine-dollar videos. And Bee drives me home because I am not fit to — twenty-seven and immortal in every way except the phone in her hand at the wheel — and neither of us signs a word the whole way, because there are no words, there was never a word, that is the whole of it, that is what I have to carry into the chair: there was never a word, and I gave them one, and a man is dying for the sentence I built over the place where his language should have been.

Faithful to what.

Four days. The width of no finger at all.


# Chapter Twenty-Eight — The Stand

There is a chair in the well of a courtroom that I have stood beside four thousand times and never once sat in. The interpreter does not sit in it. The interpreter stands at an angle to it, furniture with a face, the conduit through which the chair's occupant reaches the room. I have spent twenty-six years making sure the chair was never mine, because the chair is where you go to be doubted, to be impeached, to have a competent stranger take your account apart one clause at a time and decide what it weighs. The chair is for people whose words can be wrong.

Lockwood says my name, and I cross the well, and I sit down in it.

The clerk holds out the card. I do not need the card. I have said the words more times than the clerk has been alive, and I say them now without the card, and this is the part I rehearsed and could not rehearse, because there is no rehearsing the inside of a sentence you have meant your whole life and are about to mean backward.

"Do you solemnly swear that the testimony you are about to give will be the truth, the whole truth, and nothing but the truth."

It is not the interpreter's oath. It is the witness's, which I have never sworn, and the difference is the whole of my life narrowed to a preposition: I have always sworn to be faithful *to* something — a voice, a pair of hands, a meaning passing through me to a place I was not responsible for. Now I am sworn to a thing with no preposition. Just the truth. Mine. Coming from my own body, on the record, irreversible, the way everyone else's words have always been and mine never were.

"I do," I say.

Inez's hands settle on the steno machine. She will take down what I say. She will take down every word of it that can be typed, and lose every truth that cannot, and that is not her failing, that is the machine, that is the whole machine, and I am about to climb inside it from the witness's side and find out what it does to a person who has spent her career being the part of it that smiles.

* * *

I make myself see the room before Lockwood begins, because I taught myself long ago that you cannot render a room you have not seen, and I am about to render the worst thing I know and I want to see who is in it.

Adeyemi on the bench, patient, careful, a different judge than the one who is not here — Pryor recused, elevated, his shadow lying across this proceeding from two floors up and a campaign away, the man whose conviction this is, who will never sit in a chair like mine because he was too smart in 2009 to leave a fingerprint and too smart now to come watch the print get lifted. Donovan at the State's table, thirty-eight, notepad squared, not cruel and not stupid and entirely ready, the institutional voice of *the system worked* sitting with her pen capped because she does not need to write down what she already knows she is going to ask me.

Lockwood, rising, tired in the way he gets when the law is about to be terrible and correct at the same time.

A stranger named Okafor in the interpreter's position — my position, the furniture-with-a-face position — signing low and careful to Tobias, who is in a wheelchair now, who they brought up from Grafton in a van, thinner than four days ago, thinner than I thought a person could keep getting, the left hand ticking against the armrest, keeping its own time, the medical clock running under the legal one. He is watching me. Of course he is watching me. He has spent his whole life watching the mouth that was going to decide what he meant, and now the mouth is in the chair, and he is reading me the way he reads everyone, ahead of me, already knowing.

And in the second row, in her coat, because Irene Stillwater does not take off her coat in rooms she does not own, sixty-three years old, the woman who has distrusted me for fifteen years and never once softened it. She is not looking at me. She is looking at Tobias, giving him the one face in the room he can read, the way she did every day in 2009, the way she said she would. *Not for you,* she told me. *For him.* And next to her, Delia, who will take her own chair later on her own terms and be voiced by a mouth that is not the mouth that buried her — Delia with her forearms folded, fifteen years of a dead chest freezer in her face. And Bee at the rail, twenty-seven and furious and still, because Bee learned this week that some truths you only sign small.

Four readers. The séance has its congregation. And the medium is in the chair for once, and she is about to tell them what the medium is for.

"Ms. Okafor-Vance," Lockwood says. "You interpreted the trial of Tobias Rein in 2009."

"I did."

"You were the interpreter of record."

"I was the only interpreter of record." I hear the *only* leave my mouth and I do not soften it, and somewhere two floors up and fifteen years back a tired clerk solving a budget she could not see the shape of becomes, for one second, audible in the room. "By the second day, I was the only interpreter at all."

* * *

He walks me through it the way he walked Pryor through it, an inch at a time, the dullest possible road to the cliff. He establishes that I am the best, that both sides request me by name, that my certification is national and my career unblemished — he builds my credibility brick by brick precisely so that he can hand me the hammer, and I watch him do it, and I let him, because we agreed: the exhibit that talks. He is going to make the jury of one on that bench believe I am incapable of error, and then I am going to take that belief and break it over my own head, and the breaking is the testimony.

"In your professional opinion," Lockwood says, "having re-examined the 2009 footage — did you render Mr. Rein's signing at the moment in question faithfully and accurately?"

This is the question. This is the one we built the road to. And I have an answer rehearsed, the clean one, *no, I rendered it wrong,* the coin I was saving, and four days ago in Bee's dead-elevator dark I learned the coin does not exist, there is no *wrong,* there is no *right,* and the answer I rehearsed is one more clean render of an unrenderable thing — so I do not give it. I give the true one, which is worse, and slower, and has no shape a stenographer can love.

"I rendered it the way the room needed it rendered," I say. "I took a construction in his hands that did not resolve — that could not resolve, in his language — and I made it resolve. I gave the court a clean English sentence with a subject and a verb and an object, a man who did a thing to another man, because that is what a courtroom can convict on and what a courtroom can acquit on, and his hands were giving me neither. I rendered an ambiguity as a certainty. I have done it ten thousand times. It is not a mistake. It is the job."

"Objection." Donovan, on her feet, easy, almost gentle. "Narrative. And the witness is characterizing her own profession, not testifying to fact."

"I'll allow some latitude," Adeyemi says. "She's the expert and the actor both. That's the nature of this proceeding. Go to the specific, counsel."

Lockwood goes to the specific. He puts 14:22 on the monitor — the band already eating the bottom of the frame, the gray weather drawing closed over the place where a life is — and he asks me to describe, for the record, what is happening in Mr. Rein's hands.

And I do the thing I have refused to do for twenty-six years. I describe the gap from the inside.

I tell them about the directional verb that does not commit its direction — *toward* and *away* living in the same half-turn, a few degrees of a torso, the width of one finger laid across a man's chest deciding the rest of his life. I tell them about the classifier, the flat hand, the edge of it, a blade that I voiced as going one way and that goes, when you actually look, nowhere — suspended, unfinished, in a language that was never finished. I tell them that the face, where half his grammar lived, where the *yes* and the *no* and the *if* and the *I think* are carried, is not on the tape, was never on the tape, because the camera kept his hands and lost his face the way the record always keeps the render and loses the source.

I am rendering. That is the obscene thing I feel under my own voice as I do it: I am doing the only thing I know how to do, I am taking a thing that lives in space and time and a body and laying it down in English clean enough for Inez's fingers, and I am doing it *to myself* now, to my own crime, and it is going down smooth, it is going on the record articulate and grammatical and damning, and even this — even the confession — is a render, and even the render is too clean for the truth, and there is no bottom to it, there is no floor where the rendering stops and the thing itself begins.

"And in 2009," Lockwood says, "did anyone in that courtroom tell you, or tell the court, that your rendering was unreliable?"

"Yes," I say. "A Certified Deaf Interpreter named Delia Hart. She was in the well with me on the first day, in tandem, the way the law requires for a defendant like Tobias — a man whose language was built without full access, who needs a Deaf reader between him and a hearing one. On the second day she was re-coded off his trial onto a cheaper case to save the county a per-diem. Before they walked her out she told the prosecutor and the bench that the render at the table was unreliable."

"And what happened to that objection."

Here is the part I did not know I would have to say until I heard myself say it, the part that is the whole machine in one sentence, the part I have to deliver with the same flat fidelity I delivered everything because there is no other way I know to carry it.

"It came to the court through me," I say. "She was Deaf. The only mouth in that room that could carry her objection to the bench was mine. So I rendered it. I rendered, faithfully and accurately, to the best of my ability, the words of a Deaf professional telling the court that I could not be trusted." I stop. The lake does nothing out the tall windows. "And because I rendered it well — because I was the trusted instrument, the one they requested by name — her objection sounded like one more thing I was reliably passing through. My accuracy is what made her warning sound like noise. I voiced the woman calling me a liar so cleanly that the room heard a competent interpreter and not a Deaf expert with her hands on the truth. That is how she disappeared. I disappeared her. Faithfully."

* * *

And it is here, with my own voice still in the air saying *faithfully,* that the floor of nineteen ninety-one finally gives.

I was nineteen and the room was on the fourth floor and Meg was propped at thirty degrees so her ruined lungs could pull, and I stood where I have stood my whole life, between her and the hearing world, the only bridge. I have told this part. The doctor signed *probably* through me and I gave her *possible.* I let *won't change the ending* go small in my fingers. I built her a gentler world and she made her choice inside it and the choice cost her three weeks and then her life, and I drew from it the only lesson I could survive: *never soften again.*

What I have never let myself see — what I see now, in the chair, with the band rolling on the monitor and Tobias's left hand ticking and Irene's eyes on Tobias and not on me — is Meg's *face* while I did it.

She taught me the face. She taught me, the summer I was twelve, in her kitchen, that the grammar lives on the face, that a hearing person watches the hands and a Deaf person watches the whole sentence, the brows, the eyes, the held breath, the things the record cannot keep. She gave me my name-sign for it — the one who finishes other people's sentences, *be careful what you finish.* She was the most exact reader of hands I have ever known or will ever know, more exact than Delia, more exact than Irene, and I stood in front of her at nineteen and withheld a brow-press and let a word go small, and I have spent thirty years believing I got away with it.

I did not get away with it.

She watched my face the whole time. Of course she watched my face the whole time; she taught me where the truth lives; she would have read the gap between *probable* and *possible* the way you read a misspelled word in your own child's hand — instantly, without effort, with love. She saw me soften it. She saw her nineteen-year-old, the girl she had built a language into, stand in a hospital room and decide she had the right to choose how much of the truth a Deaf woman could stand — and she *let me.* She took the gentler word from my hands and she made her decision anyway, on the true prognosis, the one she had already read off my face underneath the false one I was signing, and she chose to go home, and she let me believe — for thirty years, into her grave and out the other side of it — that my softening had worked. That I had authored her reality and she had lived inside it. She gave me that. She protected me from the one thing she knew would break me, which was not her death. It was knowing that she had seen.

The Deaf always saw the gap. Meg saw it in 1991. Delia saw it in 2009. Irene saw it from the second row. Tobias has seen it his whole life, every mouth that ever decided what he meant. It was never the Deaf who couldn't read the room. It was the room that couldn't read them, and called its own deafness fidelity, and built a chair like this one to make it official.

My whole vow was a headstone over a grave with no body in it. *Never soften* was the wrong lesson because the softening was never the crime — the crime was believing I could author invisibly, that a hand could pass through a life and leave no print, that there was a faithful render and I was it. There was never an *exactly.* There was never glass. Meg knew that in her kitchen when I was twelve. She spent the rest of her life waiting, with the patience of a woman who taught the language, for me to find it out.

I find it out in a chair in the well of a courtroom, thirty years late, with a man dying twelve feet away because nobody ever taught me in time.

* * *

"Ms. Okafor-Vance." Lockwood's voice, bringing me back. Gentle. He has given me the silence and now he takes me to the end of the direct. "In your expert opinion — what did Tobias Rein say at fourteen twenty-two."

And this is the answer. The true one. The one that is the opposite of an answer.

"I don't know," I say. "I rendered it for nine days and I have studied it for four months and four of the best readers alive to this case sat down together last week and put it under every light we have, and we don't know. Not *I can't tell the court.* We cannot tell. The construction does not resolve, because the language it was built in does not contain the resolution — because a hearing world took the signing out of his hands when he was a child so that he would learn to speak nicely, and left him an account of the worst night of his life in a language with no word for *toward* and no word for *away.*"

I look at the bench. I make myself look at Adeyemi and not at Tobias, because if I look at Tobias I will not finish.

"There is no faithful render of what he said, Your Honor. There was never going to be one. Not by me, not by Delia, not by anyone, because his hands do not hold a sentence — they hold a wound with a knife-shape in it, and the court convicted the wound. It looked at a man it could not hear, and rather than say *we cannot hear this man* — which is the one sentence this building is built never to say — it found the cleanest mouth in the county and made his silence into language a jury could vote on. The language was mine. It was faithful and accurate and beautifully clear, and it bore no relation to a thing that happened in the world, and it is the reason he is in a cage. I am not telling you he is innocent. I am telling you that this court convicted a man it could not hear, and called the not-hearing justice, and the only evidence it ever had of what he meant was a render I authored over a silence — and I am the render, and I am telling you, under oath, that I cannot be trusted, because no one in this room ever could have been."

The room is very quiet. Adeyemi does not gavel it. Inez's fingers have stopped, because I have finally said a thing there is no key for.

I have spent my life being a pane of glass. I sat in this chair to break it, and I have, and the breaking is this: I am not glass. I never was. I am a hand. Every render I ever made, I made. I authored Tobias Rein into a sentence and I authored Meg into a gentler death and I authored my own guilt into something small enough to carry, and not one of them was a thing passing through me to a place I wasn't responsible for. There is no such place. There was never such a place. There is only the hand, and what it decides, and the rest of someone's life on the other side of the decision.

"Nothing further," Lockwood says, and sits.

* * *

For one second the room holds, the way a wave holds at the top before it knows which way it is going to fall, and in that second I let myself look at Tobias.

He is not signing. His hands are still on the armrests, even the left one, the tremor gone quiet for a moment the way a clock stops when you finally hear it. He is looking at me, and there is no triumph in his face and no forgiveness either, because he told me at Grafton he is not the priest, he is the guy I ran over, and the guy you ran over does not absolve you. But he gives me one thing. He lifts his right hand off the armrest, slow, careful, and he makes the small flat sign I have seen ten thousand Deaf hands make to ten thousand interpreters at the end of a hard day, the highest and the plainest thing one of them ever says to one of mine — *clean* — and then, because he is Tobias Rein and he has been read wrong his whole life and he will not let even this be clean, he tilts the hand a degree, the width of one finger, into the sign that is almost the same and means its opposite, and lets me see him do it, and lets me see that he knows I can read the difference, and that the difference is the whole of what we are: *not clean. True.*

I have no render for it. There is no place to put the render. So I hold his eyes and let him see that I see it, the only honest thing I have, worth almost nothing, and I give it anyway.

And then Kate Donovan stands up.

She does not hurry. She uncaps her pen, finally, the small click of it loud in the quiet, and she squares her notepad, and she looks at me with something that is not unkindness, which is so much worse, and she lets the silence run one beat longer than it needs to so that the whole room understands that everything I have just said, every true and ruinous word of it, she is about to pick up by the one corner that unmakes it.

"Ms. Okafor-Vance," she says. "You've just told this court, under oath, that no render is faithful. That you cannot be trusted." A beat. The width of one finger. "Then I have only one question for you, and I'd like you to take your time."

She sets her hand flat on the rail, the way you'd steady yourself before a long fall, the way I set mine.

"You're testifying right now through your own mouth, in your own words, no interpreter between you and this court. So tell us." Her eyes do not leave me. "Why should we believe a single word of it?"


# Chapter Twenty-Nine — Faithful to What

Here is the thing no one tells you about the chair until you are in it: the lag does not stop.

I have spent twenty-six years three seconds behind another person's hands, riding the back of the wave, and I told myself that the lag was the cost of the work, a thing I did *for* the witness, a discipline. It is not. It is the shape of being inside a sentence you did not start. And it does not let go of you because you climbed into the box and the words are now your own. Donovan stands up and uncaps her pen, and I feel the lag arrive the way it always arrives — the gap opening between the question coming at me and the answer I do not yet have — except the body in the box is mine, and the sentence forming in the air is the rest of my life, and I am three seconds behind it, holding my breath in the doorway, waiting to see the whole floor before I can speak.

She doesn't hurry. She never hurries. That is the first thing I have to survive: she is not going to be cruel, and not-cruel is the hardest cross there is, because the jury — there is no jury, there is Adeyemi, one woman on a bench who reads the briefs — because *Adeyemi* will forgive cruelty in a witness and will not forgive it in the lawyer, and Donovan knows that, and so she is going to be reasonable at me until I come apart on the reasonableness like a man drowning in a calm sea.

"Ms. Okafor-Vance." A beat. The width of one finger. "I want to make sure I have your testimony right, because it's important, and because you of all people will appreciate the value of getting a person's words exactly right."

She lets that sit. There it is — the knife, handed to me handle-first, and I am supposed to take it and cut myself on the courtesy.

"You've told this court that there is no such thing as a faithful render."

"I've told this court that there was no faithful render of what Tobias Rein said at fourteen twenty-two."

"No." Gentle. "You said more than that. Let the record reflect — you said, and I wrote it down because it seemed to matter, *there is no faithful render. There was never going to be one.* Was that a statement about one moment on one tape, Ms. Okafor-Vance, or was it a statement about your profession?"

* * *

This is where the lag becomes a danger, because in the lag is the trap, and the trap is everything I am.

The answer is *right there.* The clean answer, the accurate one, the one that protects me. *I misspoke; I was speaking to the specific construction; in the general run of cases a competent interpreter renders faithfully and this court can rely on—* I can feel the sentence assembling itself in my hands the way water finds the low place. It is a *true* sentence, or true enough, and it is grammatical, and it would resolve the ambiguity Donovan has built into her question, and it is exactly, precisely, to the millimeter, the thing I have done ten thousand times: take a construction that does not resolve and make it resolve, because the room needs it resolved, because a clean sentence is what a room can use. I could render my way out of this. I am the best in the building at rendering my way out of things.

And that is the trap. The instinct that says *be more accurate, be more careful, draw the distinction finer, out-precise her* — that instinct is the instrument that broke Tobias Rein. Donovan is not trying to make me imprecise. She is trying to make me *do the thing I do,* one more time, on the stand, in front of the only reader left — to take the unrenderable and render it, to smooth my own testimony into a sentence she can then convict me on. Her whole cross is a séance, and I am the medium, and she is asking the medium to speak so cleanly that the room forgets the medium can be wrong.

I take the three seconds. I let them be visible. Adeyemi looks up from her notes, because a three-second silence from a witness is a long time, and a three-second silence from *this* witness, the interpreter who is famous for never hesitating, is a thing she has never seen.

"Both," I say.

Donovan's pen stops.

"It was a statement about one moment on one tape. And it was a statement about my profession. They are the same statement. That is what I came here to say."

* * *

She recovers in half a second — she is good, she is so good, I would have requested her if she'd ever signed — and she does the only thing that works against a witness who has stopped defending herself, which is to make the witness's honesty into the witness's hanging rope.

"All right." She sets the pen down. She is not going to need it now; she has what she wanted, which is me, agreeing. "Then let's follow your statement where it goes. You're a certified interpreter. You've interpreted — how many criminal proceedings, over twenty-six years? Hundreds?"

"I don't keep the count."

"Hundreds." She lets the number stand without my help. "And in every one of them, a Deaf witness, or a Deaf defendant, reached the jury *through your hands.* Through your render. The render you have now sworn, under oath, can never be faithful." She turns, not to me, to the room, to the bench, the way you turn when the next sentence is not a question but a wall you are building. "Ms. Okafor-Vance. If no render is faithful — then every Deaf person you have ever interpreted was tried on a record you authored. Convicted, some of them. Acquitted, some of them. On *your* words. Not theirs."

"Yes."

"So you're not just telling this court that Tobias Rein's conviction is unsafe." Her voice does not rise. It does the opposite; it gets quiet and level and terrible, the voice you use for the thing that ends the argument. "You're telling this court that *no Deaf person in the history of this county has ever received a fair trial,* because every one of them was rendered by a human hand that, by your own sworn testimony, cannot be trusted to be faithful. You are not asking me to vacate one conviction. You are asking me to find that the courthouse cannot hear. That a Deaf defendant *cannot be tried.* That we should — what. Stop? Close the doors? Tell every Deaf victim in Cuyahoga County that their attacker walks because the only way we could ever try him is through hands you've just told us don't work?"

The room is very still. Lockwood has gone the particular gray of a man watching his own case become the prosecution's. This is the knot. This is the thing the book of my life has been building toward and the thing there is no answer to, and Donovan is not a villain, she is *right,* she is more right than Pryor was ever right, because Pryor was right by self-interest and Donovan is right by the plain machinery of the world: if the instrument is unreliable, then nothing the instrument ever carried can be believed, *including the testimony I am giving right now,* and she is about to say so.

"And one more thing," she says, "since we're being exact." A beat. She has saved this. "You're testifying right now. Through your own mouth. No interpreter between you and this court. And you have just told us, under oath, that you are an unreliable instrument for converting a person's meaning into a record." She spreads her hands — not a flutter, nothing mocking, just the simple geometry of *there it is.* "So by the logic of your own testimony, Ms. Okafor-Vance — why should this court believe a single word you have said today?"

* * *

The lag.

I am three seconds behind my own ruin and the sentence has finally finished arriving and I can see the whole floor of it now, every wall, and there is no door. She is correct. That is the thing I have to hold in my hands without dropping it: she is *correct.* An instrument that testifies to its own unreliability has, by that testimony, disqualified the testimony. It is a closed circle. It is the most elegant thing anyone has ever built against me, and it is built out of nothing but the truth I came here to tell, and for one second — I will put this on the record of my own head, the way I put everything — for one second I want to take the out, the accurate out, *but interpretation is different from testimony, Counsel, a render is a translation under time pressure and this is direct narration of my own observed experience, the two are not the same,* and it would *work,* it is a real distinction, it is a *good* distinction, it is a true thing that would let me climb out of the box still partly clean—

And I feel Meg.

Not a vision. I don't have visions; I model things, I have spent my life refusing the consolations of people who see their dead. But I feel the specific weight of her kitchen, the summer I was twelve, her hands making my name into a shape — the R at the chin and the small precise inch sideways, *the one who finishes other people's sentences, be careful what you finish* — and I feel the other thing, the thing I learned three weeks ago in a chair like this one, which is that she *saw.* She watched my nineteen-year-old face soften her own death and she let me believe I'd gotten away with it, for thirty years, because she loved me, and because she knew the lesson I would draw was the wrong one. *Never soften.* I built a cathedral on it. And the cathedral was wrong, not because softening is safe but because the whole architecture assumed there was a true render somewhere underneath, a faithful version I had failed to deliver — and there wasn't. There was never an *exactly.* The Deaf always saw the gap. It was the hearing who couldn't. And the only thing I have, the only thing that has ever been true in my hands, is to stop pretending the gap isn't there.

Donovan is asking me to be an instrument again. To climb out clean. To draw the fine distinction that makes my testimony reliable, and therefore makes me a faithful render of myself, and therefore lands me right back inside the lie that started all of this.

I am not going to do it.

"You shouldn't," I say.

* * *

Adeyemi's pen comes up off the page.

"You shouldn't believe a word I said *because I'm reliable,*" I say. "I'm not. I just told you I'm not, and I meant it, and Ms. Donovan is right that you can't take a single sentence of my testimony as the faithful, neutral, trustworthy account of what happened — because there is no such account, and I am not capable of being one, and I never was, and neither is anyone. That's not my failing as a witness. That's the truth I came to put on the record." I keep my hands flat on the rail. I am aware of them. For twenty-six years I made my hands disappear; now I am aware of every place they touch the wood. "So don't believe me because I'm faithful. I have nothing left to offer you as a faithful instrument. The only thing I can offer this court is the opposite — I can tell you, on the record, that I authored the version Tobias Rein was convicted on. Not transmitted. *Authored.* I made a choice, in a chair like this one, fifteen years ago, with three seconds and a man's life and a construction in his hands that did not resolve, and I resolved it, and I gave the room a clean sentence it could convict on, and I called it transmission, and I went home and slept. That is not testimony you have to *trust.* That is a confession. You don't believe a confession because the man is reliable. You believe it because of what it costs him to make it."

"Ms. Okafor-Vance—"

"You asked the wrong question, Counsel." I am not supposed to say that. A witness does not tell the lawyer the question is wrong; I have spent my career voicing other people who were not allowed to say it, and now no one is voicing me, and I am going to say it. "You asked whether any Deaf person can be tried if no render is faithful. And I can't answer that. It's a real question and it doesn't have a floor and I have lain awake at it. But it isn't the question in this room. The question in this room is not *can a Deaf person be tried.* The question is whether *this* court, in two thousand nine, *could have known* what Tobias Rein said. And the answer to that one isn't philosophy. It's a fact, and the fact is no."

* * *

"Move to strike," Donovan says, but there's no heat in it, because she's heard it too — she's heard the floor move under her own knot — and Adeyemi says, "Overruled, she may finish," in the flat way a judge says it when she has stopped taking notes and started listening, which is the most dangerous thing a judge can do to either side.

"It is not a question of fidelity in the abstract," I say. "It is a question of access in the particular. The court took the Certified Deaf Interpreter off his trial on the second day to save a per-diem. Delia Hart told the prosecutor and the bench, in that room, that the render was unreliable — and the only mouth available to carry her objection to the bench was *mine,* the very mouth she was objecting to, so I voiced her warning faithfully and accurately and it arrived as one more thing I was competently passing through. The court built a room with one channel and silenced everyone who could have told it the channel was broken. And then it looked at a man whose own language had been taken out of his hands as a child, a man with no first language, a man who has never once in his life said a thing and had it arrive — and rather than say *we cannot hear this man,* it found the cleanest mouth in the county and made his silence into a sentence twelve people could vote on." I look at Adeyemi and only Adeyemi. "You don't have to decide whether any Deaf person can ever be tried. You only have to decide whether *that* trial could have known what it convicted on. It couldn't. Everyone in the room who could read him was either Deaf and ruled inadmissible or hearing and certain they were faithful. I was the certain one. I'm telling you the certainty was the malfunction."

I stop. The lake does its nothing out the seventh-floor glass.

"Vacate it because the court was incapable of knowing. Not because I'm a reliable witness. I'm the least reliable instrument in the building. That's not my defense. That's the evidence."

* * *

Donovan stands there for a moment, and I watch a thing happen on her face that I will think about for the rest of my life, which is that she does not have a follow-up. She is good enough to have one for almost anything, and she does not have one for this, because there is no purchase on a witness who has stopped claiming to be trustworthy — you cannot impeach the credibility of a person who entered her own incredibility as the exhibit. The knot only tightens on someone trying to get out clean. I have stopped trying to get out at all.

She does the professional thing, which is to decline to make it worse. "Nothing further at this time," she says, and sits, and squares her notepad, and I understand that she has not been beaten — the knot is not beaten, it is still standing, *can a Deaf person be tried* is still standing in the middle of the room with no floor under it, and she will use it in her closing, and she will be right to, and the book of this will not give anyone an answer to it because there isn't one. I did not defeat her question. I moved the case out from under it. That is all I had, and it is not victory, and it is enough to make a conviction unsafe, and those are three different things and I am finally, at fifty-one, in a chair, able to tell them apart.

Adeyemi lets me step down. I cross the well — I have crossed it four thousand times and never once like this, not furniture, not a channel, a woman who said her own name on the record and meant it — and I sit at the defense table next to Lockwood, who does not look at me, who is staring at the middle distance with the particular stillness of a lawyer recalculating a case that just stopped being the case he prepared and started being something he does not yet have a name for. In the second row Irene is watching me. Not warm. Irene does not do warm. But she is watching, the way she said she would, *I want to see what you do in the room when you already know the room is deaf* — and I did not become the channel again. I turned around. I don't know if it's enough for her. It isn't supposed to be enough for her. Tobias, at the end of the table, has his right hand flat on the wood and the left one ticking beside it, and he is not signing anything, and his face gives me nothing, because he is the guy I ran over and not the priest, and the guy you ran over does not tell you it's all right.

Adeyemi takes the matter under advisement. She will rule, she says, by the end of the week. The gavel is small and the sound of it is the sound of a door not closing yet.

* * *

The verdict in *Webb* comes down the same afternoon, two floors below, in 4B, in the worst light in the building, and I am not in the room for it because I am not allowed in the room for it, because I am the interpreter who had an *episode,* and so I learn it the way the system prefers to deliver things to me now — secondhand, through a record, after the fact, the way it delivered my own removal.

Bee texts me from the hallway outside 4B where she went to stand because she could not stand not to.

*Guilty. Webb. Just now.*

I am in the seventh-floor hallway with my back against the cold marble and I read it three times. Guilty. Damon Webb put a man through a plate-glass storefront on Lorain Avenue and a jury convicted him, and three Deaf witnesses saw it, and two of them were not in the room when it ended because the trial got continued and re-empaneled around my *episode,* and the one render that mattered — the one I refused to launder, Darnelle's directional verb that would not commit which man moved first — went to the jury through Gareth Pell, who rounds, who renders a whole spatial account of a parking lot as *the vehicles collided* and sits down.

Then a second text. *Lamar's redirect went in clean tho. Pell didn't touch the ID. They got Webb on Lamar + the glass + Darnelle's first statement. Not on the part you flagged.*

And a third, after a minute, because Bee is twenty-seven and cannot leave a thing un-pushed: *I don't know if that means you mattered or didn't. Both? It convicted without the gap you wouldn't cross. Maybe that's the system working. Maybe that's just Webb being guilty in a way even Pell couldn't wreck.*

I stand in the hallway and I cannot make it resolve, and I make myself not resolve it, because resolving it is the thing I am trying to stop doing for the rest of my life. Webb is guilty. He is probably guilty in the plain way — three witnesses, the glass, the man who went backward and not down. The render I refused did not free a guilty man; the case held without it. So maybe my refusal cost nothing. Or maybe it is the first time in twenty-six years a thing I did not author stayed un-authored, a gap left open on the record as a gap, a place where the room had to convict on what it could actually know and not on a clean sentence I handed it — and a guilty man went down anyway, on the truth, without my hand inventing the part the truth didn't have. I will never know which. That is the honest answer and it is unbearable and I am going to have to bear it, because the alternative is to round it into a verdict on myself, clean enough to sleep on, and I am done sleeping on clean things.

Pell took my chair and the machine ran and a man was convicted, and somewhere in the running of it a single gap stayed a gap because I broke once, live, in front of a judge with a campaign, and refused to cross it. The machine did not need me to be faithful. It never did. That is the whole horror and the whole, small, terrible point: it ran perfectly without me, the way it ran perfectly *through* me for twenty-six years, and the only thing my entire fidelity ever added was the part that wasn't true.

* * *

Lockwood finds me in the hallway. He has his coat over his arm and the gray hasn't left him.

"She'll rule by Friday," he says. He doesn't say *we won.* He's too good a lawyer and too tired a man to say a thing he doesn't know. "Donovan's knot is still out there. She'll put it in the brief. *If you grant this, no Deaf defendant in Ohio is safe to convict.* And Adeyemi knows it's a real argument, and she knows the Eighth District is reading over her shoulder, and reversing a fifteen-year-old murder on a structural-access theory with no clean exoneration underneath it is the kind of ruling that ends careers that aren't even hers." He shifts the coat. "I'm not going to tell you it went well. I'm going to tell you it went *true,* and that those are not the same thing, and that you of all people know it."

"I know it."

He looks at me then, finally, the way he looked at me in his office a lifetime ago with a yellowed tape balanced on a banker's box, a man already apologizing with his face for what was in his mouth.

"You stopped defending yourself in there," he says. "I've put a lot of people in that chair. I have never once seen the witness stop defending themselves and have it *help.*" A pause. "It shouldn't have helped. By every rule I know, it should have ended us. I don't understand why it didn't."

I do, but I don't say it, because saying it would be one more clean sentence, and the thing I understand is not clean. It is only this: the machine cannot launder an authorship that names itself. Every other hand in this story said *I only did what I was given* — the scheduler, the prosecutor, the lawyers fighting the unseal, Pryor two floors up with his name on a cup, Pell sitting down after he rounded a man's life into *the vehicles collided.* The whole building runs on hands that pass the weight along until it lands nowhere. And I stood up and put it in my own hands, out loud, and the machine had no slot for that, because the machine is *built* to never have a slot for that. You cannot pass the weight back up a ladder where no one is holding anything. You can only set it down somewhere it can be seen. I set it down on the record. I don't know if it frees him. I know it's the first thing my hands ever did that the building couldn't use.

"Friday," I say.

"Friday." He puts his coat on. At the elevator he stops, half-turned, and he is not a man who softens things, he told me once that he knows softening is my original sin and he won't commit it at me even as a kindness, so what he says is not soft, it is just exact, which from Lockwood is the closest thing to tenderness there is.

"For what it's worth," he says. "The oath you swore this morning. *Faithfully and accurately.*" The doors open. He steps in. "I think that's the first time in fifteen years anyone said a faithful thing in a room about Tobias Rein. And it was you telling them you'd never been faithful once." The doors begin to close. "Funny old word."

And he's gone, down into the building, and I am alone in the seventh-floor hallway with the lake going flat and silver out the tall window, and somewhere below me a verdict is being written on a man I broke and a verdict has just been written on a man named Webb, and I cannot tell anyone which of my hands did good today and which did harm, because they were the same two hands, doing the same thing they have always done — deciding — only this time out loud, where it could be seen, where it would cost.

Friday. A door not closing yet.

I raise my hand to the elevator button and I notice that I notice my hand, and I think: *that is the rest of it. That is what's left. To never again not notice the hand.*


# Chapter Thirty — The Faithful Render

A ruling is a render. I did not understand that until I was on the other side of one, waiting on a woman in a robe to take the unrenderable thing that had happened to Tobias Rein and lay it down in language a higher court could read — and I understood, standing in the seventh-floor hallway on Friday morning with my coat over my arm and nowhere to be, that Adeyemi was about to do to fifteen years of a man's life exactly what I had done to two seconds of it. Take a thing that did not resolve and resolve it. Hand the building a sentence it could use.

The only question left was the one I had spent four months learning to ask. *Faithful to what.*

Friday is the word Lockwood gave me Monday and I have carried it the way you carry a date you did not choose — the way Tobias carried *9:00 a.m., Monday, August eighteenth* up from Grafton in a van. The clerk's office said ten. So at ten I am where the system prefers to keep me now, which is outside the room. I am not counsel. I am not the interpreter; a stranger named Okafor has my position in there, signing low and careful to a man in a wheelchair. I am a witness who has testified and a professional who is, as of Wednesday's letter, no longer one — *the Board will convene to consider the matter of your certification,* which is a sentence that renders a foregone conclusion as a deliberation, the way they all do. I am a person in a hallway. I came anyway. Irene taught me that much: you come to the room, even the rooms you do not own. *Especially* those.

Bee is beside me with her phone face-down on her thigh, which for Bee is a kind of prayer. She has not made a joke in nineteen minutes. I have been counting.

"She's going to say *unsafe,*" Bee signs, low, just to me, her hands close to her body in the way the Deaf sign when a thing is too important to broadcast. "If she gives it to us. That's the word. Not *innocent.* Not *exonerated.* *Unsafe.*"

"I know what the word is."

"I know you know." A beat. The width of one finger. "I'm saying it out loud so you don't let yourself hear a different one when it comes."

* * *

The doors open at 10:40 and it is Lockwood who comes out first, which tells me nothing, because Lockwood's face has been the same gray since June and the law has finally caught up to his color. He sees me. He does not smile. He crosses the hall the way you cross a room when the thing in your mouth is too heavy to carry fast.

"Vacated," he says.

I hear the word and I do the thing I promised Bee I would not do, which is reach for a cleaner one. *Won. Free. Over.* I feel them assemble in the low place where water goes, three smooth sentences I could lay down over the rough one Lockwood just handed me, and I let them go, I make myself stand in the rough one, because the rough one is the true one and I am done laundering verdicts, even the verdicts I wanted.

"On what grounds," I say. Not *is he free.* On what grounds. Tell me the render before you tell me what it means.

Lockwood almost laughs, except his face won't. "On the grounds you gave her." He says it like he still can't believe it held. "Structural language-access failure. Ineffective assistance, in that the deprivation was never raised. And — this is the part that'll get cited, this is the part the Eighth District is going to chew on for a decade — that the conviction rests on a record the court is *unable to verify the State could have produced reliably.* She wrote *the State has not shown, and on this record cannot show, that the trier of fact was presented with an accurate account of the defendant's statements.*" He stops. "She wrote *the court cannot establish what was said.* In a published opinion. With his name on it."

"She didn't write that he's innocent."

"No." Flat. "She wrote the opposite of innocent. She wrote *unknowable.* Which is worse for everyone and better for him, and you knew that going in, and you said it on the stand, and she took it." He shifts the file under his arm — thinner now, a closed thing. "The conviction is vacated. The State can retry."

The hallway tilts a half degree, the way a floor does. "Retry."

"They can. On paper. Fifteen-year-old murder, dead victim, a defendant whose own language can't carry his account, a record their own appellate judge just called unverifiable, and a key State exhibit — the tape — dying across the only two seconds that matter." He lets that sit. "They won't. Donovan already knows they won't. You don't reopen a case to retry it on the same evidence that just got it reversed. They'll let the clock run and decline. But understand me, Ruth, because you of all people will want to hear the precise word and not the kind one." He holds my eyes. "He is not acquitted. He is not cleared. He is *un-convicted.* The State will simply stop. There's a difference, and you taught me to care about it, so I'm not going to pretend it away for you on the day you'd most like me to."

"I don't want you to."

"I know you don't." Almost gentle. "That's the only thing about you the building could never use."

* * *

They bring him out at 11:15.

I have imagined this moment in a dozen shapes over four months, and every one of them was wrong, because every one of them had a door in it — a heavy door, a man walking through it into light, the cliché the whole genre of my hope was built on. There is no door. There is a corridor and a deputy and a wheelchair, and the wheelchair is the thing I did not let myself imagine, because the wheelchair says what the opinion would not: that *vacated* is a word about a record and not a word about a body, and the body has been fifteen years in a hearing prison with no interpreters and a heart they did not watch and a cancer they found late because no one could ask him where it hurt, and the body does not get vacated. The body keeps the sentence the paper struck.

Eleanor Rein is not here to see her son un-convicted. She is eighty-one in a memory-care wing in Parma and she does not always know what year holds her, and on the days she surfaces she asks for the boy she taught to speak nicely, and they tell her he is coming, because it is kinder than the truth and because the people who care for her have learned, the way the whole world learned around Tobias, that a gentle render is easier to give than a true one. I think about that. I think: *there it is again. The softening. It never stops. It is the water the whole world runs downhill toward.* And I think: *and I am standing in a hallway about to do it myself,* because every cell in me wants to go to him and make my face say *we won,* make my hands say *it's over, you're free,* render this morning into something a person can stand in — and it is not that, it was never going to be that, and the kindest thing I have, the only honest thing, is to not pretend it is.

So I do not make my face say *we won.*

The deputy wheels him to a stop in the wide part of the hall where the light comes off the lake through the tall glass, and Okafor-the-stranger steps in beside him out of professional habit, ready to be his hands, and Tobias lifts one finger off the armrest — *no* — and she steps back, because even now, especially now, he will choose who renders him, and the answer is no one. He looks at me.

I go to him. I crouch so my eyes are level with his eyes, the way you do not do with a man you pity and the way you must do with a man whose language lives in the visual field, and I wait, because it is his frame, it has always been his frame, I only ever borrowed it.

His hands come up. They are slower than June. The left one drifts and corrects. And he signs to me — not for any record, there is no record here, there is no Inez, there is no key, there is only the two of us and the width of one finger between every true thing and its forgery — and what he signs is built in the deprived, gapped, fingerspelled, idiosyncratic, entirely-his language that no stranger's hands will ever carry, and I am not a stranger, I am the woman who got it wrong on tape, and so I can read it, and I will not pretend I read all of it, because that is the lie I am retiring.

He sets the courtroom behind him with a flick of the eyes, *that, back there, done,* and then he puts something in the space between us, low, close — he fingerspells slow, twice, the way Lamar fingerspelled forty feet at me a lifetime ago: F-R-E-E. And then he does the thing that is the whole of him, the thing that out-thinks me one last time the way he has out-thought me since the visiting room at Grafton when he interrogated his own interpreter and found her guilty. He takes the word he just spelled and he holds his hands flat and open in the freed-space — and then he closes them. Slowly. Into the shape of a man holding something that is not there. He looks at his own closed empty hands. He looks at me. And he tilts his head the few degrees that, on a Deaf face, is not a gesture but a grammar, the marker that turns a statement into its own question, and he lets me see it, and he lets me see that he knows I can read it: *free — to hold what?*

I have no render for it. There is no place to put the render. He has taken the cleanest word in the English language, the word the whole morning was supposed to be *about*, and he has shown me its empty hands. *They are letting me out of the cage. Into what. Into a world that still cannot hear me. Into a life I cannot tell anyone, in any language, because the language was taken when I was a child and there is no faithful render of me and there never will be, and you, of all the mouths that ever decided what I meant, you are the only one who knows it, and knowing it is worth almost nothing, and you are going to give it to me anyway.*

"Yes," I sign. Just that. *Yes.* I do not soften it and I do not enlarge it. I do not tell him it gets better and I do not tell him I am sorry, because sorry is a render of my feeling and this is not about my feeling, Delia taught me that, the cost goes on the man and not on the instrument's grief. I only tell him that I see the empty hands, that I am not going to pretend they are full, that the one thing I have left after thirty years of being the mouth that decides is the refusal to decide this into something prettier than it is.

He holds my eyes a moment. Then he does the small flat sign from the chair four days ago — *clean* — and again, because he is Tobias Rein and he will not let even this be clean, he tilts it the width of one finger into the one that means its opposite, and lets me catch the difference, *not clean, true,* and I catch it, and that is the last thing he ever signs to me, and the deputy wheels him toward the elevators, toward a halfway placement in a hearing facility forty minutes from a Deaf world he was raised never to belong to, toward whatever a body does with the years a courthouse took and a published opinion cannot give back.

He does not look back. Why would he. The Deaf goodbye is the long one, the one that takes ten minutes at a door because no one wants to be the one who ends it. He does not give me the long one. He gives me his back, which is its own grammar, and I let him, because I ran him over, and the man you ran over does not owe you the door.

* * *

Irene comes out last, in her coat, because Irene Stillwater does not take her coat off in a room she does not own and she has never once owned this one.

She stops in front of me. She does not soften her face — Irene's face is the most honest instrument in this building and she has spent fifteen years aiming it at me like a verdict — and she signs, fast and flat and economical, the regional idiom I am still always a half-beat behind in the wild, *the one who finishes other people's sentences* now standing in front of the one person in Cuyahoga County she could never finish.

*You turned around,* she signs.

It is not warm. It is not absolution. It is a fact, entered into the record of her own eyes, the way she enters everything: *I watched, the way I said I would. You did not become the channel again. You turned around.* And then, because Irene gives nothing it does not cost the receiver to take, she adds the part that keeps it from being a gift.

*Once.* Her hands stop on it. *You turned around once, in one room, after fifteen years, with a man already half-dead and a tape already half-gone and nothing left to lose by telling the truth.* She lets the *nothing left to lose* hang, because she means it, because the worst and truest thing about my testimony is that I gave it from a chair where honesty had finally become the only move that could possibly work. *Don't mistake that for a clean thing.* The old machinery in me turns over — *but I —* and she watches it turn over, the way she has watched it the whole time, and the watching is the thing the building cannot survive, and the sentence dies in my hands before I lift it. *I'm not here to tell you you did good,* she signs. *I'm here because Tobias is out and I wanted to see the lake do nothing out the window of a building that finally had to say, on paper, that it couldn't hear him.* A pause. *That's mine. Not yours. I waited fifteen years for the building to say it, and it said it because a hearing woman finally translated her own deafness into English it could read — and I am never going to be free of how that's the only way it was ever going to happen.*

That is the sentence I will keep. Not *you turned around.* The other one. *That's the only way it was ever going to happen* — that the room could not hear the Deaf people who knew the truth in 2009, could not hear Delia's hands or Irene's hands or the half of the gallery that read Tobias clean, and only believed it when the certified hearing instrument climbed into a chair and rendered the deafness back to it in its own language, which means the séance held all the way to the end. The court convicted its own deafness in 2009 and acquitted it in 2025 and it took a hearing mouth to do both, and Irene knows it, and she is going to be right about it for the rest of her life, and there is no version of this morning that is a victory for her. A man she loves is out of a cage and broken, and the only key that ever fit the lock was the same kind of hand that built it.

*Faithful to what,* I think. *To her? I was never faithful to her. The whole machine was built so she would never be the one believed.*

She reads it off my face — of course she reads it off my face, the Deaf always read the face, it is the hearing who keep their grammar where it can't be seen — and she does not soften it for me and she does not let me drown in it either. She is too exact for either. She signs one more thing, and it is the closest to mercy Irene Stillwater will ever come, which is to say it is not mercy at all, it is just true:

*Now you know what your hands are. So watch them.*

And she turns and goes, not the long goodbye, the short one, the one that is also a grammar, and Bee exhales beside me like a woman setting down a box she has carried a long way, and I stand in the cold light and let the lake do its nothing through the tall window, and I do not render the moment into anything. I let it be the rough shape it is.

* * *

Here is the part the morning will not give me, and I make myself name it, because not-naming it would be a softening, and I am the woman who softened a prognosis and a confession and called both fidelity, and I am done.

Pryor is two floors up. There is a banner by the cafeteria with his face on it, GERALD PRYOR, a slogan about keeping families safe, and the banner is still there, because vacating the conviction did not vacate the campaign. What I gave the court — the dropped CDI, the per-diem, the objection rendered into noise, the willed framing of a deprived man as *evasive*, *deceptive*, the choice to win clean on a record he knew was maimed — went into a published opinion that names a *court* and a *process* and a *system*, and names no man. It cannot. That is the thesis I lived: the harm had no author the law can convict, because the law is the machine that launders authorship until it lands nowhere, and the man who made the willed choice in 2009 made it the way every instrument makes its choice, deniably, reasonably, one hand passing the weight up a ladder where no one is holding anything. *I tried the case the system handed me. The interpreter was certified. The jury convicted, not me. I'm a lawyer, not a linguist.* He will say it, if anyone asks, and it will be half-true, and half-true is the most dangerous thing there is, and it is enough to keep his face on the banner.

He is *tainted.* The opinion exists; the press will find it; an opponent will cut an ad. *Tainted* is not *punished.* The campaign may survive it. He will not sit in a chair like mine and have a competent stranger take his account apart one clause at a time, because he was too smart in 2009 to leave a fingerprint and too smart now to come watch the print get lifted, and the building is *built* to protect exactly that smartness — to have no slot for a hand that does not name itself. Delia walked out of court interpreting fifteen years ago carrying a guilt the system gave her and never lifted. Irene waited fifteen years for an apology the system will never sign because there is no one to sign it. The scheduler who solved a budget she could not see the shape of will retire with a clean record. Pell, who rounds, who turned a man's whole spatial account into *the vehicles collided* and sat down, is hireable Monday; I am not. The machine grinds on. One man got out of it. That is the whole verdict and it is not justice, it is the absence of one particular injustice, and those are different things, and I can finally tell them apart.

I do not know whose hand did it. That is the truth I leave the building with, the investigation that does not close. Many hands. A procedure. A budget. A man with a campaign. A certified instrument who was the best in the county at making broken things sound whole. The opinion calls it *the court*, which is the politest name for *everyone, and therefore no one,* and the politeness is the crime, and I helped write it, faithfully, for twenty-six years, one clean sentence at a time.

* * *

I am at the elevator with my coat on when the clerk catches me — not the clerk who rebuilds my name wrong every morning, a younger one, harried, with a lanyard and a problem.

"Ms. Okafor-Vance?" She gets the stress wrong too. I do not correct her. "You're — you do the sign language?" She does not wait for me to answer; the building still thinks of me as a piece of equipment, and the equipment has not been formally unplugged, the letter said *will convene,* future tense, deniable, a render of a firing as a meeting. "There's a man at intake on three, his wife's in a hearing on a protection order and the interpreter they scheduled is stuck in Lakewood, and she's trying to tell the magistrate something and nobody can — could you just—"

She does not finish. She does the small thing with her own hands that hearing people do, the little flutter, the cartoon of a language, meaning *you know, the — the hands thing,* the exact gesture Cantwell made at Lamar in a courtroom a lifetime ago to tell twelve strangers that what a Deaf man does with his body is vague, is gesture, is not quite language, is not quite trustworthy — and the clerk does not mean it that way, she means *please, a woman is trying to be heard and there is no one,* and that is the whole of it, that is the entire machine in one harried girl with a lanyard: the contempt and the need, wearing the same flutter of fingers, asking me to come be the channel one more time.

And I stand in front of the open elevator with my coat on and my career convening itself into a foregone conclusion two floors up, and I understand that this is the question. Not Donovan's knot — Donovan's knot is still out there, *can a Deaf person be tried,* still standing in the middle of the room with no floor under it, and the book of my life is not going to hand anyone an answer to it because there isn't one. This is the smaller question, the one that is actually mine, the one I have to answer not in a published opinion but in a hallway, with my own two hands, this morning, now: *a woman is trying to be heard and there is no one. Do you carry it across?*

For twenty-six years the answer was instant and proud and it was *yes, of course, I am the one who carries it across without dropping it, I add nothing, I take nothing, I am the pane of glass the sentence passes through, and a pane of glass cannot be guilty of the weather.* I built a cathedral on that sentence. The cathedral is rubble. There is no glass. There is no carrying-across that does not also decide. If I go down to three and stand between that woman and the magistrate, I will not transmit her — I will *author* her, I will take whatever her hands hold and resolve it into English a court can use, and some of what I resolve will be the width of one finger off, and the off part will become the only record there is, and I will have decided the weight of a thing that was never mine, again, the way I have decided ten thousand times, the way the whole building runs on instruments deciding and calling it transmission.

And if I do not go down, a woman trying to be heard is not heard at all.

That is the knot, made small enough to hold. There is no clean hand. There was never a clean hand. The choice is not *be faithful or unfaithful* — there is no faithful, faithful was always the lie. The choice is to go down to three knowing my hands decide, and to never again pretend they don't. To carry it across with my eyes open. To be the hand that says, every time now, in the only honest oath left: *I will render this, and the render will be mine, and I will be responsible for it, and that is not the same as faithful, and I am done saying it is.*

* * *

I raise my right hand.

Not to swear. There is no clerk to swear me, no card, no box, no judge — just a hallway and the cold lake light and a harried girl waiting for me to be a wire. I raise my right hand the way I have raised it four thousand mornings to take the oath, the gesture older than the insight, the body remembering the shape before the mind decides the meaning. *I will faithfully and accurately interpret.* Twenty-two words. The only sentence in the building I once believed completely.

And I hold the hand there, raised, in the gap, three seconds behind a sentence I have not yet begun, in the lag that never lets go, the doorway where I have spent my whole life waiting to see the whole floor before I speak — and for the first time in twenty-six years I do not let the next motion come automatic. I do not let the hand fall into the work it has always known. I hold it in the air, where I can see it, where it can be seen, and I make myself ask it, the way Tobias made me ask everything, the way Meg waited my whole life for me to ask: not *will I be faithful,* but the question underneath it, the one the whole machine is built to keep me from ever saying out loud with a clean conscience and a raised hand.

*Faithful to what.*

I notice that I notice my hand. I notice the cold of the marble through my shoes and the weight of the coat on my arm and the girl's flutter still hanging in the air and the lake going flat and silver and indifferent, and I notice my hand, raised, the instrument, mine, deciding, about to.

I do not answer her. I do not answer it for the record, because there is no record here, and because an answer would be a render, and the one thing I have finally learned to leave unrendered is the only true thing I own.

My hand is in the air.

I have not yet decided what it will say.

For the first time in my life, I know that the deciding is mine — and I let myself stand in the not-yet, with the hand raised and the sentence unspoken and the woman waiting to be heard, and I do not call it glass, and I do not let it fall, and I do not pretend I am only carrying it across.

I am the hand.

I keep it up.
